# 墨子

**Mozi**

**Text Title:** *Mozi*
Traditionally attributed to Mo Di. Fifteen juan in total.
Based on the text from the *Daoist Canon of the Orthodox Tradition*, Taiqing Section.

**Mozi, Juan One**
**1. Cherishing the Worthy (Part One)**

To enter a state and not preserve its worthy men is to court its ruin. To see the worthy and not act with urgency is to neglect one’s lord. Without the worthy, there is no urgency; without the *shi*, there is no one with whom to deliberate on the state’s affairs. That a state could survive while neglecting the worthy and forgetting the *shi* has never been known.

In ancient times, Duke Wen fled into exile yet rectified the world; Duke Huan left his state yet commanded the feudal lords; King Goujian of Yue endured the humiliation of King Fuchai of Wu, yet still became one of the worthy rulers of the Central States. These three men achieved fame and success throughout the world precisely because they had suffered humiliation and great disgrace in their own states.

The highest level is to avoid defeat; the next is to suffer defeat yet still achieve success—this is what it means to employ the people.

I have heard it said: It is not that there is no secure dwelling, but that I have no secure heart. It is not that there is no sufficient wealth, but that I have no contented heart. Thus, the noble person makes things difficult for himself and easy for others, while the common people make things easy for themselves and difficult for others.

The noble person advances without compromising his resolve; he examines his true feelings within. Though mingled with the mediocre, he harbors no resentment, for he has confidence in himself. Therefore, one who does what is difficult will surely attain what he desires; I have never heard of one who pursues only what he desires and yet avoids what he detests.

Thus, flattering ministers harm their lord, and sycophantic subordinates harm their superiors. A lord must have ministers who refuse to yield; a superior must have subordinates who speak bluntly. Those who engage in endless debate and those who merely quarrel—how can they ensure long life and preserve the state?

If ministers value their ranks and titles yet remain silent, those near at hand will be benighted, and those far away will be estranged. Resentment will take root in the hearts of the people, flattery and obsequiousness will surround the ruler, and good counsel will be blocked—then the state is in peril.

Did Jie and Zhou lack men of the world? They brought ruin upon themselves and lost the world. Therefore, it is said: To return a state’s treasures is not as good as presenting the worthy and advancing the *shi*.

Now, if there are five awls, the sharpest will be the first to break. If there are five knives, the most finely whetted will be the first to wear down. Thus, sweet wells are the first to run dry; tall trees are the first to be felled; sacred tortoises are the first to be scorched; divine serpents are the first to be exposed.

Therefore, the choking of Bi Gan was due to his uprightness; the killing of Meng Ben was due to his courage; the drowning of Xi Shi was due to her beauty; the dismemberment of Wu Qi was due to his deeds. Thus, those who excel in any way rarely escape death because of their strengths. Hence, it is said: Excess is difficult to sustain.

Therefore, even a worthy lord does not cherish ministers without merit; even a loving father does not cherish a son who brings no benefit. Thus, one who cannot bear the responsibilities of his position does not deserve that position; one who cannot bear the duties of his rank does not deserve that emolument.

A fine bow is difficult to draw, yet it can reach high and penetrate deep. A fine horse is difficult to ride, yet it can bear heavy burdens and travel far. A fine talent is difficult to command, yet it can bring honor to its lord and win respect.

Thus, the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers do not reject the small streams that fill them, and so they become great. The sage, in his affairs, does not refuse; in his dealings with things, he does not oppose. Thus, he becomes a vessel for the world.

Therefore, the waters of the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers do not come from a single source; a robe of a thousand *yi* is not made from the white fur of a single fox.

How can there be a case where one adopts the same method yet fails to achieve the same result? This is not the Way of the Universal King. Thus, Heaven and Earth do not shine with dazzling brilliance; great waters do not surge with mere torrents; great fires do not blaze with flickering flames; and the king’s virtue is not confined to Yao-like radiance. Only then can one become the leader of a thousand men. Straight as an arrow, level as a whetstone—this alone is insufficient to encompass all things. Therefore, narrow streams dry swiftly; shallow currents exhaust quickly; scorched lands yield no harvest. If these three—deep virtue, unobstructed flow, and fertile soil—do not extend beyond the palace, they cannot nourish the state.

**Cultivating the Self (Second Chapter)**
Though the gentleman engages in battle with formations, courage is his foundation; though mourning follows ritual, grief is its essence; though the scholar pursues learning, conduct is its root. Thus, if the root is not firmly planted, do not strive to flourish the branches. If those near are not won over, do not seek to attract those afar. If kin do not draw close, do not pursue external alliances. If affairs lack beginning and end, do not multiply undertakings. If a matter is not thoroughly examined, do not spread its fame.

Therefore, the former kings in governing the world first scrutinized the near to reach the far. The gentleman examines the near and cultivates it. Seeing misconduct, facing slander, he reflects upon himself. Thus, resentment diminishes and conduct improves.

Malicious words should not enter the ears; harsh sounds should not leave the mouth; the impulse to harm others’ children should not dwell in the heart. Even if there are slanderous people, they will have no foothold. Thus, the gentleman’s strength in affairs grows daily, his aspirations deepen, and his vigor flourishes.

The Way of the gentleman: in poverty, he manifests integrity; in wealth, he manifests righteousness; in life, he manifests love; in death, he manifests grief. These four virtues cannot be feigned—they must be embodied. What is stored in the heart must not exhaust love; what moves the body must not exhaust reverence; what issues from the mouth must not exhaust gentleness. Flowing through the four limbs, penetrating the skin and flesh, even as hair whitens and the temples turn purple, he does not abandon them—such is the sage alone.

One whose will is not strong will not attain wisdom. One whose words are not trustworthy will not achieve resolute action. One who clings to wealth and cannot share with others is unworthy of friendship. One who does not steadfastly uphold the Way, whose knowledge is narrow, whose judgment of right and wrong is unclear—such a person is unfit for companionship.

If the root is not firm, the branches will surely wither. One who is bold but does not cultivate himself will later grow indolent. If the source is turbid, the flow will not run clear. One whose conduct lacks sincerity will see his reputation dissipate. Fame does not arise spontaneously, nor does praise grow of itself. Only when merit is achieved and reputation established can fame and praise not be feigned—they must be embodied.

One who speaks much but acts slowly, though eloquent, will not be heeded. One who boasts of his strength and claims credit for achievements, though laborious, will not be rewarded. The wise discern with the heart but do not speak excessively; they are strong but do not boast of their merits. Thus, their fame and praise spread throughout the world.

Do not strive for quantity in speech but for wisdom; do not strive for ornamentation but for discernment. For one who possesses wisdom but lacks discernment in himself, his emotions will lead him astray.

Goodness that does not reside in the heart will not endure; conduct that is not distinguished in oneself will not stand. Fame cannot be achieved through simplicity, nor can praise be established through cunning. The gentleman carries his conduct upon his person. One who seeks profit while neglecting reputation, who forgets fame in pursuit of gain—such a person has never been fit to serve as a scholar in the world.

**The Influence of Association (Third Chapter)**
Master Mozi said: Seeing one who dyes silk, he sighed and said, “Dyed in indigo, it becomes indigo; dyed in yellow, it becomes yellow. What enters changes, and its color changes with it. After five immersions, it will have five colors. Thus, dyeing cannot be taken lightly.”

It is not only silk that is so—states, too, have their influences. Shun was influenced by Xu You and Bo Yang; Yu by Gao Yao and Bo Yi; Tang by Yi Yin and Zhong Hui; King Wu by the Grand Duke and the Duke of Zhou. These four kings were rightly influenced, and so they ruled the world, became Son of Heaven, and their merit and fame covered Heaven and Earth. When the world’s benevolent and righteous are mentioned, these four kings must be named.

Jie of Xia was influenced by Gan Xin and Tui Dong; Zhou of Yin by Chong Hou and E Lai; King Li by Li Gong Changfu and Rong Yi Zhong; King You by Fu Gong Yi and Cai Gong Gu. These four kings were wrongly influenced, and so their states were ruined, their persons perished, and they became a disgrace to the world. When the world’s unjust and shameful are mentioned, these four kings must be named.

Duke Huan of Qi was influenced by Guan Zhong and Bao Shu; Duke Wen of Jin by Jiu Fan and Gao Yan; King Zhuang of Chu by Sun Shu and Shen Yin; King Helü of Wu by Wu Zixu and Wen Yi; King Goujian of Yue by Fan Li and the Grand Minister Zhong. These five lords were rightly influenced, and so they became hegemons over the feudal lords, and their merit and fame were transmitted to later generations.

Fan Jishe was influenced by Chang Liu Shuo and Wang Zhu; Zhonghang Yin by Ji Qin and Gao Qiang; King Fuchai of Wu by Wangsun Xiong and the Grand Steward Pi; Zhibo Yao by Zhi Guo and Zhang Wu; Shang of Zhongshan by Wei Yi and Yan Chang; Kang of Song by Tang Yang and Xian Buli. These six lords were wrongly influenced, and so their states were ruined, their persons executed, their ancestral temples destroyed, their lineages extinguished, their ministers scattered, and their people fled. When the world’s greedy, violent, and oppressive rulers are mentioned, these six lords must be named.

What is it that makes a ruler secure? It is his adherence to principle, and his adherence to principle lies in right influence. Thus, the good ruler labors in selecting men and is at ease in governing his officials. The incapable ruler harms his body and exhausts his spirit, worries his heart and wearies his mind, yet his state grows ever more perilous, and his person ever more disgraced. These six lords did not fail to value their states or love their persons—they simply did not know the essentials.

Those who do not know the essentials are those whose influences are misaligned. Not only states have influences—scholars, too, have influences. If one’s friends all cherish benevolence and righteousness, are sincere, cautious, and respectful of the law, then one’s household prospers day by day, one’s person grows ever more secure, one’s name ever more honored, and in office one attains the proper order—such were the likes of Duangan Mu, Qinzi, and Fu Yue. If one’s friends all delight in arrogance and aggression, in scheming and factionalism, then one’s household declines day by day, one’s person grows ever more imperiled, one’s name ever more disgraced, and in office one loses the proper order—such were the likes of Zixi, Yi Ya, and Jian Diao. The *Odes* says: “One must choose what one embraces; one must be vigilant in what one embraces.” This is what it means.

**Modeling the Standard, IV**
Master Mozi said: Those who engage in affairs under Heaven cannot be without a standard. There are none whose affairs succeed without a standard. Even the most eminent scholars who become generals or ministers all have standards, and even the humblest artisans who ply their trades all have standards. Artisans square things with the carpenter’s square, circle them with the compass, align them with the plumb line, and level them with the hanging weight. Whether skilled or unskilled, all take these five as their standard. The skilled can meet the mark, while the unskilled, though they may not meet it, still follow it in their work and thus surpass their own efforts. Therefore, all artisans have a standard by which they measure. Now, those who govern all under Heaven or rule great states yet lack a standard by which to measure—this is not as discerning as the artisans.

What, then, should be taken as the standard for governance? Should all model themselves after their parents? There are many parents under Heaven, but the benevolent are few. If all modeled themselves after their parents, this would be modeling after what is not benevolent. To model after what is not benevolent cannot serve as a standard. Should all model themselves after their teachers? There are many scholars under Heaven, but the benevolent are few. If all modeled themselves after their teachers, this would be modeling after what is not benevolent. To model after what is not benevolent cannot serve as a standard. Should all model themselves after their rulers? There are many rulers under Heaven, but the benevolent are few. If all modeled themselves after their rulers, this would be modeling after what is not benevolent. To model after what is not benevolent cannot serve as a standard. Thus, parents, teachers, and rulers—none of these three can serve as a viable standard for governance.

What, then, should be taken as the standard for governance? Therefore, it is said: Nothing is better than modeling after Heaven. Heaven’s conduct is vast and impartial, its bestowals generous yet without claim of virtue, its illumination enduring and unwaning. Thus, the sage-kings modeled themselves after it. Having taken Heaven as their standard, in all actions and undertakings, they measured themselves against Heaven. What Heaven desired, they did; what Heaven did not desire, they ceased. Yet what does Heaven desire, and what does it abhor? Heaven surely desires that people love and benefit one another, and does not desire that they hate and harm one another.

How do we know that Heaven desires people to love and benefit one another, and does not desire them to hate and harm one another? Because it universally loves and universally benefits them. How do we know that Heaven universally loves and universally benefits them? Because it universally possesses and universally nourishes them. Now, under Heaven, no state is too small or too great—all are Heaven’s domains. No person is too young or too old, too noble or too base—all are Heaven’s subjects. Thus, none fail to raise sheep, rear dogs and swine, prepare fine millet wine and sweet liquor, and offer rich sacrifices to reverently serve Heaven. Is this not universally possessing and universally nourishing them? If Heaven indeed universally possesses and nourishes them, how could it not desire that people love and benefit one another?

Therefore, those who love and benefit others will surely receive Heaven’s blessings, while those who hate and harm others will surely incur Heaven’s calamities. Those who kill the innocent will meet with misfortune. How could it be that when people harm one another, Heaven would grant them blessings? Thus, Heaven desires that people love and benefit one another, and does not desire that they hate and harm one another.

In ancient times, the sage-kings Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu universally cared for the hundred clans under Heaven, leading them to revere Heaven and serve the spirits. Their benefiting of others was great, so Heaven blessed them, establishing them as Sons of Heaven, and all the feudal lords under Heaven paid them homage. The violent kings Jie, Zhou, You, and Li universally harmed the hundred clans under Heaven, leading them to revile Heaven and insult the spirits. Their harming of others was great, so Heaven brought them calamity, causing them to lose their states, die in disgrace under Heaven, and be vilified by later generations and their descendants—even now, the condemnation does not cease.

Thus, those who do evil and incur calamity were like Jie, Zhou, You, and Li; those who love and benefit others and receive blessings were like Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu. There have been those who loved and benefited others and received blessings, and likewise, there have been those who hated and harmed others and incurred calamities.

**Seven Calamities, V**
Master Mozi said: A state has seven calamities. What are the seven calamities?
1. When the city walls, moats, and pools cannot be defended, yet palaces and halls are constructed—this is the first calamity.
2. When enemy states reach the borders, yet none of the four neighbors come to the rescue—this is the second calamity.
3. When the people’s strength is exhausted on useless works, and rewards are bestowed upon the incapable, so that the people’s strength is spent on what is useless and treasures are depleted on entertaining guests—this is the third calamity.
4. When officials serve for emoluments, wanderers worry about returning, the ruler enforces laws and punishes his ministers, and the ministers are cowed and dare not oppose him—this is the fourth calamity.
5. When the ruler considers himself sagely and wise and does not consult on affairs, deems himself secure and strong and has no defenses, and when the four neighbors plot against him, he remains unaware and unprepared—this is the fifth calamity.
6. When those he speaks to are not loyal, and those he trusts are not faithful—this is the sixth calamity.
7. When grain and legumes are insufficient to feed the people, great ministers are insufficient to serve the state, rewards cannot please, and punishments cannot awe—this is the seventh calamity.

To govern a state with these seven calamities will surely lose the altars of soil and grain; to defend a city with these seven calamities, when the enemy arrives, the state will collapse. Where the seven calamities prevail, the state will surely suffer disaster.

All grains are what the people depend upon, and what the ruler relies on for sustenance. Thus, if the people have nothing to depend on, the ruler has no sustenance; if the people have no food, they cannot be employed. Therefore, the production of food cannot be neglected, the land cannot be left untended, and expenditures cannot be left unchecked. When all five grains are fully harvested, the ruler enjoys all five flavors; if not fully harvested, he does not enjoy them all. If one grain fails, it is called scarcity; if two fail, drought; if three fail, disaster; if four fail, dearth; if all five fail, famine. In a year of scarcity, officials from grand masters downward have their salaries reduced by one-fifth; in a drought, by two-fifths; in a disaster, by three-fifths; in a dearth, by four-fifths; in a famine, salaries are entirely suspended, and only rations are provided. Thus, when disaster and famine afflict the state, the ruler removes five-fifths of his bronze cauldrons for food, grand masters remove their suspended bells, scholars do not attend school, the ruler’s court robes remain unaltered, envoys from the feudal lords and neighboring states are served plain meals without delicacies, glue and leather are removed, roads go unweeded, horses are not fed grain, and concubines do not wear silk—this is the utmost sign of insufficiency.

Now, if a man carrying his child draws water and drops the child into a well, the mother will surely follow to rescue it. Yet when the year is disastrous and the people starve by the roadside, some even abandon their own children—this suffering is worse than the child falling into the well. How can it go unexamined? Thus, in good years, the people are kind and virtuous; in bad years, they are stingy and wicked. What constant nature do the people possess? When those who produce are few and those who consume are many, the year cannot be abundant. Therefore, it is said: when wealth is insufficient, return to the seasons; when food is insufficient, return to frugality. Thus, the ancients generated wealth in accordance with the seasons, secured the fundamentals, and used resources sparingly—then wealth was sufficient. Even the sage-kings of antiquity could not ensure that the five grains always ripened and that droughts and floods never came. Yet why were there no people freezing or starving? Because they exerted themselves in timely labor and practiced self-restraint in sustenance. Thus, the *Book of Xia* states: "Yu endured seven years of floods." The *Book of Yin* states: "Tang endured five years of drought." These were severe calamities indeed. Yet why did the people not freeze or starve? Because they generated wealth diligently and used it frugally.

Therefore, without stored grain, one cannot withstand famine; without stored weapons, even with righteousness, one cannot punish the unrighteous; without complete city walls, one cannot defend oneself; without prepared plans, one cannot respond to emergencies. It is like Qing Ji, who, without the resolve to flee, could not lightly venture out. Jie lacked preparations against Tang, so he was exiled; Zhou lacked preparations against King Wu, so he was killed. Though Jie and Zhou were honored as Sons of Heaven and possessed all under Heaven, they were both destroyed by rulers of a hundred *li*—why? Because they were wealthy and noble yet made no preparations. Thus, preparedness is the state’s weightiest concern. Food is the state’s treasure; weapons are the state’s claws; walls are its means of defense. These three are the state’s essentials. Therefore, it is said: to exhaust the treasury with lavish rewards for the undeserving, to deplete the storehouses for carriages, horses, fine robes, and curiosities, to exhaust the laborers with the construction of palaces and towers for pleasure, and to prepare extravagant coffins and multiple layers of burial garments—while alive, building terraces and pavilions, and in death, constructing grand tombs—this causes the people to suffer externally while the treasury is depleted internally, the ruler never sated with pleasure, and the people unable to bear their hardship. Thus, when the state faces enemies, it is harmed; when the people encounter famine, they perish—all due to the failure to maintain preparedness.

Moreover, food is what the sage treasures. Thus, the *Book of Zhou* states: "A state without three years’ worth of food is no true state; a household without three years’ worth of food has no true heir." This is called state preparedness.

**Chapter on Excessive Expenditure, Part Six**
Mozi said: In ancient times, when the people did not yet know how to build palaces, they dwelled on hills and mounds, living in caves. The dampness below harmed them, so the sage-kings constructed palaces. The standards for palace construction were: height sufficient to avoid dampness, walls sufficient to block wind and cold, roofs sufficient to withstand snow, frost, rain, and dew, and palace walls high enough to distinguish the rites between men and women. Beyond this, no additional expenditures of wealth or labor were made without benefit. Thus, the sage-kings built palaces for the convenience of living, not for ostentation or pleasure. They made clothing, belts, and shoes for bodily comfort, not for extravagance. By moderating their own conduct and instructing the people, the people of the world could be governed, and resources could be made sufficient.

Today’s rulers, however, build palaces differently. They heavily levy taxes on the people, violently seize their clothing and food, constructing palaces, terraces, and pavilions with winding views, adorned with blue and yellow carvings—such extravagance causes their attendants to imitate them. Thus, their wealth is insufficient to withstand famine or relieve orphans and widows, leaving the state impoverished and the people difficult to govern. If a ruler truly desires the world to be well-ordered and hates disorder, he must practice moderation in palace construction.

In ancient times, before the people knew how to make clothing, they wore animal skins and bound themselves with straw. In winter, these were not light yet warm; in summer, not light yet cool. The sage-kings deemed this inadequate for human needs, so they instructed women to weave and labor, repairing city walls—thus, the people toiled without harm. By maintaining regular governance and collecting taxes, the people spent without suffering. What truly afflicted the people was not this.

The people suffered from heavy levies of silk and hemp, bundled cloth and silk, all to make garments for them. The method for making clothing was this: in winter, layered silk was sufficient to be both light and warm. This was all that was needed. Thus, the sages made clothing to fit the body, harmonize with the skin, and suffice—it was not to dazzle the eyes or impress the foolish. At that time, sturdy chariots and fine horses were not prized, nor were carvings and embroidery admired. Why? Because the Dao guided them thus. Therefore, when the people’s resources for clothing and food were such that each household could withstand drought, flood, or famine, why was this so? They understood the essence of self-sustenance and were not deluded by external things. Thus, the people were frugal and easy to govern, and their ruler spent sparingly and was easily supplied. Storehouses were full, sufficient to meet unforeseen needs; armor and weapons were not worn out, and soldiers and commoners were not overburdened, enough to subdue the disobedient. Thus, the enterprise of hegemons and kings could be realized throughout the world.

Today’s rulers, however, make clothing differently. In winter, they wear light, warm garments; in summer, light, cool ones—all these are already provided. Yet they still heavily levy the people, violently seizing the resources for their clothing and food to make brocade, embroidery, and extravagant robes. They cast gold into hooks and use pearls and jade as ornaments; women labor over embroidery, and men carve and engrave, all to adorn themselves. This does not enhance warmth but merely exhausts wealth and labor, squandering them on useless things. From this, it is clear that their clothing is not for the body but for ostentation. Thus, the people become licentious and difficult to govern, and their rulers grow extravagant and hard to admonish. With such extravagant rulers governing such licentious people, to hope for no disorder is impossible. If a ruler truly desires the world to be well-ordered and hates disorder, he must practice moderation in clothing.

In ancient times, before the people knew how to prepare food, they ate raw and lived apart. Thus, the sages taught men to plow, sow, and cultivate, providing food for the people. Their food was only to increase qi, fill hunger, strengthen the body, and satisfy the stomach—nothing more. Thus, they spent sparingly, lived frugally, and the people were wealthy while the state was well-ordered.

Today, it is not so. Heavy levies are imposed on the people to prepare fine foods—fattened livestock, roasted and grilled fish and turtles. Great states pile up a hundred vessels; small states, ten. The spread stretches a square *zhang*, the eye cannot take it all in, the hand cannot grasp it all, the mouth cannot taste it all. In winter, the food freezes; in summer, it spoils. When rulers feast like this, their attendants imitate them. Thus, the wealthy grow extravagant, while orphans and widows freeze and starve. To hope for no disorder is impossible. If a ruler truly desires the world to be well-ordered and hates disorder, he must practice moderation in food and drink.

In ancient times, before the people knew how to make boats and carts, heavy burdens could not be moved, and distant roads could not be reached. Thus, the sage-kings created boats and carts to facilitate the people’s affairs. Their boats and carts were sturdy, light, and efficient, capable of bearing heavy loads and traveling far. They required little expenditure but brought great benefit, so the people delighted in and profited from them. Thus, laws and orders were carried out without urgency, and the people were not overburdened yet had enough. Therefore, the people turned to them.

Today’s rulers make boats and carts differently. Though sturdy, light, and efficient vessels are already provided, they still heavily levy the people to adorn them—embellishing carts with embroidery and boats with carvings. Women abandon their weaving to work on embroidery, so the people grow cold; men leave their plowing and sowing to carve and engrave, so the people go hungry. When rulers make boats and carts like this, their attendants imitate them. Thus, the people suffer both hunger and cold, leading to wickedness and banditry. The more this happens, the harsher the punishments; the harsher the punishments, the more the state falls into chaos. If a ruler truly desires the world to be well-ordered and hates disorder, he must practice moderation in boats and carts.

All things between heaven and earth, within the four seas, the nature of heaven and earth, the harmony of yin and yang—none are not as they are. Even the greatest sages cannot alter them. How do we know this is so? The sages have transmitted: for heaven and earth, there are above and below; for the four seasons, there are yin and yang; for human nature, there are men and women; for birds and beasts, there are male and female. This is the true nature of heaven and earth, which even the former kings could not change.

Though the sages of antiquity surely kept concubines, they did not let this harm their conduct. Thus, the people bore no resentment. There were no sequestered women in the palace, so there were no widowers in the world. With no sequestered women within and no widowers without, the people of the world multiplied.

Today’s rulers, however, keep concubines—great states hold thousands, small states hundreds. Thus, in the world, many men are widowed without wives, and many women are sequestered without husbands. Men miss their time, so the people grow few. If a ruler truly desires a large population and hates scarcity, he must practice moderation in keeping concubines.

These five matters are what the sages practiced frugality and moderation in, and what petty men indulge in to excess. With frugality and moderation, there is prosperity; with excess and indulgence, there is ruin. These five cannot be left unmoderated. When husband and wife are moderate, heaven and earth are in harmony; when wind and rain are timely, the five grains ripen; when clothing is moderate, the skin and flesh are at ease.

**Section Three, Chapter Seven**
Cheng Fan asked Mozi: "The sage-kings did not make music."

In ancient times, the feudal lords wearied of governing and found repose in the music of bells and drums; the high officials wearied of governing and found repose in the music of flutes and zithers; the farmers, who plowed in spring, weeded in summer, harvested in autumn, and stored in winter, found repose in the music of earthen jars. Now you say the sage-kings did not make music—this is like driving a horse without unharnessing it, or drawing a bow without releasing it. Is this not something those with blood and qi cannot endure?

Mozi replied: In former times, Yao and Shun had *Di Qi*, which served as both ritual and music. Tang exiled Jie to the great waters, encircled the world, and established himself as king. With his affairs accomplished and no great future troubles, he created music and named it *Nine Summons*. King Wu vanquished Yin and killed Zhou, encircled the world, and established himself as king. With his affairs accomplished and no great future troubles, he followed the music of the former kings and also created his own, naming it *Elephant*. King Cheng of Zhou followed the music of the former sages and named it *Zhou Yu*. Yet King Cheng’s governance of the world was not equal to King Wu’s; King Wu’s governance was not equal to Tang’s; and Tang’s governance was not equal to Yao and Shun’s. Thus, the more elaborate their music, the less their governance flourished. From this, we see that music is not the means to govern the world.

Cheng Fan said: You claim the sage-kings had no music, yet this too is music. How can you say the sage-kings had no music?

Mozi replied: The sage-kings’ commands were measured in quantity. The benefit of food is known to those who eat when hungry—this is wisdom. Without such knowledge, one is ignorant. Now, if the sages had music but little of it, this too is as good as none.

Bi Yuan states: "馁" should be "馁." *Shuowen Jiezi* says: "矮 means hunger."

In the phrase preceding "欲," the *Xian Gu* and *Jiao Zhu* editions follow Bi Yuan’s suggestion and add the character "雖."

For "止," the *Xian Gu* and *Jiao Zhu* editions follow Bi Yuan’s emendation, based on other versions, to "上."

The *Xian Gu* and *Jiao Zhu* editions, following Wang Niansun’s citation of *Qunshu Zhiyao*, repeat the two characters "奸裹."

After "日," the *Xian Gu* and *Jiao Zhu* editions follow Wang Niansun’s suggestion and add the three characters "夫子曰."

For "自作乐，命曰《九招》," the *Xian Gu* and *Jiao Zhu* editions follow Bi Yuan’s emendation, expanding it to: "又自作乐，命曰《护》，又修《九招》。"

After "乐," the *Xian Gu* and *Jiao Zhu* editions, following Wang Niansun’s emendation, add the four characters "又自作乐," which is correct.

*Mozi*, Scroll Two, "Exalting Worthiness, Part One" (Chapter Eight)

Master Mozi declared: In antiquity, lords and grandees who governed states all desired their nations to prosper, their people to multiply, and their penal administration to be well-ordered. Yet instead of prosperity, they met with poverty; instead of abundance, they met with scarcity; instead of order, they met with chaos. Thus, they lost what they desired and gained what they abhorred—what was the reason for this?

Master Mozi declared: It lies in the fact that lords and grandees governing states could not employ the principle of exalting worthiness and employing ability in their administration. For when a state possesses many men of worth and excellence, its governance is substantial; when such men are few, its governance is meager. Therefore, the paramount task of rulers is to multiply the worthy.

It may be asked: How then shall the multiplication of the worthy be safeguarded?

Master Mozi declared: Consider the case of one who desires to increase the number of skilled archers and charioteers in the state: he must enrich them, ennoble them, revere them, and praise them—only then can the state’s skilled archers and charioteers be multiplied. How much more so for men of worth and excellence, whose virtue and conduct are profound, whose speech and discourse are discerning, and whose mastery of the Dao and its safeguards is expansive? These are indeed the state’s treasures and the altars’ pillars. They too must be enriched, ennobled, revered, and praised—only then can the state’s good men be multiplied.

Thus, the sage-kings of old governed with this decree: "What is unrighteous shall not be enriched; what is unrighteous shall not be ennobled; what is unrighteous shall not be befriended; what is unrighteous shall not be drawn near." When the state’s wealthy and noble heard this, they withdrew and deliberated, saying: "Formerly, what we relied upon was our wealth and nobility. Now the ruler exalts righteousness without shunning poverty and lowliness—thus we cannot but act righteously." When those close to the ruler heard this, they too withdrew and deliberated, saying: "Formerly, what we relied upon was our closeness. Now the ruler exalts righteousness without shunning distance—thus we cannot but act righteously." When those far from the ruler heard this, they too withdrew and deliberated, saying: "Formerly, I thought remoteness left me without reliance. Now the ruler exalts righteousness without shunning remoteness—thus I cannot but act righteously." Even the ministers of distant borderlands, the attendants of the palace courtyards, the masses within the state, and the common folk of the four frontiers—all who heard this vied to act righteously.

What was the reason for this? It is that what the ruler uses to command his subordinates is one thing, and what the subordinates use to serve their ruler is one safeguard. To illustrate with a parable: there is a high wall and a deep palace. The wall is erected with care, and a single gate is hewn at the top. If a thief enters, the gate is sealed behind him, and he is sought within—then the thief has no way to escape. What is the reason for this? The ruler has grasped the essential point.

Thus, in ancient times, the sage-kings governed by ranking virtue and esteeming worthiness. Even among farmers, artisans, and merchants, if one possessed ability, they would elevate him, grant him high rank, bestow upon him generous emolument, entrust him with affairs, and empower him to issue decrees. They declared: "If rank is not exalted, the people will not revere it; if emolument is not ample, the people will not trust it; if decrees are not decisive, the people will not fear them." Conferring these three upon the enlightened was not to reward worthiness for its own sake, but to ensure the success of their undertakings.

At such times, one advanced in rank by virtue, served in office by duty, and was rewarded according to labor, with emolument apportioned by merit. Thus, no office was perpetually noble, nor were the people eternally base; those with ability were elevated, while those without were demoted. Public righteousness was upheld, and private resentments were set aside—this is what such words meant.

Thus, in ancient times, Yao elevated Shun from the southern banks of Fuze Marsh, entrusted him with governance, and the world was at peace. Yu elevated Yi from the midst of Yinfang, entrusted him with governance, and the Nine Provinces were completed. Tang elevated Yi Yin from the kitchen, entrusted him with governance, and his plans succeeded. King Wen elevated Hong Yao and Tai Dian from among the hunters, entrusted them with governance, and the western lands submitted. At such times, even those ministers in positions of generous emolument and exalted rank dared not act without reverence and caution, while even farmers, artisans, and merchants vied to encourage one another and esteem the intent.

Thus, the scholar-officials served as assistants and heirs to the succession. Therefore, when worthy men were obtained, plans were not thwarted, the ruler’s person was not wearied, reputation was established, meritorious deeds were made manifest, and evil did not arise—all because worthy men were obtained.

Therefore, Master Mozi said: "In prosperity, worthy men cannot but be elevated; in adversity, worthy men cannot but be elevated." If one desires to follow the Dao of Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, and the former kings, one cannot but esteem worthiness. For esteeming worthiness is the foundation of governance.

**Chapter Nine: Esteeming Worthiness**
Master Mozi said: Now, the kings, dukes, and great men who rule the people, preside over the altars of soil and grain, and govern the state—if they wish to preserve and not lose their position, yet do not examine esteeming worthiness as the foundation of governance, how can this be known? It is said: When the noble and wise govern the foolish and base, there is order; when the foolish and base govern the noble and wise, there is chaos. Thus, it is known that esteeming worthiness is the foundation of governance.

Therefore, the ancient sage-kings greatly honored and esteemed worthiness, employing the capable without partiality to fathers or elder brothers, without bias toward the noble or wealthy, and without favoritism for beauty. The worthy were elevated and placed above, enriched and ennobled, made officials and leaders. The unworthy were suppressed and dismissed, impoverished and debased, made subordinates and laborers. Thus, the people all encouraged one another with rewards, feared punishments, and followed one another in becoming worthy. With the worthy numerous and the unworthy few, this was called advancing worthiness.

Then the sage listened to their words, observed their conduct, examined their abilities, and cautiously appointed them to office—this was called employing ability. Thus, those capable of governing the state were made to govern the state; those capable of leading offices were made to lead offices; those capable of governing towns were made to govern towns. All those employed to govern the state, offices, and towns were the state’s worthy men.

When worthy men governed the state, they rose early and retired late, hearing lawsuits and administering governance—thus the state was well-ordered and penal laws were just. When worthy men led offices, they slept late and rose early, collecting the profits of passes, markets, mountains, forests, marshes, and weirs to fill the coffers—thus offices were well-supplied and wealth was not dissipated. When worthy men governed towns, they went out early and returned late, tilling the fields, planting crops, and gathering grain—thus grain was abundant and the people had enough to eat.

Therefore, when the state was well-ordered, penal laws were just; when offices were well-supplied, the myriad people were enriched. Above, there was silk and wine for offering to Heaven and the spirits; externally, there were furs and silk for exchanging with the neighboring feudal lords. Internally, there was food to relieve hunger and rest the weary, nourishing the myriad people; externally, there was means to attract the worthy men of the world.

Thus, above, Heaven and the spirits enriched them; externally, the feudal lords allied with them; internally, the myriad people were close to them, and worthy men flocked to them. With this, planning affairs succeeded, undertaking tasks was accomplished, defending within was secure, and punishing without was strong. Therefore, it was only the former Three Dynasties’ sage-kings—Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu—who thus reigned over the world and rectified the feudal lords, for this was their method.

Yet though it is said to follow this method, if one does not know the means to implement it, affairs will remain as if unaccomplished. Therefore, three fundamentals must be established. What are the three fundamentals? It is said: If rank is not exalted, the people will not revere it; if emolument is not ample, the people will not trust it; if decrees are not decisive, the people will not fear them.

Thus, the ancient sage-kings granted high rank, generous emolument, entrusted affairs, and empowered decrees. Was this to bestow gifts upon their ministers? No—it was to ensure the success of their undertakings.

The *Book of Songs* says:
*"I tell you of cares and concerns,
I teach you of the fragrant wine;
Who can grasp heat
Without using cool water?"*
This speaks of how the ancient rulers and feudal lords could not but hold fast to good heirs and assistants—it is like grasping heat without cool water to rinse the hands.

The ancient sage-kings, if they obtained worthy men, employed them, ennobled them with ranks, enfeoffed them with lands, and were never sated. The worthy men, if they obtained enlightened rulers, exhausted the strength of their four limbs to serve the ruler’s affairs and never wearied. If there was goodness and excellence, they attributed it to their superior. Thus, goodness and excellence resided above, while resentments and slander remained below; joy and delight were with the ruler, while sorrows and anxieties were with the ministers. Thus, the ancient sage-kings governed in this manner.

Now, the kings, dukes, and great men also wish to emulate others in esteeming worthiness and employing ability to govern, granting high rank—but emolument does not follow.

If high rank is granted without emolument, the people will not trust it. They will say: "This is not true affection for me—it is merely a pretense to make use of me." How could those used in pretense ever grow close to their superiors? Thus, the ancient kings declared: "Those who feed on governance cannot share tasks with others; those who hoard wealth cannot share emolument with others." If tasks are not shared and emolument is not distributed, then how can the worthy of the world ever come to stand beside kings, dukes, and great men? If the worthy do not come to their side, then the unworthy will remain at their left and right. With the unworthy at their side, their praises will not rightly honor the worthy, and their punishments will not rightly chastise the violent. If kings, dukes, and great men govern their states and families by such means, then their rewards will not rightly honor the worthy, and their punishments will not rightly chastise the violent. If rewards do not rightly honor the worthy and punishments do not rightly chastise the violent, then the worthy will not be encouraged, and the violent will not be deterred. As a result, at home they will not be filial to their parents; abroad they will not respect their elders in the village. Their conduct will lack restraint, their comings and goings will lack measure, and men and women will lack distinction. If put in charge of offices, they will steal; if entrusted with defending cities, they will betray. When their lord faces peril, they will not die for him; when he goes into exile, they will not follow. If tasked with judging lawsuits, their rulings will be unjust; if tasked with dividing wealth, their distributions will be unequal. When consulted on affairs, their counsel will fail; when sent to carry out tasks, they will accomplish nothing. When defending within, their guard will not hold; when punishing without, their force will not prevail. Thus, even the violent kings of the Three Dynasties—Jie, Zhou, You, and Li—lost their states and overturned their altars precisely for this reason. Why? Because they illuminated small matters but failed to illuminate great ones.

Now, when the lords and grandees cannot even make a single robe, they must rely on skilled artisans. When they cannot slaughter a single ox or sheep, they must rely on skilled butchers. Yet when it comes to these two matters, the lords and grandees do not yet know to employ the worthy and capable in governance. Only when their states fall into chaos and their altars face peril do they fail to employ the capable to bring order. Instead, they employ their relatives, or those who have suddenly grown rich and noble, or whose appearance is pleasing. But those who have suddenly grown rich and noble, or whose appearance is pleasing—are they necessarily wise and intelligent? If such are tasked with governing the state, then the state is entrusted to the unwise and unintelligent, and its descent into chaos is already foreseeable.

Moreover, when the lords and grandees favor someone for their appearance and employ them without examining their wisdom, granting office out of affection, they place those who cannot govern a hundred in charge of a thousand, and those who cannot govern a thousand in charge of ten thousand. Why is this? It is said: those who hold office enjoy high rank and generous emoluments, so they are employed out of favoritism. Yet if one who cannot govern a thousand is placed in charge of ten thousand, the office is ten times greater. The principles of governance require daily attention; if the task grows tenfold but the effort does not, if the wisdom does not increase tenfold but the office does, then one part is governed while nine are abandoned. Even if one works day and night to manage such an office, it will remain ungoverned. Why is this? Because the lords and grandees do not understand that employing the worthy and capable is the foundation of governance.

Thus, those who govern by employing the worthy and capable achieve order, as I have said; those who govern by employing the inferior bring chaos, as I have also said. Now, if the lords and grandees truly wish to govern their states, to preserve and secure them without loss, why do they not examine the principle of employing the worthy as the foundation of governance?

Moreover, is the principle of employing the worthy as the foundation of governance solely the teaching of Mozi? It is the Way of the sage-kings, the words of the former kings recorded in the *Ju Nian*, which states: "Seek sagely rulers and wise men to assist and support you." The *Yang Shi* says: "Diligently seek the most sagely, unite with them in strength and purpose to govern the world." These words show that sagacity is not lost when the worthy and capable are employed in governance.

Thus, the sage-kings of old were able to carefully employ the worthy and capable in governance, without admixture of other considerations, so that all under heaven found their proper place. In ancient times, Shun tilled the fields at Li Mountain, made pottery by the He River, and fished at Lei Marsh. Yao discovered him in the marshes of Fuze, raised him to the position of Son of Heaven, and entrusted him with the governance of the world and the rule of its people. Yi Zhi was a private servant to the daughter of the Youxin clan, personally serving as a cook. Tang discovered him and raised him to be his chief minister, entrusting him with the governance of the world and the rule of its people. Fu Yue wore coarse cloth and a hempen belt, laboring as a hired builder at Chuan Rock. Wu Ding discovered him and raised him to be one of the Three Dukes, entrusting him with the governance of the world and the rule of its people. Why did these men begin in lowliness and end in nobility, begin in poverty and end in wealth? Because the lords and grandees understood the principle of employing the worthy and capable in governance. Thus, the people suffered neither hunger without food, cold without clothing, toil without rest, nor chaos without order.

Therefore, the ancient sage-kings carefully employed the worthy and capable in governance, taking the heavens as their model. Though the heavens make no distinction between rich and poor, noble and base, near and far, kin and stranger, they raise up and honor the worthy, while suppressing and dismissing the unworthy. Then who are those who, being rich and noble, were worthy and thus received reward? They are the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties: Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu. And what reward did they receive? In governing the world, they universally loved and benefited the people, leading the myriad peoples to honor heaven, serve the spirits, and love and benefit all. Thus, heaven and the spirits rewarded them, establishing them as Sons of Heaven and fathers and mothers to the people. The myriad peoples praised them as sage-kings, and this praise continues to this day. These are the ones who, being rich and noble, were worthy and thus received reward.

Then who are those who, being rich and noble, were violent and thus received punishment? They are the violent kings of the Three Dynasties: Jie, Zhou, You, and Li. How do we know this? In governing the world, they universally hated and despised the people, leading them to revile heaven and insult the spirits, to scorn and oppress the myriad peoples. Thus, heaven and the spirits punished them, causing them to die and be executed, their descendants scattered, their households destroyed, their posterity cut off. The myriad peoples condemned them as violent kings, and this condemnation continues to this day. These are the ones who, being rich and noble, were violent and thus received punishment.

Then who are those who, though kin, were not good and thus received punishment? They are men like Gun, the eldest son of the Emperor, who was set aside for his lack of virtue and ultimately executed at the wilds of Yu. Even the scorching sun could not reach him, and the Emperor did not love him. These are the ones who, though kin, were not good and thus received punishment.

Then who are those whom heaven employed for their ability? They are men like Yu, Ji, and Gao Yao. How do we know this? The former kings’ book *The Punishments of Lü* records: "The August Emperor inquired of the lower people, and the Miao had a case. It said: 'The drought is due to the excesses below; brightness is not constant, and the widowed and orphaned are not covered.' Virtue’s might is mighty, virtue’s brightness is bright. Thus, the Three Lords were named to relieve the people’s toil. Bo Yi descended with the statutes, and the wise people maintained the punishments. Yu pacified the waters and earth, and named the mountains and rivers."

Ji excelled in sowing seeds, cultivating fine grains. The Three Lords accomplished their works, relying on the people. This speaks of the three sages: they were cautious in speech, prudent in conduct, refined in thought, and sought out the world’s hidden affairs and neglected benefits to serve heaven above—thus heaven turned toward their virtue. Below, they extended it to the myriad people, who received its benefits without end. Therefore, the former kings’ words say: This Dao, when broadly applied to the world, knows no exhaustion; when narrowly applied, it brings no distress. When cultivated and employed, the myriad people receive its benefits without end.

*The Odes of Zhou* records: "The sage’s virtue is as high as heaven, as vast as earth. Its illumination in the world is as firm as the earth, as steadfast as mountains. It does not split, does not collapse—like the sun’s light, like the moon’s brightness, constant as heaven and earth." This speaks of the sage’s virtue: resplendent, luminous, expansive, and enduring. Thus, the sage’s virtue encompasses heaven and earth.

Now, if kings and lords wish to rule the world and govern the feudal lords, without virtue and righteousness, what will they rely upon? Their doctrine must surely grasp awe-inspiring might. Yet where will kings and lords obtain such awe-inspiring might? The people’s ruin is their death. The living desire life intensely, and death they detest above all. When what they desire is unattainable and what they detest arrives repeatedly, from ancient times to the present, none have been able to rule the world and govern the feudal lords by such means.

Now, if lords wish to rule the world and govern the feudal lords, desiring their will to prevail in the world and their names to endure in later generations, they must first examine the foundation of exalting the worthy in governance. This is the profound practice of sages.

**Exalting the Worthy, Part Ten**
Master Mozi said: The kings and lords of the world all desire their states to be prosperous, their people numerous, and their laws well-ordered. Yet they do not recognize that exalting the worthy in governance is the foundation for their states and people. The root error of kings and lords lies in neglecting this foundation. If kings and lords have indeed lost this foundation, can we not illustrate it with examples?

Suppose there were a feudal lord governing his state who declared: "All in my state who are skilled in archery and charioteering, I will reward and honor. Those unskilled in archery and charioteering, I will punish and degrade." Among the knights of his state, who would rejoice and who would fear? I believe the skilled would rejoice, and the unskilled would fear. By rewarding them, I would guide them, saying: "All in my state who are loyal and trustworthy, I will reward and honor. Those disloyal and untrustworthy, I will punish and degrade." Among the knights of his state, who would rejoice and who would fear? I believe the loyal and trustworthy would rejoice, and the disloyal and untrustworthy would fear.

Now, if one governs the state and people by exalting the worthy, it encourages the good and discourages the violent. Applied broadly to the world, it encourages the good and discourages the violent throughout. Why then did I formerly esteem the Dao of Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu? Because they governed the people by facing the multitude, issuing policies that ordered the people, enabling the good in the world to be encouraged and the violent to be discouraged. Thus, exalting the worthy is identical to the Dao of Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu.

Yet today, the scholar-officials of the world, in their dwellings and speech, all exalt the worthy. But when it comes to facing the multitude, issuing policies, and ordering the people, none know to exalt the worthy and employ the capable. From this, I know that the scholar-officials of the world perceive minor matters clearly but fail to grasp the greater ones. How do I know this?

If a king or lord possesses cattle and sheep but cannot slaughter them, he must seek a skilled butcher. If he has garments but cannot tailor them, he must seek a skilled artisan. In such matters, even if they have close kin—unexpectedly wealthy, noble, or fair of face—knowing they are incapable, they do not employ them. Why? They fear the ruin of their property.

Yet when it comes to their states, it is not so. Kings and lords appoint their close kin, the unexpectedly wealthy, noble, or fair of face. Thus, their concern for their states is not as great as their concern for a broken bow, a lame horse, garments, cattle, or sheep. From this, I know that the scholar-officials of the world are clear about minor matters but unclear about major ones. This is like employing the emaciated as envoys or the deaf as music masters.

Therefore, when the ancient sage-kings governed the world, those they enriched and honored were not necessarily their close kin, the unexpectedly wealthy, noble, or fair of face. Thus, Shun plowed at Mount Li, made pottery by the riverbank, fished at Thunder Marsh, and burned shells at Changyang. Yao found him at the southern bank of Fuze and made him Son of Heaven, entrusting him with the world’s governance and the ordering of its people. Yi Yin was a servant to the daughter of the Xin clan and worked as a cook. Tang discovered and elevated him, making him one of the Three Dukes, entrusting him with the world’s governance and the ordering of its people.

Once, Fu Yue dwelled on an isle in the Northern Sea, upon barren soil, clad in coarse cloth with a rope for a belt. He labored as a hired mason, building the walls of Fu Rock. Wuding discovered and elevated him, making him one of the Three Dukes, entrusting him with the world’s governance and the ordering of its people.

Thus, when Yao elevated Shun, when Tang elevated Yi Yin, and when Wuding elevated Fu Yue, did they do so because they were close kin, unexpectedly wealthy, noble, or fair of face? They followed their words, employed their strategies, and practiced their Way—what benefited Heaven above, what benefited spirits in the middle, and what benefited the people below. For this reason, they were promoted and exalted.

The ancient sage-kings, having carefully esteemed worthies and wishing to employ them in governance, inscribed their deeds on bamboo and silk, carved them on basins and bowls, and transmitted them to later generations of descendants.

In the *Lü Xing* of the former kings’ writings, it is thus recorded: The king said, “Oh, come! You who possess states and lands, I declare to you the administration of justice. In the present age, to bring peace to the hundred clans, what words should you choose in selecting men? What reverence should you not apply to punishment? What measure should you not reach? If you can select men and reverently administer justice, the Way of Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu may be attained.”

Why is this so? It is through esteeming worthies that such heights are reached.

In the writings of the former kings, the *Jian Nian* thus states: “Ah! The sagely and martial know men, employing them as screens and aids to their own persons.”

This speaks of how the former kings governed the world: they invariably selected the worthy to serve as their close ministers and assistants. It is said: Now, all the gentlemen and noble men under heaven desire wealth and honor while detesting poverty and lowliness. It is asked: How then may one attain wealth and honor and avoid poverty and lowliness? There is no better way than to become worthy. But how is the Way of becoming worthy to be pursued? It is said: Those with strength should swiftly assist others; those with wealth should generously share with others; those with the Dao should earnestly instruct others. Thus, the hungry will be fed, the cold clothed, and the disordered governed. If the hungry are fed, the cold clothed, and the disordered governed, this is the peace of all living things.

Yet today, the wealth and honor of kings, dukes, and great men are bestowed upon their own flesh and blood—those who, without cause, enjoy wealth and honor, whose faces are fair. Now, these kin of kings, dukes, and great men—those who, without cause, enjoy wealth and honor and whose faces are fair—how can they necessarily possess wisdom? If they lack wisdom and are tasked with governing their states, the disorder of those states may be foreseen.

Now, all the gentlemen and noble men under heaven desire wealth and honor while detesting poverty and lowliness. How then may one attain wealth and honor and avoid poverty and lowliness? It is said: There is no better way than to become kin of kings, dukes, and great men—those who, without cause, enjoy wealth and honor and whose faces are fair. Yet this is not something that can be learned. Even if one lacks discernment, even if one’s virtue and conduct are as profound as those of Yu, Tang, Wen, or Wu, one will not thereby gain favor. Even if the kin of kings, dukes, and great men are deaf, blind, or crippled, even if they suddenly become like Jie or Zhou, they will not thereby lose favor.

Thus, when rewards do not correspond to worthiness and punishments do not correspond to wickedness, those who are rewarded are undeserving, and those who are punished are blameless. This causes the people to lose heart and scatter, to be discouraged from doing good. They withhold the strength of their limbs and do not labor for one another; they let their surplus wealth rot and do not share it; they conceal good teachings and do not instruct one another. Thus, the hungry are not fed, the cold are not clothed, and the disordered are not governed.

Extending this upward, we see that in ancient times, Yao had Shun, Shun had Yu, Yu had Gao Yao, Tang had his humble minister, and King Wu had Hong Yao, Tai Dian, Nan Gong Kuo, and San Yi Sheng. Through them, the world was harmonized, and the common people flourished. Thus, those near were at peace, and those far away submitted. Wherever the sun and moon shone, wherever boats and carts reached, wherever rain and dew moistened, wherever grain nourished—all received this without need for exhortation or praise.

Now, if the kings, dukes, great men, gentlemen, and noble men under heaven truly wish to practice benevolence and righteousness, to become the highest of men, to follow the Way of the sagely kings above and to bring benefit to their states and people below, they must not fail to examine this teaching on esteeming the worthy. For esteeming the worthy is the benefit of Heaven, the spirits, and the people, and the foundation of governance.

Mozi spoke, saying: In ancient times, when the people first came into being and there was yet no ordered government, their speech held divergent meanings. Thus, one person had one sense of right, two people had two senses, and ten people had ten senses. The more numerous the people, the more numerous their notions of right. Each person affirmed their own sense of right and rejected that of others, so they mutually condemned one another. Within, fathers and sons, elder and younger brothers, became resentful and hostile, scattering and unable to unite in harmony. The hundred clans of the world all harmed one another with water, fire, poison, and weapons. Even when they had surplus strength, they did not use it to aid one another; when they had excess wealth and skill, they did not share it; when they possessed good ways, they concealed them and did not teach them. The disorder of the world was like that of birds and beasts.

Now, the reason the world fell into chaos was the absence of rulers and leaders. Therefore, the worthy and capable among the world were selected and established as the Son of Heaven. Once the Son of Heaven was established, considering his power insufficient, he further selected the worthy and capable of the world and appointed them as the Three Dukes. After the Son of Heaven and the Three Dukes were established, given the vastness of the world, the distant lands and foreign peoples, and the myriad distinctions of right and wrong, benefit and harm, which could not be clearly known all at once, the world was divided into ten thousand states, and feudal lords were set up as rulers. Once the feudal lords were established, considering their power insufficient, they further selected the worthy and capable within their states and appointed them as local officials.

When the officials were all in place, the Son of Heaven issued decrees to the hundred clans of the world, saying: "When you hear of good or bad, you must report it to your superiors. What your superiors affirm, you must all affirm; what they reject, you must all reject. If your superiors err, admonish them; if your subordinates do good, recommend them. Those who align above and do not collude below—these are the ones the superiors reward and the people praise. But if you hear of good or bad and do not report it to your superiors, if you cannot affirm what your superiors affirm or reject what they reject, if you do not admonish their errors or recommend the good of your subordinates, if you collude below and do not align above—these are the ones the superiors punish and the people condemn." The superiors used this as the basis for rewards and punishments, making their scrutiny clear and trustworthy.

Thus, the village head was the virtuous person of the village. The village head issued decrees to the hundred clans of the village, saying: "When you hear of good or bad, you must report it to the district head. What the district head affirms, you must all affirm; what he rejects, you must all reject. Abandon your improper words and learn the district head’s good words. Abandon your improper conduct and learn the district head’s good conduct. Then how could the district be disordered?" Examining why the district was well-governed: the district head alone unified the sense of right in the district, and thus the district was governed.

The district head was the virtuous person of the district. The district head issued decrees to the hundred clans of the district, saying: "When you hear of good or bad, you must report it to the feudal lord. What the feudal lord affirms, you must all affirm; what he rejects, you must all reject. Abandon your improper words and learn the feudal lord’s good words. Abandon your improper conduct and learn the feudal lord’s good conduct. Then how could the state be disordered?" Examining why the state was well-governed: the feudal lord alone unified the sense of right in the state, and thus the state was governed.

The feudal lord was the virtuous person of the state. The feudal lord issued decrees to the hundred clans of the state, saying: "When you hear of good or bad, you must report it to the Son of Heaven. What the Son of Heaven affirms, you must all affirm; what he rejects, you must all reject. Abandon your improper words and learn the Son of Heaven’s good words. Abandon your improper conduct and learn the Son of Heaven’s good conduct. Then how could the world be disordered?" Examining why the world was well-governed: the Son of Heaven alone unified the sense of right in the world, and thus the world was governed.

If the hundred clans of the world all aligned above with the One in Heaven but did not align above with Heaven, the old disorder would not yet be gone. Now, when whirlwinds and bitter rains come ceaselessly, this is Heaven’s way of punishing the people for not aligning above with Heaven. Therefore, Mozi said: In ancient times, the sage-kings established the five punishments to govern their people, like the guiding threads of silk and the main ropes of nets, to restrain and gather the hundred clans of the world who did not align above with their superiors.

Mozi said: In the present age, reverting to the time when the people first came into being and had no rulers or leaders, their speech held divergent meanings. Thus, one person had one sense of right, ten people had ten senses, and a hundred people had a hundred senses. The more numerous the people, the more numerous their notions of right. Each person affirmed their own sense of right and rejected that of others, so they mutually condemned one another. Within, fathers and sons, elder and younger brothers, became resentful, all harboring thoughts of separation and unable to unite in harmony. Even when they had surplus strength, they did not use it to aid one another; when they possessed good ways, they concealed them and did not teach them; when they had excess wealth and skill, they did not share it. The disorder of the world was like that of birds and beasts. Without the norms of ruler and subject, superior and inferior, elder and younger, without the rites of father and son and elder and younger brother, the world fell into chaos.

Understanding that the people had no rulers or leaders to unify the sense of right in the world, and thus the world was in chaos, they selected the worthy, good, sagely, wise, eloquent, and discerning among the world and established them as the Son of Heaven, to undertake the task of unifying the sense of right in the world. Once the Son of Heaven was established, considering that his own ears and eyes alone could not unify the sense of right in the world, he selected the assisting, examining, worthy, good, sagely, wise, eloquent, and discerning among the world and appointed them as the Three Dukes, to join in the task of unifying the sense of right in the world.

The Son of Heaven and the Three Dukes having been established, considering the vastness of the world and that the people of distant mountains and remote lands could not be uniformly governed, they divided the world and set up the lords of ten thousand feudal states, charging them with the task of unifying the sense of right within their domains. Once the feudal lords were established, considering that their own ears and eyes alone could not unify the sense of right within their states, they selected the worthy within their domains and appointed them as generals, ministers, and great officers, extending down to the heads of villages and hamlets, to join in the task of unifying the sense of right within their states. The rulers of the Son of Heaven and the feudal lords, the leaders of the people, were thus settled.

The Son of Heaven issued decrees and promulgated teachings, saying: "Whenever one sees or hears of good, one must report it to one’s superior; whenever one sees or hears of wrong, one must likewise report it to one’s superior. What one’s superior approves, one must also approve; what one’s superior disapproves, one must also disapprove. If one has goodness, one should recommend it to one’s peers; if one’s superior has faults, one should admonish them. One should align with one’s superior in sense of right and harbor no intention of colluding with inferiors. Those whom the superior deems worthy shall be rewarded, and all the people, upon hearing of it, shall praise them. But if one sees or hears of good and does not report it to one’s superior, or sees or hears of wrong and likewise does not report it; if one cannot approve what one’s superior approves or disapprove what one’s superior disapproves; if one has goodness but does not recommend it to one’s peers, or if one’s superior has faults but one does not admonish them; if one colludes with inferiors to oppose one’s superior—those whom the superior deems guilty shall be punished, and all the people, upon hearing of it, shall condemn them."

Thus, the sage kings of antiquity in administering punishments, rewards, and commendations were most discerning and meticulous in their scrutiny, ensuring trustworthiness. As a result, all the people under heaven desired to obtain the rewards and commendations of their superiors and feared their censure and punishment. Therefore, the village heads followed the policies of the Son of Heaven and unified the sense of right within their villages. Once the village heads had unified the sense of right within their villages, they led the people of their villages to align with the heads of their townships, saying: "All the people of the village must align with the township head and not dare to collude with inferiors. What the township head approves, they must also approve; what the township head disapproves, they must also disapprove. They must discard unwholesome speech and learn the wholesome speech of the township head; they must discard unwholesome conduct and learn the wholesome conduct of the township head. The township head is indeed the worthiest in the township. If all the people of the township model themselves after the township head, how could the township not be well-governed?" Examining how the township head governs the township, by what means does he do so? It is said: "Only by his ability to unify the sense of right within the township, and thus the township is well-governed."

Once the townships within the domain were well-governed, they led the people of their townships to align with the feudal lord, saying: "All the people of the township must align with the feudal lord and not dare to collude with inferiors. What the feudal lord approves, they must also approve; what the feudal lord disapproves, they must also disapprove. They must discard unwholesome speech and learn the wholesome speech of the feudal lord; they must discard unwholesome conduct and learn the wholesome conduct of the feudal lord. The feudal lord is indeed the worthiest in the state. If all the people of the state model themselves after the feudal lord, how could the state not be well-governed?" Examining how the feudal lord governs the state and achieves good governance, by what means does he do so? It is said: "Only by his ability to unify the sense of right within the state, and thus the state is well-governed."

Once the feudal lord had governed his state and achieved good governance, he led the people of his state to align with the Son of Heaven, saying: "All the people of the state must align with the Son of Heaven and not dare to collude with inferiors. What the Son of Heaven approves, they must also approve; what the Son of Heaven disapproves, they must also disapprove. They must discard unwholesome speech and learn the wholesome speech of the Son of Heaven; they must discard unwholesome conduct and learn the wholesome conduct of the Son of Heaven. The Son of Heaven is indeed the most benevolent person under heaven. If all the people under heaven model themselves after the Son of Heaven, how could the world not be well-governed?" Examining how the Son of Heaven governs the world, by what means does he do so? It is said: "Only by his ability to unify the sense of right under heaven, and thus the world is well-governed."

Yet if one aligns with the Son of Heaven but does not align with Heaven, then the calamities of Heaven will not cease. Thus, when Heaven sends unseasonable cold and heat, untimely snow, frost, rain, and dew, when the five grains do not ripen, the six livestock do not thrive, when plagues and epidemics, whirlwinds and bitter rains arrive in succession, these are the punishments sent down by Heaven to penalize those below who do not align with Heaven. Therefore, the sage kings of antiquity understood what Heaven and the spirits desired and avoided what Heaven and the spirits detested, seeking to promote the benefit of the world and eliminate its harm. Thus, they led the people under heaven to fast, bathe, and purify themselves, preparing clear wine, sweet liquor, and pure grain offerings to sacrifice to Heaven and the spirits. In serving the ghosts and spirits, they dared not be remiss in the purity of their wine, liquor, and grain offerings; they dared not offer lean or scrawny sacrificial animals; they dared not present jade tablets, silk, or cloth that did not meet the proper measures; they dared not miss the proper times for spring and autumn sacrifices; they dared not judge cases unjustly; they dared not distribute wealth unfairly; and they dared not be negligent in their dwellings.

They said: "If the leaders are like this, then when they issue punishments and achieve victory, by what means do they do so?" It is said: "Only by governing through alignment with the superior." Thus, the sage kings of antiquity governed in this manner.

Now, the people of the world say: "In the present age, the blessings of Heaven and the spirits can be obtained. If one can vigorously pursue what is convenient and beneficial for the people, then the affection of the people can be won." Governing in this manner, when they plan affairs, their undertakings succeed; when they defend their territory, it is secure. Above, Heaven and the spirits bestow abundant favor upon their leaders; below, the people enjoy the convenience and benefit of their leaders. If Heaven and the spirits deeply favor them and they vigorously pursue their tasks, then the leaders under heaven will not yet have abandoned their roles, so why is it that the world is in chaos?

Master Mozi said: In the present age, the way of governing through rulers and leaders fundamentally differs from that of antiquity. It is like measuring with the five punishments. In former times, the sage kings established the five punishments to govern the world, yet when the Miao people later applied the five punishments, they threw the world into chaos. Was this because the punishments themselves were not good? No—it was because their use of punishments was not good. Thus, the ancient kings’ text *The Way of Punishments* states: "The Miao people, in their perversity, twisted punishments into mutilation, creating the five deadly penalties, calling them 'the law.'" This shows that those who use punishments well govern the people, while those who use them poorly turn them into instruments of slaughter. Was this because the punishments themselves were not good? No—it was because their use of punishments was not good, and so they became instruments of slaughter.

Thus, the ancient kings’ text *The Way of Decrees* states: "The mouth can utter goodness or provoke war." This means that those who use speech well bring forth goodness, while those who use it poorly create slander, treachery, and strife. Was this because the mouth itself was not good? No—it was because their use of speech was not good, and so it became slander, treachery, and strife.

Therefore, in antiquity, rulers and leaders were established to govern the people.
