They were like the guiding threads in silk or the main ropes in a fishing net, meant to mobilize the world, curb excess and violence, and unify its principles. Thus, the ancient kings’ text *The Way of Yearly Governance* states: "When establishing states and founding capitals, they appointed kings, dukes, and lords. If they acted arrogantly, they were to be restrained; if high ministers and generals acted indolently, they were to be corrected. Only those skilled in governance were to equalize the world." This describes how the ancient gods and spirits established states, capitals, rulers, and leaders—not to elevate their ranks, enrich their emoluments, or grant them ease and luxury, but to enable them to promote benefit, eliminate harm, enrich the poor, aid the few, and bring order to danger and chaos. Thus did the sage kings of antiquity act.

Now, the kings, dukes, and great officials of today administer punishments and governance in a manner contrary to this. They appoint as rulers and leaders those they find convenient, relying on their fathers, elder brothers, old friends, and trusted aides. The people know that those placed in positions of leadership are not truly fit to govern them, and so they all form cliques, conceal their actions, and refuse to align with their superiors. Thus, superiors and inferiors do not share the same principles. If superiors and inferiors do not share the same principles, then rewards and praise are insufficient to encourage goodness, and punishments and penalties are insufficient to deter violence.

How do we know this is so? Consider: if a ruler establishes governance in a state and appoints leaders for the people, saying, "This person is worthy of reward; I will reward them," yet if superiors and inferiors do not share the same principles, then what the superior rewards, the multitude condemns. The people will say, "This person lives among us, and we know them to be unworthy." Thus, even if they receive the superior’s reward, it is not enough to encourage goodness. Or if a ruler establishes governance in a state and appoints leaders for the people, saying, "This person is worthy of punishment; I will punish them," yet if superiors and inferiors do not share the same principles, then what the superior punishes, the multitude praises. The people will say, "This person lives among us, and we know them to be worthy." Thus, even if they receive the superior’s punishment, it is not enough to deter violence.

If a ruler establishes governance in a state and appoints leaders for the people, yet rewards and praise are insufficient to encourage goodness, and punishments and penalties are insufficient to deter violence, then is this not the same as the time before rulers and leaders existed, when people crouched in submission? If having rulers and leaders is no different from having none, then this is not the way to govern the people and unify the multitude.

Therefore, the sage kings of antiquity used alignment with the superior as the principle for establishing rulers and leaders. In this way, the sentiments of superiors and inferiors were communicated without obstruction. If superiors had hidden affairs or overlooked benefits, inferiors could bring them to light and utilize them. If inferiors harbored grievances or accumulated harm, superiors could discover and eliminate them. Thus, even if someone thousands of miles away did good, though their household did not yet know and their village had not yet heard, the Son of Heaven could reward them. Even if someone thousands of miles away did evil, though their household did not yet know and their village had not yet heard, the Son of Heaven could punish them. As a result, all the people under heaven trembled in fear, dared not act with excess or violence, and said: "The eyes and ears of the world are as if divine!"

The words of the former kings state: "It is not divine. It is simply that one can make the ears and eyes of others assist in seeing and hearing, make the mouths of others assist in speaking, make the minds of others assist in thinking, and make the limbs of others assist in acting. When many assist in seeing and hearing, what one perceives and knows extends far. When many assist in speaking, the reach of one’s virtuous influence is broad. When many assist in thinking, one’s plans and deliberations are swift and sound. When many assist in acting, one’s tasks are swiftly accomplished." Thus, the reason the sage kings of antiquity succeeded in their affairs, achieved their goals, and left their names to later generations was no other cause or strange circumstance—it was simply that they were able to govern through alignment with the superior.

Thus, the ancient kings’ text *The Odes of Zhou* states: "They come to see their king, seeking his standards." This describes how the feudal lords of antiquity, in spring and autumn, came to the court of the Son of Heaven to receive his stern teachings. Returning to govern their states, wherever their governance extended, none dared disobey. At that time, there was fundamentally no one who dared to disrupt the Son of Heaven’s teachings. The *Odes* says:
*"My horses are black-maned,
The six reins smooth and supple.
We gallop and drive,
Everywhere seeking counsel and deliberation."*
And again:
*"My horses are fine steeds,
The six reins like silk.
We gallop and drive,
Everywhere seeking plans and strategies."*
This is what it means.

In antiquity, when the feudal lords heard of good or evil, they galloped to report it to the Son of Heaven. Thus, rewards befitted the worthy, punishments befitted the violent, the innocent were not killed, and the guilty were not spared. This was the achievement of alignment with the superior.

Thus Master Mozi declared: Now, the kings, dukes, grandees, and gentlemen of the world—if they truly wish to enrich their states, multiply their people, order their penal governance, and secure their altars of soil and grain—they must examine with utmost care this principle of *shangtong* (alignment with the superior), for it is the very foundation.

*Shangtong, Part Thirteen*
Master Mozi stated: The task of the wise is necessarily this—to deliberate on what brings order to the state and its people and act accordingly, and to deliberate on what brings chaos to the state and its people and avoid it.

Now, what brings order to the state and its people? When those above in governance obtain the true sentiments of those below, there is order; when they do not, there is chaos. How do we know this is so? When those above in governance obtain the true sentiments of those below, they thereby clarify for the people what is good and what is not. If they truly clarify for the people what is good and what is not, then they can reward the good and punish the violent. When the good are rewarded and the violent are punished, the state is necessarily ordered. When those above in governance do not obtain the true sentiments of those below, they thereby fail to clarify for the people what is good and what is not. If they truly fail to clarify for the people what is good and what is not, then they cannot reward the good or punish the violent. When the good are not rewarded and the violent are not punished, governance in this manner will inevitably plunge the multitude into chaos. Thus, the failure to obtain the true sentiments of those below is something that cannot but be examined.

Now, how can one obtain the true sentiments of those below? Master Mozi said: Only by governing through *shangtong* and unifying the standard can this be achieved. How do we know that *shangtong* and unifying the standard can govern the world? Why not carefully examine the governance of antiquity? In ancient times, when Heaven first brought forth the people, there were no rulers or leaders, and the hundred clans acted as individuals. If the hundred clans acted as individuals, then one person had one standard, ten people had ten standards, a hundred people had a hundred standards, a thousand people had a thousand standards—extending to the point where the multitude of people could not be counted, and thus their so-called standards could not be counted either. Each regarded their own standard as right and others’ standards as wrong, leading to those with strength causing harm and those with lesser strength engaging in strife. Therefore, the world desired to unify the world’s standard. Thus, they selected the worthy and established them as the Son of Heaven. The Son of Heaven, considering his knowledge and strength insufficient to govern the world alone, selected the next best and established them as the Three Dukes. The Three Dukes, considering their knowledge and strength insufficient to assist the Son of Heaven alone, divided the land and enfeoffed the feudal lords. The feudal lords, considering their knowledge and strength insufficient to govern their territories alone, selected the next best and established them as ministers and stewards. The ministers and stewards, considering their knowledge and strength insufficient to assist their lords alone, selected the next best and established them as village chiefs and household heads. Thus, in ancient times, the Son of Heaven established the Three Dukes, and the feudal lords, ministers, stewards, village chiefs, and household heads were not chosen merely for their wealth, nobility, or leisure, but to assist in ordering governance and penal affairs. Therefore, in ancient times, when establishing states and founding capitals, they set up kings, dukes, and lords, and appointed ministers, officers, and leaders—not to indulge in pleasure, but to distinguish right from wrong and assist in governance and illumination.

Now, why is it that those above cannot govern those below, and those below cannot serve those above? It is because superiors and inferiors disdain one another. Why is this so? Because their standards differ. If those with differing standards form factions, and the superior regards someone as good, others will slander them. If that person only seeks the superior’s reward and avoids the people’s slander, then those who do good will not necessarily be encouraged, for they see the reward. If the superior regards someone as violent, they will punish them, but that person may only receive the superior’s punishment while harboring the people’s praise. Thus, the violent will not necessarily be deterred, for they see the punishment. Therefore, the superior’s rewards and praise are insufficient to encourage the good, and their slander and punishment are insufficient to deter the violent. Why is this so? Because the desire to unify the world’s standard—how can this be achieved?

Thus Master Mozi said: Why not have the household heads test this by issuing decrees and proclamations to their households, saying: "If you see anyone who loves and benefits the household, you must report it; if you see anyone who harms and plunders the household, you must also report it." If someone reports one who loves and benefits the household, they are like one who loves and benefits the household. The superior will reward them, and the multitude will praise them. If someone sees one who harms and plunders the household but does not report it, they are like one who harms and plunders the household. The superior will punish them, and the multitude will condemn them. Thus, all within the household will desire to obtain their superiors’ rewards and praise and avoid their slander and punishment. Therefore, when good words are spoken, the household head can reward the good and punish the violent. When the good are rewarded and the violent are punished, the household is necessarily ordered. Now, what brings order to the household? It is solely through governing by *shangtong* and unifying the standard.

Yet, if the way to order a household is entirely this, does it suffice for the state? It does not. The households in the world are exceedingly numerous, each regarding their own household as right and others’ households as wrong, leading to those with strength causing chaos and those with lesser strength engaging in strife. Therefore, the household heads are further made to gather their households and align with the feudal lord. The feudal lord also issues decrees and proclamations to the multitude of the state, saying: "If you see anyone who loves and benefits the state, you must report it; if you see anyone who harms and plunders the state, you must also report it. If someone reports one who loves and benefits the state, they are like one who loves and benefits the state. The superior will reward them, and the multitude will praise them."

If one sees those who harm and plunder the state yet fails to report them, they are like those who harm and plunder the state. The superior will punish them, and the multitude will condemn them. Thus, all people in the state desire to obtain the rewards and praise of their superiors while avoiding blame and punishment. Therefore, when the people see good, they speak of it; when they see evil, they speak of it. The feudal lord rewards the good and punishes the violent. When the good are rewarded and the violent are punished, the state will surely be well-governed. Yet, considering why the state is well-governed, what is the reason? It is solely because governance is conducted through aligning with the One Dao/Way.

Now that the state is well-governed, does it suffice for the world? It does not. The states in the world are exceedingly numerous, each regarding their own state as right and others’ states as wrong, leading to those with strength waging war and those with lesser strength engaging in strife. Therefore, the feudal lords are further made to select the righteousness of their states and align with the Son of Heaven. The Son of Heaven also issues decrees and proclamations to the multitude of the world, saying: "If you see anyone who loves and benefits the world, you must report it; if you see anyone who harms and plunders the world, you must also report it. If someone reports one who loves and benefits the world, they are like one who loves and benefits the world. The superior will reward them, and the multitude will praise them. If one sees those who harm and plunder the world yet fails to report them, they are like those who harm and plunder the world. The superior will punish them, and the multitude will condemn them." Thus, all people in the world desire to obtain the rewards and praise of their superiors while avoiding blame and punishment. Therefore, they report both the good and the evil they see. The Son of Heaven rewards the good and punishes the violent. When the good are rewarded and the violent are punished, the world will surely be well-governed. Yet, considering why the world is well-governed, what is the reason? It is solely because governance is conducted through aligning with the One Dao/Way.

Now that the world is well-governed, the Son of Heaven further gathers the righteousness of the world to align with Heaven. Thus, in the doctrine of aligning with the superior, the Son of Heaven who aligns with the One Dao/Way can govern the world. When applied to the feudal lords, it can govern their states; when applied to household heads, it can govern their households. Therefore, when used on a grand scale, it governs the world without deficiency; when used on a small scale, it governs a state or a household without excess. This is what is meant by the Dao/Way. Hence it is said: governing the states of the world is like governing a single household; commanding the people of the world is like commanding a single man.

Is this idea unique to Master Mozi, while the former kings lacked it? It is not so. The sage-kings all governed by aligning with the One Dao/Way, and thus the world was well-governed. How do we know this is so? In the books of the former kings, the *Great Declaration* states: "When petty men see deceit and craftiness yet do not report it, the punishment is equal." This means that those who see depravity and perversion yet fail to report it bear the same guilt as the depraved and perverse. Thus, the sage-kings of antiquity governed the world by selecting those of excellence to assist them as their wings, and those outside who aided their vision and hearing were many. Therefore, when consulting with others on matters, they were the first to understand; when undertaking tasks with others, they were the first to accomplish them. Their virtuous reputation and fame preceded others. They trusted themselves and acted accordingly, thus reaping such benefits.

There is an ancient saying: "One eye’s vision does not compare to two eyes’ vision; one ear’s hearing does not compare to two ears’ hearing; one hand’s grasp does not compare to two hands’ strength." Only by trusting oneself and acting accordingly can such benefits be attained. Thus, when the sage-kings of antiquity governed the world, if there was a worthy person a thousand *li* away, though the people of their village had not equally heard or seen them, the sage-king would reward them. If there was a violent person within a thousand *li*, though the people of their village had not equally seen them, the sage-king would punish them. Therefore, do not assume the sage-king had ears sharp and eyes bright—how could they see clearly to a thousand *li* away with a single glance or hear clearly to a thousand *li* away with a single listening? The sage-king did not go to see nor approach to hear. Yet, why were those who caused chaos, rebellion, theft, and banditry throughout the world left with no place to stand? It was because their governance through aligning with the One Dao/Way was excellent.

Thus, Master Mozi said: "In general, when making the people align with the One Dao/Way, if one does not love the people urgently, the people cannot be employed. It must be said: one must urgently love and employ them, establish trust and uphold it. Lead them with wealth and honor, and guide them with clear punishments. When governance is like this, even those who do not wish to align with me will find it impossible."

Therefore, Master Mozi said: "Now, the kings, dukes, great officials, and gentlemen of the world, if in their hearts they desire to practice benevolence and righteousness, seek to be scholars, aspire to the Dao/Way of the sages above, and benefit the states and the hundred clans below, they must examine the doctrine of aligning with the superior and recognize that aligning with the One Dao/Way is the foundation and essential method of governance."

*End of Scroll Three of Mozi*

If one earnestly attends to what the heavenly spirits deeply favor, then the blessings of the heavenly spirits may be obtained. If one earnestly attends to what benefits the myriad people, then the affection of the myriad people may be obtained. When governance is like this, plans succeed, undertakings are accomplished, defenses are secure, and punitive expeditions prevail.

One asks: "For what reason is this so?"
It is replied: "Only by employing alignment with the superior in governance."

Thus did the sage-kings of antiquity govern in this manner.

Now, the people of the world say: "In the present age, the proper leaders of the world have not yet been abolished."

The sage who takes governing the world as their duty must know from where disorder arises, only then can they govern it; without knowing from where disorder arises, they cannot govern. This is like a physician who treats a person’s illness: they must know from where the illness arises, only then can they treat it; without knowing from where the illness arises, they cannot treat it. How is governing disorder any different? One must know from where disorder arises, only then can they govern it; without knowing from where disorder arises, they cannot govern it. The sage who takes governing the world as their duty cannot but examine from where disorder arises.

Where, then, does disorder arise? It arises from lack of mutual love. When ministers and children are not filial to their lords and fathers, this is called disorder. A child loves only themselves and not their father, thus they harm their father to benefit themselves; a younger brother loves only themselves and not their elder brother, thus they harm their elder brother to benefit themselves; a minister loves only themselves and not their lord, thus they harm their lord to benefit themselves. This is what is called disorder. Even when a father is not kind to their child, an elder brother is not kind to their younger brother, or a lord is not kind to their minister, this is also what the world calls disorder. A father loves only themselves and not their child, thus they harm their child to benefit themselves; an elder brother loves only themselves and not their younger brother, thus they harm their younger brother to benefit themselves; a lord loves only themselves and not their minister, thus they harm their minister to benefit themselves. Why is this? It all arises from lack of mutual love.

Even the thieves and bandits of the world are the same. A thief loves only their own household and not another’s, thus they steal from another’s household to benefit their own; a bandit loves only their own body and not others, thus they harm others to benefit themselves. Why is this? It all stems from lack of mutual love. Even the great officers who bring disorder to other households and the feudal lords who attack other states are the same. Each great officer loves only their own household and not another’s, thus they bring disorder to another’s household to benefit their own; each feudal lord loves only their own state and not another’s, thus they attack another’s state to benefit their own. All the disorders of the world are encompassed in this. From where do they arise? They all arise from lack of mutual love.

If the world were to practice universal mutual love, and each person loved others as they love themselves, how could there be those who are unfilial or unkind? If one regarded children, younger brothers, and ministers as oneself, how could there be those who are unkind or unfilial? If one regarded another’s household as their own, who would steal? If one regarded another’s body as their own, who would harm? Thus, there would be no thieves or bandits. How could there be great officers bringing disorder to other households or feudal lords attacking other states? If one regarded another’s household as their own, who would bring disorder? If one regarded another’s state as their own, who would attack? Thus, there would be no great officers bringing disorder to other households or feudal lords attacking other states.

If the world were to practice universal mutual love, states would not attack one another, households would not bring disorder to one another, there would be no thieves or bandits, and lords, ministers, fathers, and children would all be filial and kind. In this way, the world would be well-governed. Therefore, the sage who takes governing the world as their duty cannot but prohibit hatred and encourage love. Thus, when the world practices universal mutual love, it is well-governed; when it practices mutual hatred, it falls into disorder. Therefore, Master Mozi said: "One cannot but encourage loving others." This is the reason.

**Universal Love, Part Two**

Master Mozi said: The humane person’s duty is to promote what benefits the world and eliminate what harms it. This is their duty. What, then, are the benefits of the world? What are its harms?

Master Mozi said: When states attack one another, households usurp one another, people harm one another, lords and ministers are not kind and loyal, fathers and children are not kind and filial, and elder and younger brothers are not harmonious, these are the harms of the world. Why, then, do these harms arise? Do they arise from lack of mutual love?

Master Mozi said: They arise from lack of mutual love. Now, feudal lords love only their own states and not others’, thus they do not hesitate to mobilize their states to attack others’; household heads love only their own households and not others’, thus they do not hesitate to mobilize their households to usurp others’; people love only their own bodies and not others’, thus they do not hesitate to harm others to benefit themselves. Therefore, when feudal lords do not love one another, they inevitably engage in open warfare; when household heads do not love one another, they inevitably usurp one another; when people do not love one another, they inevitably harm one another. When lords and ministers do not love one another, they are not kind and loyal; when fathers and children do not love one another, they are not kind and filial; when elder and younger brothers do not love one another, they are not harmonious. When the people of the world do not love one another, the strong will overpower the weak, the rich will insult the poor, the noble will disdain the lowly, and the cunning will deceive the foolish. All the disasters, usurpations, grudges, and resentments of the world arise from lack of mutual love. Therefore, the humane person condemns this.

Having condemned it, how should it be changed?

Master Mozi said: It should be changed through the principle of universal mutual love and mutual benefit. How, then, should the principle of universal mutual love and mutual benefit be practiced?

Master Mozi said: Regard another’s state as one’s own, regard another’s household as one’s own, regard another’s body as one’s own. Thus, when feudal lords love one another, they will not engage in open warfare; when household heads love one another, they will not usurp one another; when people love one another, they will not harm one another. The noble will not disdain the lowly, the cunning will not deceive the foolish, and all the disasters, usurpations, grudges, and resentments of the world can be prevented. The humane person praises this.

Thus, when lords and ministers love one another, they are kind and loyal; when fathers and children love one another, they are kind and filial; when elder and younger brothers love one another, they are harmonious. When the people of the world all love one another, the strong will not overpower the weak, the many will not oppress the few, the rich will not insult the poor.

Master Mozi said: Indeed, universal love would be excellent. Yet the difficulty lies in the world’s attachment to old ways. Master Mozi declared: The scholars and gentlemen of the world simply fail to recognize its benefit or discern its cause.

Consider how governing cities and waging wars—even sacrificing one’s life for fame—are tasks the common people find most arduous. Yet if the ruler delights in them, his officers and masses will readily perform them. How much more so with mutual love and mutual benefit, which differ from these? For one who loves others will surely be loved in return; one who benefits others will surely be benefited in return. One who hates others will surely be hated in return; one who harms others will surely be harmed in return. What difficulty could there be? The obstacle lies only in the fact that superiors do not govern by it, and scholars do not practice it.

In former times, Duke Wen of Jin favored his officers’ coarse attire. Thus, Duke Wen’s ministers all wore robes of coarse wool bound with leather belts, and caps of bleached silk. They wore these when entering to see their lord and when attending court. Why was this so? Because the ruler delighted in it, his ministers followed suit.

In former times, King Ling of Chu favored his officers’ slender waists. Thus, King Ling’s ministers all restricted themselves to one meal a day, held their breath to tighten their belts, and leaned against walls to rise. Within a year, the court was filled with those pale from starvation. Why was this so? Because the ruler delighted in it, his ministers were able to do it.

In former times, King Goujian of Yue favored his officers’ courage. He trained and harmonized his ministers, then set fire to a boat and tested his warriors, saying: “All the treasures of Yue are here!” King Goujian personally drummed to urge his men forward. Hearing the drumbeat, they charged in disarray, trampling the flames to their deaths—over a hundred on either side. Then the king sounded the gong to withdraw.

Thus Master Mozi said: To eat little, wear coarse clothes, or sacrifice one’s life for fame—these are things the common people of the world all find difficult. Yet if the ruler delights in them, the masses can do them. How different is this from mutual love and mutual benefit! For one who loves others will surely be loved in return; one who benefits others will surely be benefited in return. One who hates others will surely be hated in return; one who harms others will surely be harmed in return. What difficulty could there be? The obstacle lies only in the fact that superiors do not govern by it, and scholars do not practice it.

Yet today, the gentlemen of the world say: “Indeed, mutual love would be excellent. However, it is an impracticable thing—like lifting Mount Tai to leap across the Yellow River.” Master Mozi said: This is not a proper analogy. To lift Mount Tai and leap across the Yellow River would require exhausting all one’s strength, and from ancient times to the present, no one has ever been able to do it. But mutual love and mutual benefit are different. The ancient sage-kings practiced them.

How do we know this? In ancient times, Yu governed the world. To the west, he opened the Western River and Yu Bao to drain the waters of Qu, Sun, and Huang. To the north, he built the Fang, Yuan, Zhuo, and Zhu embankments, the rear chambers of Hou, and the sluices of Xue Pool. He drilled through Dizhu and carved out Dragon Gate to benefit the people of Yan, Dai, Hu, Mo, and the Western River. To the east, he drained the marshes of Lu and built dikes at Zhuo Ze, dredging the Nine Channels to guide the waters of the eastern lands and benefit the people of Ji Province. To the south, he channeled the Jiang, Han, Huai, and Ru rivers eastward, directing them into the Five Lakes to benefit the people of Chu, Jing, Yue, and the southern tribes. This was Yu’s work, and it is what I now call mutual benefit.

In former times, King Wen governed the western lands. Like the sun and moon, his radiance shone in all four directions and throughout the western lands. He did not allow large states to bully small states, the multitude to oppress the orphaned and widowed, or the violent and powerful to seize the millet, grain, dogs, and pigs of the tillers. Heaven looked down with favor upon King Wen’s compassion. Thus, the elderly without children found care to live out their years; the lonely without brothers found companionship among the living; and the young who had lost their parents found support to grow up. This was King Wen’s work, and it is what I now call mutual love.

In former times, King Wu was about to perform the Feng sacrifice at Mount Tai. In the *Annals*, it is recorded: “O Mount Tai! The virtuous descendant of Zhou, the king, has come to perform the great sacrifice. The great affair accomplished, the benevolent still act to reverently serve the Shang, Xia, Man, Yi, and Mo tribes. Though he had kinsmen of Zhou, they were not like the benevolent. If the myriad regions bore guilt, it would rest with me alone.” This was King Wu’s work, and it is what I now call mutual love.

Thus Master Mozi said: Today, the gentlemen of the world sincerely desire the scholars to be wealthy and hate their poverty; they desire the world to be well-governed and hate its chaos. They should practice mutual love and mutual benefit. This is the method of the sage-kings and the Way to govern the world. It cannot but be diligently pursued.

**Chapter Sixteen: Universal Love (Part II)**

Master Mozi said: The task of the benevolent is necessarily to seek what benefits the world and remove what harms it. Now, in the present age, what is the greatest harm in the world? It is when large states attack small states, great families disrupt small families, the strong plunder the weak, the many oppress the few, the cunning deceive the foolish, and the noble arrogantly despise the humble. These are the harms of the world. Moreover, rulers who are not kind, ministers who are not loyal, fathers who are not compassionate, and sons who are not filial—these are also harms of the world. Furthermore, when people today despise others, wielding blades, poison, water, and fire to mutually injure and kill—these are also harms of the world.

Let us trace the origin of these many harms. Where do they come from? Do they arise from loving and benefiting others? It must be said: No, they arise from hating and harming others. Now, when we distinguish the names in the world—those who hate and harm others—is this universal or partial? It must be partial. Yet this partiality is precisely what gives rise to the world’s great harms. Therefore, partiality is wrong.

Master Mozi said: To reject something, there must be something to replace it. If one rejects something without offering a replacement, it is like using water to put out fire—such an argument will surely fail. Thus Master Mozi said: Universal love must replace partiality.

Why can universal love replace partiality? Suppose one treats another’s state as one’s own. Who would then raise their state to attack another’s? Treating it as one’s own, one would act for the other as for oneself. Suppose one treats another’s capital as one’s own. Who would then raise their capital to assault another’s? Treating it as one’s own, one would act for the other as for oneself. Suppose one treats another’s family as one’s own. Who would then raise their family to disrupt another’s? Treating it as one’s own, one would act for the other as for oneself.

Now, if states and capitals do not attack one another, and households do not disrupt or harm one another—is this a harm to the world or a benefit? It must be said: a benefit to the world. Let us trace back to the origin of all benefits. From where do they arise? Do they arise from hating and harming others? It must be said: no. They must arise from loving and benefiting others. When we distinguish the names throughout the world, is it partiality or universality that loves and benefits others? It must be said: universality. Thus, when universality is practiced in interaction, does it indeed give rise to the greatest benefit in the world? Therefore, Master Mozi said: universality is right.

Moreover, I previously stated: the task of the humane person is necessarily to strive to promote the world’s benefits and eliminate its harms. Now, tracing back to the origin, we see that universality gives rise to the world’s greatest benefits, while partiality gives rise to its greatest harms. Therefore, Master Mozi said: partiality is wrong and universality is right—they differ as clearly as square and round.

Now, I will rightly seek and obtain the world’s benefits, taking universality as the standard. Thus, ears and eyes assist one another in seeing and hearing, limbs are fully strong to act and serve, and those with the Dao freely instruct one another. Therefore, the elderly without wives or children have support to complete their years, and the young, weak, and orphaned without parents have refuge to grow to maturity. Now, if only universality is taken as the standard, such are its benefits.

Yet why do all under heaven, upon hearing of universality, oppose it? What is the reason for this? Nevertheless, the scholars of the world who criticize the words of universality have not ceased. They say: it is good, but can it truly be implemented? Master Mozi said: if it is implemented yet deemed impossible, this too would be a criticism. Moreover, where is there goodness that cannot be implemented?

Let us tentatively advance two men and compare them. Suppose one man holds to partiality and the other to universality. The partial man says: how can I treat my friend’s body as my own, or my friend’s kin as my own kin? Thus, when he retreats and observes his friend, if the friend is hungry, he does not feed him; if cold, he does not clothe him; if ill or diseased, he does not attend to him; if dead, he does not bury him. Such are the words and deeds of the partial man.

The universal man’s words are not so, nor are his deeds. He says: I have heard that to be a noble man in the world, one must treat one’s friend’s body as one’s own and one’s friend’s kin as one’s own kin—only then can one be a noble man in the world. Thus, when he retreats and observes his friend, if the friend is hungry, he feeds him; if cold, he clothes him; if ill or diseased, he attends to him; if dead, he buries him. Such are the aspirations and deeds of the universal man.

Now, between these two, are their words opposed and their deeds contrary? If we make these two men keep their word and act resolutely, so that their words and deeds align like tallying tokens, with no word left unfulfilled—then let me ask: suppose there is a vast plain here, and one dons armor and helmet to go forth, not knowing whether life or death awaits. Or suppose a lord’s minister is sent on a distant mission to Ba, Yue, Qi, or Jing, and it is uncertain whether he will return or not. Let me ask: in such cases, to whom would one entrust one’s household, support one’s kin, and entrust one’s wife and children? Would one entrust them to the universal man or the partial man? I believe that in such a situation, even the foolish men and women of the world, though not universal themselves, would necessarily entrust them to the universal man. If this is so, then to speak against universality while choosing it in practice is to acknowledge its worth.

Yet why do the scholars of the world, upon hearing of universality, still oppose it? Their criticism has not ceased. They say: perhaps one can choose men, but one cannot choose rulers. Let us tentatively advance two rulers and compare them. Suppose one ruler holds to universality and the other to partiality. The partial ruler says: how can I treat the bodies of my myriad people as my own? This is far from the sentiment of the world. Human life upon the earth is but a brief moment—like a team of four horses galloping past a crack. Thus, when he retreats and observes his myriad people, if they are hungry, he does not feed them; if cold, he does not clothe them; if ill or diseased, he does not attend to them; if dead, he does not bury them. Such are the words and deeds of the partial ruler.

The universal ruler’s words are not so, nor are his deeds. He says: I have heard that to be an enlightened ruler in the world, one must first treat the bodies of the myriad people as one’s own, and only afterward attend to oneself—only then can one be an enlightened ruler in the world. Thus, when he retreats and observes his myriad people, if they are hungry, he feeds them; if cold, he clothes them; if ill or diseased, he attends to them; if dead, he buries them. Such are the words and deeds of the universal ruler.

Now, between these two rulers, are their words opposed and their deeds contrary? If we make these two rulers keep their word and act resolutely, so that their words and deeds align like tallying tokens, with no word left unfulfilled—then let me ask: suppose in this year there is a plague, and the myriad people suffer hardship, cold, and hunger, with many dying and their bodies filling the ditches. Between these two rulers, whom would one follow? I believe that in such a situation, even the foolish men and women of the world, though not universal rulers themselves, would necessarily follow the universal ruler.

To speak yet not embrace universality is to choose words and deeds that contradict. Yet why is it that all under heaven hear of universality only to reject it? Nevertheless, the scholars of the world who oppose universality have not ceased, saying: "Universality is indeed benevolence and righteousness. Yet how can it be practiced?" I compare the impracticability of universality to lifting Mount Tai and leaping over the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers. Thus, universality is but a wish—how could it be a realizable thing?

Master Mozi said: Lifting Mount Tai to leap over the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers—from ancient times to the present, since the birth of humankind, this has never been done. Yet as for mutual love and mutual benefit, the former sage-kings—the Six Kings—personally practiced them. How do we know the former sage-kings personally practiced them? Master Mozi said: I did not exist in the same era or time as they, nor did I personally hear their voices or see their faces. I know this from what they inscribed on bamboo and silk, carved into metal and stone, engraved on basins and bowls, and transmitted to later generations.

*The Great Declaration* says: "King Wen was like the sun and moon shining together, illuminating the four directions and the western lands." These words describe King Wen’s universal love for all under heaven, vast and boundless—like the sun and moon illuminating the world without partiality. Thus, King Wen practiced universality. Even the universality of which Master Mozi speaks takes its model from King Wen.

Moreover, not only *The Great Declaration* speaks thus; even *Yu’s Oath* is the same. Yu said: "All you assembled multitudes, heed my words. It is not that I, this humble one, dare to act with reckless disorder. The Miao people are foolish and rebellious, and thus I carry out Heaven’s punishment. As I have led you to battle the drought and march against the Miao..." Yu’s campaign against the Miao was not to seek greater wealth or honor, nor to indulge his ears and eyes, but to promote the benefit of all under heaven and eliminate its harms. Thus, Yu practiced universality. Even the universality of which Master Mozi speaks seeks its model in Yu.

Furthermore, not only the oaths and commands are thus; even *Tang’s Address* is the same. Tang said: "I, this humble Lü, dare to offer a black bull and declare to the Supreme Lord and Sovereign Above: Now there is a great drought, and it is I who bear the blame. I do not dare to conceal goodness nor pardon faults; it is all recorded in the Emperor’s heart. If the myriad regions have faults, let them be upon me. If I have faults, let them not extend to the myriad regions." These words show that Tang, though noble as Son of Heaven and wealthy as lord of all under heaven, did not shrink from offering himself as a sacrifice to pray to the Supreme Lord and spirits. Thus, Tang practiced universality. Even the universality of which Master Mozi speaks takes its model from Tang.

Moreover, not only the oaths, commands, and Tang’s address are thus; even the *Zhou Poems* are the same. The *Zhou Poems* say:
"The kingly Way is vast and boundless,
Impartial and without faction.
The kingly Way is level and smooth,
Without faction or partiality.
Straight as an arrow, smooth as a whetstone,
What the noble man treads, what the petty man beholds."
Do my words not speak of the Way? In ancient times, Kings Wen and Wu upheld justice, equally rewarding the worthy and punishing the violent, without favoring kin or brothers. Thus, Wen and Wu practiced universality. Even the universality of which Master Mozi speaks takes its model from Wen and Wu.

Yet why is it that all under heaven hear of universality only to reject it? Nevertheless, those who oppose universality have not ceased, saying: "Does this not harm the interests of one’s parents and thus fail to be filial?" Master Mozi said: Let us examine the considerations of a filial son who plans for his parents. Do I not know whether a filial son, in planning for his parents, wishes others to love and benefit them, or to hate and harm them? Judging by reason, he must wish others to love and benefit them. Then how should I first act to achieve this? If I first act to love and benefit others’ parents, will they then repay me by loving and benefiting mine? Or if I first act to hate others’ parents, will they then repay me by loving and benefiting mine? It must be that I first act to love and benefit others’ parents, and then they will repay me by loving and benefiting mine. Thus, the exchange of filial sons is indeed unavoidable. Should we not first act to love and benefit others’ parents? Or do we consider the filial sons of the world to be misguided and insufficient as a standard?

Let us examine what the former kings recorded in the *Greater Odes*:
"No word goes unanswered,
No virtue goes unrewarded.
Cast me a peach, and I return a plum."
These words mean that those who love others will surely be loved, and those who hate others will surely be hated.

Yet why is it that the scholars of the world all hear of universality only to reject it? Do they consider it difficult and impossible to practice? Yet there have been things more difficult than this that were possible.

In ancient times, King Ling of Jing favored slender waists. During his reign, the gentlemen of Jing ate no more than one meal a day, rising only by propping themselves up and walking only by leaning against walls. Thus, restrained eating was deemed most difficult to practice—yet once practiced, it pleased King Ling. Though it had not yet spread through the world, the people could be swayed, for they sought to align themselves with their ruler.

Long ago, King Goujian of Yue prized courage. He trained his officers and ministers for three years, yet deemed their understanding insufficient. He set fire to his ships, sounded the drums, and advanced—his warriors fell in the front ranks, perishing in water and flame in numbers beyond count. At that moment, none retreated without the drum’s command; the warriors of Yue could be called resolute. Thus, burning oneself was deemed most difficult to practice—yet once practiced, it pleased King Goujian. Though it had not yet spread through the world, the people could be swayed, for they sought to align themselves with their ruler.

In former days, Duke Wen of Jin favored plain attire. During his time, the gentlemen of Jin wore coarse cloth robes, unlined sheep-fur coats, hempen caps, and straight-soled shoes. They entered to see Duke Wen and departed to attend court in such dress. Thus, plain attire was deemed most difficult to practice—yet once practiced, it pleased Duke Wen. Though it had not yet spread through the world, the people could be swayed, for they sought to align themselves with their ruler.

Therefore, restrained eating, burning ships, and plain attire—these were the most difficult things in the world to practice. Yet once practiced, they pleased the rulers, and though they had not yet spread through the world, the people could be swayed. Why was this? Because they sought to align themselves with their superiors.

Now, as for mutual benefit shared universally, this brings advantage and is easy to practice—its benefits are beyond calculation. Yet I believe there are none among the rulers who advocate for it. If only there were rulers who did, encouraging it with rewards and praise, and enforcing it with penalties and punishments, then I believe people would embrace universal love and mutual benefit as naturally as fire rises upward or water flows downward—impossible to prevent throughout the world.

Thus, universality is the Dao of the sagely kings, the means by which kings, dukes, and great ministers secure their peace, and the reason the common people have sufficient clothing and food. Therefore, the noble person would do well to examine universality and strive to practice it: as a ruler, be benevolent; as a minister, be loyal; as a father, be kind; as a son, be filial; as an elder brother, be affectionate; as a younger brother, be respectful.

Thus, the noble person should desire to be a benevolent ruler, a loyal minister, a kind father, a filial son, an affectionate elder brother, or a respectful younger brother. For universality cannot but be practiced—this is the Dao of the sagely kings and the greatest benefit for the myriad people. *Mozi, Scroll Four ends here.*

Here is the translation:

---

Now, suppose there is a man who enters another’s garden and orchard, stealing their peaches and plums. The multitude hears of it and condemns him; the ruler who governs above, if he apprehends him, punishes him. Why is this? Because he harms others to benefit himself. When it comes to seizing another’s dogs, pigs, geese, or swine, his unrighteousness exceeds that of entering another’s garden to steal peaches and plums. Why is this? Because he harms others all the more, his lack of humaneness grows ever greater, and his crime becomes all the more grave. When it comes to entering another’s stable and pen to take their horses and cattle, his lack of humaneness and righteousness exceeds that of seizing another’s dogs, pigs, geese, or swine. Why is this? Because he harms others all the more; if he harms others all the more, his lack of humaneness grows ever greater, and his crime becomes all the more grave. When it comes to killing an innocent person, stripping them of their robes and furs, and taking their halberds and swords, his unrighteousness exceeds that of entering another’s stable and pen to take their horses and cattle. Why is this? Because he harms others all the more; if he harms others all the more, his lack of humaneness grows ever greater, and his crime becomes all the more grave. In such cases, the rulers of the world all know to condemn it, calling it unrighteous. Yet when it comes to the great act of attacking a state, they do not know to condemn it; instead, they follow and praise it, calling it righteous. How can this be said to distinguish between righteousness and unrighteousness?

Killing one person is called unrighteous, and it necessarily incurs a death penalty. If we apply this reasoning further, killing ten people is tenfold unrighteousness, and it necessarily incurs ten death penalties. Killing a hundred people is a hundredfold unrighteousness, and it necessarily incurs a hundred death penalties. In such cases, the noble persons of the world all know to condemn it, calling it unrighteous. Yet when it comes to the great unrighteousness of attacking a state, they do not condemn it; instead, they follow and praise it, calling it righteous. Truly, they do not recognize its unrighteousness. Thus, they record their words to bequeath to later generations. If they knew it was unrighteous, why would they record its unrighteousness to bequeath to later generations?

Now, suppose there is a man who, having seen little black, calls it black, but having seen much black, calls it white—one would say this man does not know the distinction between white and black. Or suppose one who, having tasted little bitterness, calls it bitter, but having tasted much bitterness, calls it sweet—one would surely say this man does not know the distinction between sweet and bitter. Now, when it comes to minor wrongs, they know to condemn them. But when it comes to the great wrong of attacking a state, they do not know to condemn it; instead, they follow and praise it, calling it righteous. Can this be said to know the distinction between righteousness and unrighteousness? Thus, we know that the noble persons of the world are confused about the distinction between righteousness and unrighteousness.

*Condemning Offensive Warfare, Part Two*

Master Mozi said: In ancient times, when kings, dukes, and great ministers governed the state, they truly desired to scrutinize praise and blame, ensure proper rewards and punishments, and prevent errors in criminal administration. Therefore, Master Mozi said: In ancient times, there was a saying: "If planning does not succeed, then use the past to know the future, use the visible to know the hidden. If planning is like this, it can be known." Now, when troops are mobilized, marching in winter, they fear the cold; marching in summer, they fear the heat—this cannot be done in both winter and summer. In spring, it disrupts the people’s plowing, planting, and cultivation; in autumn, it disrupts the people’s harvesting and gathering. If even one season is disrupted, the number of common people who die from hunger, cold, and starvation will be beyond counting.

Now, let us calculate the military supplies: bamboo arrows, feathered banners, tents, armor, shields, and spears—those that are worn out, broken, and unusable are beyond counting. Add to this spears, halberds, dagger-axes, swords, and war chariots—those that are shattered, broken, and unusable are beyond counting. Add to this the oxen and horses that depart well-fed but return emaciated, or depart and die without returning—their number is beyond counting. Add to this the long and distant roads, where provisions are cut off and rain does not follow, and the number of common people who die is beyond counting. Add to this the lack of stable dwellings, untimely meals, and irregular hunger and fullness, and the number of common people who fall ill and die on the road is beyond counting. The loss of armies is beyond counting; the complete annihilation of armies is beyond calculation. Thus, the number of ghosts and spirits who lose their descendants is also beyond counting.

The state enacts policies that deprive the people of their strength and abolish their benefits to an extreme degree. Yet why do they do this? They say: "I covet the fame of victory in attack and the benefits to be gained, so I do this." Master Mozi said: If we calculate what is gained by victory, it is of no use. If we calculate what is obtained, it does not compare to what is lost. Now, attacking a city of three *li* and an outer wall of seven *li*—if this can be done without sharp weapons, without killing, and merely by seizing it, then so be it. But killing many must number in the tens of thousands, and killing few must number in the thousands, before a city of three *li* and an outer wall of seven *li* can be taken.

Now, a state of ten thousand chariots may empty its numbers by the thousands without victory and retreat; its vast territory may number in the tens of thousands without victory and be abandoned. Thus, land is what they have in excess, while people are what they lack. Now, to exhaust the deaths of the people, to intensify the suffering of high and low, and to contend for empty cities—this is to discard what they lack and further what they have in excess. Governing in this way is not the proper task of the state.

Those who embellish offensive warfare say: "To the south, they carved out the kings of Wu; to the north, they subdued the lords of Qi and Jin. When they were first enfeoffed in the world, the extent of their lands did not reach several hundred *li*, and the multitude of their people did not reach several hundred thousand. Because of offensive warfare, the breadth of their lands reached several thousand *li*, and the multitude of their people reached several million. Thus, offensive warfare cannot be abandoned."

Master Mozi said: Even if four or five states gain profit from it, it is still called not following the Dao. It is like a physician’s medicine for those who are ill. Now, suppose there is a physician who mixes his medicinal incantations for all the ill in the world and administers medicine to them. If ten thousand people take this medicine, and only four or five benefit from it, it would still be called not practicing medicine properly.

Thus, the filial son does not feed his parents through such means, nor does the loyal minister feed his lord. In ancient times, when states were enfeoffed throughout the world, those of earlier times knew through what their ears had heard, and those of later times saw with their own eyes—those destroyed by offensive warfare were beyond counting.

How do we know this to be so? In the east, there was the state of Ju, small in size, which, though situated between great states, failed to respectfully serve them. Those great states, in turn, did not follow Ju’s lead nor seek its benefit. As a result, the Yue people to the east encroached upon and diminished its territory, while the Qi people to the west annexed and took possession of it. The reason Ju perished between Qi and Yue was precisely offensive warfare. Though in the south, Chen and Cai likewise perished between Wu and Yue for the same reason. Though in the north, states like Juyi and Buzhuohe perished between Yan, Dai, the Hu, and the Mo—again, because of offensive warfare.

Therefore, Master Mozi said: In ancient times, the lords and great men desired gain and detested loss, sought security and shunned danger. Thus, offensive warfare cannot but be condemned. Those who embellish the words of offensive warfare say: "They could not rally and employ their multitudes, and so perished. I can rally and employ my multitudes—who in the world would dare not submit to me?"

Master Mozi said: Even if you can rally and employ your multitudes, how do you compare to King Helü of Wu in ancient times? King Helü of Wu trained for seven years, donning armor and wielding weapons, marching three hundred *li* before making camp. He passed through Zhulin, emerged from the perilous paths of Ming’ai, and fought at Baihu, cutting through the heart of Chu to receive the homage of Song and Lu. By the time of King Fuchai, he again attacked Qi, encamping at Wenshang and battling at Ailing, where he crushed the Qi forces and entrenched them at Mount Tai. Turning east to attack Yue, he crossed the Three Rivers and Five Lakes, entrenching them at Kuaiji. The nine Yi states all submitted without exception. Yet afterward, he failed to reward the orphaned, bestow alms upon the common folk, or rely on his own strength. Instead, he boasted of his achievements, praised his own wisdom, neglected his teachings, and proceeded to build the Terrace of Gusu, which remained unfinished after seven years. In this way, Wu fostered discontent and weariness. When King Goujian of Yue saw that the upper and lower ranks of Wu were at odds, he rallied his forces to renew his campaigns. Entering the northern suburbs, relocating the great inner palace, and besieging the royal palace, he brought about the destruction of Wu.

In former times, Jin had six generals, among whom Zhibo was the strongest. Calculating the breadth of his lands and the multitude of his people, he sought to resist the feudal lords, aspiring to the swift renown of offensive warfare. Thus, he meticulously selected his crack troops, arrayed his chariots and soldiers, and attacked the Zhonghang clan, seizing their lands. Confident in his schemes, he then attacked the Fan clan and utterly defeated them. Having merged three clans into one, he did not stop there but laid siege to Zhao Xiangzi at Jinchang. At this point, Han and Wei conferred, saying: "As the ancient saying goes, 'When the lips are gone, the teeth grow cold.' If Zhao perishes in the morning, we shall follow by evening. If Zhao perishes in the evening, we shall follow by morning." The *Book of Songs* says: "If the fish and water do not unite, what will become of them on land?" Thus, the three lords united their hearts and strength, opened the gates and cleared the roads, donned armor and roused their troops. Han and Wei attacked from without, Zhao from within, and together they struck Zhibo, crushing him utterly.

Therefore, Master Mozi said: There is an ancient saying: "The noble man does not use water as a mirror but uses men. A mirror of water shows only the face’s appearance; a mirror of men reveals fortune and misfortune." If you consider offensive warfare profitable, have you not reflected on the affair of Zhibo? That it brings misfortune rather than fortune can already be known.

*Against Offensive Warfare, Part Three*

Master Mozi said: What is the explanation for the world’s praise of goodness today? Is it because such actions align with the benefit of Heaven above, the spirits in the middle, and the people below, and thus are praised? Or is it not for these reasons that they are praised? Even the most foolish among the people would say: It is because such actions align with the benefit of Heaven above, the spirits in the middle, and the people below that they are praised.

Today, what the world universally upholds are the laws of the sage-kings. Yet if the feudal lords of the world continue to engage in offensive warfare and annexation, they merely possess the name of praising righteousness without examining its reality. This is like a blind man who, when asked to distinguish between white and black, cannot discern the objects—how can he be said to truly know the difference?

Thus, the wise of ancient times, in planning for the world, necessarily considered the rightness of an action before proceeding. Only then did they act without hesitation, swiftly achieving success, attaining their desires, and aligning with the benefit of Heaven, the spirits, and the people. This is the way of the wise.

Therefore, the benevolent rulers of antiquity who held the world necessarily reversed the doctrines of great states, unified the harmony of the world, and gathered all within the four seas. They led the people of the world to serve the Lord on High, the mountains and rivers, and the spirits with the labor of farming. Their benefit to the people was great, and their achievements were vast. Thus, Heaven rewarded them, the spirits enriched them, and the people praised them, making them noble as the Son of Heaven, wealthy with all under Heaven, their names joining Heaven and Earth, enduring to this day without fading. This is the way of the wise, the means by which the former kings held the world.

Yet the lords and great men, the feudal lords of the world today, act otherwise. They meticulously select their crack troops, array their chariots and soldiers, forge sturdy armor and sharp weapons, and proceed to attack and invade blameless states.

Upon breaching the borders of a state, they reap its crops, fell its trees, raze its walls and ramparts to fill its moats and ditches, slaughter its sacrificial livestock, and demolish its ancestral temples. They ruthlessly kill its people, trample its elderly and weak, and carry off its treasures. When the troops advance, they are exhorted at the clamor of battle: "To die in service is supreme; to kill many is next; to be wounded is the least." How much worse, then, is it to be the first to flee in defeat? Such a crime is punishable by death without mercy! This is how they rally their forces.

Yet to annex states and annihilate armies, to ravage the people and disrupt the legacy of the sages—can this truly be deemed beneficial to Heaven? To seize the people of Heaven and attack the cities of Heaven is to slaughter the people of Heaven, desecrate the abodes of the spirits, overthrow the altars of the soil and grain, and slaughter the sacrificial victims. This is surely not in accord with Heaven’s benefit. Can it be deemed beneficial to the spirits? To kill their worshippers, extinguish the lords of the spirits, abolish the former kings, and ravage the people—causing the common folk to scatter—this is surely not in accord with the spirits’ benefit. Can it be deemed beneficial to the people? To kill them for the sake of profit is already excessive. Moreover, when one calculates the costs, this exhausts the very foundation of life, depleting the resources of the people under Heaven beyond measure. Thus, it is surely not in accord with the people’s benefit.

Now, those who lead armies speak of the many ways such actions bring no advantage: the general lacks courage, the officers are undisciplined, the weapons are dull, the troops are untrained, the army is too small, the soldiers are disunited, their might is insufficient, their harms are not enduring, their struggles are not swift, their defenses are not strong, their resolve is not firm, and the feudal lords of allied states are doubtful. When allied lords are doubtful, the enemy’s plans take shape, and their intentions grow weak. If one proceeds with such deficiencies, the state loses its troops, and the people abandon their labors.

Now, observe the states that delight in attack and conquest: when they mobilize their forces, nobles and commoners alike must number in the thousands, with conscripts and laborers reaching the hundreds of thousands, before they can set their armies in motion. Such campaigns may last years or, at best, months. During this time, the ruler has no leisure to attend to governance, officials cannot manage their offices, farmers cannot tend their fields, and women cannot spin or weave. Thus, the state loses its troops, and the people abandon their labors.

Moreover, their chariots and horses are worn out, their tents and canopies, the supplies of the three armies, and the provisions for armor and weapons—even if only one-fifth remain, it is still insufficient. Then there are those who perish along the roads: the routes are long, provisions cannot keep pace, and when the time comes to eat and drink, the laborers suffer from hunger, cold, exhaustion, illness, and disease, dying in droves in the ditches. The harm this inflicts upon the people is profound, a grave calamity for all under Heaven. Yet the lords and great men delight in such actions—this is to take pleasure in the destruction of the people of the world. Is this not perverse?

Now, among the warlike states of the world—Qi, Jin, Chu, and Yue—if these four were to have their way, their populations would increase tenfold, yet they would still be unable to fill their lands. This is a case of too few people and too much land. Yet they continue to fight over territory, thereby diminishing what is already insufficient while further increasing what is already in excess.

Now, the lords who delight in attack and conquest further embellish their arguments to refute Mozi, saying: "Is not the use of force to punish unrighteousness beneficial?" In ancient times, Yu campaigned against the Three Miao, Tang overthrew Jie, and King Wu defeated Zhou—all were established as sage-kings. Why is this so? Mozi replied: "You have not examined the nature of my words or understood their meaning. What they did was not called 'attack' but 'punishment.'"

In the time of the Three Miao, great chaos arose. Heaven’s decree was upon them: omens appeared, blood rained for three days, dragons emerged in the temples, wailing filled the markets, the earth split to the springs, and the five grains mutated, causing the people great distress. Gaoyang then commanded the Dark Palace, and Yu personally grasped Heaven’s auspicious mandate to campaign against the Three Miao. Lightning and thunder guided the way; a spirit with the face of a man and the body of a crow, bearing jade-like tokens, attended him. Yi loosed arrows at the Miao’s omens, and the Miao’s forces fell into chaos, soon to be subdued. After Yu had conquered the Three Miao, he demarcated the mountains and rivers, distinguished the ranks of beings, established the great norms, and ensured that spirits and people did not transgress. The world was then at peace—this was Yu’s punishment of the Three Miao.

As for Jie of Xia, Heaven’s mandate was withdrawn: the sun and moon were untimely, cold and heat arrived in disorder, the five grains withered and died, ghosts wailed in the state, and cranes cried for over ten nights. Heaven then commanded Tang at the Biao Palace, entrusting him with Xia’s great mandate: "The virtue of Xia is in great disorder. I have already ended its mandate from Heaven. Go and punish it, and you shall surely be capable." Tang dared to lead his forces to the borders of Xia, where the Lord on High sent dark forces to destroy Xia’s cities. Soon after, a spirit came to announce: "The virtue of Xia is in great disorder. Go and attack it, and I shall surely make you capable." Having received the mandate from Heaven, Heaven sent a blazing fire to the northwest corner of Xia’s cities. Tang, leading Jie’s forces, conquered Xia, assembled the feudal lords at Bo, presented the decree of Heaven, and proclaimed it to the four quarters. The feudal lords of the world dared not but submit—this was Tang’s punishment of Jie.

As for King Zhou of Shang, Heaven did not order his virtue: the sacrifices were untimely, and for ten nights, the sun and moon rained down upon Bo. The Nine Tripods shifted, women took on ominous appearances, ghosts wailed at night, women became men, Heaven rained flesh, thorns grew on the roads, and the king’s elder brother indulged himself without restraint.

A red crow bearing a jade tablet descended upon the altar of Qi in Zhou, proclaiming: "Heaven commands King Wen of Zhou to punish Yin and take possession of the realm." Taidian came to submit, the Yellow River yielded the Green Chart, and the earth produced the divine steed Huang. King Wu fulfilled his merit, dreaming of three spirits who declared: "I have already drowned Zhou of Yin in the virtue of wine. Go and attack him, and I shall surely make you greatly capable." King Wu then struck down the madman, overthrowing Shang to establish Zhou, and Heaven bestowed upon him the banner of the yellow crow. Having conquered Yin, the king completed the work of the August Emperor, apportioning sacrifices to the various spirits, honoring the former kings of Yin, and extending his reach to the four barbarians. Thus, none under Heaven dared not submit, and he inherited Tang’s legacy—this was King Wu’s punishment of Zhou.

Viewing these three sage-kings, it was not called "attack" but "punishment." Yet rulers who delight in aggression embellish their arguments to refute Mozi, saying: "Do you consider attack and conquest unrighteous? Do they not benefit the world?" In ancient times, Chu’s Xiong Li first campaigned in the region of Mount Sui, King Goujian of Yue emerged from Youju to found his state, Tang Shu and Lü Shang established Qi and Jin. Each of these began with territories of only a few hundred *li*, yet through the annexation of states, they came to rule a quarter of the world. How is this so?

Mozi replied: "You have not examined the categories of my words nor understood their reasons. In antiquity, when the Son of Heaven first enfeoffed the feudal lords, there were over ten thousand. Now, through the annexation of states, over ten thousand have been destroyed, leaving only four standing. This is like a physician whose medicine cures only four out of ten thousand—he cannot be called a good doctor." Such rulers further adorn their claims, saying: "It is not that I lack gold, jade, women, or land. I seek to establish a righteous name under Heaven and attract the feudal lords through virtue."

Mozi said: "If one could truly establish a righteous name under Heaven and attract the feudal lords through virtue, the submission of the world could be awaited in an instant. Yet the world has long been mired in aggression, like a colt broken to the saddle. If one could demonstrate trustworthiness and prioritize the benefit of the feudal lords, then when a great state acts unjustly, all would share its sorrow; when it attacks a small state, all would come to its rescue. If a small state’s walls are incomplete, they would be repaired; if its grain stores are exhausted, they would be replenished; if its silk and cloth are insufficient, they would be supplied. By thus demonstrating to great states, the rulers of small states would rejoice. If others toil while we rest, our armor and weapons grow strong. With generosity and relief in times of urgency, the people will surely turn to us. By replacing aggression with governance, our attacks will be twice as effective. Calculating the cost of our campaigns and exposing the exhaustion of the feudal lords, we may surely secure order and advantage. Supervising with rectitude and justifying our name, we must strive to be lenient with our people and trustworthy with our troops. By thus instructing the armies of the feudal lords, we would be invincible under Heaven, and our benefits to the world would be beyond count. This is the true benefit of the world, yet if kings, dukes, and great officials remain unaware and persist in such practices, they may be called ministers who know not how to benefit the world."

Thus, Mozi declared: "Now, if the kings, dukes, gentlemen, and officers of the world truly desire to seek the world’s benefit and eliminate its harm, they must recognize that the incessant practice of aggression is indeed the greatest harm under Heaven. If one wishes to practice benevolence and righteousness, to strive for the rank of superior scholar, to follow the Way of the sage-kings above and seek the benefit of states and common people below, then one must uphold the doctrine of *non-aggression*—this is what cannot be left unexamined." *Mozi, Scroll Five ends. Scroll Six.*

**Moderation in Expenditure, Part One (Chapter Twenty)**

When the sage governs a single state, the state’s resources may be doubled. When he governs the entire world, the world’s resources may be doubled. This doubling is not achieved by seizing land from without, but by taking the existing state and removing what is superfluous—thus doubling its wealth.

When the sage-king governs, every decree he issues and every affair he initiates, every convenience for the people and every expenditure of wealth, is done without waste. Therefore, wealth is not squandered, the people are not overburdened, and the benefits they receive are manifold.

What is the purpose of clothing and furs? In winter, they ward off the cold; in summer, they shield from the heat. The principle of making garments is this: if they provide added warmth in winter or added coolness in summer, they are retained. If they do not, they are discarded.

What is the purpose of palaces and dwellings? In winter, they block wind and cold; in summer, they shelter from heat and rain. If they provide added security against thieves and bandits, they are retained. If they do not, they are discarded.

What is the purpose of armor, shields, and the five weapons? To defend against raiders, rebels, and bandits. When raiders, rebels, and bandits arise, those with armor, shields, and the five weapons prevail, while those without are defeated. Thus, the sage creates armor, shields, and the five weapons. The principle of making them is this: if they are lighter, more durable, and harder to break, they are retained. If they are not, they are discarded.

What is the purpose of boats and carts? Carts travel over hills and land, boats traverse rivers and valleys, connecting the benefits of the four directions. The principle of making boats and carts is this: if they are lighter and more efficient, they are retained. If they are not, they are discarded.

In all these things, nothing is made without purpose. Therefore, wealth is not squandered, the people are not overburdened, and the benefits they receive are manifold.

If we eliminate the extravagant preferences of great men—amassing pearls, jades, exotic birds, beasts, dogs, and horses—and instead increase the quantity of clothing, dwellings, armor, shields, the five weapons, boats, and carts, would this not multiply their number severalfold? Such a task is not difficult. What, then, is truly difficult? Only the doubling of the population is difficult. Yet even the population can be doubled.

In ancient times, the sage-kings established this law: a man at twenty years of age must not fail to establish a household; a woman at fifteen must not fail to serve a husband. This was the law of the sage-kings. After the sage-kings passed, the people followed a different standard. Those who wished to marry early did so at twenty; those who wished to marry late did so at forty. Comparing the early and late, this lagged ten years behind the sage-kings’ law. If a woman bears a child every three years, she may have two or three children in that time. Would this not allow the people to marry earlier and thus double the population? Indeed, it would.

Now, those who govern the world today employ many methods that diminish the people. They impose heavy labor upon the people, levy exorbitant taxes, and leave the people’s resources insufficient—so many freeze or starve to death that they cannot be counted. Moreover, the rulers dispatch armies to invade neighboring states: some campaigns last a full year, others mere months, keeping men and women apart for long stretches. This is one of the ways they diminish the people. Others die from unstable living conditions, irregular meals, or illness brought on by toil; still more perish in raids, ambushes, assaults on cities, or battles in the wild—so many that they cannot be counted. Are these not the very policies by which those who govern today multiply the means of diminishing the people?

When the sages governed, they did not resort to such measures. When those who are not sages govern, do they not multiply the ways of harming the people? Thus Master Mo declared: “Abandon the useless—this is the great benefit of the sage-kings’ Way for all under Heaven.”

**Moderation in Use, Part Two**

Master Mo said: In ancient times, the enlightened kings and sages who ruled all under Heaven and governed the feudal lords did so because they cherished the people with utmost care, served them with unwavering loyalty, and brought them benefit with the greatest generosity. Their loyalty and trust were interwoven, and they continually demonstrated what was advantageous—thus the people never wearied of them throughout their lives, nor did their influence wane even twenty years after their passing. This is how the enlightened kings and sages of old ruled all under Heaven and governed the feudal lords.

Therefore, the sage-kings of antiquity established laws for moderation in use, decreeing: Let all the hundred artisans under Heaven—those who transport goods, build carts, weave mats, craft pottery, forge metal, fell timber, or work as carpenters—each devote themselves to their respective crafts. The rule was: Whatever suffices to supply the people’s needs, any additional expense that does not further benefit the people must cease.

The sage-kings also established laws for food and drink, decreeing: Let it suffice to fill the void, sustain the qi, strengthen the limbs, and sharpen the ears and eyes—then stop. Do not pursue the utmost refinement of the five flavors or the perfect harmony of fragrances; do not seek rare delicacies or exotic goods from distant lands. How do we know this to be so?

In ancient times, when Yao governed the world, he soothed the lands of Jiao to the south and descended upon Youdu in the north; from east to west, wherever the sun rose and set, none failed to submit in homage, establishing a profound love. Millet and sorghum were not doubled, and broths were not layered. They drank from earthen mounds and sipped from clay vessels, ladling with gourds. Bowing, rising, and turning in ceremonial decorum—such rites the sage-kings did not observe.

The sage-kings of old established laws for clothing, decreeing: In winter, wear garments of felted red, light yet warm; in summer, wear garments of fine linen, light yet cool—then stop. Any expense that does not further benefit the people, the sage-kings did not undertake.

In ancient times, the sage-kings, seeing that fierce birds, cunning beasts, and violent men harmed the people, thereupon taught the people to bear arms. They wore swords daily: for stabbing, they pierced; for striking, they severed; even when struck from the side, they did not break—such was the advantage of the sword.
