Armor as clothing was light and effective; when in motion, weapons followed—such was the advantage of armor. Chariots served to carry heavy loads and reach distant places; riding them brought safety, pulling them brought ease. Safe, they did not harm people; swift, they hastened arrival—such was the advantage of the chariot.

When the sage-kings saw that great rivers and broad valleys could not be crossed, they devised boats and oars, sufficient for passage—then stopped. Even when the Three Dukes and feudal lords arrived, boats and oars were not altered, and ferrymen did not adorn themselves—such was the advantage of boats.

The sage-kings established laws for frugal burials, decreeing: Three garments suffice to rot the flesh; a coffin three inches thick suffices to rot the bones. Dig the grave deep enough not to reach the springs, ensuring no leakage—then stop. Once the dead are buried, the living should not mourn for long with grief.

In ancient times, when humans first came into being, there were no palaces or houses, so they dwelled in caves dug into hills and mounds. The sage-kings considered this and thought: Though digging caves may shield from wind and cold in winter, in summer, the lower parts grow damp, the upper parts heavy with moisture, fearing harm to the people’s qi. Thus, they constructed palaces and houses for benefit.

What, then, were the principles for building palaces and houses? Master Mozi said: Let their sides block wind and cold, their tops shield from snow, frost, rain, and dew; let their interiors be clean and bright for sacrifices; let their walls suffice to separate men and women—then stop. Any expense that does not further benefit the people, the sage-kings did not undertake.

*[Sections omitted: "Moderation in Use, Part Two" (22), "Frugal Burial, Part One" (23), "Frugal Burial, Part Two" (24), "Frugal Burial, Part Three" (25)]*

Master Mozi said: The benevolent, in their planning for the world, differ not from the filial child planning for their parents. How does the filial child plan for their parents? They say: If the parents are poor, they strive to enrich them; if the people are few, they strive to increase them; if the multitude is chaotic, they strive to govern them. When faced with these tasks, if their strength is insufficient, their resources inadequate, or their wisdom lacking, only then do they desist. They dare not withhold their remaining strength, conceal their plans, or leave benefits unexploited without doing all they can for their parents. Such are the three duties in the filial child’s planning for their parents.

Even the benevolent’s planning for the world follows this same pattern. They say: If the world is poor, strive to enrich it; if the people are few, strive to increase them; if the multitude is chaotic, strive to govern it. When faced with these tasks, if their strength is insufficient, their resources inadequate, or their wisdom lacking, only then do they desist. They dare not withhold their remaining strength, conceal their plans, or leave benefits unexploited without doing all they can for the world. Such are the three duties in the benevolent’s planning for the world.

Now, since the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties passed away, the world has lost its righteousness. Later generations of noble men have debated: Some regard lavish burials and prolonged mourning as benevolence, righteousness, and the duty of a filial child. Others regard them as neither benevolent nor righteous, nor the duty of a filial child. These two parties, though their words oppose each other and their actions contradict, both claim: "We follow the Dao of Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu." Yet their words oppose, and their actions contradict. Thus, later generations of noble men are all perplexed by the words of these two parties.

If one is indeed perplexed by the words of these two parties, then let us tentatively put them into practice in governing the state and the myriad people and observe: In calculating lavish burials and prolonged mourning, which of the three benefits—enriching the poor, increasing the few, or pacifying danger and governing chaos—do they truly serve? If, in my judgment, following their words and employing their plans, lavish burials and prolonged mourning can indeed enrich the poor, increase the few, pacify danger, and govern chaos, then this is benevolence, righteousness, and the duty of a filial child. Those who plan for others cannot but encourage it. The benevolent, seeking to promote this for the world, will make it so that the people praise it and never abandon it.

But if, in my judgment, following their words and employing their plans, lavish burials and prolonged mourning cannot enrich the poor, increase the few, pacify danger, or govern chaos, then this is neither benevolence nor righteousness, nor the duty of a filial child. Those who plan for others cannot but discourage it. The benevolent, seeking to promote this for the world, will make it so that the people praise it and never abandon it.

Or if, following their words and employing their plans, lavish burials and prolonged mourning truly cannot enrich the poor, increase the few, pacify danger, or govern chaos, then this is neither benevolence nor righteousness, nor the duty of a filial child. Those who plan for others cannot but oppose it. The benevolent, seeking to eliminate this, will make it so that the world rejects it and people condemn it, never to be practiced again.

Moreover, to promote the world’s benefit and eliminate its harm—yet today, the disorder of the state and the people is unprecedented from ancient times to the present. How do we know this to be so? Now, the scholars and gentlemen of the world still largely doubt whether lavish burials and prolonged mourning are correct or incorrect, beneficial or harmful.

Thus Master Mozi declared: Let us then examine this matter. Even if we do not adopt the words of those who advocate lavish burials and prolonged mourning, yet when it comes to affairs of state, this much remains: among the kings, dukes, and great officials who suffer bereavement, the coffins and outer coffins must be layered thick, the burial deep, the mourning garments numerous, the embroidered patterns elaborate, and the tomb mounds vast. As for the deaths of ordinary gentlemen and commoners, they nearly exhaust their households. When feudal lords die, their treasuries are emptied, and then gold, jade, pearls, and precious stones are heaped upon the body, bound with cords and knots, while chariots and horses are interred in the grave pit. Moreover, they must furnish many canopies, tripods, drums, silken banners, ritual vessels, halberds, swords, feathered ornaments, and leather goods, burying them all with the dead. If they wish to satisfy their desire for human sacrifice, they say: when the Son of Heaven is buried, the many number in the hundreds, the few in the tens; when generals and great officials are buried, the many number in the tens, the few in the single digits.

What then are the rules for observing mourning? They say: weeping and wailing without order, tears flowing as sackcloth and hemp hang heavy, dwelling in a mourning hut, sleeping on rough mats with a clod of earth for a pillow. They further compel one another to force themselves not to eat, feigning hunger, and to wear thin garments, feigning cold, so that their faces become gaunt and sallow, their complexions darkened and dull, their ears and eyes no longer sharp, their hands and feet no longer strong—rendering them useless. It is also said: the conduct of a superior man in mourning must be such that he can only rise with support and walk with a staff, thus enduring for three years.

If we follow such words and practice such ways, and kings, dukes, and great officials observe this, they will surely be unable to attend court early to manage the five offices and six ministries, clear wastelands, or fill granaries. If farmers observe this, they will surely be unable to go out early and return late to plow, sow, plant, and cultivate. If artisans observe this, they will surely be unable to repair boats and chariots or craft vessels and utensils. If women observe this, they will surely be unable to rise early and retire late to spin, weave, and dye.

In careful calculation, lavish burials amount to burying much accumulated wealth, while prolonged mourning amounts to long prohibitions on work. Wealth that has been amassed is buried away; those who might later produce are long forbidden to do so. To seek prosperity in this way is like forbidding plowing yet hoping for a harvest—there is no possible argument for attaining wealth. Thus, in seeking to enrich the household, it is already impossible. If one wishes to increase the population, can this be the way? The argument for that, too, is impossible.

Should the governance of the state now be founded upon lavish burials and prolonged mourning, then when the ruler dies, mourning shall last three years; when father or mother dies, mourning shall last three years; when wife or eldest son dies, all five shall mourn for three years. Thereafter, uncles, brothers, illegitimate sons, clansmen, paternal aunts, sisters, nephews, and maternal uncles shall each observe mourning for a set number of months—thus emaciation and wasting shall surely be regulated. Faces shall become sunken and sallow, complexions darkened and soot-like, ears and eyes dull, hands and feet feeble, rendering one unfit for use.

It is further said: The superior man, in observing mourning, must lean upon others to rise and walk with a staff—thus shall he endure for three years. Should such laws and words be followed as the Way, then hunger and privation shall be as this. Hence, in winter the people shall not withstand the cold, in summer they shall not endure the heat, and those who fall ill and die shall be beyond counting. This greatly disrupts the union of men and women, and in seeking to increase the population, it is like ordering a man to bear a sword while hoping for his longevity—there is no possible argument for attaining a multitude. Thus, in seeking to multiply the people, it is already impossible.

Does one wish to govern with penal laws? Can this be the way? The argument for that, too, is impossible. Should the governance of the state now be founded upon lavish burials and prolonged mourning, the nation shall surely grow poor, the people few, and penal governance chaotic. Should such laws and words be followed as the Way, those above shall be unable to attend to governance, and those below shall be unable to engage in work. If those above cannot attend to governance, penal laws shall surely be in disorder; if those below cannot engage in work, clothing and food shall surely be insufficient. Should they be insufficient, younger brothers seeking their elder brothers shall not find them, and the undutiful shall surely resent their elder brothers; sons seeking their parents shall not find them, and the unfilial shall surely resent their parents; ministers seeking their ruler shall not find him, and the disloyal shall surely rebel against their superiors.

Thus, the people of perverse and licentious conduct shall go forth without clothing and return without food. Within, they shall lack sustenance, and together they shall turn to wanton violence, becoming impossible to restrain. Hence, thieves and bandits shall multiply while those who govern shall be few. To first multiply thieves and bandits and then seek order is like ordering a man to bear three burdens at dawn and forbidding him to falter—there is no possible argument for attaining governance. Thus, in seeking to govern with penal laws, it is already impossible.

Does one wish to prevent great states from attacking small states? Can this be the way? The argument for that, too, is impossible. Thus, in former times, when the sage-kings had passed, the world lost its righteousness, and the feudal lords waged war by force. In the south were the kings of Chu and Yue, and in the north the lords of Qi and Jin—all honed their troops to attack, conquer, and govern the world through annexation. Therefore, the reason great states do not attack small states is that the latter possess abundant stores, well-maintained walls and moats, and harmony between ruler and subject—thus great states do not delight in attacking them. Without stores, with walls and moats unmaintained, and without harmony between ruler and subject, great states shall surely attack them.

Should the governance of the state now be founded upon lavish burials and prolonged mourning, the nation shall surely grow poor, the people few, and penal governance chaotic. Should it be poor, there shall be no stores. Should the people be few, those to maintain walls and moats shall be few. Should governance be chaotic, they shall be unable to prevail in battle and unable to hold their defenses. In seeking to prevent great states from attacking small states, it is already impossible.

Does one wish to seek the blessings of the Supreme Lord and the spirits? Can this be the way? The argument for that, too, is impossible.

Now, should governance be founded upon lavish burials and prolonged mourning, the nation shall surely grow poor, the people few, and penal governance chaotic. Should it be poor, this is to discard fine wine and sweet ale without cooling or purifying them. Should the people be few, those who serve the Supreme Lord and the spirits shall be few. Should governance be chaotic, sacrifices shall not be offered in due season or measure.

If one now prohibits the service of the Supreme Lord and the spirits, and governs thus, the Supreme Lord and the spirits shall begin to regard them from above, saying: "Do We prefer having such people, or having none at all?" They shall say: "Whether We have such people or none at all, there is no difference." Then, as before, the Supreme Lord and the spirits shall send down upon them the punishments of calamity and misfortune, and cast them aside. Is this not indeed what they deserve?

Thus, the ancient sage-kings established laws for burial, saying: "A coffin of three inches is sufficient to rot the body. Three garments of mourning are sufficient to cover the foulness." When burying, it should be neither so deep as to reach the springs below nor so shallow as to allow the stench to rise above. The grave mound should resemble a field plowed three times—no more. Once the dead are buried, the living must not mourn for long but swiftly return to their affairs. People should do what they are capable of, exchanging mutual benefit. Such were the laws of the sage-kings.

Yet those who advocate lavish burials and prolonged mourning say: "Though lavish burials and prolonged mourning may not enrich the poor, increase the few, stabilize the perilous, or order the chaotic, they are the Way of the sage-kings." Master Mozi said: Not so.

Of old, Yao taught the Eight Di in the north. When he died on the road, he was buried on the northern slope of Mount Yao. He was clothed in three garments of mourning, and his coffin was made of wood from the valley. It was bound with kudzu, and after the peach-wood bier was set down, they wailed. The grave mound was unsealed, level with the ground. After the burial, oxen and horses trampled over it.

Shun taught the Seven Rong in the west. When he died on the road, he was buried in the marketplace of Nanshi. He was clothed in three garments of mourning, and his coffin was made of wood from the valley, bound with kudzu. After the burial, the people of the marketplace trampled over it.

Yu taught the Nine Yi in the east. When he died on the road, he was buried on Mount Kuaiji. He was clothed in three garments of mourning, and his coffin was made of paulownia wood, three inches thick. It was bound with kudzu, the joints unsealed, the seams uncaulked. The grave was dug neither so deep as to reach the springs below nor so shallow as to allow the stench to rise above. After the burial, the remaining earth was gathered atop it, and the grave mound resembled a field plowed three times—no more.

If we observe these three sage-kings, then lavish burials and prolonged mourning are indeed not the Way of the sage-kings. These three kings were all exalted as Sons of Heaven, wealthy with all under Heaven. Did they worry that their resources were insufficient? They established such laws for burial.

Yet today, the kings, dukes, and great men conduct burials differently. They must have an outer coffin and an inner coffin, three layers of leather curtains, jade disks and ornaments in full array, halberds, swords, tripods, drums, jars, and basins, embroidered silks and plain weaves, ten thousand mourning garments, carriages, horses, and female musicians—all prepared. The grave mound must be as grand as a mountain. This halts the people’s work and squanders their wealth beyond measure. Such practices are utterly useless.

Thus, Master Mozi said: Formerly, I began by saying: If one adopts their words, employs their plans, and calculates that lavish burials and prolonged mourning can enrich the poor, increase the few, stabilize the perilous, and order the chaotic, then it is benevolent, righteous, and the duty of a filial son. One who plans for others cannot but encourage it. But if one adopts their words, employs their plans, and finds that lavish burials and prolonged mourning truly cannot enrich the poor, increase the few, stabilize the perilous, or order the chaotic, then it is not benevolent, not righteous, and not the duty of a filial son. One who plans for others cannot but oppose it.

Thus, in seeking to enrich the nation, one achieves the very opposite—poverty. In seeking to increase the people, one achieves the very opposite—fewness. In seeking to order governance, one achieves the very opposite—chaos. In seeking to prevent great states from attacking small states, it is already impossible. In seeking the blessings of the Supreme Lord and the spirits, one incurs misfortune instead.

Examining above, it contradicts the Way of Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu. Examining below, it aligns with the deeds of Jie, Zhou, You, and Li. From this perspective, lavish burials and prolonged mourning are not the Way of the sage-kings.

Yet those who advocate lavish burials and prolonged mourning say: "If lavish burials and prolonged mourning are indeed not the Way of the sage-kings, why do the gentlemen of the Central States persist in them without ceasing or choosing otherwise?" Master Mozi said: This is what is called "convenient to their habits and justified by their customs."

Of old, in the state of Zhuomu to the east of Yue, when the eldest son was born, they dismembered and ate him, calling it "beneficial to the younger brother." When the grandfather died, they carried the grandmother on their backs and abandoned her, saying: "A ghost’s wife cannot dwell with us." The rulers above made this their governance, and the people below made it their custom. They persisted in it without ceasing or choosing otherwise. Is this truly the Way of benevolence and righteousness? This is what is called "convenient to their habits and justified by their customs."

To the south of Chu, there was the state of the Yanren. When their kin died, they rotted the flesh and discarded it, then buried the bones, and only then were they deemed filial sons.

To the west of Qin, there was the state of Yibing. When their kin died, they gathered firewood and burned the body, the smoke rising upward, calling it "ascending to the distant." Only then were they deemed filial sons. The rulers above made this their governance, and the people below made it their custom. They persisted in it without ceasing or choosing otherwise. Is this truly the Way of benevolence and righteousness? This is what is called "convenient to their habits and justified by their customs."

If we observe these three states, their practices are indeed meager. If the gentlemen of the Central States observe them, their practices are indeed lavish. Compared to those, they are excessively lavish; compared to these, they are excessively meager. Thus, burial must have its proper measure.

Thus, clothing and food are the benefits of life, yet even these must be moderated; burial is the benefit of death—how could it alone be without moderation? Master Mozi established his regulations for burial: a coffin three inches thick, sufficient to rot the bones; three garments, sufficient to rot the flesh; the depth of the grave dug so that below, it does not reach dampness, and above, qi does not escape; the mound enough to mark the place—this is all. Weep when going, weep when returning, then return to managing the resources of clothing and food, and together attend to sacrifices to express filial piety toward one’s kin.

Thus it is said: Master Mozi’s regulations do not lose sight of the benefits of life and death—this is it. Therefore, Master Mozi said: Now, the scholar-gentlemen of the world who claim they wish to practice benevolence and righteousness, seek to be superior men, aspire above to follow the Way of the sage-kings, and below to benefit the states, families, and common people, must take moderation in funerals as governance and cannot but examine this.

If one provides meals but the food is insufficient to nourish the living, and the expenses are such that they cannot sustain the people’s needs, then one should stop. Any additional expenditure that does not bring benefit to the people is something the sage-kings would not do.

To plan for others, one cannot but encourage moderation. If one intends to model their words and adopt their strategies, then elaborate burials and prolonged mourning truly cannot determine wealth or poverty, increase or decrease, stabilize peril or govern chaos. This is neither benevolent nor righteous, nor is it the duty of a filial child. Those who plan for others cannot but discourage such practices. The benevolent will seek to eliminate them—who in the world would allow the people to condemn them and persist in such conduct for a lifetime?

Master Mozi said: Now, if the scholar-gentlemen of the world wish to practice benevolence and righteousness, seek to be superior men, and above aspire to follow the Way of the sage-kings while below benefiting the states, families, and common people, they must take moderation in funerals as governance. If they do not examine this, then they are not acting as those who plan for others. How can they be considered benevolent and righteous? How can they be called superior men? Thus, those who claim to follow the Way of the sage-kings yet do not practice moderation in funerals are like one who speaks of archery but does not draw the bow, or one who discusses driving but does not mount the carriage—how can they reach their destination?

Master Mozi said: Now, the scholar-gentlemen of the world know the small but not the great. How do we know this? We know it from how they conduct themselves in their households. If one living in a household offends the head of the family, there are still neighboring households to flee to. Yet even then, relatives, brothers, and acquaintances will admonish one another, all saying: "One must be vigilant! One must be cautious! How can one live in a household and offend the head of the family and still consider it acceptable?" This is not only true for households—it is the same even for states. If one living in a state offends the ruler, there are still neighboring states to flee to. Yet even then, relatives, brothers, and acquaintances will admonish one another, all saying: "One must be vigilant! One must be cautious! Who would live in a state, offend the ruler, and still consider it acceptable?" In such cases, there are places to flee, and the warnings are already so grave. How much more severe should the warnings be when there is nowhere to flee?

Moreover, there is a saying: "Where can one hide in the light of day? Where can one flee if one offends?" The answer is: nowhere to flee. For the heavens cannot be concealed in forests, valleys, or secluded gates where no one dwells—brightness will surely reveal all. Yet the scholar-gentlemen of the world suddenly forget to admonish one another regarding Heaven. This is how I know that the scholar-gentlemen of the world know the small but not the great.

Now then, what does Heaven desire, and what does it detest? Heaven desires righteousness and detests unrighteousness. Thus, if we lead the people of the world to act in accordance with righteousness, we are doing what Heaven desires. If we do what Heaven desires, Heaven will also do what we desire. Now, what do we desire, and what do we detest? We desire blessings and prosperity and detest misfortune and calamity. Yet if we lead the people of the world into misfortune and calamity, how can we know that Heaven desires righteousness and detests unrighteousness?

It is said: Where there is righteousness, there is life; where there is no righteousness, there is death. Where there is righteousness, there is wealth; where there is no righteousness, there is poverty. Where there is righteousness, there is order; where there is no righteousness, there is chaos. Thus, Heaven desires life and detests death, desires wealth and detests poverty, desires order and detests chaos. This is how we know that Heaven desires righteousness and detests unrighteousness.

Furthermore, righteousness is governance. Governance does not flow from below to above; it must flow from above to below. Thus, the common people exhaust their strength in service, yet before they can govern themselves, the officers govern them. The officers exhaust their strength in service, yet before they can govern themselves, the generals and ministers govern them. The generals and ministers exhaust their strength in service, yet before they can govern themselves, the Three Dukes and feudal lords govern them. The Three Dukes and feudal lords exhaust their strength in governance, yet before they can govern themselves, the Son of Heaven governs them. And before the Son of Heaven can govern himself, Heaven governs him. The Son of Heaven governs the Three Dukes, feudal lords, officers, and common people. The scholar-gentlemen of the world clearly understand that Heaven governs the Son of Heaven, yet the people of the world do not clearly understand this.

Thus, in ancient times, the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties—Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu—wished to make clear to the people of the world that Heaven governs the Son of Heaven. Therefore, none failed to groom oxen and sheep, raise dogs and pigs, prepare pure grain and sweet wine, and offer sacrifices to the Supreme Lord and spirits, seeking blessings from Heaven. I have never heard of the people of the world seeking blessings from the Son of Heaven. This is how I know that Heaven governs the Son of Heaven.

Therefore, the Son of Heaven is the most exalted and wealthiest in the world. Thus, those who desire wealth and exaltation must align with the will of Heaven and cannot but comply. Those who comply with the will of Heaven—practicing universal love and mutual benefit—will surely receive reward. Those who defy the will of Heaven—practicing mutual hatred and harm—will surely receive punishment.

Now then, who has complied with the will of Heaven and received reward? Who has defied it and received punishment? Master Mozi said: The sage-kings of the Three Dynasties—Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu—complied with the will of Heaven and received reward. The tyrannical kings of the Three Dynasties—Jie, Zhou, You, and Li—defied the will of Heaven and received punishment.

Now, why did Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu receive reward? Master Mozi said: They served Heaven above, served the spirits in the middle, and loved the people below. Thus, the will of Heaven said: "These are those whom I love—I love them universally. These are those whom I benefit—I benefit them universally." Those who love others do so broadly; those who benefit others do so deeply. Therefore, they were made noble as Sons of Heaven, wealthy with all under Heaven, and their legacy endured for ten thousand generations. Their goodness was proclaimed, spreading throughout the world, and to this day they are called sage-kings.

Now, why did Jie, Zhou, You, and Li receive punishment? Master Mozi said: They reviled Heaven above, deceived the spirits in the middle, and despised the people below. Thus, the will of Heaven said: "These are those whom I love—I detest them separately. These are those whom I benefit—I harm them mutually." Those who detest others do so broadly; those who harm others do so deeply. Therefore, they did not complete their allotted years, their dynasties were cut short, and to this day they are reviled as tyrannical kings.

Now, how do we know that Heaven loves the people of the world? Because it illuminates them universally. How do we know it illuminates them universally? Because it possesses them universally. How do we know it possesses them universally? Because it nourishes them universally. How do we know it nourishes them universally? It is said: Within the four seas, all who eat grain—none fail to groom oxen and sheep, raise dogs and pigs, prepare pure grain and sweet wine, and offer sacrifices to the Supreme Lord and spirits.

Heaven possesses the people—how could it not love them? Moreover, I say that killing even one innocent person must bring one inauspicious consequence. Who kills the innocent? It is man. To whom does the inauspicious consequence belong? It is Heaven. If one claims that Heaven does not love the people of the world, then why does Heaven bring inauspicious consequences when men kill one another? This is how I know that Heaven loves the people of the world.

Those who follow the will of Heaven practice governance through righteousness. Those who oppose the will of Heaven practice governance through force. But how is righteous governance to be achieved? Master Mozi says: A great state does not attack small states; a powerful family does not usurp humble families. The strong do not plunder the weak; the noble do not despise the lowly; the cunning do not deceive the foolish. This must benefit Heaven above, spirits in the middle, and men below. With threefold benefit, nothing is not benefited, so the finest names under Heaven are bestowed upon them—they are called sagely kings.

Those who practice governance through force are the opposite: their words contradict this, their actions oppose it, like reckless charioteers. A great state attacks small states; a powerful family usurps humble families. The strong plunder the weak; the noble despise and oppress; the cunning deceive the foolish. This does not benefit Heaven above, spirits in the middle, or men below. With threefold harm, nothing is benefited, so the vilest names under Heaven are bestowed upon them—they are called tyrannical kings.

Master Mozi says: I possess the will of Heaven, like a wheelwright’s compass or a carpenter’s square. The wheelwright and carpenter hold their compass and square to measure the square and round of all under Heaven, saying: what conforms is right, what does not conform is wrong.

Now, the books of the gentlemen and knights of the world cannot be fully recorded, their words cannot be fully counted. They persuade rulers above and persuade officials below, yet in matters of benevolence and righteousness, they are far indeed. How do I know this? I say: I have obtained the luminous standard of all under Heaven to measure them.

**Heaven’s Will, Part Twenty-Seven**
Master Mozi says: Now, the gentlemen of the world who wish to practice benevolence and righteousness must not fail to examine the source of righteousness. Since one cannot but examine the source of righteousness, then from where does righteousness arise? Master Mozi says: Righteousness does not arise from the foolish and lowly; it must arise from the noble and wise. How do I know that righteousness does not arise from the foolish and lowly, but must arise from the noble and wise? I say: Righteousness is good governance. How do I know that righteousness is good governance? I say: When the world has righteousness, it is ordered; when it lacks righteousness, it is chaotic. Thus I know that righteousness is good governance. The foolish and lowly cannot govern the noble and wise; only after the noble and wise govern can they govern the foolish and lowly. This is how I know that righteousness does not arise from the foolish and lowly, but must arise from the noble and wise.

Then who is noble? Who is wise? I say: Heaven is noble, Heaven is wise—nothing more. Thus righteousness indeed arises from Heaven.

Now, the people of the world say: The Son of Heaven is nobler than the feudal lords, and the feudal lords are nobler than the great officials—this is clear and evident. Yet I do not know whether Heaven is nobler and wiser than the Son of Heaven. Master Mozi says: I know that Heaven is nobler and wiser than the Son of Heaven. How? When the Son of Heaven does good, Heaven can reward him. When the Son of Heaven is tyrannical, Heaven can punish him. When the Son of Heaven suffers illness or misfortune, he must fast, bathe, and purify himself, prepare pure grain and sweet wine, and offer sacrifices to Heaven and the spirits—then Heaven can remove his afflictions. Yet I do not know whether Heaven seeks blessings for the Son of Heaven. This is how I know that Heaven is nobler and wiser than the Son of Heaven, and it is not only this.

I also know it from the way of the *Xun Tian Ming Bu Jie* in the books of the former kings, which says: "The luminous and wise, great indeed, oversee the ruler and issue commands below." This speaks of Heaven’s nobility and wisdom over the Son of Heaven. Is there anyone else who is noble and wise besides Heaven? I say: Heaven is noble, Heaven is wise—nothing more. Thus righteousness indeed arises from Heaven.

Therefore, Master Mozi says: Now, the gentlemen of the world who truly wish to honor the Dao and benefit the people, who seek to examine the root of benevolence and righteousness, must not fail to be cautious of Heaven’s will. Since one must be cautious of Heaven’s will, then what does Heaven desire and what does it detest? Master Mozi says: Heaven’s will does not desire great states to attack small states, or powerful families to disrupt humble families. It does not desire the strong to oppress the few, the cunning to scheme against the foolish, or the noble to despise the lowly—these are what Heaven does not desire. Beyond this, it desires that people use their strength to assist one another, use their Dao to teach one another, and share their wealth with one another. It also desires that rulers diligently listen to governance and that subjects diligently attend to their tasks. If rulers diligently listen to governance, the state will be well-ordered. If subjects diligently attend to their tasks, resources will be sufficient. If the state is well-ordered and resources are sufficient, then internally, there will be pure wine and sweet grain to offer sacrifices to Heaven and the spirits; externally, there will be jade rings and pearls to present to neighboring states. The grievances of feudal lords will not arise, and weapons will not be raised at the borders. Internally, there will be means to feed the hungry and rest the weary, to nourish the myriad people—then ruler and minister, above and below, will be kind and loyal; father and son, elder and younger brother, will be compassionate and filial.

Thus, only by clearly understanding and following Heaven’s will, and broadly implementing it throughout the world, will governance be well-ordered, the people harmonious, the state prosperous, and resources abundant. The hundred clans will all have warm clothes and full bellies, living in comfort without worry. Therefore, Master Mozi says: Now, the gentlemen of the world who truly wish to follow the Dao and benefit the people, who seek to examine the root of benevolence and righteousness, must not fail to be cautious of Heaven’s will.

Moreover, the Son of Heaven’s possession of the world differs in no way from the feudal lords’ possession of their territories within their four borders. Now, do the feudal lords desire that their ministers, states, and myriad people bring harm upon one another within their four borders? Yet if one dwells in a great state and attacks small states, or dwells in a great house and disrupts small households, seeking thereby to gain rewards and praise, such things shall never be attained—punishments and penalties will surely come. The Heavenly realm’s possession of the world is no different in this regard. If one dwells in a great state and attacks small states, or dwells in a great capital and assaults lesser cities, seeking thereby to obtain blessings and emoluments from Heaven, such blessings and emoluments shall never be attained—calamities and misfortunes will surely come. Now, if one does what Heaven does not desire and fails to do what Heaven desires, then Heaven, too, shall not do what people desire but shall do what people do not desire.

What is it that people do not desire? It is sickness, suffering, and misfortune. If one does not do what Heaven desires but does what Heaven does not desire, this is to lead the myriad people of the world into the midst of calamity and misfortune. Thus, the sage-kings of antiquity clearly knew what Heaven and the spirits bless and what they abhor, and so they sought to promote the world’s benefit and eliminate its harm. Therefore, Heaven’s cold and heat were moderated, the four seasons harmonized, yin and yang, rain and dew timely; the five grains ripened, the six livestock thrived, and illness, plague, pestilence, famine, and disaster did not arise.

Thus, Master Mozi says: Now, the gentlemen of the world who truly wish to follow the Way and benefit the people, who seek to examine the root of benevolence and righteousness, must not fail to be cautious of Heaven’s will.

Moreover, in the world there are those who are deemed unbenevolent and inauspicious, such as sons who do not serve their fathers, younger brothers who do not serve their elder brothers, and ministers who do not serve their rulers. The gentlemen of the world call these inauspicious. Yet Heaven encompasses all under Heaven and loves them, nurturing the myriad things to benefit them—even to the tip of a hair—so that the people may obtain and enjoy these benefits. Can this be called otherwise? Yet they alone fail to repay Heaven and do not recognize this as unbenevolent and inauspicious. This is what I call the gentleman’s clarity in small matters but blindness in great ones.

Furthermore, I know that Heaven’s love for the people is profound, for it has established the sun, moon, and stars to illuminate and guide them; ordained the four seasons—spring, autumn, winter, and summer—to order and regulate them; sent down thunder, snow, frost, rain, and dew to make the five grains and hemp grow, enabling the people to obtain and profit from them; arrayed mountains, rivers, streams, and valleys to distribute the hundred affairs; and oversees the people’s good and evil, appointing kings, dukes, marquises, and earls to reward the worthy and punish the violent. It has provided metals, wood, birds, and beasts for the cultivation of the five grains and hemp, to serve as the people’s clothing and food. From antiquity to the present, this has never ceased. Now, if there were a man here who loved his child as tenderly as this, exhausting his strength and single-minded devotion to benefit him.

When this child grows up and offers no repayment to the father who sought his benefit, all the noble men under Heaven would call him unfilial and inauspicious. Now, the Dao of Heaven embraces all under Heaven and loves them, nurturing the myriad things to benefit them—even to the finest hair’s tip—yet if it were not for Heaven’s work, the people could not obtain these benefits, and this would be deemed wrong. Yet they alone offer no repayment to Heaven and do not recognize this as unfilial and inauspicious. This is what I mean when I say that noble men perceive the small but fail to perceive the great.

Moreover, my proof that Heaven loves the people deeply is not limited to this alone. It is said: “He who kills the innocent, Heaven bestows misfortune upon him.” Who are the innocent? They are the people. Who bestows misfortune? It is Heaven. If Heaven did not love the people deeply, why would it declare that those who kill the innocent will be visited with misfortune by Heaven? This is how I know that Heaven loves the people deeply.

Furthermore, my proof that Heaven loves the people deeply is not limited to this alone. It is said: Those who love and benefit others, aligning with Heaven’s intent, receive Heaven’s rewards. Those who hate and harm others, defying Heaven’s intent, receive Heaven’s punishments. Who are those who love and benefit others, aligning with Heaven’s intent and receiving Heaven’s rewards? They are the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties: Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu. What did Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu practice? They practiced inclusiveness, not partiality. Inclusiveness means: a great state does not attack a small state, a great family does not disrupt a small family, the strong do not plunder the weak, the many do not oppress the few, the cunning do not deceive the simple, and the noble do not disdain the humble. Observing their deeds, they benefited Heaven above, spirits in the middle, and people below. With these threefold benefits, nothing was left unbenefited—this is called the virtue of Heaven. They gathered the finest reputations of all under Heaven and bestowed them upon these kings, saying: “This is benevolence, this is righteousness—loving and benefiting others, aligning with Heaven’s intent, and receiving Heaven’s rewards.” Not only this, but their deeds were inscribed on bamboo and silk, engraved on metal and stone, carved on plates and basins, and transmitted to later generations of descendants. What was the purpose? To record those who loved and benefited others, aligned with Heaven’s intent, and received Heaven’s rewards.

The *Huang Yi* states: “The Lord said to King Wen, ‘I cherish your luminous virtue. You do not raise your voice or change your countenance, nor do you alter the seasons with harsh measures. Unknowing and unperceiving, you follow the Lord’s principles.’” The Lord approved of his compliance with the law and principles, so He raised Yin to reward him, making him noble as the Son of Heaven, wealthy with all under Heaven, and his fame endures to this day. Thus, those who love and benefit others, aligning with Heaven’s intent and receiving Heaven’s rewards, have left a lasting legacy.

Who are those who hate and harm others, defying Heaven’s intent and receiving Heaven’s punishments? They are the violent kings of the Three Dynasties: Jie, Zhou, You, and Li. What did Jie, Zhou, You, and Li practice? They practiced partiality, not inclusiveness. Partiality means: a great state attacks a small state, a great family disrupts a small family, the strong plunder the weak, the many oppress the few, the cunning deceive the simple, and the noble disdain the humble. Observing their deeds, they did not benefit Heaven above, spirits in the middle, or people below. With these threefold harms, nothing was left unharmed—this is called the bane of Heaven. They gathered the vilest reputations of all under Heaven and bestowed them upon these kings, saying: “This is not benevolence, not righteousness—hating and harming others, defying Heaven’s intent, and receiving Heaven’s punishments.” Not only this, but their deeds were also inscribed on bamboo and silk, engraved on metal and stone, carved on plates and basins, and transmitted to later generations of descendants. What was the purpose? To record those who hated and harmed others, defied Heaven’s intent, and received Heaven’s punishments.

The *Da Ming* states: “Zhou exceeded his proper bounds and dwelling, refusing to serve the Supreme Lord, abandoning the spirits of his ancestors and neglecting their sacrifices. He declared, ‘I have fate; there is no need for toil.’” Thus, Heaven also abandoned Zhou and did not protect him. That Heaven abandoned Zhou and did not protect him was because he defied Heaven’s intent. Thus, those who hate and harm others, defying Heaven’s intent and receiving Heaven’s punishments, are clearly known.

Therefore, Master Mo’s understanding of Heaven is no different from a wheelwright’s compass or a carpenter’s square. Now, if a wheelwright holds his compass to measure the roundness or lack thereof in all under Heaven, he says: “What aligns with my compass is round; what does not align is not round.” Thus, roundness and lack thereof can both be known. Why is this? Because the standard for roundness is clear. Likewise, a carpenter holds his square to measure the squareness or lack thereof in all under Heaven, saying: “What aligns with my square is square; what does not align is not square.” Thus, squareness and lack thereof can both be known. Why is this? Because the standard for squareness is clear.

Thus, Master Mo’s grasp of Heaven’s intent serves above to measure the governance of the kings, dukes, and great ministers of the world, and below to gauge the literary pursuits and spoken discourses of the myriad people. Observe their conduct: that which aligns with Heaven’s intent is called virtuous conduct; that which defies Heaven’s intent is called unvirtuous and wrong. Observe their discourses: that which aligns with Heaven’s intent is called virtuous discourse; that which defies Heaven’s intent is called unvirtuous discourse. Observe their governance: that which aligns with Heaven’s intent is called virtuous governance; that which defies Heaven’s intent is called unvirtuous governance. Therefore, establish this as the standard, set this as the model, and use it to measure the benevolence or lack thereof in the kings, dukes, great ministers, and high officials of the world—it is as clear as distinguishing black from white.

Hence, Master Mo says: Now, if the kings, dukes, great ministers, and gentlemen of the world truly wish to follow the Dao to benefit the people and examine the root of benevolence and righteousness, they cannot but align with Heaven’s intent. Aligning with Heaven’s intent is the standard of righteousness.

**Heaven’s Will, Part Twenty-Eight**
Master Mo said: What is the cause of the world’s chaos? It is that the gentlemen of the world are clear about minor matters but unclear about major ones. How do we know they are clear about minor matters but unclear about major ones? Because they are unclear about Heaven’s intent. How do we know they are unclear about Heaven’s intent? Consider how people conduct themselves in households. If one living in another’s household commits an offense, there are still other households to flee to for refuge. Yet fathers warn their sons, elder brothers warn their younger brothers, saying: “Be cautious, be prudent! Living in another’s household, if you are not cautious and prudent, how much more so when living in another’s state?” Now, if one living in another’s state commits an offense, there are still other states to flee to for refuge. Yet fathers warn their sons, elder brothers warn their younger brothers, saying: “Be cautious, be prudent! Living in another’s state, one cannot but be cautious and prudent.” Now, all people dwell in the world and serve Heaven. If they offend Heaven, there is nowhere to flee for refuge. Yet none know to admonish one another—this is how I know they are ignorant of great matters.

Thus, Master Mo says: Be cautious, be prudent! You must do what Heaven desires and abandon what Heaven detests. What does Heaven desire? What does it detest? Heaven desires righteousness and detests unrighteousness. How do we know this? Righteousness is correctness. How do we know righteousness is correctness? When the world has righteousness, it is ordered; without righteousness, it is chaotic. Thus, I know righteousness is correctness. Yet correctness does not arise from below to correct what is above; it must arise from above to correct what is below. Therefore, commoners cannot correct themselves without being ordered by scholars; scholars cannot correct themselves without being ordered by great officers; great officers cannot correct themselves without being ordered by feudal lords; feudal lords cannot correct themselves without being ordered by the Three Dukes; the Three Dukes cannot correct themselves without being ordered by the Son of Heaven; and the Son of Heaven cannot govern himself without being ordered by Heaven. Now, the gentlemen of the world all understand that the Son of Heaven corrects the world but do not understand that Heaven corrects the Son of Heaven.

Thus, the sages of old explained to people: If the Son of Heaven does good, Heaven can reward him. If the Son of Heaven commits wrongs, Heaven can punish him. If the Son of Heaven’s rewards and punishments are unjust or his judgments unfair, the world suffers from plagues, disasters, untimely frost, and dew. The Son of Heaven must then fast, rear pure oxen, sheep, and dogs, prepare refined grain offerings and sweet wine, and pray to Heaven for blessings. I have never heard of Heaven praying to the Son of Heaven for blessings—this is how I know Heaven is weightier and more venerable than the Son of Heaven. Therefore, righteousness does not arise from the foolish and lowly but must arise from the noble and wise. Who is wise? Heaven is wise. Thus, righteousness truly arises from Heaven.

Now, if the gentlemen of the world wish to practice righteousness, they cannot but align with Heaven’s intent. What does aligning with Heaven’s intent entail? It entails universally loving the people of the world. How do we know it entails universally loving the people of the world? Because it entails universally nourishing them. How do we know it entails universally nourishing them? From ancient times to the present, there has been no distant or isolated land where people do not rear pure oxen, sheep, and dogs, prepare refined grain offerings and sweet wine, and reverently sacrifice to the Supreme Lord, mountains, rivers, and spirits—this is how I know they universally nourish them. If they universally nourish them, they must universally love them. It is like the rulers of Chu and Yue: if the King of Chu nourishes only those within his four borders, he loves only the people of Chu; but now Heaven nourishes the entire world, so I know it universally loves the people of the world.

Moreover, Heaven’s love for the people does not stop at material things. Now, in the states of the world where people consume grain, killing an innocent person is deemed inauspicious. Who kills the innocent? People do. Who bestows innocence? Heaven does. If Heaven truly did not love these people, why would it deem killing the innocent inauspicious and bestow misfortune upon the killer? Indeed, Heaven’s love for the people is profound and distinct—this can be known. How do we know Heaven loves the people? I know it because the worthy invariably reward the good and punish the violent. How do we know the worthy invariably reward the good and punish the violent? I know it from the sagely kings of the Three Dynasties of old.

Thus, the sagely kings of the Three Dynasties—Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu—universally loved the world. Following this, they brought benefit to it, transforming the hearts of the people and leading them to revere the Supreme Lord, the mountains and rivers, and the ghosts and spirits. Heaven, seeing that they loved what it loved and benefited what it benefited, bestowed rewards upon them, placing them in high positions, establishing them as the Sons of Heaven to serve as models, and naming them "sages." This serves as evidence of their rewarding the good.

Therefore, the tyrannical kings of the Three Dynasties—Jie, Zhou, You, and Li—universally harmed the world. Following this, they plundered it, corrupting the hearts of the people and leading them to revile and insult the Supreme Lord, the mountains and rivers, and the ghosts and spirits. Heaven, seeing that they did not love what it loved and harmed what it benefited, imposed punishments upon them, causing their fathers and sons to scatter, their states to perish, their altars of soil and grain to be lost, and their own persons to suffer misfortune. Thus, the common people of the world united in condemning them, and for ten thousand generations, their descendants continued to revile them without cease. They were named "failed kings." This serves as evidence of their punishing the violent.

Now, if the scholar-officials of the world wish to practice righteousness, they must not fail to align with the will of Heaven. It is said: aligning with the will of Heaven is universality; opposing the will of Heaven is partiality. Universality as a Way is righteous rectitude; partiality as a Way is forceful rectitude.

It is asked: What is righteous rectitude?
It is said: The great do not attack the small; the strong do not bully the weak; the many do not plunder the few; the cunning do not deceive the foolish; the noble do not disdain the humble; the rich do not arrogantly scorn the poor; the robust do not seize from the aged. Thus, among the various states of the world, none would use water, fire, poison, or weapons to harm one another. In serving above, this benefits Heaven; in the middle, it benefits the spirits; below, it benefits the people. With threefold benefit and nothing unbenefited, this is called the virtue of Heaven. Therefore, all who act in accordance with this are sagely and wise, humane and righteous, loyal and kind, compassionate and filial. For this reason, they gather all the world’s good names and are adorned with them.

Why is this so? It is because they align with the will of Heaven.

Someone asked: "What is meant by *forceful rectitude*?"
The answer: The great attack the small; the strong bully the weak; the many plunder the few; the cunning deceive the foolish; the noble disdain the humble; the rich arrogantly scorn the poor; the robust seize from the aged. Thus, among the various states of the world, all employ water, fire, poison, and weapons to harm one another. In serving above, this does not benefit Heaven; in the middle, it does not benefit the spirits; below, it does not benefit the people. With threefold harm and nothing benefited, this is called *banditry*. Therefore, all who act in accordance with this are rebels and usurpers, thieves and robbers, inhumane and unrighteous, disloyal and unkind, uncompassionate and unfilial. For this reason, they gather all the world’s evil names and are burdened with them.

Why is this so? It is because they oppose the will of Heaven.

Thus, Master Mozi established the will of Heaven as a standard, like a wheelwright’s compass or a carpenter’s square. Now, wheelwrights use compasses and carpenters use squares to distinguish between round and square. Therefore, Master Mozi established the will of Heaven as a standard, by which I know how far the gentlemen and nobles of the world have strayed from *rightness*.

How do I know they have strayed so far from *rightness*? Consider this: when the lord of a great state is lenient, he says, "I rule a great state, yet I do not attack small states—how then can I be great?" Thus, he selects his most cunning advisors, assembles his chariots and troops, and attacks blameless states. They breach their borders, reap their crops, fell their trees, and destroy their city walls to fortify their own moats. They burn ancestral temples and slaughter sacrificial animals. Those who resist are violently seized, while those who do not are bound and taken away. The men are made into servants and laborers, the women into millers and cooks. Yet these lords who delight in conquest do not recognize this as inhumane and unrighteous; instead, they boast to neighboring lords, saying, "I have attacked a state, crushed an army, and slain so many generals!" The neighboring lords, likewise unaware that this is inhumane and unrighteous, prepare gifts of furs and silk, dispatch envoys, and offer congratulations. Thus, these lords who delight in conquest, though they do not recognize this as inhumane and unrighteous, record their deeds on bamboo and silk and store them in their archives. Their descendants, wishing to follow their ancestors’ ways, will say, "Why not open our archives and admire our forebears’ glorious methods?" They will not say, "These were the ways of Wen and Wu, the true *rectitude*." Instead, they will say, "I have attacked a state, crushed an army, and slain so many generals!" Thus, these lords who delight in conquest do not recognize this as inhumane and unrighteous, nor do their neighboring lords. This is why conquest continues unceasingly, generation after generation. This is what I mean when I say they do not recognize great matters.

But what of small matters? If someone were to enter another’s garden and take their peaches, plums, melons, or ginger, they would be punished upon discovery, and the crowd would condemn them. Why? Because they reap the fruits of another’s labor, taking what is not theirs. How much worse, then, is it to breach another’s walls, seize their sons and daughters, plunder their treasuries, steal their gold, jade, silk, and fine fabrics, or burn their stables and steal their cattle and horses? How much worse is it to kill even one innocent person?

Yet when kings, dukes, and great officials govern, if someone kills an innocent person, breaches another’s walls, seizes their sons and daughters, plunders their treasuries, steals their gold, jade, silk, and fine fabrics, or burns their stables and steals their cattle and horses—or even steals peaches, plums, melons, or ginger—they mete out punishments no different from those of the ancient sage-kings Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu. Yet today, the lords of the world still invade, attack, and annex one another, killing thousands upon thousands of innocent people. They breach walls, seize sons and daughters, plunder treasuries, steal gold, jade, silk, and fine fabrics, burn stables, steal cattle and horses, and take peaches, plums, melons, and ginger—all by the thousands upon thousands. And they call this *rightness*!

Thus, Master Mozi said: "Those who reproach me for this are no different from those who cannot distinguish between black and white or sweet and bitter." Consider this: if someone were shown a little black and called it black, but shown much black and called it white, they would say, "My eyes are confused; I cannot tell black from white." Or if someone tasted a little sweetness and called it sweet, but tasted much and called it bitter, they would say, "My mouth is confused; I cannot tell sweet from bitter." Yet when kings and great officials govern, if someone kills a person in their own state, they punish it. But if someone kills many people in a neighboring state, they call it *civilized rightness*—how is this different from confusing black and white or sweet and bitter?

Therefore, Master Mozi established the will of Heaven as a standard. It is not only Master Mozi who took the will of Heaven as a model; the *Way of Great Xia* in the books of the former kings states: "The Lord said to King Wen, 'I cherish and make bright your virtue. Do not be loud in voice or harsh in countenance; do not prolong summer with change. Act without cunning, without knowledge, following the patterns of the Lord.'" This is how the proclamation instructed King Wen to take the will of Heaven as a model and follow the patterns of the Lord.

Now, if the gentlemen and nobles of the world truly wish to practice *humaneness* and *rightness*, to become superior men, to uphold the *Way* of the sage-kings above and benefit their states and people below, they cannot fail to examine the will of Heaven.

The will of Heaven is the warp of *rightness*. Now, the gentlemen of the world who discuss *rightness* often say: "If one possesses wealth and nobility, then one may establish *rightness*." Yet this is not the *rightness* that Heaven desires. Heaven desires those who love others and benefit others; it does not desire those who harm others and rob others. Moreover, how can it be that those who possess wealth and nobility alone may establish *rightness*?

Consider this: when Heaven sends down its seasons, it does not favor the great states over the small, nor the noble over the humble. It bestows its *virtue* and *power* equally upon the wise ruler and the foolish ruler alike. Now, if one wishes to love others and benefit others, to practice *humaneness* and *rightness*, one cannot but take the will of Heaven as a model.

For what does Heaven desire, and what does it detest? Heaven desires *rightness* and detests *unrightness*. Thus, when one leads the people of the world to practice *rightness*, one is acting in accordance with the will of Heaven. When one acts in accordance with the will of Heaven, one is being filial toward Heaven. When one is filial toward Heaven, one is certain to be rewarded. When one leads the people of the world to practice *unrightness*, one is opposing the will of Heaven. When one opposes the will of Heaven, one is being unfilial toward Heaven. When one is unfilial toward Heaven, one is certain to incur misfortune.

Now, what does it mean to act in accordance with the will of Heaven? It means to love others without partiality and to benefit others without expectation. What is the origin of this? It lies in not attacking other states, not disturbing the people’s households, not killing the innocent. Where there is no attack on other states, no disturbance of the people’s households, no killing of the innocent, how can there be harm to others or robbery of others? How can there be those who possess wealth and nobility alone establishing *rightness*?

Thus, the will of Heaven is the warp of *rightness*. The gentleman who wishes to practice *rightness* cannot but examine the will of Heaven.

**Mozi, Book Eight**
**Chapter Thirty: "On Ghosts, Part Two"**
**Chapter Thirty-One: "On Ghosts, Part Three"**

Mozi said: When the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties passed away, the world lost its *rightness*, and the feudal lords relied on force to rule. Thus, rulers and ministers, superiors and inferiors ceased to act with kindness and loyalty; fathers and sons, elder and younger brothers ceased to act with compassion, filial piety, brotherly respect, integrity, and goodness. Governors failed to exert themselves in hearing and administering governance, while the common people failed to exert themselves in their labors. The people turned to licentiousness, violence, banditry, and theft, using weapons, poison, water, and fire to harm the innocent on roads and paths, seizing others’ carriages, horses, clothes, and furs for their own gain—all these evils arose from this. Hence, the world fell into chaos.

What was the reason for this? It was because people doubted the existence of ghosts and spirits and did not understand that ghosts and spirits reward the worthy and punish the violent. If today the people of the world truly believed that ghosts and spirits reward the worthy and punish the violent, how could the world be in chaos?

Those who deny the existence of ghosts say: "Ghosts and spirits simply do not exist." Day and night, they teach this to the world, causing the multitudes to doubt whether ghosts and spirits exist or not. This is why the world is in chaos.

Therefore, Mozi said: If the kings, dukes, ministers, and gentlemen of the world truly wish to seek what benefits the world and eliminate what harms it, they must not fail to examine whether ghosts and spirits exist or not. Since it is clear that this matter cannot be left unexamined, how then should we investigate it?

Mozi said: The way to determine the existence or non-existence of ghosts and spirits must be based on the actual knowledge of the multitude—what they have heard and seen. Those who have not heard or seen them may claim they do not exist. But why not inquire in every village and hamlet, from ancient times to the present, since the beginning of human life? Have there ever been those who have seen the forms of ghosts and spirits or heard their voices? If so, how can one say ghosts and spirits do not exist? If no one has heard or seen them, then can one say they do exist?

Those who deny the existence of ghosts say: "The number of those in the world who claim to have heard or seen ghosts and spirits is beyond counting. But who has truly heard or seen the forms of ghosts and spirits?"

Mozi said: If we rely on what the multitude has collectively seen and heard, then consider the case of Du Bo in ancient times. King Xuan of Zhou killed his minister Du Bo without just cause. Du Bo said: "My lord has killed me without cause. If the dead are without knowledge, then let it be. But if the dead have knowledge, within three years, I will surely make my lord know it." Three years later, King Xuan of Zhou assembled the feudal lords for a hunt in the Pu Field. Hundreds of chariots followed, thousands of men filled the wilds. At midday, Du Bo, riding a white horse and a plain carriage, clad in red robes and cap, holding a red bow and red arrows, pursued King Xuan of Zhou. He shot an arrow into the king’s chariot, piercing his heart and breaking his spine. The king choked and died in his chariot. At that time, none of the Zhou followers present failed to see it, and none at a distance failed to hear of it. It was recorded in the *Spring and Autumn Annals* of Zhou. Rulers used this to instruct their ministers, fathers to warn their sons, saying: "Be cautious, be vigilant! Those who kill the innocent will not escape misfortune. The schemes of ghosts and spirits are swift and relentless!" Judging by this account, how can the existence of ghosts and spirits be doubted?

Not only does this account attest to it, but consider also the case of Duke Mu of Zheng. One day at noon, while he was in the ancestral temple, a spirit entered through the left side of the gate. It had the body of a crow, wore plain white garments with three sashes, and its face was square. Duke Mu of Zheng saw it and was terrified, trembling. The spirit said: "The Lord on High has enjoyed your bright virtue and grants you a lifespan of nineteen years. He will make your state prosperous, your descendants flourish, and ensure you do not lose your position." Duke Mu bowed twice and touched his head to the ground, saying: "May I ask who you are?" The spirit replied: "I am Gou Mang." If we take Duke Mu’s personal encounter as evidence, how can the existence of ghosts and spirits be doubted?

Not only do these accounts attest to it, but consider also the case of Duke Jian of Yan, who killed his minister Zhuang Ziyi without just cause. Zhuang Ziyi said: "My lord has killed me without cause. If the dead are without knowledge, then let it be. But if the dead have knowledge, within three years, I will surely make my lord know it." A year later, Duke Jian of Yan was about to hold the ancestral chariot race. The Yan ancestral race was as grand as the sacrifices to the altars of soil and grain in Qi, the mulberry groves in Song, and the Cloud Dream Marsh in Chu—an occasion where men and women gathered to watch. At midday, as Duke Jian was about to race on the ancestral path, Zhuang Ziyi, carrying a red staff, struck him and pinned him to his chariot. At that time, none of the Yan followers present failed to see it, and none at a distance failed to hear of it. It was recorded in the *Spring and Autumn Annals* of Yan. The feudal lords transmitted the account, saying: "Those who kill the innocent will not escape misfortune. The punishments of ghosts and spirits are swift and relentless!" Judging by this account, how can the existence of ghosts and spirits be doubted?

Not only do these accounts attest to it, but consider also the time of Duke Wen of Song, when there was a minister named Gu Gu. Once, while performing a sacrifice, the sacrificial victim was led out with a staff. The spirit addressed Gu Gu, saying: "Gu Gu, why are the jade disks and ritual vessels not filled to the proper measure? Why are the wine and sweet liquor not pure, the music and offerings not solemn? Why are the sacrificial animals not whole and fat? Why are the spring, autumn, winter, and summer sacrifices not performed at the proper times? Is this your doing, or is it Duke Wen’s?" Gu Gu replied: "Duke Wen is young and weak, still in swaddling clothes. How could he know of these matters?"

The official minister Gu Gu alone bore the responsibility for this. The spirit raised its staff and struck him, slaying him upon the altar. At that moment, none among the attending Song people failed to witness it, and none at a distance failed to hear of it—it was recorded in the *Spring and Autumn Annals* of Song. The feudal lords transmitted the account, declaring: "Those who are not reverent and cautious in sacrifices—such is the relentless punishment of ghosts and spirits!" Judging by this account, how can the existence of ghosts and spirits be doubted?

Not only does this account attest to it, but consider also the time of Duke Zhuang of Qi, when there were two men named Wang Liguo and Zhongli Jiao. These two had been litigating for three years, yet the case remained unresolved. The Duke of Qi, hesitant to execute them for fear of wrongful punishment, was equally hesitant to acquit them for fear of letting the guilty go free. Thus, he had them share a sheep and swear an oath at the altar of the state’s spirit. Both men agreed. Thereupon, they dug a pit, slaughtered the sheep, and sprinkled its blood. As the oath of Wang Liguo was being recited to its end, and the oath of Zhongli Che had only reached its midpoint, the sheep suddenly rose and gored him, breaking his leg. The spirit then struck him with a peachwood staff, slaying him at the oath-taking site. At that moment, none among the attending Qi people failed to witness it, and none at a distance failed to hear of it—it was recorded in the *Spring and Autumn Annals* of Qi. The feudal lords transmitted the account, declaring: "Those who first make false oaths—such is the relentless punishment of ghosts and spirits!" Judging by this account, how can the existence of ghosts and spirits be doubted?

Thus, Master Mozi said: "Even in the deepest ravines, the vastest forests, the most secluded valleys—places where no man dwells—one must still act with utmost caution, for ghosts and spirits observe all." Now, those who deny the existence of ghosts say: "How can the perceptions of the common people’s ears and eyes suffice to resolve such doubts?" How then do they aspire to be esteemed gentlemen of the world while dismissing the testimony of the multitude’s ears and eyes?

Master Mozi replied: "If the perceptions of the multitude’s ears and eyes are deemed untrustworthy and insufficient to resolve doubts, then do not the ancient sage-kings of the Three Dynasties—Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu—serve as proper models?" Indeed, from those of average understanding upward, all would say: "The ancient sage-kings of the Three Dynasties are worthy models." If the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties are truly worthy models, then let us examine their deeds. When King Wu attacked Yin and executed Zhou, he had the feudal lords divide the sacrifices, saying: "Let the close kin receive the inner sacrifices, and the distant kin receive the outer sacrifices." Thus, King Wu must have believed in the existence of ghosts and spirits, for when he attacked Yin and executed Zhou, he had the feudal lords divide the sacrifices. If ghosts and spirits did not exist, why would King Wu have divided the sacrifices?

Nor was this true only of King Wu’s deeds. All sage-kings, when bestowing rewards, did so at the ancestral temple, and when executing punishments, did so at the altar of the soil. Why bestow rewards at the ancestral temple? To announce that the distribution was just. Why execute punishments at the altar of the soil? To announce that the judgment was impartial.

Not only do the records speak thus, but moreover, the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties—Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou—when first establishing their states and building their capitals, each day would select the land’s central altar to establish as the ancestral temple. They would choose the tallest and most luxuriant trees to erect as the sacred altars. They would select the state’s most filial, virtuous, and upright elders to serve as invocators and ancestral masters. They would choose the finest, fattest, and most robust of the six livestock to serve as sacrificial victims. Jade tablets, bi-disks, and huang-ornaments were measured by wealth to determine their scale. They would select the most fragrant and golden of the five grains to prepare as wine, sweet liquor, and sacrificial offerings, so that the wine, sweet liquor, and grain offerings would rise and fall with the harvest’s abundance.

Thus, the ancient sage-kings in governing the world necessarily placed ghosts and spirits before men—this was their way. Therefore, it is said: the selection of officials and the examination of their merits came first; sacrificial vessels and robes were all stored in the treasury; invocators, ancestral masters, and their attendants were all appointed in the court; sacrificial victims were not kept with the common herds. Thus did the ancient sage-kings administer governance.

The ancient sage-kings made the service of ghosts and spirits their paramount duty, for ghosts and spirits are of profound importance. Yet they feared that later generations and descendants would fail to understand this, so they inscribed it on bamboo and silk to transmit to posterity. Still, they worried that these records might rot, decay, or vanish, leaving descendants without knowledge, so they carved them onto basins and bowls, engraved them on metal and stone, to lend them weight. They feared that later generations might fail to revere and uphold these teachings, so in the writings of the former kings, the sages—even a foot of silk or a single scroll—spoke repeatedly of the existence of ghosts and spirits, emphasizing it again and again.

For what reason? Because the sage-kings made this their duty.

Those who insist there are no ghosts say: "Ghosts and spirits simply do not exist." This directly contradicts the duty of the sage-kings. To contradict the duty of the sage-kings is not the Way of the true noble person. Now, those who insist there are no ghosts say: "In the writings of the former kings, there is not a foot of silk or a single scroll that speaks repeatedly of the existence of ghosts and spirits, emphasizing it again and again—what texts record such things? What texts affirm them?"

Master Mozi said: The *Book of Zhou* in the *Greater Odes* contains this. The *Greater Odes* says:
*"King Wen is on high,
Oh, radiant in Heaven.
Though Zhou is an ancient state,
Its mandate is ever renewed.
The glory of Zhou is not dim,
For the Lord’s decree is timely.
King Wen ascends and descends,
Ever at the Lord’s side.
Reverent is King Wen,
His good name unceasing."*
If ghosts and spirits did not exist, how could King Wen, after death, remain at the Lord’s side? This is how I know the *Book of Zhou* affirms ghosts.

Yet if only the *Book of Zhou* spoke of ghosts while the *Book of Shang* did not, it would not suffice as a model. Let us then examine the *Book of Shang*, which says:
*"Alas! In ancient times, when the Xia dynasty had not yet brought disaster upon itself, the hundred beasts and creeping things, even the flying birds, all followed the proper order. The earthworms and the human-faced creatures—how dared they harbor rebellious hearts? The mountain and river spirits, too, dared not disturb the peace. If one could truly act with sincerity, this would bring harmony to all under Heaven and preserve the lands below."*
Consider why the mountain and river spirits dared not disturb the peace: it was because they aided and counseled Yu. This is how I know the *Books of Shang and Zhou* affirm ghosts.

Yet if only the *Book of Yu* spoke of ghosts while the *Book of Xia* did not, it would not suffice as a model. Let us then examine the *Book of Xia*. The *Oath of Yu* says:
*"At the great battle of Gan, the king commanded six men at his side to descend and receive the oath in the central army, saying: ‘The House of Youhu has defied the Five Phases and abandoned the Three Norms. Heaven will cut off its mandate.’ He declared: ‘At high noon, today I contend with the House of Youhu for the fate of a single day. Now, you ministers, great officers, and common people, I do not seek your fields, lands, or treasures—I am carrying out Heaven’s punishment. If the left flank fails in its duty to the left, or the right flank fails in its duty to the right, it is a failure of command. If the charioteers do not govern their horses properly, it is a failure of command.’ Thus, rewards were given at the ancestral temple, and punishments were meted out at the altar of the soil. Why were rewards given at the ancestral temple? To show that the distribution of commands was just. Why were punishments meted out at the altar of the soil? To show that the judgment of cases was proper."*

Thus, the ancient sage-kings necessarily used ghosts and spirits to reward the worthy and punish the violent. Therefore, rewards were always given at the ancestral temple, and punishments were always meted out at the altar of the soil. This is how I know the *Book of Xia* affirms ghosts.

Thus, the *Classic of Documents*—the *Book of Xia*, followed by the *Books of Shang and Zhou*—speaks repeatedly of the existence of ghosts and spirits, emphasizing it again and again. For what reason? Because the sage-kings made this their duty.

Judging by the explanations in these texts, how can the existence of ghosts and spirits be doubted? In ancient times, on an auspicious day, the ding-mao day, the Zhou dynasty performed the sacrifice to the soil altar, annually offering to the spirits of the soil to prolong life and longevity. If there were no ghosts and spirits, how could such offerings prolong life and longevity?

Therefore, Master Mozi said: If ghosts and spirits truly reward the worthy and punish the violent, this principle, when applied to the state and extended to the myriad people, is indeed the Way to govern the state and benefit the people. To deny this is to claim that when officials govern the courts and bureaus without purity or integrity, or when men and women disregard proper distinctions, ghosts and spirits witness it. When the people engage in licentiousness, violence, banditry, theft, or use weapons, poison, water, or fire to harm the innocent on the roads, or seize others’ carriages, horses, clothes, and furs for their own gain, ghosts and spirits observe it.

Thus, officials dare not govern the courts and bureaus without purity or integrity. They dare not fail to reward the good or punish the violent. The people dare not engage in licentiousness, violence, banditry, or theft, or use weapons, poison, water, or fire to harm the innocent on the roads, or seize others’ carriages, horses, clothes, and furs for their own gain. Such acts cease, and none dare transgress.
