In the hidden and obscure, they emulate the clarity of ghosts and spirits. In the open and manifest, even a single person fears the judgment from above. Thus, all under Heaven is well-governed. The clarity of ghosts and spirits extends even to the hidden depths of broad marshes, mountain forests, and deep valleys—they surely know all. The punishments of ghosts and spirits cannot be evaded by wealth, nobility, great numbers, or strength, nor by martial prowess, sturdy armor, or sharp weapons—they will surely prevail.

To deny this, consider the past: King Jie of Xia, though he was Son of Heaven and possessed all under Heaven, reviled Heaven above and insulted the spirits, while below he brought calamity and arrogance upon the myriad people. He defied the August Lord and violated the great norms of the mountains and rivers. Thus, Heaven sent Tang to execute its bright punishment. Tang, with nine chariots in formation, advanced like a flock of crows, riding in great triumph. He charged through the masses, capturing and subduing the mighty Xi. Thus, though King Jie of Xia was Son of Heaven and possessed all under Heaven, though he had the mighty Xi, who could wrestle tigers and rhinoceroses and kill men with a gesture, though his people numbered in the millions and his warriors filled the marshes and mounds, he could not withstand the punishment of the spirits. This is what I mean when I say the punishments of ghosts and spirits cannot be evaded by wealth, nobility, great numbers, or strength, nor by martial prowess, sturdy armor, or sharp weapons.

Moreover, this is not the only instance. King Zhou of Yin, though he was Son of Heaven and possessed all under Heaven, reviled Heaven above and insulted the spirits, while below he brought calamity and arrogance upon the myriad people. He cast aside the elderly, murdered children, cruelly tortured the innocent, and disemboweled pregnant women.

The widowed, orphaned, and destitute wailed in despair, with none to turn to. Thus, Heaven sent King Wu to execute its luminous punishment. With a hundred chariots of war and four hundred tiger-like warriors, he first dispatched envoys to observe the states and spy on the enemy, then joined battle with the men of Yin at the Wilds of Mu. The king captured Fei Zhong and E Lai, and the masses scattered in flight. King Wu pursued them into the palace, felled the thousand-year catalpa, broke Zhou’s body and bound it with red cords, then hoisted it upon a white banner as a warning to the feudal lords of the world.

Thus, though King Zhou of Yin was Son of Heaven and possessed all under Heaven, though he had mighty men like Fei Zhong, E Lai, and Chong Hou Hu who could point at the few and kill, though his people numbered in the millions and his warriors filled the marshes and mounds, he could not withstand the punishment of the spirits. This is what I mean when I say the punishments of ghosts and spirits cannot be evaded by wealth, nobility, great numbers, or strength, nor by martial prowess, sturdy armor, or sharp weapons.

Moreover, the saying of the ancient sages goes: *"Though the crime be small, destroy the clan; though the merit be slight, extinguish the lineage."* This means that what the ghosts and spirits reward, no matter how small, they will surely reward; what they punish, no matter how great, they will surely punish.

Now, those who deny the existence of ghosts ask: *"Do you mean to harm the interests of loyal kin and act against filial piety?"* Master Mozi replied: *"The ghosts of ancient and present times are none other than these: there are heavenly ghosts, ghosts of mountains and rivers, and those who become ghosts after death. Today, there are cases where sons die before their fathers, and younger brothers before their elder brothers. Though this may seem so, the common saying goes: 'The firstborn dies first.' If this is true, then those who die first are either fathers or mothers, elder brothers or sisters. Now, when we purify wine and millet, prepare offerings, and perform sacrifices with reverence, if ghosts and spirits truly exist, then we are feeding our parents, elder brothers, and sisters—is this not a great benefit? If ghosts and spirits do not exist, then we have merely wasted the wealth spent on wine, millet, and offerings. Yet even if we discard these into the ditches and ravines, within our clans and without in our villages, all may gather to share in the food and drink. Even if ghosts and spirits do not exist, this still serves to unite the people and foster goodwill among neighbors. Now, those who deny ghosts say: 'Ghosts and spirits do not exist at all,' and thus they refuse to share in the wealth spent on wine, millet, and sacrificial animals. Are they not simply hoarding these resources? What benefit do they gain? This contradicts the teachings of the sage kings, opposes the conduct of filial children among the people, and is no way to be a superior man in the world. Therefore, Master Mozi said: 'When I perform sacrifices, I do not merely cast offerings into the ditches and ravines. Above, it serves to commune with the blessings of the spirits; below, it unites the people and fosters goodwill among neighbors. If the spirits exist, then we are feeding our parents, brothers, and sisters—is this not a great benefit to the world?' Thus, Master Mozi said: 'If the kings, dukes, great officials, and gentlemen of the world truly wish to seek the welfare of all under Heaven and eliminate its harms, they must treat ghosts and spirits as real and honor them with clarity. This is the Way of the sage kings.'*

**Against Music, Part 32**
Master Mozi said: *"The task of the benevolent is necessarily to seek the welfare of all under Heaven and eliminate its harms. Let this be the standard for the world: if it benefits the people, do it; if it does not, stop. Moreover, the benevolent, in their deliberations for the world, do not act for the beauty of their eyes, the pleasure of their ears, the sweetness of their mouths, or the comfort of their bodies, thereby depleting the people’s resources for clothing and food. The benevolent do not do this. Thus, Master Mozi’s opposition to music is not because the sounds of great bells, drums, zithers, pipes, flutes, and reed organs are not joyful, nor because carved and painted patterns are not beautiful, nor because the flavors of fattened and roasted meats are not sweet, nor because lofty towers and deep chambers are not comfortable. Though my body knows comfort, my mouth knows sweetness, my eyes know beauty, and my ears know joy, when I examine it above, it does not accord with the affairs of the sage kings, and when I measure it below, it does not benefit the people. Therefore, Master Mozi said: 'Music is wrong.' Now, though kings and great officials do not create musical instruments for the sake of the state, they do not merely gather clay and break earth to make them. They must heavily tax the people to produce the sounds of great bells, drums, zithers, pipes, flutes, and reed organs. It is like the sage kings’ creation of boats and carts—I dare not oppose it. The ancient sage kings also heavily taxed the people to make boats and carts. Once completed, they asked: 'How shall we use them?' They said: 'Boats are for water, carts for land. The noble rest their feet, and the common people rest their shoulders and backs.' Thus, the people contributed their wealth to provide these, yet they did not resent it. Why? Because it returned to benefit the people. If musical instruments likewise benefit the people, then I dare not oppose them. But the people have three great afflictions: the hungry cannot eat, the cold cannot dress, and the weary cannot rest. Yet in such times, should we strike great bells, beat drums, pluck zithers, blow pipes and flutes, and brandish shields and axes, how can the people’s resources for clothing and food be obtained? I do not believe this is necessarily so."*

Leaving this aside, consider now: when a great state attacks a small state, when a powerful clan oppresses a weaker one, when the strong plunder the weak, the many tyrannize the few, the cunning deceive the simple, the noble despise the humble—when bandits, rebels, thieves, and robbers arise together, unrestrained and unchecked. Yet in such times, should we strike great bells, beat drums, pluck zithers, blow pipes and flutes, and brandish shields and axes, how can the chaos of the world be governed and set right? I do not believe this is necessarily so.

Thus Master Mozi said: Suppose we heavily tax the people to produce the sounds of great bells, drums, zithers, pipes, and flutes, seeking to promote the world’s benefit and eliminate its harm—it would be of no avail. Therefore, Master Mozi said: Music is wrong.

Now, when kings and nobles, high ministers and great officials stand upon lofty towers and spacious pavilions to observe, the bells are like suspended cauldrons—if they are not struck, what joy can be derived? Their reasoning must be that they must be struck. Yet if they are not struck, the old and slow cannot be employed. The old and slow have dull ears and dim eyes, weak limbs, discordant voices, and clumsy movements. They must employ those in their prime, whose ears and eyes are sharp, whose limbs are strong, whose voices are harmonious, and whose brows are nimble. If men are made to perform this, they abandon the times for plowing, planting, and cultivating; if women are made to perform this, they abandon their spinning, weaving, and sewing. Now, when kings and nobles, high ministers and great officials indulge in music, they waste the people’s time for clothing and food—this is excessive. Therefore, Master Mozi said: Music is wrong.

Once the sounds of great bells, drums, zithers, pipes, and flutes are prepared, if the nobles grandly perform and listen alone, what joy can be derived? Their reasoning must be that they must share it with the lowly. Yet if they do not share it with the noble, the noble neglect their governance; if they share it with the lowly, the lowly neglect their tasks. Now, when kings and nobles, high ministers and great officials indulge in music, they waste the people’s resources for clothing and food—this is excessive. Therefore, Master Mozi said: Music is wrong.

In former times, Duke Kang of Qi established the *Music of Ten Thousand*, yet ten thousand men could not be clothed in coarse hemp nor fed with husks and dregs. He said: "If food and drink are not fine, their faces and complexions are unworthy to behold; if clothing is not fine, their bodies and movements are unsightly and emaciated." Thus, they must eat millet and meat, wear embroidered silks—those who do not engage in producing clothing and food yet consume the people’s resources. Therefore, Master Mozi said: Now, when kings and nobles, high ministers and great officials waste the people’s resources for clothing and food to indulge in music—this is excessive. Therefore, Master Mozi said: Music is wrong.

Humans are fundamentally different from birds, beasts, deer, crows, and insects. Birds, beasts, deer, crows, and insects use their feathers and fur for clothing, their hooves and claws for shoes, and water and grass for food and drink. Thus, the males need not plow, plant, or cultivate, nor the females spin, weave, or sew—their resources for clothing and food are already provided. Humans are not like this: those who rely on their strength govern, while those who do not rely on their strength do not govern. If the noble do not diligently govern, then laws and policies fall into chaos; if the lowly do not diligently work, then resources and supplies become insufficient.

If the gentlemen of the world do not accept my words, then let us briefly enumerate the world’s duties and observe the harm of music. Kings and nobles rise early and retire late to hear lawsuits and administer governance—this is their duty. Gentlemen exhaust their strength and employ their wisdom to manage internal offices and externally gather the profits of passes, markets, mountains, forests, and marshes to fill granaries and treasuries—this is their duty. Farmers rise early and return late to plow, plant, and cultivate, amassing grain—this is their duty. Women rise early and sleep late to spin, weave, and sew, producing much hemp, silk, kudzu, and fine cloth—this is their duty.

Now, if kings and nobles delight in music and listen to it, they cannot rise early and retire late to hear lawsuits and administer governance—thus, the state falls into chaos and the altars of soil and grain are endangered. If gentlemen delight in music and listen to it, they cannot exhaust their strength and employ their wisdom to manage internal offices and externally gather profits to fill granaries and treasuries—thus, granaries and treasuries remain empty. If farmers delight in music and listen to it, they cannot rise early and return late to plow, plant, and cultivate—thus, grain is insufficient. If women delight in music and listen to it, they cannot rise early and sleep late to spin, weave, and sew—thus, cloth is not produced.

It is asked: What causes the noble to neglect governance and the state to abandon its tasks? It is music. Therefore, Master Mozi said: Music is wrong.

How do we know this is so? It is said: The *Punishments of the Officials* in the books of the former kings, those of Tang, record this.

It is said: When they dance the *Huan* in the palace halls, this is called the "shaman’s wind." Its punishment: for the noble, two *wei* of silk are exacted; for the commoner, two *bo* of yellow cloth. It is then declared: Alas! The dancing sways and sways, the yellow words are most manifest—Heaven’s Mandate does not endure, and the Nine Lands perish thereby. Heaven is not pleased, and descends in false omens; that household must mourn a loss.

Examining why the Nine Lands perished, it was solely from pursuing adorned music. At the *Wu Guan*, it is said: Qi indulged in excess and wanton joy, feasting in the wilds, clanging bells and striking chimes with force, drowning in wine and glutting on food in the fields. The *Wan* dance spread its wings, its glory resounding far and wide—Heaven did not take it as a model. Thus, above, the heavenly spirits did not approve; below, the myriad people did not benefit.

Therefore, Master Mozi said: Now, all the gentlemen and lords of the world who wish to seek the benefit of all under Heaven and remove its harms must recognize that music, as a thing, cannot but be prohibited and stopped.

*Mozi, Scroll Eight, concludes*
*Mozi, Scroll Nine: "Against Music, Part Two" (missing), "Against Music, Part Three" (missing), "Against Mandate, Part One" (missing)*

Master Mozi declared: In ancient times, the kings, dukes, and great men who governed states all desired their states to be prosperous, their people numerous, and their governance orderly. Yet instead of prosperity, they found poverty; instead of a multitude, they found few; instead of order, they found chaos. Thus, they lost what they sought and gained what they detested—why was this so?

Master Mozi declared: It is because those who cling to the doctrine of fate have mingled abundantly among the people. The words of those who cling to fate say: If fate decrees wealth, one is wealthy; if fate decrees poverty, one is poor. If fate decrees a multitude, there is a multitude; if fate decrees few, there are few. If fate decrees order, there is order; if fate decrees chaos, there is chaos. If fate decrees long life, one lives long; if fate decrees early death, one dies young. Though one may strive with all one’s might, what benefit is there?

Above, this doctrine pleases kings, dukes, and great men; below, it governs the people’s undertakings. Thus, those who cling to fate are unvirtuous. Therefore, the words of those who cling to fate must be clearly examined. How then shall we clearly examine this doctrine?

Master Mozi declared: One must establish a standard. To speak without a standard is like trying to determine morning and evening while standing atop a potter’s wheel. The distinctions between right and wrong, benefit and harm cannot be clearly known. Thus, speech must have three measures.

What are the three measures? Master Mozi declared: There is the measure of foundation, the measure of origin, and the measure of application. What is the foundation? Above, it is founded upon the affairs of the ancient sage-kings. What is the origin? Below, it examines the actual experiences of the people’s ears and eyes. What is the application? It is applied in governance and penal law, observing whether it benefits the state, the people, and the populace. These are what are called the three measures of speech.

Yet today, the scholars and gentlemen of the world who claim fate exists have never truly examined the affairs of the sage-kings. In ancient times, the chaos wrought by Jie was received and rectified by Tang; the chaos wrought by Zhou was received and rectified by King Wu. The world had not changed, nor had the people altered. Under Jie and Zhou, the world was in chaos; under Tang and Wu, the world was ordered—how can this be called fate?

Yet today, the scholars and gentlemen of the world who claim fate exists have also not examined the books of the former kings. The books of the former kings, which issued state decrees and disseminated laws among the people, did they ever contain such sayings as: "Fortune cannot be sought, misfortune cannot be avoided; reverence is of no benefit, violence incurs no harm"? The laws by which judgments were heard and punishments meted out—did the former kings’ penal codes ever say: "Fortune cannot be sought, misfortune cannot be avoided; reverence is of no benefit, violence incurs no harm"? The oaths by which armies were arrayed and troops advanced or retreated—did the former kings’ oaths ever say: "Fortune cannot be sought, misfortune cannot be avoided; reverence is of no benefit, violence incurs no harm"?

Thus, Master Mozi declared: Though I have not exhaustively enumerated them, the fine books of the world cannot all be counted, but in broad terms, these five are sufficient. Now, if we do not seek the words of those who cling to fate, we need not necessarily accept them—is this not a proper rejection? To employ the words of those who cling to fate is to overturn the righteousness of the world. Those who overturn the world’s righteousness are the ones who establish fate, the critics of the common people. To propagate the criticism of the common people is to extinguish the people of the world.

Then what is the purpose of desiring the virtuous to be in high positions? It is said: When the virtuous are in high positions, the world will surely be ordered. The spirits of Heaven, mountains, and rivers will surely have a lord to rely upon, and the myriad people will receive great benefit. How do we know this?

Master Mozi said: In ancient times, Tang was enfeoffed in Bo, where he adjusted the long and short to make a territory of a hundred *li*. With his people, he practiced universal love and mutual benefit, sharing in times of plenty. He led his people to revere Heaven above and serve the spirits. Thus, the spirits of Heaven enriched him, the feudal lords allied with him, the people cherished him, and worthy scholars flocked to him. Before his generation had passed, he reigned over the world and governed the feudal lords.

Formerly, King Wen was enfeoffed in Qi Zhou, where he adjusted the long and short to make a territory of a hundred *li*. With his people, he practiced universal love and mutual benefit. Thus, those near were at peace with his governance, and those far were drawn to his virtue. Those who heard of King Wen all hastened to him. The incapable and unworthy, whose limbs were not benefited, remained and longed, saying: "How might King Wen’s land reach me? Then would I not also be like King Wen’s people?" Thus, the spirits of Heaven enriched him, the feudal lords allied with him, the people cherished him, and worthy scholars flocked to him. Before his generation had passed, he reigned over the world and governed the feudal lords.

As previously stated: When the virtuous are in high positions, the world will surely be ordered. The spirits of Heaven, mountains, and rivers will surely have a lord to rely upon, and the myriad people will receive great benefit. This is how we know it.

Thus, the ancient sage-kings issued decrees and commands, establishing rewards and punishments to encourage the worthy. Therefore, at home, they were filial and kind to their kin; abroad, they were respectful and dutiful in their villages. In sitting and dwelling, they had moderation; in going out and coming in, they had restraint. Men and women had distinction. Thus, when managing government offices, there was no theft; when guarding cities, there was no collapse or rebellion. When the lord faced peril, they died for him; when he went into exile, they followed him. These are what the rulers rewarded and the people praised.

The words of those who cling to fate say: What the rulers reward is simply what fate had already destined to be rewarded, not because of worthiness. What the rulers punish is simply what fate had already destined to be punished, not because of violence. Therefore, at home, they are not kind and filial to their kin; abroad, they are not respectful and dutiful in their villages. In sitting and dwelling, they lack moderation; in going out and coming in, they lack restraint. Men and women lack distinction. Thus, when managing government offices, there is theft; when guarding cities, there is collapse and rebellion. When the lord faces peril, they do not die for him; when he goes into exile, they do not follow him.

These are what the rulers punish and the people condemn.

Those who cling to fate say: What the rulers punish is simply what fate had already destined to be punished, not because of violence. What the rulers reward is simply what fate had already destined to be rewarded, not because of worthiness. If one takes this as a principle for being a lord, one is unrighteous; for being a minister, one is disloyal; for being a father, one is unkind; for being a son, one is unfilial; for being an elder brother, one is unvirtuous; for being a younger brother, one is undutiful. Those who stubbornly adhere to this give rise to inauspicious words and follow the way of the violent.

How, then, do we know that fate is the way of the violent? In ancient times, the impoverished among the people were gluttonous in food and drink, lazy in their duties, and thus lacked sufficient clothing and resources, bringing upon themselves the worries of hunger, cold, and starvation. Yet they did not say, "I am weary and unworthy; I do not work diligently." Instead, they said, "My fate is destined to be poor and suffering." The violent kings of ancient times could not endure the excesses of their ears and eyes, the perversity of their hearts, and their disobedience to their kin, leading to the loss of their states and the overthrow of their altars of soil and grain. Yet they did not say, "I am weary and unworthy; I govern poorly." Instead, they said, "My fate is destined to lose them."

In *The Announcement of Zhong Hui*, it is said: "I have heard from the Xia people that they falsified the Mandate of Heaven and spread it among the people. The Lord punished their evil, and their armies were utterly defeated." This speaks of Tang’s rejection of Jie’s clinging to fate. In *The Great Declaration*, it is said: "Zhou of Yin dwelled in ease, refusing to serve the Lord on High and the ghosts and spirits, bringing disaster upon his ancestral spirits and cutting off their sacrifices." Yet he said, "My people have their fate; there is no need for concern." Thus, Heaven abandoned him, casting him aside and refusing to protect him. This speaks of King Wu’s rejection of Zhou’s clinging to fate.

If we now adopt the words of those who cling to fate, then rulers will not heed governance, and the people will not attend to their duties. If rulers do not heed governance, then penal laws and administration will fall into chaos. If the people do not attend to their duties, then resources and supplies will be insufficient. Above, there will be nothing to provide for the grand sacrifices of wine and sweet liquor, to offer sacrifices to the Lord on High and the ghosts and spirits, to bestow grace upon the worthy and capable throughout the world. Abroad, there will be nothing to respond to the visits of the feudal lords’ envoys; at home, there will be nothing to feed the hungry, clothe the cold, and care for the elderly and weak. Thus, fate brings no benefit to Heaven above, no benefit to the ghosts and spirits in the middle, and no benefit to the people below. Those who stubbornly adhere to this give rise to inauspicious words and follow the way of the violent.

Therefore, Master Mozi said: Now, if the gentlemen and scholars of the world truly desire wealth for the world and detest its poverty, desire order and detest chaos, they cannot but reject the words of those who cling to fate. This is the great harm of the world.

**Against Fate, Part Three (36)**
Master Mozi said: Whenever one speaks or engages in literary discourse as a Way, one must first establish standards of rightness. If one speaks without rightness, it is like placing the markers for dawn and dusk on a potter’s wheel—even with the most skillful craftsman, one cannot obtain correctness. Yet today, the true and false in the world cannot be discerned, so speech must have three methods.

What are these three methods? There is the method of grounding, the method of tracing origins, and the method of application. In grounding, one examines the will of Heaven and the spirits and the affairs of the sage-kings. In tracing origins, one verifies with the books of the former kings. How does one apply them? By issuing them as penal laws. These are the three methods of speech.

Now, some gentlemen and scholars of the world claim that fate does not exist. How do I know whether fate exists or not? By the evidence of the common people’s ears and eyes. What is heard and seen is said to exist; what is neither heard nor seen is said not to exist. Yet have they ever examined the sentiments of the people? From ancient times to the present, since the beginning of human life, has anyone ever seen the form of fate or heard the voice of fate? There has never been such a thing. If one considers the common people foolish and unworthy, their ears and eyes insufficient to serve as standards, then why not examine the transmitted words and flowing sayings of the feudal lords? From ancient times to the present, since the beginning of human life, has anyone ever heard the voice of fate or seen the form of fate? There has never been such a thing. Then why not examine the affairs of the sage-kings?

The sage-kings of old promoted filial sons and encouraged them to serve their parents, honored the worthy and good and encouraged them to do good, issued decrees and mandates to instruct, and employed rewards and punishments to encourage and deter. In this way, the chaotic could be ordered, and the endangered could be secured. If one does not believe this, consider: what Jie threw into chaos, Tang put in order; what Zhou threw into chaos, King Wu put in order. The world did not change, nor did the people alter, yet when the rulers changed their governance, the people’s teachings were transformed. Under Tang and Wu, there was order; under Jie and Zhou, there was chaos. Peace and danger, order and chaos, depend on the rulers’ issuance of governance—how can one speak of fate? Those who say there is fate are simply incorrect.

Now, those who speak of fate say: "I did not create this for later generations; from the Three Dynasties onward, such words have been transmitted and circulated." How should one respond to this? One should say: Do those who speak of fate not recall the sagely and good people of the Three Dynasties? Or do they recall the violent and unworthy people of the Three Dynasties? How do we know this?

In ancient times, the knights and grand officers were cautious in speech and wise in conduct. Above, they had means to admonish their rulers and lords; below, they had means to instruct and guide their people. Thus, above, they received the rewards of their rulers and lords; below, they received the praise of their people. The reputations of the knights and grand officers were not forgotten, and their words have been transmitted to the present day. The world says it was by their own efforts.

Those who neglect the governance of their state and the welfare of their people, indulge in useless extravagance, and oppress the common folk, causing the lower ranks to grow estranged from their superiors—such rulers leave their nations desolate and ruined, their own bodies subject to punishment and execution. They cannot claim, "I have seen fate at work."

Thus, the violent kings of the Three Dynasties did not restrain their indulgence in sensual pleasures, nor did they guard against the perversion of their hearts and wills. Outwardly, they galloped in hunting and archery; inwardly, they drowned in wine and revelry, exhausting themselves in worthlessness. When their governance and policies failed, they inevitably declared, "It is my fate, and thus it must be." Even the impoverished commoners of the Three Dynasties followed this pattern: failing to serve their kin and superiors with goodness, despising reverence and frugality while favoring ease and simplicity, craving food and drink yet shirking labor—when their clothing and provisions fell short, leaving them to suffer cold, hunger, and want, they did not say, "I am worthless and slow in my work," but instead insisted, "My fate is inherently one of poverty." Even the deceitful people of the Three Dynasties acted no differently.

The doctrine of fate, embellished and propagated, has long misled the simple and unlearned. The sage-kings, recognizing this danger, inscribed their warnings on bamboo and silk, carved them into metal and stone. In the *Announcement of Zhong Hui* from the books of the former kings, it is said: "I have heard that in the Xia, men falsely claimed the Mandate of Heaven, proclaiming it to the people below. The Lord detested this, and thus their armies were defeated." These words condemn Jie of Xia for his belief in fate, a doctrine that Tang and Zhong Hui jointly rejected.

In the *Great Declaration* of the former kings, it is further stated: "Zhou of Yin dwelled in arrogance, refusing to serve the Supreme God, abandoning the sacrifices to his ancestral spirits. He declared, 'My people have their fate; let them not be burdened with toil.' Thus, Heaven cast him aside, no longer protecting him." These words condemn Zhou for his belief in fate, a doctrine that King Wu refuted with the *Great Declaration*.

In the *Three Dynasties’ Lost States*, it is written: "You must not exalt the notion of fate bestowed by Heaven." The *Three Lost States* likewise deny the existence of fate. In the *Decree of the Duke of Shao*, it is commanded: "Be reverent! There is no Mandate of Heaven—only we two must not spread falsehoods, nor claim that what we have not received descends from Heaven."

In the poetry and documents of the Shang and Xia, it is said: "The doctrine of fate was created by violent kings." Now, if the gentlemen and scholars of the world wish to discern right from wrong and weigh benefit against harm, they must vigorously oppose the doctrine of fate. Those who cling to fate are the greatest harm to the world, and thus Master Mozi rejects it.

**Against Fate, Part Three (Chapter 37)**
Master Mozi said: In all speech and discourse, one must first establish a standard before speaking. Without a prior standard, it is like trying to determine east and west while spinning a potter’s wheel—though one may debate morning and evening, no definitive conclusion can ever be reached. Thus, speech has three methods.

What are the three methods? They are: verification, investigation, and application. How does one verify? By examining the deeds of the former sage-kings. How does one investigate? By observing the consensus of the people’s senses and experiences. How does one apply? By implementing policies in the state and observing their effects on the myriad people. These are the three methods.

Thus, when the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties—Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu—governed the world, they declared: "We must promote filial sons and encourage them to serve their parents, honor the worthy and good, and instruct them in virtue." Therefore, they issued policies and teachings, rewarding the good and punishing the violent. By doing so, they believed the world’s chaos could be brought to order, and the peril of the altars of soil and grain could be stabilized.

If one doubts this, consider: the disorder caused by Jie was rectified by Tang; the disorder caused by Zhou was rectified by King Wu. During these transitions, the world did not change, nor did the people alter, yet when the rulers transformed their governance, the people’s customs shifted. Under Jie and Zhou, the world was in chaos; under Tang and Wu, the world was at peace. The order of the world was the achievement of Tang and Wu; the chaos of the world was the fault of Jie and Zhou. From this, we see that safety and danger, order and disorder, depend on the governance of those above—how then can one speak of fate?

Thus, when Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu governed the world, they declared: "We must ensure the hungry are fed, the cold are clothed, the weary find rest, and the disordered are brought to order." As a result, they gained illustrious reputations throughout the world. How could this be attributed to fate? It was clearly the result of their efforts.

Now, the worthy and good, who honor virtue and value the Dao and its arts, receive the rewards of kings, dukes, and great men above, and the praise of the myriad people below. They too gain illustrious reputations throughout the world. How could this be attributed to fate? It is also the result of their efforts.

Yet those who today assert the existence of fate—do they not recall the sagely and good people of the Three Dynasties? Or do they recall the violent and unworthy? If we examine their claims, they must not be referring to the sagely and good of the Three Dynasties, but rather the violent and unworthy. Indeed, those who today insist on the existence of fate point to the violent kings of the Three Dynasties—Jie, Zhou, You, and Li—who, though noble as Sons of Heaven and wealthy as rulers of all under Heaven, did not restrain their sensual desires or guard against the perversion of their hearts. Outwardly, they galloped in hunting and archery; inwardly, they drowned in wine and revelry, neglecting the governance of their states and the welfare of their people. Indulging in useless extravagance and oppressing the common folk, they ultimately lost their ancestral temples. Yet they did not say, "I am worthless and weak in governance," but instead insisted, "My fate was inherently one of loss."

Even the weak and unworthy common folk of the Three Dynasties were no different. Unable to serve their kin, rulers, and elders with goodness, they deeply despised reverence and frugality, favoring simplicity and ease. Greedy for food and drink, they were lazy in their duties, so their clothing and provisions fell short, and they thus suffered the worries of hunger, cold, and want. Yet they did not say, "I am weak and unworthy, my efforts are insufficient," but instead insisted, "My fate was inherently one of poverty."

The false people of the Three Dynasties were just the same. When the violent kings arose, they impoverished and ruined them—all because they doubted the multitude, despised simplicity, and troubled the former sagely kings. This was already a concern long before. Thus, it was inscribed on bamboo and silk, carved into metal and stone, engraved on basins and bowls, and passed down to later generations of descendants. What writings remain? The *Total Virtue of Yu* records: "Truly, it is not that Heaven is unmanifest, but that the people do not preserve it. Once the heart turns to evil, Heaven inflicts blame. If one does not carefully guard their virtue, how can Heaven’s mandate be preserved?"

The *Announcement of Zhong Hui* states: "I have heard that the people of Xia falsely claimed the Mandate of Heaven below, and the Lord thus increased their punishment, bringing ruin to their hosts." To call what is not into being is called "falsification." If something exists and is called so, how could that be called falsification? In the past, Jie clung to the notion of fate and acted accordingly, so Tang composed the *Announcement of Zhong Hui* to refute him.

The words of the *Great Declaration* say, in the *Departure of Fa*: "Alas, noble men! Heaven has manifest virtue, and its conduct is most evident. The mirror is not far off—it lies in the former Yin kings. They claimed that men have fate, that reverence cannot be practiced, that sacrifices are of no benefit, and that violence brings no harm. Heaven is not constant; the Nine Provinces perished because of this. Heaven is not compliant; prayers brought down their ruin. Only we, the Zhou, received the great mandate from the Supreme Lord." In the past, Zhou clung to the notion of fate and acted accordingly, so King Wu composed the *Great Declaration* and the *Departure of Fa* to refute him.

He said: "Why do you not examine the records of Shang, Zhou, Yu, and Xia, or consult the chapters of the *Ten Bamboo Slips*? None of them contain such claims—what then should be done?" Therefore, Master Mozi said: "When the gentlemen of the world today engage in literature or speak, it is not merely to exercise their tongues or benefit their lips and jaws. In truth, they seek to govern their states, cities, and the myriad people with proper punishments and policies."

Now, why do the kings, dukes, and great officials rise early and retire late, hearing lawsuits and managing governance, laboring all day to distribute duties without daring to rest or grow weary? They say: "If we are strong, there will be order; if not, there will be chaos. If we are strong, there will be peace; if not, there will be danger. Thus, we dare not rest or grow weary."

Why do the ministers and great officers exhaust the strength of their limbs and strain their minds, managing offices within and gathering the profits of passes, markets, mountains, forests, marshes, and weirs without daring to rest or grow weary? They say: "If we are strong, we will be honored; if not, we will be disgraced. If we are strong, we will be glorious; if not, we will be shamed. Thus, we dare not rest or grow weary."

Why do farmers rise early and return late, striving in plowing, planting, and cultivation, amassing much grain without daring to rest or grow weary? They say: "If we are strong, we will be rich; if not, we will be poor. If we are strong, we will be full; if not, we will be hungry. Thus, we dare not rest or grow weary."

Why do women rise early and sleep late, striving in spinning, weaving, and fine work, producing much hemp, ramie, and cloth without daring to rest or grow weary? They say: "If we are strong, we will be rich; if not, we will be poor. If we are strong, we will be warm; if not, we will be cold. Thus, we dare not grow weary."

Now, if even those who are not kings, dukes, or great officials truly believed in fate and acted accordingly, they would surely cease hearing lawsuits and managing governance. Ministers and great officers would cease managing offices. Farmers would cease plowing, planting, and cultivation. Women would cease spinning, weaving, and sewing. If kings, dukes, and great officials ceased hearing lawsuits and managing governance, and ministers and great officers ceased managing offices, then I say the world would surely fall into chaos. If farmers ceased plowing, planting, and cultivation, and women ceased spinning, weaving, and sewing, then I say the world’s clothing and provisions would surely be insufficient.

If one were to govern the world in this way, serving the spirits of Heaven above, the spirits would not respond; providing for the hundred clans below, the people would gain no benefit and would inevitably scatter, becoming unusable. Thus, defenses would not hold, and punishments would not prevail. This is why even the violent kings of the Three Dynasties—Jie, Zhou, You, and Li—lost their states and overthrew their altars of soil and grain.

Therefore, Master Mozi said: "Now, the scholars and gentlemen of the world who truly seek to promote the world’s benefit and eliminate its harm must reject the words of those who claim fate exists. Fate is what violent kings fabricated and impoverished men relied upon—it is not the speech of the benevolent. Now, those who practice benevolence and righteousness must carefully examine and strongly refute this."

*Against the Confucians, Part One (Chapter 38, missing)*
*Against the Confucians, Part Two (Chapter 39)*

The Confucians say: "Loving one’s kin has its methods, and honoring the worthy has its degrees." They speak of the differences between close and distant, noble and base. Their rites say: "For the mourning of parents, three years; for the eldest son, three years; for uncles, brothers, and sons by concubines, one year; for distant relatives, five months." If the duration of mourning is based on closeness, then the closer one is, the longer the mourning, and the more distant, the shorter. Thus, a wife and eldest son would mourn as long as a father. If it is based on nobility, then this would mean honoring one’s wife and son as much as one’s parents, while treating uncles and elder brothers of the clan as inferior to one’s son.

How could there be a greater perversion? When their kin die, they line the doors with white, climb onto the roof to peer into wells, poke into rat holes, and rummage through washbasins in search of the departed. If they truly believe the dead are there, their delusion is extreme. If they know the dead are gone yet still search, their deceit is profound. When taking a wife, they personally go to receive her, bowing and trembling like servants, holding the reins and offering the carriage strap as if facing a stern parent. The rites and solemnity of the wedding ceremony rival those of ancestral sacrifices. They invert the proper order, defying and dishonoring parents, elevating wives and children while wives and children encroach upon their superiors. Can such conduct toward parents be called filial?

The tradition holds that when welcoming a wife, she serves at one ancestral sacrifice, and the son will guard the ancestral temple—hence its gravity. To this, we respond: This is a falsehood. A man’s elder brother guards the ancestral temple for decades, and when he dies, his mourning period is brief, while his younger brother’s wife continues the ancestral sacrifices without interruption. Thus, mourning a wife or son for three years cannot be for the sake of guarding or serving the sacrifices. They claim that grieving for wives and children with great lamentation is to honor kinship. But to exalt what should be secondary and belittle what should be primary—is this not the height of treachery?

Some stubbornly cling to the doctrine of fate, arguing: "Longevity and early death, wealth and poverty, safety and danger, order and chaos—all are fixed by heaven’s decree and cannot be altered. Rewards and punishments, fortune and misfortune have their limits; human knowledge and effort cannot change them." If officials believe this, they will neglect their duties. If commoners believe it, they will abandon their work. Neglect leads to disorder, and slackened farming leads to poverty—poverty and disorder are the roots of misgovernment. Yet the Confucians teach this as the Way, thereby demeaning the people of the world.

Moreover, they adorn rites and music to lead people astray, prolong mourning with feigned sorrow to deceive their kin, preach fate to excuse poverty while living in idleness, abandon fundamentals and duties to seek ease. Greedy for food and drink, lazy in their labors, they fall into hunger and cold, perilously close to starvation, with no means to escape. They exhaust human *qi*, hoarding like rats and gazing like sheep, leaping like hares. The nobleman laughs at them, and they grow angry, retorting: "You fool! What do you know of true Confucians!"

In summer, they beg for wheat and grain. Once the five grains are harvested, they follow grand funerals, bringing along their entire families to feast until sated, attending to multiple burials until satisfied. They rely on others’ households for sustenance, depend on others’ fields for their livelihood, and when the wealthy have a funeral, they rejoice greatly, saying: "This is our source of food and clothing."

The Confucians say: "The noble man must wear the speech of antiquity to be humane." We reply: What they call "ancient" was all once new, and the ancients wore it—thus they were noble men. Then must one imitate what is *not* the attire of noble men, speak what is *not* the speech of noble men, to be humane?

They also say: "The noble man follows but does not innovate." We reply: In ancient times, Yi invented the bow, Zi made armor, Xizhong built the chariot, and Qiaochui crafted the boat. Then are today’s tanners, armorers, wheelwrights, and shipbuilders all noble men, while Yi, Zi, Xizhong, and Qiaochui were petty men? Furthermore, what they follow must have been created by someone—then all they follow are the ways of petty men.

They say: "The noble man, when victorious, does not pursue the fleeing, casts aside the quiver without shooting, and if the enemy is strong, assists them in righting their chariot." We reply: If all were humane, there would be no need for such rules—they would naturally guide one another by the principles of right and wrong in their choices. The ignorant would follow the wise, the uninformed would yield to the informed, the wordless would submit to reason, and upon seeing goodness, they would reform. Why would they need such constraints? But if violent men clash in brutal conflict, and the victor refrains from pursuing the fleeing, discards the quiver without shooting, and helps the enemy right their chariot, even if he did all this, he could not be called a noble man.

Imagine a brutal and cruel state where a sage arises to rid the world of harm, raising an army to punish the wicked. The victorious general issues orders to his soldiers: "Do not pursue the fleeing, cast aside the quiver without shooting, and if they falter, help them right their chariot." The violent and lawless would survive, the world’s harm would remain unchecked, and this would be to spare the murderers of parents and deeply degrade the age—no greater injustice exists.

They also say: "You are like a bell: strike it, and it rings; do not strike it, and it remains silent." We reply: The humane man serves his lord with utmost loyalty, serves his parents with filial devotion, strives for goodness and excels, and admonishes when there is fault—this is the way of a minister. But to ring only when struck, to remain silent unless questioned, to conceal knowledge and strength, to wait passively for inquiry before responding—even if his lord or parents faced great benefit or harm, he would not speak unless asked. If a great rebellion were brewing, bandits about to rise, or a trap about to spring, and he alone knew while others did not, even with his lord and parents present, he would not speak unless questioned—such a man is a traitor to great disorder.

To serve as a minister thus is disloyal, as a son is unfilial, as a younger brother is undutiful, and in dealings with others is untrustworthy. In a court that values silence, people act only for profit. Though he fears speaking last, if his lord’s words bring no benefit, he folds his hands, gazes downward, and feigns deep contemplation, saying: "It is only that he has not studied this." Whom does he serve in haste? His conduct strays far indeed.

The single Way of learning and practice is humanity and rightness. In great matters, it governs people; in small matters, it qualifies one for office. Applied broadly, it benefits all; applied narrowly, it guides the self. It does not dwell in unrighteousness, nor act against reason. It strives to advance the world’s benefit, adapting to circumstances, ceasing when benefit is achieved—this is the noble man’s Way. But what we have heard of Confucius’ conduct is fundamentally opposed to this.

Duke Jing of Qi asked Yanzi: "What sort of man is Confucius?"

Yanzi did not respond. The Duke asked again, yet he still did not respond. Duke Jing said: "Many have spoken to me of Kong Qiu, all calling him a worthy man. Now that I ask you, why do you not answer?"

Yanzi replied: "Ying is unworthy and cannot recognize a worthy man. Nevertheless, I have heard that one called a worthy man, upon entering a state, must strive to harmonize the intimacy between ruler and ministers and quell the resentment between high and low. Kong Qiu, however, was so harsh that when he learned of Duke Bai’s plot, he presented him with Shi Qi. The ruler’s person nearly perished, and Duke Bai was executed. I have heard that a worthy man, when favored by superiors, does not act in vain, and when trusted by subordinates, does not endanger them. His words, heard by the ruler, must benefit the people; his teachings, followed by those below, must accord with those above. Thus his words are clear and easily understood, his conduct easy to follow. His actions in righteousness can be made manifest to the people, and his plans and considerations can be communicated to ruler and ministers. Now Kong Qiu, with deep deliberation, conspired to serve a traitor; with exhaustive thought and all his knowledge, he acted perversely. He incited those below to disorder those above, teaching ministers to kill their ruler—this is not the conduct of a worthy man. Entering another’s state, he allied with that state’s traitors—this is not the kind that acts with rightness. Knowing a man to be disloyal, he hastened to aid him in rebellion—this is not the way of humanity and rightness. Fleeing others before plotting, avoiding others before speaking, his actions in righteousness cannot be made manifest to the people, his plans and considerations cannot be communicated to ruler and ministers. Your servant Ying sees no difference between Kong Qiu and Duke Bai, and so I did not respond."

Duke Jing said: "Alas! Many have deceived me. Were it not for you, Master, I would have gone my whole life without knowing that Kong Qiu was the same as Duke Bai."

When Kong Qiu came to Qi, he had an audience with Duke Jing. The Duke was pleased and wished to enfeoff him at Nixi, so he informed Yanzi. Yanzi said: "This cannot be. The Ru are idle and self-indulgent; they cannot be used to instruct those below. They delight in music and lead others astray; they cannot be made to govern closely. They establish fate and neglect affairs; they cannot be made to hold office. They venerate mourning and prolong grief; they cannot be made to show compassion to the people. They wear elaborate robes and force their demeanor; they cannot be made to guide the masses. Kong Qiu, with his grand appearance and meticulous adornments, bewitches the world. With strings and songs, drums and dances, he gathers disciples. With the elaborate rites of ascent and descent, he displays propriety. With the ostentatious steps of haste and circling, he impresses the crowd. Ru learning cannot be used to deliberate on the world; their exhausting thoughts cannot be put to use. Even a long life cannot exhaust their studies; in their prime, they cannot perform their rites. Accumulated wealth cannot satisfy their pleasures. With ornate and perverse arts, they scheme to ingratiate themselves with the ruler of the age. With grand music and entertainment, they corrupt the people. Their Way cannot be expected to last through the ages; their learning cannot be used to guide the masses. Now, my lord, if you enfeoff him, it will benefit Qi’s customs, but it will not serve to guide the state and lead the people."

Thus, the lord deferred the enfeoffment, receiving him with reverence yet never inquiring into his Way. Confucius, enraged at Duke Jing and Yanzi, then cultivated ties with Jiayi Zi and the gate of Tian Chang, informing Nanguo Huizi of his ambitions before returning to Lu. Soon after, hearing that Qi was about to invade Lu, he said to Zigong: "Ci! The time to launch great affairs is now." He dispatched Zigong to Qi, where he met with Tian Chang through Nanguo Huizi, urging him to attack Wu and instructing Gao, Guo, Bao, and Yan to refrain from interfering with Tian Chang’s rebellion, while also encouraging Yue to attack Wu. Within three years, Qi and Wu were shattered by the calamity of war, their corpses piled high as the plains of Wei—this was the retribution for Confucius.

When Confucius served as Minister of Justice in Lu, he abandoned the public good to side with the Ji family. The Ji clan coerced the ruler of Lu into exile, and when the Ji lord and the townspeople clashed at the city gate, the gate’s crossbar was torn down. Confucius was stranded between Cai and Chen, reduced to eating wild greens without grain, and after ten days, Zilu prepared a suckling pig for him. Confucius ate without asking where the meat had come from. Someone offered him a robe and fine wine, and he drank without inquiring into their origins.

When the Lord of Chen welcomed Confucius, he would not sit unless the mat was properly aligned, nor eat unless the meat was cut correctly. Zilu approached and asked: "Why does your conduct here differ so from that in Cai and Chen?" Confucius replied: "Come, let me tell you. Before, when we were desperate, we did not refuse ill-gotten means to survive. When sated, we feigned virtue to adorn ourselves—what greater deceit and depravity could there be?"

Confucius once sat with his disciples and remarked: "When Shun approached his blind father, Gu Sou, was the world not in peril? Was the Duke of Zhou not a true man? Why then did he abandon his home and take refuge elsewhere?" Confucius’s actions were the product of his heart’s machinations. His followers and disciples all emulated him: Zigong and Zilu aided Kong Li in Wei, Yang Hu sowed chaos in Qi, Fo Ban rebelled in Zhongmou, and they sought to carve up the land and maim the people—no greater harm could be done.

For disciples to succeed their masters, they must refine their words, model their conduct, and strive until their strength or wisdom is exhausted. Yet Confucius’s conduct was as described—how then can the Ru scholars not be doubted?

His later wife, upon seeing the corpse at the door, did not gather the remains. The Ru scholars transmitted this, saying: "The Way of the former kings was to conceal their private affairs, and their words and attire were to be followed." If people speak of this, then it is not the teaching of the former kings. The Ru scholars’ attire was like this—how could it be imitated? Moreover, the two Ru scholars of Lu, in their conduct, made things clear and easy to follow.

Formerly, when Duke Jing of Qi questioned Yanzi, saying: "Formerly, I lived for mere survival; now, I live for mere righteousness." Yanzi replied: "Who would follow such a path? If one lives for mere survival, then righteousness is discarded; if one lives for mere righteousness, then survival is cast aside. How can this be the Way?" The Duke said: "Excellent!" The Ru scholars’ teachings are like refuse—how can they be of use?

Thus, achievement comes only after attainment.
Cessation arises from duration.
Form is the division within the whole.
Necessity is unceasing persistence.
Knowledge is capacity.
Levelness is equal height.
Deliberation is seeking.
Sameness is the mutual exhaustion of length with vessels.
Knowledge is contact.
The center is equal length.
Understanding is clarity.
Thickness is having great extent.
Benevolence is embodied love.
Midday is the south of the vessel.
Righteousness is benefit.
Straightness is alignment.
Ritual is reverence.
A circle is a single center with equal length.
Action is doing.
A square is four corners with meticulous angles.
Substance is flourishing.
Doubling is becoming two.
Loyalty is striving for benefit through earnest effort.
The beginning is the foremost part of the body without sequence.
Filial piety is benefiting one’s parents.
Having a question is the center.
Trustworthiness is words aligning with intent.
A question does not extend to the periphery.
Together is self-initiated.
Inquiry is questioning emptiness.
Naming is creating designation.
Fullness is having without exception.
Integrity is acting against wrong.
Firmness and whiteness do not exclude each other.
Command is not what is self-initiated.
Harmony is mutual attainment.
Responsibility is a scholar diminishing oneself to enhance what one does.
Resemblance is having both mutual and non-mutual harmony.
Courage is the daring of the will.
Order is unquestioned and unceasing harmony.
Strength is the vigor of punishment.
Law is what is followed to be so.
Life is the dwelling of punishment and knowledge.
Together is what is so.
Externality is knowing without knowledge.
Explanation is what clarifies.
Dreaming is taking the external to be so.
Impossibility is mutual exclusion.
Equanimity is knowing without desire or aversion.
Disputation is contending over possibility.
Victory in disputation is correctness.
Benefit is what is attained with joy.
Doing is exhausting knowledge and aligning with desire.
Harm is what is attained with aversion.
Completion is fulfillment or absence.
Governance is seeking attainment.
Command is designation and cause.
Praise is clarifying excellence.
Names are universal, categorical, and private.
Slander is clarifying wrongdoing.
"Saying" is transfer, elevation, or imposition.
Elevation is modeling reality.
Knowledge is inquiry, explanation, and intimacy.
Names and reality unite to form.
Speech is the expression of elevation.
Hearing is broad and intimate.
Moreover, speech affirms.
Seeing is embodiment and exhaustion.
Ruler, minister, and commoner are interconnected through agreement.
Unity is vessel, appropriateness, and necessity.
Merit is benefiting the people.
Desiring vessel and weighing benefit, while also detesting vessel and weighing harm.
Reward is the superior repaying the merit of the subordinate.
Doing is preservation, absence, change, dissolution, governance, and transformation.
Crime is violating prohibitions.
Sameness is doubling, embodiment, unity, and category.
Punishment is the superior repaying the crime of the subordinate.
Difference is duality, embodiment, non-unity, and non-category.
Sameness and difference both reside in the one.
Sameness and difference intersect to yield existence and non-existence.
Duration is encompassing different times.
Guardianship is encompassing different places.
Hearing is the acuity of the ear.
Exhaustion is when something has no room ahead for even a foot.
Following what is heard to grasp its meaning is the discernment of the heart.
Exhaustion is nothing not being so.
Speech is the fluency of the mouth.
Beginning is the present moment.
Holding to speech to reveal intent is the disputation of the heart.
Transformation is evident change.
Assent is not uniformly beneficial.
Diminishment is partial removal.
Mastering explanations and tones is beneficial.
Skillful turning seeks its cause.
Greatly increasing sameness, the storehouse, is change.
Some follow.
Cessation distinguishes the Way by cause.
Read this text horizontally.
Vessels are without exception.
*Canon Below, Forty-First*

Circling the root.
When laws are the same, observe the law; when laws differ, observe what is appropriate.
Movement and cessation, categories with human action, reside in sameness.
What exists with another—between existence and coexistence, which truly exists?
Disparate explanations make analogical reasoning difficult, residing in the great and small.
The five phases do not constantly overcome; the explanation lies in appropriateness.
Things share names: two and state, love, food and summoning, white and seeing, beauty and, husband and shoe.
Unity, when partially abandoned, is said to be inherently so; the explanation lies in cause.
It cannot be partially removed to become two; the explanation lies in seeing and togetherness, one and two, breadth and sequence.
Desire and aversion as benefit and harm lie in appropriateness.
Inability without harm lies in harm.
Diminishment without harm lies in surplus.
Different categories do not encroach; the explanation lies in measure.
Knowing without using the five senses lies in duration.
Partial removal does not increase or decrease; the explanation lies in cause.
Necessary heat lies in suddenness.
False necessity lies in its not being so.
Knowing why one does not know lies in taking by name.
The reason for a thing’s being so, the reason for knowing it, and the reason for making it known need not be the same; the explanation lies in illness.
Nothing does not necessarily await existence; the explanation lies in what is said.
Doubt lies in encounter.
Sequence, encounter, and passing.
Apprehension without doubt lies in existence and non-existence.
Unity and combination may or may not recur; the explanation lies in resistance.
Moreover being so cannot be rectified, yet does not hinder application; the explanation lies in appropriateness and opposition.
Things are one body; the explanation lies in their being together as one and only this.
Equalizing what is severed or not lies in what is equalized.
Space may follow; the explanation lies in long space and duration.
Yao’s righteousness, born in the present yet residing in antiquity, differs in time; the explanation lies in what is righteous.
Two stand before a mirror; the reflection arrives. Many appear few; the explanation lies in the sparse region.
A dog is a hound, yet killing a dog is not killing a hound—it is permissible; the explanation lies in doubling.
Mirror position: one small and easy, one large and vessel-like; the explanation lies in the center’s inner and outer.
Commanding Yin and beauty lies in command.
A round mirror reflects one.

The "Not Hard-White" lies in [its nature].
The size of Jing—though deep, its sinking is shallow—lies in its completeness.
No duration exists apart from space.
"Hard-White" lies in dependence.
Using a railing as a conduit;
Assuming ignorance lies in intent.
That which is not yet so but will be so lies in thus inferring it.
Intent cannot be known; it lies in its utility through action.
Shadows do not follow; it lies in transformation.
One is less than two yet more than five; it lies in establishment.
Two shadows stand still; it lies in duplication.
If not halved, it cannot be renewed, then it does not move; it lies in the point.
When a shadow arrives, at noon there is a point and shadow’s length; it lies in the point.
It may not exist, yet once existing cannot be removed; it lies in what has always been so.
A shadow’s reflection is called "welcoming"; it lies in transmission.
A jar cannot be carried; it lies in grasping.
The size of shadows lies in the distance of the jar from the ground.
Space advances without proximity; it lies in dispersion.
Heaven must be a jar; it lies in attainment.
Movement follows duration; it lies in before and after.
Unyielding and unshaken; it lies in overcoming.
Those who share one standard are all alike, as squares summon one another; it lies in the square.
A contract and its branch tablet; it lies in thinness.
Mad assertions cannot distinguish differences; it lies in what cannot be.
That oxen and horses are not oxen, yet share the permissible, lies in inclusion.
The leaning cannot be corrected; it lies in shaving.
Following this and that, this and that are the same; it lies in difference.
Pushing it must go; it lies in discarding the material.
Call and response share the same concern; it lies in merit.
Buying without valuing; it lies in bargaining its price.
Hearing what one does not know as if one knows it, yet possessing both knowledge and ignorance, lies in being told.
A price is fitting and thus great; it lies in exhaustiveness.
Taking words as exhaustive—exhaustiveness lies in those words.
Fear without reason; it lies in the heart’s absence.
Only my saying is not a name and thus cannot be; it lies in the tablet.
Some exceed names; it lies in reality.
Infinity does not harm inclusiveness; it lies in whether it is full or known.
Knowing it or not, sufficiency lies in having no means; exhaustiveness lies in this.
Not knowing its number yet knowing its exhaustion; it lies in the enlightened.
To say debate has no victor is necessarily incorrect; it lies in debate.
Not knowing where it resides does not harm loving it; it lies in the loss of a child.
No yielding is impossible; it lies in the beginning.
That benevolence and righteousness are external and internal—internal lies in the countenance’s creation.
In one, there is knowledge and ignorance; it lies in existence.
The benefit of learning lies in the critic.
Pointing to two yet inescapable; it lies in using two plainly.
The validity of criticism does not depend on majority or minority; it lies in what can be affirmed or denied.
What is known yet cannot be pointed to lies in spring, the fleeing minister, dogs and hounds, and the noble.
Not criticizing is exhaustiveness; it lies in not denying.
Knowing a dog yet calling oneself ignorant of a hound is an error; it lies in duplication.
Things are not extreme; it lies in being thus.
Responding after understanding intent lies in not knowing who is speaking.
Seeking the higher by taking the lower lies in moisture.
This being so and this being the same lies in not being a state.
**Explanations of the Canons, Part One, Forty-Second**

Thus, a minor cause: having it does not necessarily make it so, but lacking it necessarily makes it not so.
It is a part, like having a point.
A major cause: having it necessarily makes it not so, like seeing comes from being seen.

A part is like one of two, the tip of a foot-rule.
Knowing material is knowing; what enables knowing must know, like clarity.
Deliberation is deliberating with one’s knowledge to seek, yet not necessarily obtaining it, like squinting.
Knowing is knowing by one’s knowledge passing through things and being able to depict them, like seeing.
Understanding is understanding by one’s knowledge discussing things, and that knowledge being evident, like clarity.
Benevolence is loving oneself not for one’s own use, unlike loving a horse—evident like clarity.
Righteousness is taking the world as fragrant and being able to benefit it, not necessarily using it.
In ritual, the noble are addressed by office, the base by name, yet both have respect and disrespect—differences lie in rank.
Action is what is done without a good name.
Action is what is done with a good name.
Skill is like being a thief.
Sincerity is the manifestation of one’s will and qi, making others like oneself, unlike gold’s sound or jade’s sheen.
Loyalty is not benefiting the weak child Hai, but stopping when entering the chamber.
Filial piety is taking one’s parents as fragrant and being able to benefit them, not necessarily obtaining it.
Truthfulness is not relying on one’s words being correct, making one see the city and find gold.
Meeting is encountering others, following the crowd.
Harmony is building this platform for that purpose, yet not doing so.
Integrity is acting only for oneself, knowing it is bait.
Commands are not carried out if not from oneself.
Responsibility is doing what one dislikes to accomplish what others urgently need.
Courage is being commanded for daring to do this, not harmed for not daring to do that.
Strength is called "below" in relation to weight, and weight is old.
Life is the life of a pillar—commerce cannot be certain.
External.
Dream.

Equanimity is vast and open.
Profit is delighting in obtaining this—then this is profit.
Its harm is not that this harms.
Obtaining this and detesting it—then this is harm.
Its profit is not that this profits.
Governance: when my affairs are well-ordered, others govern north and south.
Praise arises only from one’s conduct, and from one’s words being heeded.
One makes others supervise it.
Slander arises only from one’s conduct, and from one’s words being heeded.
Praise is declaring with elegant names, citing that actuality.
Thus speech is what all mouths can do, what issues from the people.
The people are like a painted likeness.
Speech is saying that words are like stones reaching their mark.
Moreover: from the day before is "about to," from the day after is "already," and in the present it is also "about to."
Like the stone, the ruler is one who bears such a name.
Merit does not await the season, like clothing or fur garments.
Merit does not await the season, like clothing or fur garments.
Reward.
Crime does not lie in prohibition; only harm is without crime—this is perilous indulgence.
The superior repays the inferior’s merit.
Punishment is the superior repaying the inferior’s crime.
Pity is when two people together see this pillar, like serving a ruler.
Now is long; past and present are also "about to."
Space binds east, west, home, south, north.
Exhaustion is when something cannot contain even a foot—there is exhaustion; when nothing cannot contain a foot—there is no exhaustion.
Ending is merely stopping movement.
Beginning: time may have duration or lack duration; the beginning should lack duration.
Transformation is like a quail completely becoming a quail.
Diminution is partial; the rites of inclusion are complete.
Its form may depart or remain—what remains is called diminution.
A ring is the people’s midday.
A storehouse is a partitioned cave—its appearance is constant.
Movement: partial sacrifice follows; the door pivot avoids warping.
Cessation without duration is not cessation—it is like "ox, not horse," like passing the pillar.
Cessation with duration is not cessation—it is like "horse, not horse," like a person crossing a bridge.
Necessity is called the certainty of the platform.
If brothers are one in agreement and one in disagreement, necessity is not necessary—this is not necessity.
Sameness: swiftness and madness share the same length.
In the heart’s self-affirmation, they proceed similarly.
Thickness is only having no greatness.
A circle is a compass tracing branches.
A square is a carpenter’s square revealing branches.
Doubling is two feet and one foot, merely removing one.
A point is this without sameness.
Hearing is called what lies between.
Hearing is what lies between.
A foot is before the partitioned cave and after the point, not between the point and the inner partition.
Reaching is not the reaching of equality.
A furnace is emptiness.
Between two woods, it is called where there is no wood.
Fullness is without fullness or thickness.
For the foot, there is nowhere it does not obtain.
Obtaining two: hardness and difference in location do not mutually fill, mutually oppose—this is mutual externality.
A remainder is when foot and foot are both incomplete.
A point has no point yet is complete.
A foot may be complete or incomplete.
The remainder of hardness and whiteness is mutually complete.
The remainder of forms is not mutually complete.
A point.
Comparison requires two points to be possible.
Sequence is without thickness yet can have thickness.
A model is the intent, compass, and square all together—it can serve as a model.
Thus, when it is so, the people are like the model.
That which is universally ox is not ox.
Two: there is no way to negate it.
Disputation: some call it ox, some call it not ox—this is contending over that, not both being correct.
Not both being correct, one must be incorrect—it is not like calling it a dog.
Action is desiring to move one’s finger, yet wisdom does not know its harm—this is wisdom’s fault.
If wisdom is as cautious as in writing, nothing is lost to its harm.
Yet if one still desires to separate from it, then separate from it.
This is like eating dried meat.
The harm and benefit of scratching are unknown, yet desiring to scratch is not using doubt to stop desire.
The harm and benefit outside the shop are unknown.
Rushing toward it and gaining strength—then do not rush; this is using doubt to stop desire.
Observe the principle of exhausting knowledge and aligning with desire.
Moving the finger is not foolishness, nor is cutting it off wisdom—what is done and what is not.
What is done and what is doubted are not planning.
Completion: making clothes is achieving.
Curing illness is perishing.
Command is ordering, speaking—speaking.
It need not become damp.
Thus, it must await the completion of what is done.
Naming: things are universal; actuality must await many patterns.
Calling it a horse is categorical—if the actuality is such, it must bear this name.
Calling it salt is private—this name is limited to this actuality.
Sound issues from the mouth, all bearing names, like surnames and given names.
Calling it dog or hound is naming; dog or hound is enumeration.
Scolding a dog is addition.
Knowledge is transmission and reception—hearing.
The method is not flawed—explanation.
Observing with the body is direct experience.
That by which it is called is the name.
That which is called is the actuality.
Name and actuality paired is agreement.
Intention and conduct are action.
Hearing is sometimes being told—transmission.
Observing with the body is direct experience.
Seeing is the body of the moment.
Both are exhaustive.
In ancient times: weapons stood, returning to the center, the artisan’s intent—this is correctness.
Being fitting is appropriateness; it is not that it must not be otherwise—it must be so.
The sage employs without insisting; what must be so can be without doubt.
Balance is two without partiality.
Action: building a platform early is preservation.
Illness is perishing.
Buying sacrificial wine is exchange.
Exhausting the night is sweeping away.
Following growth is governance.
Exhausting purchase is transformation.
Two names for one actuality is redundant sameness.
Not external to inclusion is bodily sameness.
Dwelling together in a room is united sameness.
Having a basis for sameness is categorical sameness.
Difference is two necessarily differing—two.
Not connected is not bodily.
.

Differing in location is non-convergence.
Having no commonality is non-categorization.
Where sameness and difference intersect, fortune’s household thrives.
Compassion encompasses presence and absence.

Comparison.
Measurement is quantity—more or less.
Unraveling thread to return to the garden is departure and arrival.
The crow’s break uses paulownia: hardness and softness.
The sword’s edge is early death: life and death.
Dwelling in a room with a child: the child is neither elder nor younger.
Two severed victories: black and white.
The center is the periphery.
Debating conduct, conduct, conduct, and learning’s substance: right and wrong.
Difficult lodging: completion and incompletion.
Brothers: both depart together.
The body remains while the will travels: existence and perishing.
Huo as a surname: origin.
Market propriety: nobility and baseness.
Assent: leaping over the city wall to shoulder the burden and halt.
Following one another, departing one another, foreknowledge, rightness, possibility—these are the five colors.
Length and shortness, front and back, lightness and weight—grasping them.

Holding to compliance is difficult to achieve; speech strives for its completion—nine principles seek to grasp it.
Method: modeling takes sameness to observe skillful transmission; modeling selects this and rejects that, inquiring into causes to observe propriety.
If among people there are those with blackness and those without, halt the black people.
If there are those who love people and those who do not, loving people in the heart—which is fitting for the heart?
When they raise what is so, taking this to be so, then raise what is not so and question it.
Though the sage may have wrongs, he does not condemn them; rectifying the five assents, all enter into knowledge with explanation.
Exceeding the five assents is like bearing debt—without justification, without explanation.
Employing the five assents is like naturalness.

Expository Sayings, Lower Forty-Third
Halt: they take this to be so and explain it as so; I take this to be not so and doubt it as so.
Calling four-footed beasts, living crows, and all things—these are matters of size.
If this is so and that must be so, then they are together.
For the elk, same name; together they intrude, not together they are two, three, or closure.
Wrap, liver, lungs, child—these are loves.
Tangerine and cogongrass: eating and gathering.
White horses are many in whiteness; seeing horses is not many in seeing—whiteness and seeing differ.
Beauty is not necessarily beautiful; not necessarily beautiful is akin to violence.
To do wrong by a person is not to do wrong—like being a husband without being a husband, making shoes to buy clothes is about the husband and the shoes.
Two and one perish; not perishing with one is partial departure.
Not yet.

There is form and substance, and then it is named;
Without form and substance, there is no naming.
Not like spreading and beauty—calling it so makes it inherently beautiful; calling it not so makes it not beautiful; without naming, there is retribution.
Seeing and not seeing separate; one and two do not fill each other—breadth follows hardness and whiteness.
Lifting without heaviness does not match the needle—it is not the task of strength.
For the crumbling of the grasp, doubling is not the task of wisdom.
Like the ears and eyes differing.
Wood and night—which is longer? Wisdom and grain—which is more? Rank, kinship, conduct, market—among these four, which is nobler?
Elk and Huo—which is taller? Elk and Huo—which is Huo?
Thread and zither—which is zither?
Partiality: both are one without change.
Borrowing: borrowing must be not so and then borrowing occurs.
The dog borrows Huo, just as the clan is Huo.
Things may harm it—this is so.
Seeing it is wisdom.
Auspiciousness commands it, employing wisdom.

The Doubtful Reed—when made into a task, it becomes a scholar’s tool; when made into a cow-shed, it brings summer cold—such is the reed.
Lift it, and it is light; discard it, and it is heavy—this is not the work of strength.
Smoothness follows carving—this is not the work of skill.
Like stone and feather, it follows its nature.
The ruin of the drinker comes from wine, as from the midday sun—this cannot be wisdom.
Is it folly?
Or is it wisdom to take one’s own view as right?
It is folly.
Together, they are one—like the four hooves of ox and horse.
Only thus: ox and horse are as they are.
Count the oxen, count the horses—ox and horse are two.
Count oxen-and-horses—ox and horse are one.
Like counting fingers: five fingers, yet five is one.
The Long Vault—moving yet having place, the vault.
The vault: south and north exist in the "now" and "not," the vault moves yet endures.
Without hardness, whiteness is obtained—necessarily they fill each other.
In the present, Yao’s good governance exists among the ancients.
If the ancients exist in the present, then Yao could not govern.
Shadow: when light arrives, shadow vanishes.
If it exists, it ceases even in the farthest past.
Shadow: two lights flank one light—the single light is the shadow.
