The man who reflects light shines like an archer’s shot.
The man below appears high; the man above appears low.
Feet block the lower light, thus forming shadow at rest.
The head blocks the upper light, thus forming shadow below.
Distance and proximity have their limits with light—thus the shadow is contained within.
Shadow: the sun’s light reflects back onto a man, so the shadow lies between sun and man.
Shadow: a wooden coffin’s shadow is short and large.
When the wood is upright, the shadow is long and small.
In size relative to the wood, the shadow is larger than the wood—not merely smaller.
From afar or near, facing or upright, the mirror’s reflection is few, its appearance—whether fair or dark—differs with distance, coffin, uprightness, and light.
Shadows should converge together; departures should coincide.
Both use the north.
The mirror’s scent—nothing is unreflected in the mirror.
The scent of shadows is numberless, yet must pass through the upright.
Thus, in the same place, their forms are together, yet the mirror divides.
Within the mirror’s center.
The nearer the mirror is to the center, the larger the reflection and the shadow.
Farther from the center, the smaller the reflection and the shadow.
Yet it must be upright.
Rising from the center’s green upright, extending its straightness.
Outside the center, the nearer the mirror is to the center, the larger the reflection and the shadow.
Farther from the center, the smaller the reflection and the shadow.
Yet it must change, aligning with the upright and extending its straightness.
The mirror: the nearer the viewer, the larger the reflection and the shadow.
Farther away, the smaller the reflection and the shadow.
Yet it must be upright.
The shadow passes the upright.
Thus, lifting a balance beam with a crossbar—why does it not bend like a heavy weight?
The pole overcomes the weight.
When the right crossbar is tied with a rope, no addition causes bending—the pole does not overcome the weight.
If weight is added to one side of the balance, it must tilt.
When weight and counterweight are equal, they balance—then the root is short and the tip long.
If both sides are weighted equally, the tip must descend, for the tip gains the counterweight.
Lifting requires strength; pulling requires none—what is lifted does not stop at the point of application.
The rope controls the lift, like piercing with an awl.
In lifting, the long and heavy descend; the short and light ascend.
The higher ascends, the more it gains; the lower descends, the more it loses.
When the rope is straight and weight and counterweight are equal, it is centered.
In pulling, the higher loses more; the lower gains more.
When the weight above is exhausted, the lift is complete.
Two wheels high, two wheels as wheels—a cart’s ramp.
Weight its front, string its front.
Load the string at the front, load the string at the axle, and suspend weight at the front.
This is the ramp: lift and lift again, then it moves.
All weight: if not lifted above, not pulled below, not forced from the side, then it descends straight.
Earth—something harms it.
The one who breaks the ramp cannot obtain it—it is straight.

Here the abandoned measuring-rod lies on level ground—weight does not descend, for there is no counterweight.
When the rope draws the axle, it is as if pulling crosswise from within a boat.
Lean, double, resist, harden—if one lies propped, it is not upright.
Who pairs white stone with plain stone? That is the method.
Place a square stone one foot above the ground, set a fulcrum beneath it, and suspend a silk thread from above, letting it just reach the square stone—if it does not descend, it is a pillar.
When the thread is glued to the stone, it is lifting.
When the thread snaps, it is pulling.
Before the thing changes, its name shifts—this is gathering.
In trade, knife and grain set each other’s price.
When knives are light, grain is not dear; when knives are heavy, grain is not cheap.
The royal knife does not change, but grain changes—when grain changes yearly, so does the knife’s value.
It is like a fasting boy.
When the price is exhausted, all that remains is what was not suspect.
What does not depart in good order is called "Buddha."
A jar’s price may be fitting or not, just as a jar may be desired or not.
Like a ruined state’s fasting chamber, marrying off a child who has no child.
In war, one does not necessarily die or live; hearing of battle, one does not necessarily survive.
Before, there was no fear—now, there is fear.
Some know that this is not that, or that this is not in that, yet still call this south or north, passing by and taking themselves as right.
At first, they called this south—thus now they call this south.
To discuss knowledge: without knowledge, there is no means.
To say "this is called" is not the same as "that is called"—then they are different.
If the same, one might call it a dog, or one might call it a hound.
If different, one might call it an ox, or an ox might be called a horse.
With neither prevailing, there is no dispute.
Disputation is when some call it right and some call it wrong—the correct one prevails.
Wine without deference is not yet deference; at the beginning, it cannot be deferred.
In stone, there is one, yet hardness and whiteness are two—and both are in the stone.
Thus there is knowledge of one, and lack of knowledge of the other—this is permissible.
If you know this, but do not know what I first raised, it is weighty.
Then you know this, but do not know what I first raised.
This is one.
To say there is knowledge of one and lack of knowledge of the other...
If you know it, then when you point to what you know and tell me, I will know it.
To point to both is to make two.
To weigh the pointing is to make three straight.
If you insist on pointing only to what I raise and not to what I do not raise, then you cannot point alone.
What one wishes to convey does not transmit—intent remains unchecked.
Moreover, what you know is this, and what you do not know is this—then this is knowing and not knowing the same thing. How can it be one, to say there is knowledge of one and lack of knowledge of the other?
As for spring, its grasp is inherently unpointable.
A fleeing minister does not know his whereabouts; dogs and hounds do not know their names.
A leaver is one who cannot manage both.
To know a dog is weighty; to know a hound is also weighty—then it is excessive.
Without weight, it is not excessive.
In communication, when asked, "Do you know hunger?" one replies, "What do you mean?"
If they say "almsgiving," then one knows.
If one does not ask what "hunger" means but directly answers without knowing, it is excessive.
Moreover, a response must respond—when asked, the response should be timely, and long responses have depth and shallowness.
Heaven’s constancy is centered; soldiers are long in their place.
A hall is where it exists; its child is what exists.
If one relies on what exists to ask about the hall, how can it exist?
If one takes the hall as primary and asks what exists, what exists?
This is one: taking what exists as primary to ask about where it exists, and taking where it exists as primary to ask about what exists.
Five elements combine: water, earth, fire—fire separates thus.
Fire melts metal—fire is abundant.
Metal consumes charcoal—metal is abundant.
Their union is the storehouse of wood; wood separates from wood.
If one knows the number of elk and fish, only what is beneficial matters—there is no desire or aversion.
Harming life shortens lifespan; the explanation lies in "few connections"—whose love is this?
Having much grain, some wish to prevent harm.
It is like wine in relation to man.
Moreover, forgiving benefits others—if it is love, then only forgiveness is not governance.
Removing excess from the sated brings sufficiency without harm; harming the sated is like injuring an elk without a spleen.
Moreover, there is harm before benefit in knowledge—like treating blindness in the blind.
Knowledge sees with the eyes, and the eyes see with fire, but fire does not see.
Only with the five senses does knowledge last, but not appropriately.
Seeing with the eyes is like seeing with fire.
Fire means fire’s heat, not the heat of fire.
I have something like seeing the sun with knowledge.
Mix what is known and what is not known and ask about it—then one must say: this is what I know, and this is what I do not know.
Taking and leaving are both possible—this is knowing both.
Without, as if without, there is existence and then none.
Without heaven’s collapse, there is none and then none.
To doubt without speaking is meaningless.
Zang is dead now, but in spring, obtaining Wen Wen’s death is possible.
Moreover, it is still so.
Moreover, moreover, it must be so.

Moreover, it must cease, and it must cease.
Moreover, ceasing only after effort is made means it must cease after effort is made.
Equilibrium: when a hair is evenly suspended, it is light, yet if pulled taut, it snaps—this is not equilibrium.
In equilibrium, its snapping is no snapping at all.
Yao and Huo: some judge people by name, others by reality.
To praise a wealthy merchant as a friend is to judge by name.
To point at a leaping rabbit is to judge by reality.
Yao’s righteousness is a sound that endures to this day, while the reality of what it signifies lies in antiquity.
It is as precarious as standing at the city gate or at Zang’s tomb.
A dog is a canine; to call it a "killed dog" is acceptable, like two things slipping free.
An envoy is one who is sent.
If I send myself, I do not send myself—yet I am still sent.
A halberd sent is still sent; a halberd not beautiful is still sent—a halberd.
Jing’s sinking refers to carving cowrie shells.
Thus, sinking shallowly is not the same as carving shallowly.
Like exchanging one-fifth of something, as with the transmission of a pillar’s load: when seen, its meaning remains unchanged, for prior knowledge and intent align.
If a pillar is lighter than autumn, its meaning becomes vast and boundless.
Hammer, mallet, and awl all serve in shoemaking—they are usable.
To complete embroidered shoes beyond the mallet’s work, or to complete the mallet’s work beyond embroidered shoes, is to exceed the task.
One contains five, and one is contained in five—twelve in total.
Not splitting anew: advancing forward to take.
Forward, and the center remains unhalved, like a point.
Taking from front and back, the point lies in the center.
Splitting must halve; without giving or not halving, it cannot be split anew.
It may not exist, but once supplied, it must be supplied and cannot be absent.
Duration may be finite or infinite.
The "correct nine" is nowhere it does not centrally suspend—like a game of pitch-pot.
The pivot of the universe cannot be singly raised—it is the cosmos.
One who travels first covers what is near, then what is far.
A traveler is a traveler; they must first go near, then far.
Far cultivation is near cultivation; priority and posteriority lie in duration.
People’s cultivation must endure through time.
One square’s appearance exhausted, all squares share a principle yet differ—some wood, some stone—yet this does not harm their mutual alignment.
Exhausting appearances is like squares: all things are thus.
An ox’s madness and a horse’s frenzy differ because an ox has teeth while a horse has a tail.
To argue that an ox is not a horse on these grounds is untenable.
Both possess attributes, neither wholly possesses nor wholly lacks them.
To say an ox and a horse are dissimilar because an ox has horns while a horse does not is to cite dissimilar categories.
To claim their dissimilarity based on an ox having horns and a horse not is a reckless assertion—like an ox having teeth and a horse a tail.
To deny an ox is not an ox is acceptable.
Thus, to say an ox is or is not an ox is also acceptable.
Therefore, to say "ox-horse is not ox" is not yet permissible, nor is "ox-horse is ox."
Thus, some cases are permissible, others not—yet to say "ox-horse is ox" is neither permissible nor impermissible.
Moreover, an ox is not two, a horse is not two, yet ox-horse is two.
Thus, an ox is not a non-ox, a horse is not a non-horse, yet ox-horse is neither ox nor horse—this presents no difficulty.
That: in rectifying names, "that" and "this" are permissible.
"That" limited to "that," "this" limited to "this"—"that" and "this" are not permissible.
If "that" is also "this," then "that" and "this" are permissible.
If "that" and "this" are limited to "that" and "this," and if they are thus, then "that" is also "this" and "this."
In leading, there is no fault; nothing is encompassed, like a shield.
In harmonizing, there is no fault; it is service, unavoidable.
To lead without harmony is to fail to learn.
With little knowledge and no learning, one must remain ignorant.
To harmonize without leading is to fail to teach.
With knowledge and no teaching, merit swiftly fades.
To make one rob another’s clothes incurs a penalty, light or heavy.
To make one give another wine may be generous or meager.
What is heard outside is what is unknown.
Some say the color of one inside the room is like this color—this is knowing the unknown as if it were known.
It is like calling white black—who prevails?
It is like its color.
If whiteness must be white, now knowing its color is like whiteness, one knows its whiteness.
Names clarify what is known to rectify what is unknown, not to cast doubt on what is known with what is unknown.
Like measuring an unknown length with a ruler.
Externally, it is intimate knowledge; within the room, it is explanatory knowledge.
To insist is impermissible.
Words of coming and going are permissible—this is not insistence, so there is permissibility.
That person’s words are impermissible; if taken as appropriate, they must be inexact.
Only to say this is permissible for Huo, yet it is not that Huo—this is to affirm what is affirmed.
What cannot be said should not be insisted upon.
If one insists on what is said, then my words do not proceed.
If one does not insist on what is said, then they do not proceed.
Without south: if finite, it can be exhausted; if infinite, it cannot.
Whether finite or infinite is unknown, thus whether exhaustible or inexhaustible is unknown, and whether people are filled or not is unknown.
Thus, whether people can be exhausted or not is also unknown.
Yet to insist that people can be exhaustively loved is insistence.
If people do not fill what is finite first, then people are finite.
Exhausting the finite presents no difficulty.
Filling the infinite means the infinite is exhausted—exhausting the finite presents no difficulty.
Not knowing their number, how can one know the full pattern of loving the people?
Perhaps the question is misplaced.
To exhaustively question people is to exhaustively love those questioned.
If one does not know their number yet knows the full pattern of loving them, this presents no difficulty.
Benevolence is benevolent love.
Righteousness is benefit.
Love and benefit are this.
What is loved and benefited is that.
Love and benefit do not serve as inner and outer to each other; what is loved and benefited also do not serve as outer and inner.
To claim benevolence is internal and righteousness external, citing love and what is benefited, is a reckless assertion.

If the left eye exits and the right eye enters, this is learning—yet one may not know that learning is without benefit, hence the instruction is given: this is correct. To know that learning is without benefit is teaching; to teach that learning is without benefit is earnest. In debate and criticism, whether criticism is valid or not depends on the validity of the principle: if the principle can be criticized, even much criticism is correct; if the principle cannot be faulted, even little criticism is wrong. Now to claim that much criticism is invalid is like measuring length by shortness. Not criticizing is not criticizing oneself. Not faulting criticism is not faulting what can be faulted. What cannot be faulted is not faulting criticism. Things may be extremely long or extremely short, yet none are longer than this, none shorter than this—this is correctness. What is not this is nothing more incorrect than this. Taking high and low as the measure of good and not good is not as fitting as mountains and marshes. Dwelling in lowliness is better than dwelling in eminence, for the low is what the high requests. If not this, then this is both this and thus. Now if this pattern is in this but not in that, then this is not patterned. If this is not patterned, then it is this yet not patterned. Now if this is not patterned in this but patterned in [textual note #57], then pattern and this, patterned and not patterned, are spoken of as the same. *Mozi, Scroll Ten, Concludes*

#55 Wang Yinzhi conjecturally adds the character *you* (有) after *niu* (牛). #56 *Xian Gu* omits the character *ke* (可). #57 *Yu* (於) is written as *yu* (与) in *Xian Gu*.

**Mozi, Scroll Forty-Four**

Heaven’s love for humanity is less than the sage’s love for humanity;
yet its benefit to humanity surpasses the sage’s benefit to humanity.
The great person’s love for the petty person is less than the petty person’s love for the great person;
yet their benefit to the petty person surpasses the petty person’s benefit to the great person.

To love Zang because he is one’s kin is not to love one’s kin;
to benefit Zang because he is one’s kin is not to benefit one’s kin.
To take pleasure as beneficial to one’s child and desire it for the child is to love the child;
to take pleasure as beneficial to one’s child and seek it for the child is not to benefit the child.

Within what the body embodies, weighing light and heavy is called *weighing*.
Weighing is not for the sake of *this* nor against the sake of *not-this*.
Weighing is correctness.

Severing a finger to preserve the arm—
amidst benefit, one takes the greater; amidst harm, one takes the lesser.
Taking the lesser amidst harm is not to take harm but to take benefit.
What one takes is what people grasp.

Encountering a bandit and severing a finger to save the body is benefit;
the encounter with the bandit is harm.
Severing a finger or severing a wrist, the benefit to the world is the same—there is no choice.
Life and death are equally beneficial; there is no choice in this.

Killing one person to preserve the world is not killing one person to benefit the world.
Killing oneself to preserve the world is killing oneself to benefit the world.

Amidst affairs, weighing light and heavy is called *seeking*.
To seek is not *for* it.
Taking the lesser amidst harm, seeking is for *rightness*, yet not *for* rightness.

To tell the violent of Heaven’s *this* is to affirm their nature;
to sing to the violent of Heaven’s *not-this* is to deny their nature.

When all those who hold fast to their positions have already acted, and I act in accordance with their positions,
what they hold fast to is based on what I do.
If those who hold fast to their positions have not yet acted, and I act in accordance with their positions,
then what they hold fast to is based on what I do.

The violent person does what I do to affirm Heaven’s *not-this* as *this*—this is their nature.
What cannot be corrected is corrected.

Taking the greater amidst benefit is not a matter of necessity;
taking the lesser amidst harm is a matter of necessity.
Taking what one does not yet possess is taking the greater amidst benefit;
abandoning what one already possesses is taking the lesser amidst harm.

Rightness may be thick or thin—thicken what should be thick, thin what should be thin—this is called *ethical gradation*.
Virtue and conduct, rulers and superiors, elders and seniors, kin and relatives—these are all to be thickened.
To thicken the senior is not to thin the junior.
Thicken the close.
Thick.
Thin the distant, thin.
When closeness is utmost, thinness does not reach.
Rightness, though thickened for kin, does not match conduct but follows conduct.

To thicken Yu for the world is for Yu’s sake.
To thicken love for Yu for the world is to love as Yu’s people love.
Thickening Yu’s addition to the world does not add to Yu’s thickness in the world.
It is like hating the bandit’s addition to the world yet not adding to the bandit’s hatred in the world.

Loving others does not exclude oneself; oneself is within what is loved.
Oneself being within what is loved, love extends to oneself.
The love of oneself in ethical gradation is love for others.

The sage hates illness, not peril and hardship.
The correct body does not move; it desires others’ benefit, not others’ harm.

The sage does not act for the sake of his chamber because of Zang’s sake—it lies in Zang.
The sage cannot attend to the affairs of a child.
The sage’s principle: when kin die, it is for the world.
Thickening kin is a matter of division—dying for them, the body thirsts for the rise of benefit.

To have thickness and thinness without ethical gradation is to rise in benefit for oneself.

**Explanatory Sayings:**
Explanatory sayings are not like the white horse—grasping the foal and seeking to explain it, Mo Di’s explanation is not so.
The great dance of the great fisherman is not so.
Three things must be complete before life is sufficient.

Zang’s love for himself is not for the sake of those who love themselves.
Thickness does not exclude oneself.
Love has no thickness or thinness—elevating oneself is not worthy.
Rightness is benefit; unrightness is harm.
Intent and effect distinguish them.

There is having in the Qin horse, there is having in the horse—this is knowing the horse that comes.
Loving the many ages is like loving the few ages.
Universal love is the same in this.
Loving the present age and loving future ages are like the people of this age.

Ghosts are not human.
The elder’s ghost is the elder.
The world’s benefit is vast.

The sage has love but no benefit—this is Ni Ri’s saying, a guest’s saying.
The world has no people—this is Mozi’s saying.
Yet it remains.
To desire what cannot be avoided is not to desire what is not desired.

It is not killing Zang.
Killing the bandit exclusively is not killing the bandit.

**General Learning: Loving Others**
The small circle’s roundness is the same as the great circle’s roundness.
Whether a square reaches a foot or does not reach a bell, its failure to reach is the same—the difference lies in near and far.
This is a jade tablet, this is jade.
To intend the pillar is not to intend the wood.
To intend the wood of this pillar is so.
To intend the person of this finger is not to intend the person.
To intend the capture is to intend the bird.

Intent and effect cannot follow one another.
Benefiting others is for their sake.
Enriching people is not for their sake.
There is acting to enrich people.
Enriching people is governing people—there is acting as a ghost in this.
To reward and praise the benefit of one person is not to reward and praise the benefit of people.
Nor does it reach the point of having no value for people.

Knowing one benefit for kin is not yet filial; nor does it reach the point of knowing not to seek benefit for oneself from kin.

Knowing that this world has thieves, one loves the whole world.
Knowing that this chamber has a thief, one does not love the whole chamber.
Knowing that one person is a thief, one does not love both persons.
Though it be but one person who is a thief, if one does not know where he is, one detests all the weak.

What the sages first attend to is the correspondence of name and reality.
Name and reality need not match.
If this stone is white, even if this stone is broken, it is entirely the same as white.
If this stone is only large, it is not the same as large—there is a convenience in such a designation.

What is named by form and appearance must be known as a certain thing, and thus one knows that thing.
What cannot be named by form and appearance is not known as a certain thing, yet knowing that thing is permissible.

In all matters of position and fate, whatever enters into their midst is deemed correct; to depart from them is deemed incorrect.
In matters of position and fate, as with those of a village or the states of Qi and Jing, all are deemed correct.
In matters of form and appearance, as with mountains, hills, chambers, and ancestral temples, all are deemed correct.

Knowing and intent differ.
There are similarities: shared weight, shared tools, shared connections, shared kind, shared name, shared mound, shared Zhou, shared "this," shared "so," and shared root.
There are differences: opposition, non-conformity.
Some differences arise from similarity; some similarities give rise to difference.

One may say: this is so and thus it is so; this is so and thus it is not so; this is change; this is force.
Deep—make it deep; shallow—make it shallow; increase—make it increase; honor—make it honor.
Examine the sequence, compare the causes, and return to the beginning.
Next, examine the origin of names and sounds, and again request repetition.

Those who correct flawed speech are aided by their requests.
In all matters of grasping and desiring good or evil, one need not be aided by their requests.
The sage’s critique of value: benevolence without partial love, for partial love arises from calculation.
Former calculations are not today’s calculations;
former love of others is not today’s love of others.
The love of a servant’s love for others arises from calculating the servant’s benefit;
it is not the calculation of Zang’s benefit, yet the love of Zang’s love for others is the love of the servant’s love for others.
To remove such love and benefit the world is impossible.

Former knowledge of a wall is not today’s knowledge of a wall.
Though honored as the Son of Heaven, one’s benefit to others is no greater than that of a common man.
Two sons serving their parents—one encounters fortune, the other misfortune—yet their parents are alike.
It is not that one’s conduct is superior, nor that it is augmented.
External forces cannot increase our benefit.

If Zang were to die and the world suffered harm, I would nurture Zang ten thousand times more, yet my love for Zang would not increase.
The difference between a tall person and a short person lies in their similarity of form, hence they are the same.
The person of a finger and the person of a head differ.
The human body is not of one form, hence it differs.

A sword and a sword hilt differ; the sword is named by form and appearance, and since their forms are not one, they differ.
The wood of a poplar and the wood of a peach are the same.
In all matters not named by measurement or number, breaking them still leaves them as they are.

Thus, one person’s finger is not one person; it is one person’s finger that is one person.
One side of a square is not a square; the side of a square piece of wood is the square piece of wood.

That which arises from cause, grows by principle, and proceeds by kind—
to establish a statement without clarity on its origin is forgetfulness.
People today cannot act without the Dao; merely having strong limbs yet lacking clarity in the Dao, their distress is imminent.
Speech proceeds by kind; to establish a statement without clarity on its kind is to invite distress.

Thus, insidious speech finds its kind in the trembling of millet.
The sage, acting for the world, finds his kind in pursuing the lost.
Some live long, some die young—their benefit to the world is alike, their kind in the praise of stone.
A hundred thousand lives in a single day—love does not increase, their kind in aversion to harm.
Love for two generations may differ in depth, yet their love is alike, their kind in the patterns of a snake.
Love being alike, to choose and kill one of them finds its kind in the fruit of the jujube, not the thorn.

To inquire after a person’s illness is to inquire after the person;
to detest a person’s illness is not to detest the person.
A person’s ghost is not the person;
a brother’s ghost is the brother.
The ghost of a sacrifice is not the sacrificed person;
the ghost of a sacrificed brother is the sacrificed brother.

If a horse’s eyes are dappled, it is called a dappled horse;
if a horse’s eyes are large, it is not called a large horse.
If a cow’s hair is yellow, it is called a yellow cow;
if a cow’s hair is abundant, it is not called an abundant cow.

One horse is a horse; two horses are horses.
A horse with four legs is one horse with four legs, not two horses with four legs.
One horse is a horse.
If one of two horses is white, it is two horses of which one is white, not one horse of which one is white.
This is the case of one affirmation and one negation.

*Geng Zhu*, Forty-Sixth

Master Mozi grew angry with Gengzhuzi. Gengzhuzi said, "Am I not yet superior to others?"
Master Mozi replied, "Suppose I am about to ascend the Great Pass. I have a fine steed and a sheep to drive—whom should I urge on?"
Gengzhuzi said, "You should urge on the fine steed."
Master Mozi asked, "Why urge on the fine steed?"
Gengzhuzi replied, "The fine steed is capable of bearing responsibility."
Master Mozi said, "I also consider you capable of bearing responsibility."

Wumazi said to Master Mozi, "In wisdom and intelligence, how do ghosts and spirits compare to the sage?"
Master Mozi replied, "The wisdom and intelligence of ghosts and spirits surpass that of the sage as keen ears and sharp eyes surpass the deaf and blind. Formerly, King Kai of the Xia sent Fei Lian to smelt metal from the mountains and rivers and cast it into tripods at Kunwu. He then had Xi Nan divine at Bai Ku’s altar, saying: 'When the tripod is complete, with three legs and square, it will cook without fire, store without lifting, and move without being carried. It shall be offered at the ruins of Kunwu, facing upward.' The diviner’s interpretation said: 'It shall be received.' White clouds gathered, now south, now north, now west, now east. Once the Nine Tripods were completed, they were passed among the Three States. The Xia lost them, the Yin received them; the Yin lost them, the Zhou received them. The transmission among the Xia, Yin, and Zhou lasted several hundred years. If sages were to gather their worthy ministers and their most astute advisors to deliberate, could they foresee events hundreds of years hence? Yet ghosts and spirits knew them. Thus it is said: the wisdom and intelligence of ghosts and spirits surpass that of the sage as keen ears and sharp eyes surpass the deaf and blind."

Zitu Yu and Xianzi Shuo asked Master Mozi, "In practicing righteousness, what is the greatest task?"
Master Mozi said, "It is like building a wall. Those who can build, build; those who can carry earth, carry earth; those who can tamp, tamp. Only then is the wall completed. Practicing righteousness is the same. Those who can debate, debate; those who can expound the texts, expound the texts; those who can act, act. Only then is the work of righteousness accomplished."

Wumazi said to Master Mozi, "You embrace all under Heaven with love, yet no benefit is seen; I do not love all under Heaven, yet no harm is seen. Neither of us has achieved results—why do you alone affirm yourself and negate me?"
Master Mozi replied, "Suppose there is a fire here. One person brings water to extinguish it, while another adds fuel to intensify it. Neither has yet achieved results—whom would you value between the two?"
Wumazi said, "I affirm the intention of the one bringing water and negate the intention of the one adding fuel."
Master Mozi said, "I likewise affirm my intention and negate yours."

Master Mozi sent Gengzhuzi to Chu. Several disciples visited him, offering only three measures of grain and treating him poorly as a guest. The disciples later said to Master Mozi, "Gengzhuzi’s stay in Chu is of no benefit. Several of us visited him, gave him only three measures of grain, and treated him poorly."
Master Mozi said, "It is not yet certain." Before long, Gengzhuzi sent ten pieces of gold to Master Mozi, saying, "Your disciple dares not die in vain. Here are ten pieces of gold—I wish you to use them."
Master Mozi said, "Truly, it was not yet certain."

Wumazi said to Master Mozi, "In your practice of righteousness, people do not see you grow illustrious, ghosts do not see you grow rich, yet you persist in it. You must be mad."
Master Mozi replied, "Suppose you had two servants here. One acts only when he sees you; when he does not see you, he does not act. The other acts whether he sees you or not. Which of the two would you value?"
Wumazi said, "I would value the one who acts whether he sees me or not."
Master Mozi said, "Then you too value madness."

A disciple of Zixia asked Master Mozi, "Does the noble man have a state?"
Master Mozi said, "The noble man has no state."
The disciple of Zixia said, "Even dogs and swine have a state—how can a scholar have no place?"
Master Mozi said, "Alas! Your words compare conduct to dogs and swine—alas!"

Wumazi said to Master Mozi, "To abandon the people of today and praise the Great Kings is to praise dry bones. It is like a carpenter who knows dry wood but not living wood."
Master Mozi replied, "All under Heaven live by the Way of the Great Kings. To praise the Great Kings is to praise the source of life for all under Heaven. To be able to praise them and not do so is not virtuous."

Master Mozi said, "The jade disk of He, the pearl of Marquis Sui, the Three Thorns and Six Rarities—these are what the feudal lords call fine treasures. Can they enrich the state, increase the people, govern punishments and policies, and secure the altars of soil and grain? It is said: they cannot. What makes fine treasures valuable is their ability to benefit. The jade disk of He, the pearl of Marquis Sui, the Three Thorns and Six Rarities cannot benefit people—they are not the fine treasures of all under Heaven. Now, if one governs the state with righteousness, the people will surely increase, punishments and policies will surely be well-ordered, and the altars of soil and grain will surely be secure. What makes fine treasures valuable is their ability to benefit the people. Righteousness can benefit people, so it is said: righteousness is the fine treasure of all under Heaven."

Lord Zigao of She asked Confucius about governance, saying, "What is it like to govern well?"
Confucius replied, "To govern well is to draw near those who are distant and renew those who are old."
Master Mozi, hearing this, said, "Lord Zigao of She did not grasp his question, and Confucius did not grasp how to answer it."

Lord Zigao of She surely knew that good governance draws near those who are distant and renews those who are old—so what did he truly ask? He asked *how* to do so. If one does not explain to others what they do not understand, but instead tells them what they already know, this is why Lord Zigao of She did not grasp his question, and Confucius did not grasp how to answer it.

Master Mozi said to Lord Wen of Luyang: "When a great state attacks a small state, it is like a child playing at being a horse. When a child plays at being a horse, his legs are wearied by the effort. Now, when a great state attacks a small state, the attacking side’s farmers cannot plow, and their women cannot weave, for they must devote themselves to defense. The side under attack is likewise in chaos—its farmers cannot plow, and its women cannot weave, for they must devote themselves to the assault. Thus, when a great state attacks a small state, it is like a child playing at being a horse."

Master Mozi said: "Words sufficient to be put into practice should be made constant; those sufficient to be acted upon should not be made constant. To make constant what is insufficient to be acted upon is to indulge in empty talk."

Master Mozi sent Guan Qianche to escort Gaoshi Zi to Wei. The Lord of Wei bestowed upon him a generous stipend and installed him in the countryside. Gaoshi Zi attended court three times and spoke exhaustively, yet none of his words were put into action. He left and went to Qi, where he saw Master Mozi and said: "Because of you, the Lord of Wei bestowed upon me a generous stipend and installed me in the countryside. I attended court three times and spoke exhaustively, yet none of my words were put into action—this is why I left. Might the Lord of Wei now take me for a madman?"

Master Mozi said: "If you left for the sake of the Dao, what harm is there in being called mad? In ancient times, the Duke of Zhou was not Guan Shu, yet he declined the Three Ducal Offices and remained in Shangyan. People all called him mad. Later generations praised his virtue, extolled his name, and it endures to this day. Moreover, I have heard that one who acts with righteousness does not avoid slander or seek praise. If you left for the sake of the Dao, what harm is there in being called mad?"

Gaoshi Zi said: "In leaving, how could I dare not act for the sake of the Dao? Formerly, you said: 'When the world is without the Dao, the humane scholar does not dwell in abundance.' Now the Lord of Wei is without the Dao, yet I coveted his stipend and rank—this would make me one who sinks others for personal gain."

Master Mozi was pleased and summoned Zi Qinzi, saying: "Listen to this. I have always heard of those who turn their backs on righteousness to seek stipends, but in Gaoshi Zi, I have seen one who turns his back on stipends to seek righteousness."

Master Mozi said: "When the world’s vulgar gentlemen are poor, if you call them rich, they grow angry; if you say they lack righteousness, yet call them righteous, they rejoice. Is this not perverse?"

Gong Mengzi said: "Our ancestors had but three models."

Master Mozi said: "Which ancestors do you mean when you say they had but three models? You do not understand that people had ancestors before them. If later generations turn against me, do I bear any fault? I turn against those who come after."

Master Mozi said: "This is like an army in retreat—would the stragglers seek rewards?"

Gong Mengzi said: "The gentleman does not create; he merely transmits."

Master Mozi said: "Not so. Among those who are not gentlemen, in ancient times, the good were not suppressed, but today, the good do not arise. Among those of lesser quality who are not gentlemen, in ancient times, the good were not promoted, but if one had goodness oneself, one would put it into action, wishing goodness to arise from oneself. Now, to suppress and not put into action is no different from not promoting those who act. I hold that in ancient times, the good were suppressed, but today, the good should be put into action, so that goodness may increase."

Wuma Zi said to Master Mozi: "You and I differ—I cannot practice universal love. I love the people of Zou more than those of Yue, the people of Lu more than those of Zou, the people of my village more than those of Lu, the people of my household more than those of my village, my kin more than the people of my household, and myself more than my kin, for they are closer to me. If someone strikes me, I feel pain; if they strike another, I do not feel their pain. Why should I feel distress when those close to me are not harmed, yet feel distress when those distant are harmed? Thus, I have myself and would kill others to preserve myself; I would not kill myself to benefit others."

Master Mozi said: "Do you intend to conceal your doctrine, or will you proclaim it to others?"

Wuma Zi said: "Why would I conceal my doctrine? I will proclaim it to others."

Master Mozi said: "In that case, if one person approves of you, one person will want to kill you to benefit themselves; if ten people approve of you, ten people will want to kill you to benefit themselves; if the whole world approves of you, the whole world will want to kill you to benefit themselves. If one person disapproves of you, one person will want to kill you, taking you for one who spreads inauspicious words; if ten people disapprove of you, ten people will want to kill you, taking you for one who spreads inauspicious words; if the whole world disapproves of you, the whole world will want to kill you, taking you for one who spreads inauspicious words. Whether they approve of you or disapprove of you, they will want to kill you—this is what is called a mouth that brings constant calamity, a body that invites constant murder."

Master Mozi said: "Do your words not seek benefit? If you speak without regard for benefit, it is empty talk."

Master Mozi said to Lord Wen of Luyang: "Suppose there is a man here who, when offered fine sheep, oxen, and sacrificial animals, merely has someone cut and prepare them, and finds the meal more satisfying than any other. Yet when he sees someone making a cake, he sneaks over and steals it, saying, 'Spare me your food.' Does he not know whether his days and months are sufficient, or does he have a thieving sickness?"

Lord Wen of Luyang said: "He has a thieving sickness."

Master Mozi said: "Chu’s three Yi territories lie fallow and cannot all be opened; its numerous soldiers cannot all be counted. Yet when it sees the border towns of Song and Zheng, it sneaks over and steals them. Is this different from the other case?"

Lord Wen of Luyang said: "It is the same—it truly has a thieving sickness."

Master Mozi said: "When Jisun Shao and Meng Bo Chang administered the government of Lu, they could not trust one another and prayed at the forbidden shrine, saying, 'If only we could be in harmony.' This is like covering one’s own eyes and praying at the forbidden shrine, saying, 'If only I could see everything.' Is this not absurd?"

Master Mozi said to Luo Hua Wu: "I have heard that you delight in courage."

Luo Hua Wu replied: "Indeed. I have heard that in my village there is a brave man, and I must go and kill him."

Master Mozi said: "In the world, none do not desire what they love and measure what they hate. Now, when you hear there is a brave man in your village, you must go and kill him—this is not delighting in courage, but hating it."

*End of Scroll Eleven of Mozi*

*Scroll Twelve of Mozi*
*Valuing Righteousness (Forty-Seventh)*

Master Mozi said: "Of all things in the world, none is more precious than righteousness. Suppose one were to say to another: 'I will give you caps and shoes, but I must sever your hands and feet—would you accept?' Surely one would not. Why? Because caps and shoes are not as precious as hands and feet. Or suppose one were to say: 'I will give you the world, but I must kill you—would you accept?' Again, one would surely not. Why? Because the world is not as precious as one’s own body. Yet men will contend to the death over a single word—this is to value righteousness above one’s own body. Thus it is said: Of all things in the world, none is more precious than righteousness."

Master Mozi, traveling from Lu to Qi, visited an old friend, who said to him: "Nowadays, no one in the world practices righteousness. Why do you alone wear yourself out for it? You would do better to stop." Master Mozi replied: "Suppose a man had ten sons, but only one tilled the fields while the other nine remained idle. The one who tilled would have to work all the harder. Why? Because many eat, but few cultivate. Now, when no one in the world practices righteousness, you should be urging me on, not trying to stop me."

Master Mozi journeyed south to Chu and was received by King Hui of Chu. The king, pleading age, sent Mu He to meet with him instead. Master Mozi spoke with Mu He, who was greatly pleased and said: "Your words are truly excellent. Yet our lord is the greatest king in the world—would he not dismiss them as the work of a lowly man and refuse to adopt them?" Master Mozi replied: "It depends only on whether they can be put into practice. Consider medicine: the root of a common herb, if taken by the Son of Heaven, can cure his illness. Would he say, 'This is but the root of a common herb,' and refuse to take it? Now, when farmers pay their taxes to the great lords, the lords use them to prepare wine, sweet liquor, and fine grain offerings to sacrifice to the Lord on High and the spirits. Would they say, 'This is the work of lowly men,' and refuse to accept them? Thus, though a man may be lowly, when compared above to the farmer and below to the herb, is he not more valuable than the root of a common plant? Moreover, has your lord never heard the story of Tang? Long ago, when Tang was about to visit Yi Yin, he ordered the son of the Peng family to drive his carriage. Midway, the son asked: 'Where is my lord going?' Tang replied: 'I am going to visit Yi Yin.' The son said: 'Yi Yin is but a lowly man in the world. If my lord wishes to see him, he need only summon him for an audience, and Yi Yin would consider it an honor.' Tang replied: 'You do not understand.'"

Here the medicine is at hand—if one eats it, one’s ears grow sharper and one’s eyes brighter, so I would surely delight in it and force myself to take it. Now Yi Yin to our state is like a skilled physician or a potent medicine. Yet you would keep me from seeing Yi Yin—this is to wish me ill. Thereupon he dismissed the son of the Peng family and would not let him drive. Only when this was done could it be right.

Master Mozi said: In all words and deeds, whatever benefits Heaven, the spirits, and the common people, do; whatever harms Heaven, the spirits, and the common people, abandon. In all words and deeds, whatever accords with the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties—Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu—do; whatever accords with the tyrannical kings of the Three Dynasties—Jie, Zhou, You, and Li—abandon.

Master Mozi said: What is sufficient to guide conduct, make constant; what is insufficient to guide conduct, do not make constant. To make constant what is meant to guide conduct is to be glib.

Master Mozi said: One must rid oneself of the six biases. In silence, reflect; in speech, instruct; in action, serve. If one makes these three the charioteers of one’s three faculties, one will surely become a sage. One must rid oneself of joy, anger, pleasure, sorrow, and attachment, and employ only benevolence and righteousness. Let hands, feet, mouth, nose, and ears all be engaged in righteousness, and one will surely become a sage.

Master Mozi addressed his disciples: If one is committed to righteousness yet unable to fulfill it, one must not reject its Way. It is like a carpenter who measures but cannot cut—he must not reject his marking line.

Master Mozi said: The gentlemen of the world, when asked to govern a single hamlet, if unable, decline; yet when asked to serve as minister of a state, though unable, they accept. Is this not perverse?

Master Mozi said: Now suppose a blind man says, “What is large is white; what is black is dark.” Even one with keen eyesight could not correct him. But if you mix white and black and ask the blind man to choose, he cannot know. Thus when I say the blind man does not know white from black, it is not because of his words but because of his choice. Now the gentlemen of the world all name benevolence—even Yu and Tang could not improve upon it. Yet when benevolence and its opposite are mixed and the gentlemen of the world are asked to choose, they cannot know. Thus when I say the gentlemen of the world do not know benevolence, it is not because of their words but because of their choice.

Master Mozi said: Now in the use of one’s person, one is not as careful as a merchant with a single bolt of cloth. A merchant with a single bolt of cloth does not dare to weave carelessly but must choose the finest. Yet the scholar in the use of his person does otherwise: whatever his desires incline him to, he does. If his transgressions are grave, he incurs punishment; if slight, he suffers disgrace. Thus the scholar in the use of his person is not as careful as the merchant with a single bolt of cloth.

Master Mozi said: The gentlemen of the world wish their righteousness to be fulfilled yet resent others helping them cultivate their conduct. This is like wishing a wall to be built yet resenting others helping to construct it—is this not perverse?

Master Mozi said: The sage-kings of old wished to transmit their Way to later generations, so they wrote it on bamboo and silk, inscribed it on metal and stone, and passed it down to their descendants, wishing them to follow it. Now if one hears the legacy of the former kings yet does not act, this is to discard their transmission.

Master Mozi traveled south to the state of Wei, carrying many books in his cart. Xian Tangzi saw this and found it strange, saying: “Our Master taught Gong Shang Guo, ‘Estimate what is crooked and straight, and that is all.’ Now you carry so many books—what need is there?” Master Mozi said: “In the past, the Duke of Zhou read a hundred chapters in the morning and received seventy scholars in the evening. Thus the Duke of Zhou assisted the Son of Heaven, and his cultivation endures to this day. I above have no lord’s affairs to attend to, and below no farming hardships to endure—how dare I abandon this? I have heard that in matters of common purpose, there are often errors. Yet the people’s understanding is not uniform, which is why I carry many books. Now if one’s mind is like Guo’s, often contrary to what is subtle and refined, and in matters of common purpose one already knows the essentials, then one need not be taught with books. Why do you find this strange?”

Master Mozi said to Gong Liang Huanzi: “Wei is a small state, situated between Qi and Jin, like a poor family between rich ones. If a poor family imitates the rich in their lavish clothing and food, they will surely perish quickly. Now in your household, you adorn hundreds of carriages, feed hundreds of horses on beans and grain, and clothe hundreds of women in embroidered silks. If I took the expense of adorning carriages and feeding horses, along with the wealth spent on embroidered clothing, to support scholars, there would be enough for over a thousand. If disaster strikes, and you place a hundred men in front and several hundred behind, with hundreds of women before and after, which would be safer? I think supporting scholars is safer.”

Master Mozi served in Wei, but the one he served soon returned. Master Mozi asked: “Why have you returned?” The man replied: “He spoke to me insincerely. He said he would wait for me with a thousand measures, but gave me only five hundred, so I left.” Master Mozi said: “If he had given you more than a thousand measures, would you have left?” The man replied: “I would not.” Master Mozi said: “Then it was not because he was insincere but because he gave too little.”

Master Mozi said: The gentlemen of the world regard men of righteousness as less worthy than those carrying grain. Now suppose a man here, carrying grain, rests by the roadside, wanting to rise but unable. A gentleman sees him, regardless of age or status, and must help him up. Why? Because it is righteous.

Now, when a gentleman acts for righteousness, revering and transmitting the Dao of the former kings to instruct others, even if they do not delight in it and put it into practice, they further slander and denigrate him. Thus, the gentlemen of the world regard men of righteousness as less worthy than those carrying grain.

Master Mozi said: Merchants travel in all four directions, their market prices shifting with trust and movement. Though they face the perils of passes and bridges, the dangers of bandits and thieves, they persist in their course. Yet when a scholar sits and speaks of righteousness, free from the perils of passes and bridges or the dangers of bandits and thieves—this is a matter of trust and movement beyond measure—yet they do not act. The calculation of profit by men of wealth is not as keen as that of merchants.

Master Mozi traveled north to Qi and encountered a diviner. The diviner said: “The Emperor will slay the black dragon in the north today, and your complexion is black—you must not go north.” Master Mozi paid no heed, proceeded north, and then returned. The diviner said: “I told you that you must not go north.” Master Mozi replied: “If southerners cannot go north and northerners cannot go south, and among them are those with black complexions and those with white, why do none succeed? Moreover, the Emperor slays the green dragon in the east on *jia-yi* days, the red dragon in the south on *bing-ding* days, the white dragon in the west on *geng-xin* days, and the black dragon in the north on *ren-gui* days. If we follow your words, we would forbid all movement below. This would constrict the heart and empty the world. Your words cannot be used.”

Master Mozi said: “My words are sufficient for use. To abandon them and change one’s thoughts is like forsaking the harvest to cling to grain. To dismiss my words with contrary speech is like hurling eggs at a stone—even if all the eggs in the world were cast, the stone would remain unchanged, indestructible.”

**Gong Meng, Forty-Eighth**

Gong Mengzi said to Master Mozi: “The gentleman holds himself in readiness; when questioned, he speaks, and when not questioned, he remains silent. It is like a bell: struck, it rings; left unstruck, it does not ring.” Master Mozi replied: “This saying contains three points, of which you now know only one, and even that, you do not fully understand. If a ruler indulges in licentious violence within the state, and one advances to remonstrate, it is called insolence; if one offers counsel through his attendants, it is called meddlesome speech. This is what perplexes the gentleman. If a ruler governs and the state faces peril, like a crossbow about to loose its bolt, the gentleman must remonstrate. Yet the ruler’s interest lies in such matters, so even if unprompted, the bell must ring. If a ruler undertakes unrighteous and aberrant conduct, even if he obtains a masterful strategy that could be employed in military campaigns to attack an innocent state, and he seizes upon it, he will surely use it. This expands territory and increases taxes and counterfeit wealth, but the outcome will bring disgrace. Neither the attacked nor the attacker benefits—both suffer harm. In such cases, even if unprompted, the bell must ring.

Moreover, you say: ‘The gentleman holds himself in readiness; when questioned, he speaks, and when not questioned, he remains silent. It is like a bell: struck, it rings; left unstruck, it does not ring.’ Yet now, without being struck, you speak—does this mean you are the one who rings without being struck? Does this mean you are not a gentleman?”

Gong Mengzi said to Master Mozi: “Truly good men—who does not know them? They are like fine jade, kept hidden yet brimming with inner essence. They are like beautiful women, secluded yet sought after by many. If they go forth and parade themselves, no one takes notice. Now you follow people closely and persuade them—why so laborious?”

Master Mozi replied: “In this disordered age, those who seek beautiful women are many, and though the women do not go forth, people seek them in great numbers. Yet those who seek goodness are few. If one does not vigorously persuade others, no one will know. Consider two men of equal virtue here. One acts as a diviner for others, while the other remains secluded. Whose essence is greater?”

Gong Mengzi said: “The one who acts as a diviner for others has the greater essence.”

Master Mozi said: “Righteousness is the same. Those who go forth and persuade others achieve greater good. Why, then, do you not go forth and persuade others?”

Gong Mengzi, wearing a *zhangfu* cap, a *bohu* robe, and Confucian garb, came to see Master Mozi and said: “Does the gentleman first don his attire and then act, or does he act and then don his attire?”

Master Mozi replied: “Action does not depend on attire.”

Gong Mengzi said: “How do you know this?”

Master Mozi said: “In ancient times, Duke Huan of Qi wore a tall hat and broad sash, a gold sword and wooden shield, and governed his state—his state was well-governed. Duke Wen of Jin wore coarse cloth and a sheepskin robe, a leather belt for his sword, and governed his state—his state was well-governed. King Zhuang of Chu wore a bright cap with braided tassels and a crimson robe, and governed his state—his state was well-governed. King Goujian of Yue cut his hair and tattooed his body, and governed his state—his state was well-governed. These four rulers differed in attire, yet their conduct was the same. From this, I know that action does not depend on attire.”

Gong Mengzi said: “Well said. I have heard that dwelling on goodness brings misfortune. Let me set aside my *bohu* robe and change my *zhangfu* cap. May I see you again?”

Master Mozi said: “Please, let us meet as you are. If you must set aside your *bohu* robe and change your *zhangfu* cap before meeting me, then action truly depends on attire.”

Gong Mengzi said: “The gentleman must speak the ancient language and wear ancient attire before he can be humane.”

Master Mozi said: “In ancient times, King Zhou of Shang had his minister Fei Zhong, who were violent men in the world, while Jizi and Weizi were sages in the world. They spoke the same language, yet some were humane and others were not.”

Master Zhou of Dan was a sage of the world, while Guan Shu was a violent man of the world—though they wore the same attire, one was humane and the other was not. Thus, it does not lie in ancient attire or ancient language.

Moreover, you emulate the Zhou but not the Xia; your "ancient" is not truly ancient.

Gong Mengzi said to Master Mozi: “In the ranks of the ancient sage-kings, the most sagely stood as the Son of Heaven, and the next stood as ministers and grandees. Now, Confucius transmitted the *Odes* and *Documents*, examined ritual and music, and was versed in the myriad things. Had Confucius lived in the time of the sage-kings, would he not have been made Son of Heaven?”

Master Mozi said: “Wisdom requires revering Heaven, serving the spirits, loving others with frugality, and uniting these as knowledge. Now you say that because Confucius transmitted the *Odes* and *Documents*, examined ritual and music, and was versed in the myriad things, he could have been Son of Heaven—this is like counting a man’s teeth to determine his wealth.”

Gong Mengzi said: “Poverty and wealth, long life and early death, are clearly ordained by Heaven and cannot be diminished or increased.” He added: “The gentleman must study.”

Master Mozi said: “To teach people to study while holding to fate is like ordering someone to wear a hat while removing his hair.”

Gong Mengzi said to Master Mozi: “There is right and wrong, but no auspicious or inauspicious omens.”

Master Mozi said: “The ancient sage-kings all regarded ghosts and spirits as divine, capable of bringing fortune or misfortune, and thus held to auspicious and inauspicious omens. This is how they governed and their states were secure. From Jie and Zhou onward, all regarded ghosts and spirits as undivine, incapable of bringing fortune or misfortune, and thus held to no auspicious or inauspicious omens. This is how their governance fell into chaos and their states were imperiled. The former kings’ writings, which you also possess, say: ‘Arrogance, issuing from you, is inauspicious.’ This means that doing evil incurs punishment, and doing good incurs reward.”

Master Mozi said to Gong Mengzi: “In mourning rites, when the lord, parents, wife, or eldest son dies, one wears three years of mourning; for paternal uncles, brothers, and cousins, one year; for clan members, five months; for aunts, sisters, maternal uncles, and nephews, several months of mourning. Yet some, when asked about not mourning, recite three hundred *Odes*, strum three hundred *Odes*, sing three hundred *Odes*, and dance three hundred *Odes*. If we follow your words, when will the gentleman have time to attend to governance? When will the common people have time to engage in their work?”

Gong Mengzi said: “When the state is in chaos, govern it; when governed, then perform ritual and music. When the state is well-governed, engage in work; when wealthy, then perform ritual and music.”

Master Mozi said: “When the state is well-governed, if governance is abandoned, the state’s order will also be abandoned. When the state is wealthy, it is because of work; if work is abandoned, the state’s wealth will also be abandoned. Thus, even in a well-governed state, one must encourage without ceasing—only then is it possible. Now you say: when the state is well-governed, perform ritual and music; when in chaos, govern it. This is like digging a well when choking or seeking a physician when dead. The violent kings of the Three Dynasties—Jie, Zhou, You, and Li—indulged in music and song, heedless of their people. Thus, they were punished and executed, and their states became wastelands—all following this path.”

Gong Mengzi said: “There are no ghosts or spirits.” Yet he also said: “The gentleman must study sacrificial rites.”

Master Mozi said: “Holding that there are no ghosts while studying sacrificial rites is like learning guest rites without guests, or weaving a fish net without fish.”

Gong Mengzi said to Master Mozi: “You consider three years of mourning wrong, yet your three days of mourning are also wrong.”

Master Mozi said: “For you to deem three years of mourning wrong while upholding three days of mourning is like calling someone who carries a child disrespectful.”

Gong Mengzi said to Master Mozi: “If one knows there is someone wiser than oneself, can this be called knowledge?”

Master Mozi said: “The foolish know there are those wiser than themselves, yet can the foolish be called wise?”

Gong Mengzi said: “Three years of mourning is learning to long for one’s parents.”

Master Mozi said: “The knowledge of an infant is merely to long for its parents. When parents cannot be obtained, it wails without ceasing—what is the reason for this? It is the height of foolishness. Then how can the Ru’s knowledge surpass that of an infant?”

Master Mozi said: “I asked a Ru: ‘Why make music?’ He replied: ‘Music is for joy.’”

Master Mozi said: “You have not answered me. Now, if I ask: ‘Why build a house?’ and you say: ‘In winter to avoid the cold, in summer to avoid the heat, and to separate men and women,’ then you have told me the reason for building a house. But when I ask: ‘Why make music?’ and you say: ‘Music is for joy,’ this is like saying: ‘Why build a house?’ and answering: ‘A house is for a house.’”

Master Mozi said to Master Cheng: “The Ru’s Way is sufficient to ruin the world in four policies. The Ru regard the Dao as unclear and ghosts as undivine; Heaven and the spirits are displeased—this is sufficient to ruin the world. They also advocate lavish burials and prolonged mourning, with thick coffins, multiple layers of clothing, and sending off the dead as if relocating. They wail for three years, rise only with support, walk only with a staff, ears hearing nothing, eyes seeing nothing—this is sufficient to ruin the world. They further indulge in stringed songs and dances, practicing music—this is sufficient to ruin the world. They also hold that fate exists, that poverty and wealth, long life and early death, order and chaos, safety and danger are fixed and cannot be diminished or increased. If those above act accordingly, they will not govern; if those below act accordingly, they will not work—this is sufficient to ruin the world.”

Master Cheng said: “How severe is your condemnation of the Ru!”

Master Mozi said: “The Ru fundamentally do not have these four policies; if I speak of them, then it is condemnation.”

Now the Ru indeed possess these four policies, yet when I speak of them, it is not slander—it comes from what I have heard in contention. Master Cheng had no reply and departed.

Master Mozi said: “He is deluded.” Returning, he later sat down and resumed, saying: “Earlier, sir, your words contained points worth hearing. Yet if one follows your reasoning, it would mean neither praising Yu nor condemning Jie and Zhou.”

Master Mozi said: “Not so. To respond with fitting words, to weigh arguments and act accordingly—this is wisdom. If the attack is severe, my defense is severe; if the attack is slight, my defense is slight. To respond with fitting words and weigh arguments is like shouldering a yoke to strike at a moth.”

Master Mozi debated with Master Cheng, invoking Confucius. Master Cheng said: “You are not a Ru—why invoke Confucius?”

Master Mozi replied: “This is a matter of what is fitting and cannot be altered. Now, when birds sense the distress of heat and drought, they fly high; when fish sense it, they dive deep. In such circumstances, even Yu or Tang, were they to devise a plan, could not alter this. Birds and fish may be called foolish, yet even Yu and Tang would still follow their nature. Now, have I never invoked Confucius?”

A visitor to Master Mozi’s gate said to him: “Sir, you regard ghosts as spiritually enlightened and capable of bringing misfortune to people…”

Another visitor to Master Mozi’s gate, strong in body and keen in thought, wished to follow and study under him. Master Mozi said: “Study for now, and I will employ you.” He urged him with kind words to learn.

A year later, the student demanded employment from Master Mozi. Master Mozi said: “I will not employ you. Have you not heard the saying from Lu? In Lu, there were five brothers. When their father died, the eldest son was fond of wine and refused to bury him. The four younger brothers said: ‘If you do not bury him, we will buy you wine.’ They urged him with kind words to bury their father. After the burial, he demanded wine from his four brothers. They replied: ‘We have not given you wine yet. You buried your father, and we buried ours—was ours alone to bury? If you did not bury him, others would have ridiculed you, so we urged you to do so. Now, you act with virtue, and we act with virtue—is ours alone to be virtuous? If you do not study, others will ridicule you, so we urge you to learn.’”

Another visitor to Master Mozi’s gate, when asked by Master Mozi, “Why not study?” replied: “None in my clan studies.”

Master Mozi said: “Not so. Does one who loves beauty say, ‘None in my clan loves it, so I do not’? Does one who desires wealth and honor not desire it? Those who love beauty and desire wealth and honor strive for them even against others’ example. Fortune favors the good and brings misfortune to the violent. Now, I have served you for a long time, yet fortune has not come. Perhaps your words contain some flaw? Or do ghosts and spirits lack clarity? Why have I not received fortune?”

Master Mozi said: “Though you have not received fortune, how can my words be at fault? And how can ghosts and spirits be unclear? Have you heard of the punishment for concealing criminals?” The visitor replied: “I have not heard of it.” Master Mozi said: “Suppose there is a man here, and you have ten sons. Could you praise all ten while praising yourself just once?” The visitor replied: “I could not.” Master Mozi continued: “Or suppose there is a man here with a hundred sons. Could you praise them all your life while never praising yourself?” The visitor replied: “I could not.” Master Mozi said: “Concealing even one person is a crime. Now, the number you conceal is so great—you will incur a heavy punishment. How can you seek fortune?”

Master Mozi fell ill, and Die Bi approached to ask: “Master, you regard ghosts and spirits as clear, able to bring fortune and misfortune, rewarding the good and punishing the unvirtuous. Now, you are a sage—why are you ill? Perhaps your words contain some flaw? Or do ghosts and spirits lack clarity?” Master Mozi said: “Even if I am ill, how does that prove they are unclear? There are many ways one may fall ill: some from cold or heat, some from toil and hardship. If a house has a hundred doors but leaves one open, how can thieves not enter? The Way is the great vessel of the world—why must one force others to follow it?”

Some of Master Mozi’s disciples who had returned to study archery said to him: “We wish to learn.” Master Mozi said: “You cannot. The wise must measure their strength and act only within their capacity. Even a warrior of the state, fighting while supporting another, cannot succeed. Now, you are not warriors of the state—how can you master learning and archery both?”

The disciples said to Master Mozi: “Gaozi says: ‘Your words uphold the Way, yet your conduct is deeply flawed.’ We ask to abandon you.” Master Mozi said: “You cannot. To cite my words while slandering my conduct is still better than having nothing. Suppose there is a man here—Di is deeply unvirtuous, yet he reveres Heaven, serves ghosts, and loves others. Though deeply unvirtuous, he is still better than having nothing. Now, Gaozi speaks eloquently, expounding on benevolence and the Way without slandering me. Even if Gaozi slanders you, he is still better than having nothing.”

The disciples said to Master Mozi: “Gaozi surpasses others in benevolence.” Master Mozi said: “It is not necessarily so. Gaozi’s benevolence is like a lame man claiming to be tall or a blind man claiming to be broad—it cannot last.” Gaozi said to Master Mozi: “I govern the state and administer its affairs.” Master Mozi said: “Governing requires speaking with the mouth and acting with the body. Now, you speak with your mouth but do not act with your body—this is disorder in yourself. If you cannot govern yourself, how can you govern the state’s affairs? Withdraw for now; your own self is in disorder.” Thus concludes Scroll Twelve of the Mozi.

#31 Wang Niansun, referencing the *Lu Wen* chapter and the *Taiping Yulan*, adds the character "enter" after "follow." #32 From "Now, as for righteousness" to this point, the *Xian Gu* edition moves it below "those who love beauty and desire wealth and honor do not look to others yet force themselves to act." #33 Bi Yuan notes that the characters "slander you" are inverted. #34 Bi Yuan emends "lame" to "limb."

**Scroll Thirteen of the Mozi: "Lu’s Questions" (Forty-Ninth Chapter)**

The Lord of Lu said to Master Mozi: “I fear Qi will attack us—can we be saved?”
Master Mozi said: “Yes. In ancient times, the sage-kings of the Three Dynasties—Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu—were mere lords of a hundred *li*, yet through loyalty and righteous conduct, they won the world. The tyrannical kings of the Three Dynasties—Jie, Zhou, You, and Li—through arrogance, resentment, and violence, lost the world. I hope Your Lordship will, above, revere Heaven and serve the spirits, and below, love and benefit the people. Prepare generous gifts of furs and silk, humble your words, and present gifts to the neighboring lords on all sides. Placate the state and serve Qi—then the crisis can be averted. If not, I fear there will be nothing to be done.”

When Qi was about to attack Lu, Master Mozi said to Xiang Ziniu: “To attack Lu is Qi’s great error. In the past, King Wu of Wu marched east to attack Yue, driving them to Mount Kuaiji; he marched west to attack Chu, forcing King Zhao into exile in Sui; he marched north to attack Qi, seizing the crown prince and returning to Wu. The feudal lords repaid his aggression, the people suffered under his labors, and refused to serve him. Thus his state was left desolate, and he himself met with execution. In the past, Zhibo attacked Fan and Zhonghang, annexing the lands of the Three Jin. The feudal lords repaid his aggression, the people suffered under his labors, and refused to serve him. Thus his state was left desolate, and he himself met with execution. Therefore, when a great state attacks a small state, it is mutual destruction—the transgression will inevitably rebound upon the state itself.”

Master Mozi said to the King of Qi: “Suppose there were a knife here, and you tested it on a man’s head, cutting cleanly through—would you call it sharp?”
The King said: “Sharp.”
Master Mozi said: “If you tested it on many heads, cutting cleanly through each time—would you call it sharp?”
The King said: “Sharp.”
Master Mozi said: “The knife is indeed sharp, but who will bear the misfortune?”
The King said: “The knife gains the benefit, but those who test it bear the misfortune.”
Master Mozi said: “To annex states, overthrow armies, and slaughter the people—who will bear the misfortune?”
The King lowered his head, pondered, and said: “I will bear the misfortune.”

When Lord Wen of Luyang was about to attack Zheng, Master Mozi heard of it and stopped him, saying: “Suppose within the four borders of Lu, the great cities attacked the small cities, the great families raided the small families, killed the people, and seized their oxen, horses, dogs, pigs, cloth, silk, grain, and goods—what would you think?”
Lord Wen of Luyang said: “Within the four borders of Lu, all are my subjects. If the great cities attacked the small cities, the great families raided the small families, and plundered their goods, I would severely punish them.”
Master Mozi said: “Heaven encompasses all under Heaven, just as you encompass the four borders of your state. Now, if you raise troops to attack Zheng, would Heaven’s punishment not also be inevitable?”
Lord Wen of Luyang said: “Why do you stop me from attacking Zheng? My attack on Zheng accords with Heaven’s will. The people of Zheng have killed their fathers for three generations—Heaven has already punished them, making their state suffer for three years without recovery. I will assist Heaven in its punishment.”
Master Mozi said: “The people of Zheng have killed their fathers for three generations, and Heaven has punished them, making their state suffer for three years without recovery. Heaven’s punishment is sufficient. Now, if you raise troops to attack Zheng, saying, ‘My attack on Zheng accords with Heaven’s will,’ it is like this: Suppose a man’s son is violent and unfilial, so his father beats him. Then the neighbor’s father takes up a club and strikes him, saying, ‘I strike him in accord with his father’s will’—would this not be perverse?”

Master Mozi said to Lord Wen of Luyang: “When you attack a neighboring state, kill its people, and seize its oxen, horses, grain, and goods, you record it on bamboo and silk, engrave it on metal and stone, cast it as an inscription on bells and tripods, and pass it down to later generations, saying: ‘None surpass us in greatness.’ Now, if a lowly man were to attack his neighbor’s household, kill its people, and seize its dogs, pigs, food, clothing, and furs, and also record it on bamboo and silk, inscribe it on ritual vessels, and pass it down to later generations, saying: ‘None surpass me in greatness’—would this be acceptable?”
Lord Wen of Luyang said: “From your words, I see that what the world calls acceptable may not necessarily be so.”

Master Mozi said to Lord Wen of Luyang: “The gentleman of the world today knows small things but not great things. Suppose there were a man here who stole a dog or a pig—you would call him unrighteous. But if he stole a state or a city, you would call it righteous. It is like seeing a small white thing and calling it white, but seeing a large white thing and calling it black. Thus the gentleman of the world knows small things but not great things—this is what such words describe.”

Lord Wen of Luyang said to Master Mozi: “South of Chu there is a state called Qiao where they eat people. When the eldest son is born, they butcher and eat him, calling it ‘fitting for the younger brother.’ If the child is beautiful, they present him to their lord, and if the lord is pleased, he rewards the father. Is this not a vile custom?”
Master Mozi said: “Even in the customs of the Central States, it is the same. To kill a father and reward his son—how is this different from eating one’s son and rewarding his father? If one does not employ benevolence and righteousness, how can one condemn the barbarians for eating their children?”

When the jade-worker of the Lord of Lu died, the Lord of Lu executed the one responsible, and the people of Lu praised and followed this example. Master Mozi heard of this and said: “Execution is meant to fulfill the will of the dead. Now, to follow praise and adopt it is like taking a severed head as a model for submission.”

Lord Wen of Luyang said to Master Mozi: “Someone told me of a loyal minister—when ordered to bow, he bows; when ordered to look up, he looks up. When at rest, he is still; when called, he responds. Can this be called a loyal minister?”
Master Mozi said: “When ordered to bow, he bows; when ordered to look up, he looks up—this is like a shadow. When at rest, he is still; when called, he responds—this is like an echo. What benefit will you derive from a shadow or an echo? What I call a loyal minister is one who, when his lord has faults, subtly admonishes him; when he himself has virtues, he offers them to his lord without boasting. Outwardly, he conceals his lord’s errors and brings forth his lord’s virtues, elevating them without aligning below. Thus, goodness and excellence reside above, while grievances and complaints remain below; comfort and joy belong to the lord, while sorrow and hardship fall to the minister. This is what I call a loyal minister.”

The Lord of Lu said to Master Mozi: “I have two sons—one loves learning, the other loves distributing wealth to others. Which should I make heir?”
Master Mozi said: “It is not yet clear. Sometimes what is rewarded may not be right. Jie’s reverence was not for bestowing fish; the mantis hunts insects, not out of love. I wish my lord would examine their intentions and achievements together.”

A man of Lu sent his son to study under Master Mozi. The son died in battle, and the father reproached Master Mozi.
Master Mozi said: “You wanted your son to study under me. Now he has completed his studies, died in battle, and you are angry—this is like wanting to be admired, then resenting admiration when it comes. Is this not misguided?”

In the southern borderlands of Lu, there was a man named Wu Lü who made pottery in winter and farmed in summer, comparing himself to Shun. Master Mozi heard of him and went to see him.
Wu Lü said to Master Mozi: “Virtue, virtue—what need is there for words?”
Master Mozi said: “What you call virtue—do you also labor to aid others and share your wealth with them?”
Wu Lü said: “I do.”
Master Mozi said: “I have calculated this. Suppose I farmed enough to feed the world—even at my most productive, it would equal the work of one farmer. Dividing this among the world, each person would get no more than a pint of grain. Even if they gained a pint, it would not satisfy the world’s hunger—this is already clear. Suppose I wove enough to clothe the world—even at my most productive, it would equal the work of one woman. Dividing this among the world, each person would get no more than a foot of cloth. Even if they gained a foot, it would not warm the world’s cold—this is already clear. Suppose I donned armor, took up weapons, and rescued the lords from peril—even at my strongest, I would be but one warrior. One warrior cannot withstand three armies—this is already clear. Therefore, I believe it is better to recite the Dao of the former kings, seek their teachings, understand the words of the sages, and examine their expressions. First, persuade the kings, dukes, and great men; next, the common people and humble scholars. If the kings, dukes, and great men use my words, the state will surely be well-governed. If the common people and humble scholars use my words, their conduct will surely be refined. Thus, though I do not farm, I feed the hungry; though I do not weave, I clothe the cold.
