My merit surpasses that of those who farm to feed and weave to clothe. Therefore, though I do not farm or weave, my merit surpasses that of farming and weaving.”

Wu Lü said to Master Mozi: “Virtue, virtue—what need is there for words?”
Master Mozi said: “If the world did not know how to farm, and I taught them to farm, would my merit be greater than if I farmed alone?”
Wu Lü said: “Teaching others to farm would bring greater merit.”
Master Mozi said: “If I attacked an unrighteous state, beating the drum to lead the masses into battle, would my merit be greater than if I fought alone without drumming?”
Wu Lü said: “Leading the masses into battle would bring greater merit.”
Master Mozi said: “If the common people and humble scholars of the world, though few understand virtue, teach the world about virtue, their merit would also be great. Why not speak of it? If you could drum and advance toward virtue, would my virtue not progress further?”

Master Mozi sent Gong Shangguo to Yue. Gong Shangguo persuaded the King of Yue, who was greatly pleased and said to him: “If you can bring Master Mozi to Yue to instruct me, I will cede a territory of five hundred *li* from the former lands of Wu to enfeoff Master Mozi.”
Gong Shangguo agreed. He then prepared fifty chariots to welcome Master Mozi from Lu, saying: “I used your master’s Dao to persuade the King of Yue. The king was greatly pleased and said to me, ‘If you can bring Master Mozi to Yue to instruct me, I will cede a territory of five hundred *li* from the former lands of Wu to enfeoff him.’”
Master Mozi said to Gong Shangguo: “What do you make of the King of Yue’s intentions? If he will listen to my words and employ my Dao, then I will go. I will eat according to my hunger and dress according to my size, comparing myself to a humble servant—how could I accept an enfeoffment? But if Yue will not listen to my words or employ my Dao, and I go anyway, then I would be displaying virtue for show. Such display is the same in the Central States—why must it be in Yue?”

Master Mozi traveled, and Wei Yue asked: “Having met with the lords of the four directions, with whom will you speak first?”
Master Mozi said: “When entering a state, one must choose the most pressing matters and attend to them.”

When a state is benighted and disordered, speak to it of exalting the worthy and unifying standards. When a state is impoverished, speak to it of frugality in expenditures and funerals. When a state is steeped in music and debauchery, speak to it of rejecting music and denying fate. When a state is licentious, perverse, and without ritual, speak to it of revering Heaven and serving the spirits. When a state is bent on aggression and domination, speak to it of universal love and condemning warfare. Thus does one “choose the most pressing matters and attend to them.”

Master Mozi departed to serve Duke Cao’s son and returned to Song after three years. Gazing at Master Mozi, he said: “When first I studied at your gate, I wore coarse cloth and ate plain broth. If I received food in the morning, I might not have it by evening, yet I still made offerings to the spirits. But under your governance, my household has grown prosperous from its beginnings. Though prosperous, I have scrupulously made offerings to the spirits. Yet my retainers die in great numbers, my livestock do not multiply, and I am mired in illness. I do not see how your Way can be put to use.”

Master Mozi said: “It is not so. The spirits desire many things from men. They desire that those in high office and with generous emoluments yield them to the worthy, and that those with abundant wealth share it with the poor. Do the spirits desire only the plucking of scallions and the offering of lungs? Now you occupy high office and receive generous emoluments, yet you do not yield them to the worthy—this is one inauspicious sign. You have abundant wealth, yet you do not share it with the poor—this is a second inauspicious sign. You serve the spirits with nothing more than offerings, and then ask, ‘Why has illness befallen me?’ This is like closing only one of a hundred gates and asking, ‘How could thieves have entered?’ Seeking blessings from spirits so offended—how could it be possible?”

The Lu invocators offered a single piglet in sacrifice and sought a hundred blessings from the spirits. When Master Mozi heard of this, he said: “This cannot be. If one gives little to others yet expects much in return, others will only fear that one might bestow something upon them. Now you offer a single piglet and seek a hundred blessings from the spirits—you should fear that they will demand oxen and sheep in sacrifice. The sage-kings of old served the spirits with nothing more than offerings. Now you offer a piglet and seek a hundred blessings—your wealth would be better spent in poverty.”

Peng Qingshengzi said: “The past can be known, but the future cannot.” Master Mozi said: “Suppose your parents were a hundred *li* away and you learned they had encountered danger, with only a day to reach them. If you arrived in time, they would live; if not, they would die. Now suppose you had a sturdy carriage and fine horses here, and also a broken-down cart and a team of four worn-out nags. If you had to choose, which would you take?” Peng Qingshengzi replied: “I would take the fine horses and sturdy carriage to arrive quickly.” Master Mozi said: “Then why doubt what lies ahead?”

Meng Shan praised Prince Wen of Wu, saying: “When the disaster of Duke Bai befell him, the duke seized Prince Wen and pressed an axe and iron hook to his waist, pointing a spear at his heart. He said, ‘Become king, and you will live; refuse, and you will die.’ Prince Wen replied, ‘How you insult me! You killed my kin and now offer me the state of Chu in delight. Even if I gained the world by such means, it would be unrighteous—I would not do it. How much less would I accept the state of Chu?’ And so he refused. Was Prince Wen not humane?”

Master Mozi said: “It was difficult, yet not truly humane. If he believed the king was without the Way, why did he not accept the throne and govern? If he believed Duke Bai was unrighteous, why did he not accept the kingship, execute Duke Bai, and restore the king? Thus I say it was difficult, yet not truly humane.”

Master Mozi sent Sheng Chuo to serve Xiang Ziniu. Xiang Ziniu thrice invaded Lu territory, and Sheng Chuo thrice followed him. When Master Mozi heard of this, he sent Gao Sunzi to request his return, saying: “I sent Chuo to assist in curbing arrogance and correcting transgressions. Now Chuo enjoys generous emoluments and deceives his master. His master thrice invades Lu, and Chuo thrice follows—this is like whipping a horse to make it bolt. I have heard that to speak of righteousness yet not practice it is to defy enlightenment. Chuo is not ignorant of this; his emoluments have overcome his sense of right.”

Formerly, the men of Chu and Yue fought on the river in boats. The men of Chu advanced with the current and retreated against it; when they saw an advantage, they advanced, and when they saw no advantage, they retreated to avoid difficulty. The men of Yue advanced against the current and retreated with it; when they saw an advantage, they advanced, and when they saw no advantage, they retreated swiftly. Thus the men of Yue, though outmatched, encircled and defeated the men of Chu.

Gongshuzi traveled south from Lu to Chu and first devised implements for naval warfare. He made hooks and crossbows: those who retreated were hooked, and those who advanced were repelled. Measuring the length of the hooks and crossbows, he designed weapons accordingly. The men of Chu were disciplined, while the men of Yue were not, so the men of Chu, though outmatched, encircled and defeated the men of Yue.

Gongshuzi, pleased with his ingenuity, said to Master Mozi: “In naval warfare, I have hooks and crossbows. I wonder if your righteousness also has hooks and crossbows?”

Master Mozi said: “The hooks and crossbows of my righteousness surpass yours in naval warfare. My hooks and crossbows hook with love and repel with reverence. Without hooking with love, there is no intimacy; without repelling with reverence, there is haste. Familiarity without intimacy leads to swift estrangement. Thus mutual love and mutual reverence are like mutual benefit. Now you hook to restrain others, and others hook to restrain you; you repel others with force, and others repel you with force. Mutual hooking and mutual repelling are like mutual harm. Thus the hooks and crossbows of my righteousness surpass yours in naval warfare.”

Gongshuzi carved bamboo and wood into a magpie, which flew for three days without descending. Gongshuzi considered himself supremely skillful. Master Mozi said to him: “Your making of a magpie is not as good as my making of a linchpin. In an instant, I can carve a piece of wood three *cun* long and bear a weight of fifty *dan*.”

Thus, what is called "skillful" is that which benefits others; what does not benefit others is called "clumsy."

Gongshuzi said to Master Mozi: “Before I met you, I desired to take Song. After meeting you, though Song is offered to me, if it is unrighteous, I will not take it.”

Master Mozi replied: “Before I met you, you desired to take Song. After I met you, though I offer Song to you, if it is unrighteous, you refuse to take it—this is as if I have given Song to you. If you devote yourself to righteousness, I will further grant you the world.”

**Chapter Fifty**
Gongshu Ban constructed cloud-ladders for Chu, and upon their completion, intended to attack Song. Master Mozi, hearing of this, set out from Qi, traveling ten days and nights until he reached Ying, where he met Gongshu Ban.

Gongshu Ban asked: “What instructions do you bring, Master?”

Master Mozi said: “In the north, there is one who insults me. I wish to borrow your strength to kill him.”

Gongshu Ban was displeased.

Master Mozi said: “I offer ten *jin* of gold.”

Gongshu Ban replied: “By my principles, I fundamentally do not kill people.”

Master Mozi rose and bowed twice, saying: “Allow me to explain. I have come from the north and heard that you are constructing ladders to attack Song. What crime has Song committed? Chu has surplus land but insufficient people. To kill those who are insufficient while contending for what is already in surplus cannot be called wisdom. To attack Song, which is innocent, cannot be called benevolence. To know this and not remonstrate cannot be called loyalty. To remonstrate and fail cannot be called strength. To refrain from killing the few yet kill the many cannot be called understanding categories.”

Gongshu Ban was convinced.

Master Mozi said: “Then, will you cease?”

Gongshu Ban replied: “I cannot. I have already spoken of this to the king.”

Master Mozi said: “Why not introduce me to the king?”

Gongshu Ban agreed.

Master Mozi met the king and said: “Suppose there is a man here who, possessing a fine carriage, covets his neighbor’s shabby robe; who, having millet and meat, covets his neighbor’s husks and dregs. What sort of man would this be?”

The king said: “He must have a thieving sickness.”

Master Mozi said: “Chu’s territory spans five thousand *li*—this is like the fine carriage compared to a broken cart. Chu has Yunmeng, filled with rhinoceroses, deer, and elk; the Jiang and Han rivers teem with fish, soft-shelled turtles, and alligators, making it the richest in the world. Song, by contrast, has no pheasants, hares, or foxes—this is like millet and meat compared to husks and dregs. Chu has tall pines, patterned catalpas, *geng* and *ge* trees, and camphorwood, while Song has no tall trees—this is like brocade compared to a shabby robe. For Your Majesty to attack Song on these three counts is of the same kind as the man I described.”

The king said: “Well said! Nevertheless, Gongshu Ban has made cloud-ladders for me, and I must take Song.”

Thereupon, Master Mozi met Gongshu Ban again. He untied his belt to represent a city wall and used a wooden tablet as a weapon. Gongshu Ban deployed nine variations of siege engines, and Master Mozi countered each one. When Gongshu Ban’s offensive tools were exhausted, Master Mozi’s defensive measures still had surplus.

Gongshu Ban, frustrated, said: “I know how to counter you, but I will not speak of it.”

Master Mozi likewise said: “I know how you intend to counter me, but I will not speak of it.”

The king of Chu asked the reason. Master Mozi said: “Gongshu Ban’s intent is merely to kill me. If I am killed, Song will have no one to defend it and can be attacked. However, my disciple Qin Guli and three hundred others are already holding my defensive tools, awaiting the Chu invaders atop Song’s walls. Even if you kill me, you cannot exterminate them.”

The king of Chu said: “Well said! I will not attack Song.”

Master Mozi returned, passing through Song. It rained, and he took shelter in a gateway, but the gatekeepers would not admit him. Thus it is said: Those who govern with spirit are unknown to the multitude for their merit; those who contend in the open are known to all.

**End of Scroll Thirteen of the Mozi**

The annotations by Wang Niansun note: "函" should be "亟," and the same applies below. #2 Wang Niansun notes: after "非," the character "此" is missing. #3 Bi Yuan emends "敖" to "败." #4 The *Xian Gu* **End of Scroll Fourteen of the Mozi**
*Defending the City Gates, Fifty-Second*

Qin Guli asked Master Mozi: "According to the words of the sages, when the phoenix and crow do not appear, the feudal lords rebel against the states of Yin and Zhou, and armor and weapons arise throughout the world—great states attack small ones, the strong seize the weak. If I wish to defend a small state, what should be done?"

Master Mozi said: "What kind of attack are you defending against?"

Qin Guli replied: "In the present age, the common methods of attack are: scaling ladders, grappling hooks, battering rams, siege towers, smoke, flooding, *kang*¹, *yuan*², tunnels, ant-like assaults, rolling logs, and chariots. How does one defend against these twelve methods?"

Master Mozi said: "Repair the city moat, prepare the defensive implements, store sufficient grain, ensure harmony between superiors and subordinates, and secure aid from neighboring feudal lords—this is how one endures. Yet even if the defenders are skilled, if the ruler does not employ them properly, it is as if the city cannot be defended. If the ruler appoints defenders but they lack ability, and the ruler still relies on them, it is as if the city cannot be defended. Thus, defenders must be both skilled and honored by the ruler before the city can be held.

Now, the methods of defending a city: for the city gates, suspend *xian*⁵ mechanisms—twenty feet long and eight feet wide, with two matching sides. The crossbars⁶ should be spaced three inches apart, and earth should be packed above them, no more than two inches thick. The trench should be fifteen feet deep, its width matching the gate’s span, its length determined by available labor. At the trench’s end, suspend a basket large enough for one person. When it is full, bore holes in all gates and doors. Prepare *zi*⁸ for each: two covers, each with two layers, one bored and tied with a four-foot rope.

To counter fire chariots, shoot flaming arrows at the city gates. Bore holes in the gates to create a scaffold, plaster it, and hold water-soaked hemp ropes⁹. Use grass¹⁰ baskets to extinguish fires. Thinly reinforce the gate planks, boring holes half a foot deep, one inch apart, inserting *yi*¹¹ pegs two inches long, with one inch exposed, spaced seven inches apart, and thickly plaster them to resist fire. For each gate, suspend a water vessel to douse fires, with capacities ranging from three *dan* upward, varying in size.

The gate’s vertical posts and crossbars must be encircled with metal bands, secured with molten bronze or iron. The gate’s locking mechanism should be double-layered, reinforced with iron to ensure strength. For the bolt, make it two feet long, and the locking bar one *xian* thick. Seal it with the guard’s seal, and have someone regularly inspect the seal’s condition and the depth of the locking hole. Gatekeepers must not carry axes, adzes, chisels, saws, or mallets.

On the city walls, place a *qu* every two paces—a vertical post thirteen feet tall, with a ten-foot crossbar¹², and a six-foot shield. Every two paces, set up a *da*, nine feet wide and twelve feet long¹³. Every two paces, place one each of a long staff, long axe, and long mallet, along with twenty spears, distributed at two-pace intervals. Every two paces, station a wooden crossbow, capable of shooting over fifty paces. Prepare many arrows, avoiding bamboo shafts; *hu*, *zhao*, *pi*, and elm are acceptable. Seek iron arrowheads from Qi, and use them to shoot at enemy scaling hooks and siege towers.

Every two paces, pile stones weighing at least one *jun*, five hundred in total. If fewer, use *kang* siege plows and *bi* shields, all well-made. Every two paces, stack twenty large bamboo hats, each a full armspan in circumference and ten feet long. Every five paces, place a jar for water, with a ladle holding one *dou*. Every five paces, pile five hundred *gou shi* (dog corpses), each three feet long, tied with hemp, their ends sharpened and firmly bound. Every ten paces, stack twenty *bo* logs, each over two armspans in circumference and eight feet long.

Every twenty-five paces, set up a *#14* with an iron trough capable of holding one *dan* of stones, prepared for boiling water. Also keep sand, no less than one thousand *dan*. Every thirty paces, erect a watchtower, extending four feet beyond the parapet, three feet wide, four feet long, with planks on three sides, tightly sealed, and covered in summer. Every fifty paces, place a *jie* chariot, its wheels fitted with iron hubcaps. Every fifty paces, build a well enclosure, walled eight feet high. Every fifty paces, establish a square stronghold, secured with locks and keys. Every fifty paces, stack firewood, no less than three hundred *dan*, well covered with plaster to prevent external fire from igniting it.

Every hundred paces, construct a *long cong* siege tower, rising five *zhang* from the ground, three stories high, its base eight feet wide at the front and thirteen at the rear, tapering upward proportionally. Every hundred paces, build a wooden tower, nine feet wide at the front and seven feet high, with its axle positioned at the parapet, extending twelve feet beyond the wall. Every hundred paces, set up two *#15* and twenty *yong*, using wood to make connected striking devices.

Store one hundred water vessels, each holding four *dou* to six *shi*¹⁶. Every hundred paces, pile fifty bundles of miscellaneous stalks, each over two armspans in circumference. Every hundred paces, erect a *lu* shield, four feet wide and eight feet tall. Prepare *chong shu* (battering ram techniques). Every hundred paces, place one thousand *you du* (hidden shields), three feet wide and four feet tall.

Every two hundred paces, build a watchtower within the city, twenty-five feet two inches wide and twenty feet long, extending five feet beyond the wall’s pivot. The city wall’s width should be three to four paces to allow for patrols. The parapet should be three feet wide and two feet five inches high. The steps should be two feet five inches high, three feet wide and long, with each *tang* six feet apart. At the four corners of the city wall, construct *tong yi* (child towers) five feet high, housing four officers.

Every seven feet on the wall, place a *qu*, fifteen feet long, with a three-foot *li*, five inches from the parapet. The *fu* should be twelve feet long, with a six-foot arm. Bore a hole halfway through the post, extending five inches inward. For the *fu yu*¹⁸, bore a hole in the *qu*, with the *fu*’s front end four inches below the parapet. Dig pits for the *li qu*, cover them with tiles, and in winter, use horse blankets to keep warm. All await commands, or use tiles to make pits."

Every thousand paces along the wall, erect a *biao* (signal pole) ten feet tall, with those responsible for draining water holding and shaking it. Every fifty paces, construct a latrine identical to those below. Those using the latrine must not carry weapons. Every thirty paces on the wall, place a *jie che* (mobile barricade cart), though it shall not be used by those in formation. Every fifty paces, build a rampway two feet five inches high and ten paces long. Every fifty paces, construct a *lou luan* (turret platform), ensuring the bolts and braces are sturdy and reinforced.

Every hundred paces, build a *shi lou* (watchtower), with its outer gate opening outward and flanked by drainage channels. For the tower, add a *jie mu* (protective screen) and extend a *zhan* (scaffold) outward to aid external defense. No chambers may be built on the wall; any structures that could provide concealment must be entirely removed. Within the inner roads at the city’s base, every hundred paces, store *ji* (straw bundles); no stockpile should hold less than three thousand *dan*, and they must be well-coated. On the wall, every ten men shall have a *shizhang* (squad leader), subordinate to one officer, one *bo wei* (cloth-armored captain).

Every hundred paces, construct a *ting* (guard post) with walls fourteen feet high and four feet thick, featuring double-leaf gates that can be secured from within. The *ting wei* (post captain) must be selected from those of orderly conduct, loyalty, trustworthiness, and competence. Two encampments share one well for cooking. Ash, chaff, firewood, fodder, and horse blankets must all be carefully stored.

Defensive preparations on the wall include: *qu zhan* (drainage channels and screens), *ji che* (mobile barricades), *xing zhan* (mobile scaffolds), *xing lou* (mobile towers), *dao* (scaling ladders), *he gao* (crane-like siege engines), *lian ting* (linked shields), long axes, long hammers, long chisels, *ju* (hooked poles), *fei chong* (flying assault bridges), *xuan kou* (suspended hooks), and *pi qu* (battering rams).

Every fifty paces, build a tower with a *que nei* (sparrow’s nest) beneath the parapet. Every three feet, place a *xin gao* (firewood rack), ensuring each is two spans in circumference and four and a half feet long, with clean fuel. For tiles and stones: those weighing over two *sheng* are suitable. On the wall, every fifty paces, stockpile *she* (missile ammunition). Place iron grapnels alongside them in the same location.

For timber: logs two spans in circumference and twelve feet long or longer must have their bases well-trimmed, called *chang cong* (long supports); thirty are needed every fifty paces. Wooden bridges should be three *zhang* long, no fewer than fifty in number. When urgent repairs are required, construct a *lei bi* (rampart wall) and cover it with tiles afterward. Use ceramic jars holding ten *sheng* or more, ten every fifty paces, to store water for immediate use. Fifty-two of these are to be placed every ten paces.

At the four corners of the city wall, build high *mo si* (observation platforms) with reinforced chambers below, where observers shall stand to monitor the enemy’s movements. They must report the enemy’s formations, advances, retreats, and shifts in position; failure to do so results in execution. If the enemy digs tunnels inward, dispatch tunnel engineers to counter by digging around them, and prepare crossbows inside to respond.

Gather from the people’s homes any pestles, timber, tiles, or stones that can be used for city defenses and bring them up to the wall. Those who disobey orders shall be executed.

When constructing the wall, place a *ju shu* (construction station) every seven feet and a *lei* (rampart section) every five paces. For every five *zhu* (rammed layers), use a *ti* (rammer). Long axes have eight-foot handles. Every ten paces, place a long *xian* (pike) with an eight-foot handle. Every ten paces, set a *lan* (barrier) with a long hammer, its handle six feet long and head one foot long, and axes at both ends. Every three paces, place a [missing character].

The principles of defending a besieged city are as follows: walls must be thick and high, moats deep and wide, towers and platforms well-maintained, defenses well-repaired and effective, firewood and provisions sufficient for over three months, the populace carefully selected, officers harmonious, high ministers with meritorious service to the ruler numerous, the lord trustworthy and just, and the people endlessly content. Otherwise, their ancestral graves lie here; otherwise, the abundance of mountains, forests, grasslands, and marshes provides ample resources.

Otherwise, the terrain is difficult to assault yet easy to defend.

Otherwise, there are deep grievances against the enemy and great merit before the ruler.

Otherwise, rewards are clear and trustworthy while punishments are severe and sufficiently dreaded.

Within the city wards, households must each maintain defenses to the left, right, front, and rear, as on the city walls.

If the city is small but the populace large, relocate the elderly and weak from the outskirts into the city or a larger stronghold.

When the enemy approaches and their assault is certain, the defenders shall first strip the city’s outer palisades, but do not burn them. While the enemy is at the walls, rotate officers and soldiers at their posts, yet do not replace the laborers; laborers must not ascend the walls.

When the enemy is at the walls, herd all livestock into enclosures and inspect them. Plow and stockpile grain at the city’s base, one stockpile every hundred paces, each containing five hundred measures.

No buildings shall stand within the city gates. Establish a circular administrative hall for officials, with a four-foot parapet. The inner walkways shall be enclosed, secured with two crossbars and one bolt.

Beyond the city’s open ground, a hundred paces from the moat, all walls, fences, trees—large and small—must be demolished and removed. Clear them away.

Wherever the enemy may advance—whether along narrow paths or near the city’s open ground—construct watchtowers. Erect bamboo poles at the center of the sky.

Build a great tower in the hall’s courtyard, high enough to overlook the city. The courtyard shall be open on all sides, with pathways to receive visitors. When guests await an audience, summon the three elders and left-guard officials from within to deliberate. What is to be done?

Master Mozi said: Are you asking about the defense against tunnelers?

To prepare against tunneling, construct high towers within the city for vigilance.

When these fourteen measures are fully implemented, the people will no longer be inclined to ascend the walls. Only then can the city be defended. If even one of the fourteen is lacking, even the most skilled defenders cannot hold it.

**Defense Regulations:**
For every fifty paces, assign ten adult men, twenty able-bodied women, and ten elderly or children—totaling forty people per fifty paces. At the base of the city walls, station one person per pace, twenty people every twenty paces. Scale these numbers according to the city’s size to ensure sufficient defense.

If the defenders press forward like a swarming ant formation, the enemy will be forewarned, and the advantage will lie with the defenders while the attackers suffer.

When the enemy attacks in force—with a host of a hundred thousand—they shall not advance in more than four divisions. The upper guard covers five hundred paces, the middle formation three hundred, and the lower guard fifty. For any front narrower than a hundred and five paces, the defenders gain the advantage while the attackers falter.

A five-hundred-pace front requires a thousand adult men, two thousand able-bodied women, and a thousand elderly or children—totaling four thousand to meet the assault. These are the numbers for defense and guard duty. Assign the elderly and children who cannot fight to guard positions on the walls not directly engaged.

When troops depart the city, they must carry clear markers. Ensure all officers and civilians are aware of this. Any group of one hundred or more leaving without proper markers, or accompanied by unfamiliar persons, shall be treated as suspicious. Commanders of a thousand or more must halt such groups and prevent their departure. If officers or soldiers accompany them, all shall be executed, and the matter reported to the authorities. This is a critical prohibition in city defense, for it is where treachery arises—it must be scrutinized without fail.

**Scouting the Enemy:**
Watch for enemy movements. If the enemy behaves unusually—erecting walls, piling earth, or if water in wells turns unusually turbid—these are signs of tunneling. Immediately reinforce the city’s inner walls with earth directly opposite such points.

Dig wells within the city, one every five paces, adjacent to the city walls. On high ground, dig to a depth of a *zhang* and five *chi*; on lower ground, dig until reaching the water table at three *chi*. Order potters to craft jars holding forty *dou* or more, securing them with thin leather straps. Place these jars in the wells, and have keen-eared listeners lie prone to detect the direction of tunneling. Then dig inward to intercept the tunnels.

Order potters to craft "moon-bright" tubes, two *chi* and five *cun* long with a six-*wei* circumference, split lengthwise. Join the halves and place them within the tunnels, one facing upward, the other downward. Plaster the outer supports thoroughly, ensuring no gaps. Do not burn the supports; instead, plaster them well, including the joints, to prevent leaks. Repeat this on both sides, advancing together with the inner tunnel.

At the tunnel’s base, place a *kang* or *ji* (basket), but do not fill it. The *ji-kang* shall be five *bao* long, with both sides arranged symmetrically. At the tunnel’s inner mouth, construct a *ao* (kiln-like chamber) large enough to hold seven or eight bundles of mugwort. Repeat this for all tunnels, using four *mi* (bellows) per chamber.

When the tunnel is about to be breached, strike it with a *jie-gao* (battering ram). Beat drums and discard the *zun* (vessels) to create noise. Ensure those skilled in such matters do not leave the tunnel’s mouth.

Join planks to match the tunnel’s height and width, allowing tunnelers to advance with the planks. Chisel the planks to accommodate spears, spacing them closely enough to defend the openings.

If the tunnels meet, block them with a *you* (shield). Use spears to defend the openings, preventing blockage. If an opening is blocked, reinforce the planks and relay them past the obstruction, then seal the next opening. Chisel the planks well, ensuring smoke can pass. When smoke flows freely, beat drums and discard the vessels to fill the tunnel with smoke.

Listen to the tunnel’s left and right, swiftly cutting off the enemy’s advance. If the enemy’s tunnel converges with yours, block it with firewood and plaster to prevent burning the planks. Thus, the enemy’s subterranean assault will fail.

Cut mugwort stalks to a *chi* in length and place them in the *ao* chamber. First, build a wall to face the tunnel, connecting it to the planks.

Dig wells adjacent to the city walls, one every three *zhang*, adjusting their placement based on the outer terrain’s width. Take care not to err. If the city’s interior is lower than its exterior, tunneling from within will be difficult.

Dig three or four wells on the city walls, placing newly cut wood inside. Listen from within to pinpoint the tunnel’s location, then dig to intercept it.

When the tunnels are about to meet, prepare a *jie* cart. Use sturdy poles for the frame and equip it with sharp axes. Command three strong men to operate the *jie* cart, ramming the tunnel. Pour over ten *shi* of filth into the breach.

Hasten to place this in the well, with mugwort also piled above, filling seven-tenths of it. Cover the well’s mouth with a basin to prevent smoke from escaping upward, and seal the sides tightly. Beat the bellows swiftly. Transport the materials using carts.

Bind a bundle of firewood with hemp ropes coated in mud. Prepare an iron chain, suspending it directly opposite the enemy’s tunnel entrance. The iron chain should be three *zhang* long, with a ring at one end and a hook at the other.

The tunnel should be seven *chi* high and five *cun* wide, with pillars spaced one *chi* apart—one pillar every two *chi*. Place a base beneath each pillar, with two pillars sharing a single load-bearing block weighing eleven *jin*. Both pillars should be of the same material, supporting the crossbeam that bears the earth. The pillars should be two and a half spans in circumference, firmly bearing the earth’s weight. Ensure no pillar intersects another.

Dig two kilns for the tunnels, constructing them as arched chambers. Assign one officer and one attendant to each, and be sure to provide water. Seal the tunnel entrances with carts positioned on both sides, covering them with a roof. Apply mud to the top, adjusting the height and width according to the tunnel’s dimensions. Have men stand four to five *chi* inside the tunnel to secure the structure.

When the enemy contends at the inner entrance, pivot and seal it to form a kiln. For every three enemy soldiers carrying mugwort, station one of your own men lying in wait a *chi* away. Place the mugwort to one side, guarding it with two men who must not leave their post.

Prepare iron tools four and a half *chi* long, as thick as an iron *fu shuo*, with blades on both ends. Position them a *chi* away from the tunnel opening, angling them upward to strike the enemy’s center. The tools should be seven *chi* long.

Inside the tunnel, fashion sharp iron rings and chains, with two tunnels in operation. Dig wells on the city walls, and once the tunnel is nearly connected, stand on the planks and chisel a narrow opening. Then shift the planks and chisel another. Equip the *jie* cart with two frames, planting stakes on either side and affixing hooks at both ends.

Assign fifty laborers to the tunnels, with an equal number of men and women. On the city walls, create sparrow holes, three *chi* deep and as wide as the exterior, one every five paces. The sparrow holes should accommodate a straight pole, six *chi* high at the top and three *chi* at the bottom, spaced as needed.

Fill the outer moat, leaving a gap of seven *chi* to suspend a beam. Where the city walls are too narrow for a moat, do not dig one. Place a deafening kiln every thirty paces on the walls, inserting five sections of *tan ju* reeds. When the drums sound below the city, light the reeds, then beat the drums again to insert the reeds into the sparrow holes, illuminating the exterior.

All *jie* carts should be fitted with iron components. The cart’s pillar should be seventeen *chi* long, with four *chi* embedded in the ground. The frame should be over three *zhang* long, up to three *zhang* and five *chi*, with a horse’s cheekpiece two *chi* and eight *cun* long. Test the cart’s strength to determine its resistance, ensuring three-quarters of its force is directed upward.

For the *jie* cart, the frame should be three *chi* long, with two-thirds of its force upward and the horse’s cheekpiece centered. The cheekpiece should be two *chi* and eight *cun* long, and the frame twenty-four *chi* long, with the lower portion unused. Reinforce the resistance using large cartwheels.

The *jie* cart’s crossbar should be twelve and a half *chi* long. All *jie* carts should be fitted with iron components, and the carts should be positioned accordingly.

If the enemy approaches the moat, construct a water conduit four *chi* deep, reinforcing it with stakes. Space them every ten *chi*, covering them with a crescent-shaped structure to await orders.

Take wood two *chi* and four *cun* in circumference, split it lengthwise, place charcoal and fire inside, and seal it with the conduit. Then hurl it using the *jie* cart.

For the rapid-plow hurler, it should be two *chi* and five *cun* long, with a circumference of two *wei* or more. The lacquered *dai* is seven *cun* long, with a width of six *cun*, and its carving remains unfinished. The "dog-runner" is seven *cun* wide and one *chi* eight *cun* long, with fleas four *cun* long, and dog ears attached to it.

Master Mozi said: The method of defending a city requires tallying the timber within the city. What ten men can lift is called a *ten-qie*; what five men can lift, a *five-qie*. All weights are measured by the *qie* according to the number of people. For firewood and fuel, the strong have their *qie*, the weak have theirs—each proportionate to their strength. Whatever the *qie* of weight requires, the officers and people each bear their fitting share. If the city runs out of food, a great slaughter ensues.

Dig a great trench five paces from the city gate. On high ground, it should be three *zhang* deep; on low ground, it should reach the water table. Place traps within it, and above, construct a drawbridge with mechanical ingenuity. When filled with firewood and earth, it forms a passable path. Flanking it with trenches and ramparts prevents scaling or crossing. When the enemy approaches, the drawbridge is released, capturing them. If the enemy grows fearful and suspicious, they will scatter and disperse.

**Defense Against High Assault (Fifty-Third)**
Qinzi bowed twice and asked: May I inquire—if the enemy piles earth to tower over our city, hauling firewood and soil upward to build a *yangqian*, advancing with shields and mantlets to press against the walls, while archers and crossbows ascend—what should be done?

Master Mozi replied: You ask about the *yangqian*? It is the tactic of the clumsy, sufficient only to exhaust their resources, not to harm the city. Build a defensive platform to overlook the *yangqian*, extending twenty *chi* to the left and right, with a walkway thirty *chi* long. Arm it with strong crossbows, deploy mechanical devices, and employ ingenious contraptions—thus the *yangqian*’s assault will fail.

**Defense Against Overlooking with Linked Crossbows**
Use a linked-crossbow cart, with a main shaft square and one *chi* on each side, its length matching the city wall’s thickness. It has two axles and three wheels, the wheels housed within a basket. The basket is weighted above and below. On either side stand two vertical posts, with protective posts flanking them. The protective posts are round inside, with an inner diameter of four *cun*. The crossbows are bound to the posts on both sides, their strings hooked to the main bowstring. The crossbow arms align with the basket front and back. The basket is eight *chi* high, and the crossbow axle is three *chi* five *cun* above the lower basket. The linked-crossbow mechanism is cast in bronze, weighing one *dan* and thirty *jin*. The bowstring puller is as long as a deer’s leg. The basket is three and a half *wei* in circumference, with hooks and spurs on both sides, each three *cun* square. The wheels are one *chi* two *cun* thick. The bronze spurs on the arms are one *chi* four *cun* wide, seven *cun* thick, and six *chi* long. The horizontal arms extend beyond the basket, with a span of one *chi* five *cun*, and spurs six *cun* long, three *cun* thick, matching the basket’s length. They have sights and can be adjusted up and down. The counterweight is one *dan*, made from timber five *cun* in circumference. The arrows are ten *chi* long, their tips bound with cord. They are shot like halberd darts, using a winch to draw them. The arrows rise three *chi* above the crossbow arms. Use as many crossbows as needed, with sixty arrows in circulation—no shortage of small arrows.

Ten men operate this cart. When the enemy approaches, construct high towers to shoot from the roads, while the city walls respond with nets and arrows.

*(Sections Fifty-Four and Fifty-Five are missing.)*

**Preparations Against Scaling Ladders (Section Fifty-Six)**

When Qin Guli served Master Mozi for three years, his hands and feet grew calloused, his face and eyes were blackened by smoke, and he toiled in service without daring to voice his desires. Master Mozi, pitying him, prepared wine, dried meat, and jujubes, and sat with him beneath the shade of a great tree to rest. There, he summoned Qin.

Qin bowed twice and sighed. Master Mozi asked, "What is it you desire?" Qin bowed twice more and said, "I venture to ask about the Way of defense."

Master Mozi replied, "Not now, not now. In ancient times, there was a state of Wei that neither cherished its people within nor formed alliances without. It slighted the many with the few, and the strong with the weak. Thus, its ruler perished, its state was lost, and it became a laughingstock under heaven. You, too, must be cautious, lest you bring ruin upon yourself."

Qin bowed again, kowtowing, and begged to inquire further about the Way of defense. He asked, "If the enemy is numerous and fierce, their allies encircle our moat, their troops advance together, their cloud ladders are deployed, their assault preparations are complete, their warriors are many, and they vie to seize our city—what is to be done?"

Master Mozi said, "You ask about the cloud ladders? The cloud ladder is a cumbersome device, difficult to move and adjust. In defense, construct a mobile rampart with interlocking towers encircling the city’s interior. Determine its width based on the terrain’s breadth or narrowness, and lay straw within the encirclement, but do not make it too broad. The method for the mobile rampart is this: raise it twenty *chi* high, with battlements atop, ten *chi* wide. Extend projections twenty *chi* to the left and right, matching the rampart’s height and width.

"Bore sparrow holes and set up traps outside, with mechanisms, battering rams, axes, and hooks matching the width of the ramparts. Intermix these with chisels and swords. Assign ten strong men to wield the rams and five to hold the swords. Order observers to watch the enemy’s approach, signal with drums, and shoot from both sides. Shoot repeatedly, triggering mechanisms to entangle them. From the city walls, rain down arrows, stones, sand, and burning charcoal. Pour firewood, boiling water, and hot oil to aid the defense. Enforce rewards and punishments strictly, maintaining calm as the norm, and act swiftly to prevent the enemy from devising countermeasures. Thus, the assault of the cloud ladders will be defeated.

"Construct mobile battlements, each six *chi* high in ascending tiers, with swords facing outward, released by mechanisms. If the ram approaches, retract them; if not, deploy them. Bore sparrow holes every three *chi*, ensuring that the hooks for dropping objects are firmly set. Use carts to push and pull them.

"Outside the city, ten *chi* from the walls, dig a trench ten *chi* deep. Fell all trees, large and small, cutting them at the base, and use ten-*chi* lengths as stakes. Bury them deeply and mix them together, tamping firmly to prevent removal. Every twenty paces, create a killing zone with a barrier ten *chi* thick. Each killing zone has two gates, five *chi* wide. One gate should have a shallowly buried stake, left untamped for easy removal. Align the city’s defenses with the trench gates and set up straight barriers.

"Hang fire pots every four *chi*, and place cauldrons every five paces, with furnaces and charcoal at the gates. Lure the enemy in completely, then ignite the fire to burn the gates, followed by the hanging fire pots. Stand ready with halberds, filling the width of the ramparts. Between every two halberds, place one fire pot at the gate. All should stand holding drums and torches, ready to ignite them at once. If the enemy extinguishes the fire and renews the attack, lower the fire pots again. The enemy will suffer greatly and withdraw. Then order our troops to sally forth from the hidden gates on the left and right to strike their rear, commanding valiant warriors and generals to listen for the city drums to signal their advance and retreat.

"Routinely send out troops to harass them. At midnight, raise a clamor on all four sides of the city walls. The enemy will surely be thrown into chaos, and with this, their army will be broken and their generals slain. Have our forces wear white garments and use signals to recognize one another. Thus, the assault of the cloud ladders will be defeated."

*(Section Fifty-Seven is missing.)*
**Preparations Against Water Attacks (Section Fifty-Eight)**

Surrounding the city within the moat, construct a perimeter road eight paces wide. In preparation against water attacks, carefully assess the elevation of all four sides. Where the city’s terrain is low-lying, dig inward and downward, excavating deeply to channel spring water. Install latrine pits within tiled wells, and when the external water reaches a depth of over ten feet, bore water channels into the city.

Fashion ten *lin* from boats, each *lin* manned by thirty men. Each man shall wield a crossbow and carry four directional markers. Ensure the boats are well-equipped with oars and rollers. Form each twenty boats into a squadron, selecting thirty stalwart and strong men to crew each vessel—twenty of them skilled in directional markers, clad in armor and helmets, with ten wielding spears. First, house these elite troops in separate quarters, providing for their parents, wives, and children as hostages. When the water is ready to be released, deploy the *lin* with rollers to reinforce the outer embankments, while archers on the city walls provide rapid support.

*(Section Fifty-Nine is missing. Section Sixty is missing.)*

**Preparations Against Assaults (Section Sixty-One)**
At every hundred paces along the city wall, construct an assault gate. For each assault gate, build a kiln-like chamber (*yao’ao*), extending four to five feet into the gate. Erect a tiled roof above the gate to prevent rainwater from entering. The officer in charge of sealing the assault gate shall use a two-wheeled cart bound with wooden beams, plastered with clay, and secured within the gate. Measure the gate’s width to ensure the cart fits four to five feet inside. Place the *yao’ao* beside the gate, and store within it bundles of firewood and mugwort. If the enemy breaches the gate, lower the cart’s support and seal it. Sound the drum and set the kiln ablaze.

**Preparations Against Tunneling (Section Sixty-Two)**

**Master Qin bows twice and again bows twice, saying:**
"May I dare ask—of the ancients who excelled in assault, some tunneled through earth to breach the city, binding pillars and setting them aflame to collapse our walls. Once breached, the city may strike its people.

The *daxie* measures a foot in length, the flea five inches. Place two *daxie* in crosswise alignment, ensuring they lie level; if unlevel, they will not function. Both ends must be sharp.

When tunneling troops or siege-engine troops advance, carefully assess the width of their assault formation and order the *ya* to dig counter-tunnels of equal breadth, ensuring they match the enemy’s approach. Bundle and stack timber, preparing sufficient firewood for barricades. Do not plant trees in front; instead, use logs seventeen feet long for the outer face. Arrange firewood barricades crosswise and lengthwise, then reinforce the outer face with thick plaster to prevent soil from leaking through. Ensure the counter-tunnel is wide and sturdy enough to support a city wall thirty-five feet high or more. Prop it with firewood, timber, and earth, securing it tightly.

Adjust the length of the front face in advance, joining it with fleas to ensure it can bear the plaster and form a solid rampart. Plaster the exterior thoroughly to prevent burning or tearing.

For a great city, construct a postern gate fifteen feet high and four feet wide. Build an outer gate with a crossbeam—two logs placed before the gate, their ends carved to fit into sockets above. Erect a suspended beam with drunken bores, cutting through the wall to lay a plank bridge. Angle the outer bore and line it with planks, sloping it to match the city’s height.

Within the city, use existing earthworks to form outer defenses. Bore a chamber fifteen feet deep and fill it with firewood, ready to be ignited when needed. Extend listening posts along the wall, constructing double-tiered towers. Beneath these, dig into the inner side of the outer wall to a depth of fifteen feet and a width of twelve. Equip the towers—or modern listening posts—with strong men to command the defense and skilled archers to loose arrows, supported by ample supplies of broad-headed arrows.

Prepare defensive platforms along the parapets, six feet high and four feet wide, all fitted with shields, crossbows, and spear racks. Install rotating crossbows: the machine six feet long, the stock one foot. Join two poles to form a roller two feet long, with a central groove for the arm, which extends to the crossbar. Place one every twenty paces, assigning skilled archers to assist, with no man leaving his post.

Erect a tower every hundred paces along the wall, each supported by four pillars and connected by walkways. The lower section ten feet high, the upper nine feet, with a width and depth of sixteen feet each—all reinforced for stability. Construct a bulwark every thirty paces: nine feet high, ten feet wide, eight feet tall, with a three-foot-wide bore and a two-foot depth, all secured.

On the wall, prepare fire drills, their length adjusted to the city’s height, with tinder placed at the ready. Every nine feet, station a crossbow, a halberd, a mallet, an axe, and mugwort, along with piles of three stones and surplus arrows.

The *qu* measures sixteen feet long, its shaft ten feet; the arm six feet, with a three-foot stock. Plant the *qu* no more than three feet from the parapet. The assault bridge is eight feet long and seven feet wide, its wooden frame five feet wide, with a straight central plank and ropes at both ends. When under attack, assign one man to raise and lower it, never leaving his post. Place an assault cart every twenty paces along the wall, except where troops are stationed.

Every thirty paces, set a *yao’ao* kiln. Those handling fire must use cloth, hemp sacks, and leather basins, one every ten paces. The handle is eight feet long, the container holding two *sheng* or more, up to thirty. Use worn or new cloth six feet long, with a central grip and a ten-foot handle, one every ten paces, secured with thick ropes.

Station a water vessel every ten paces, each holding three *dan* or more, with varying sizes mixed together."

Basins and ladles, two sets each. Prepare dry rations for the soldiers, two *dou* per person, to guard against overcast and rain, storing them in dry places. Order the defenders to deliver meals within the city and to the patrols outside. Prepare tools and equipment, crushing iron sand and gravel into fragments for *huai* ladles. Instruct potters to make thin jars, each holding one *dou* or more up to two *dou*, to be used immediately, bound in threes with tight bindings. Reinforce the parapets on the city wall. The assault bridges stand twelve feet high, with notches at one-foot intervals. Construct wicket gates, each with two leaves, so they may be closed independently.

When defending against attacks on the moat, contend with fire, sounding the drums and drums of provisions. Inside and outside the ramparts, pile firewood as barriers. Set up *lingding* poles every three *zhang*, with fire hooks attached. Station one man every ten paces within the firewood, armed with crossbows at half draw, encircled by *gouxi* shields. Place a wall every seven paces.

When the enemy approaches our city, it is an urgent and extraordinary situation. Be vigilant against tunnel attacks. If a tunnel is suspected to correspond with the enemy, dig urgently. If the tunnel is not yet breached, take care not to pursue. For countering tunnel attacks, dig a counter-tunnel every twenty paces, ten feet high and ten feet deep, following the previous method. Three feet below the surface, dig a ten-pace tunnel branching left and right, each ten feet high and wide. Place two jars level with the city wall, covering them with planks and listening through a well-like shaft. Every five paces, use barriers of pine or cypress as tunnel doors. Each door has two crossbars, both as long as the door, with rings attached. Pile stones outside the tunnel entrance to a height of seven feet, adding firewood on top. Do not construct steps or use stones; instead, use a suspended ladder for entry and exit.

Prepare bellows and leather bags, the latter made of oxhide. The bellows have two jars, connected by a bridge to a drum with a hundred and ten beats. Each bag holds forty *shi* of black earth. Burn charcoal within, filling the bellows and sealing them to prevent qi from escaping. When the enemy approaches within five hundred paces of our tunnel, whether their tunnel is higher or lower, if it does not yet reach ours, use a large chisel to seek a connection. If our tunnel meets the enemy’s, encircle them without pursuit, and prepare to retreat northward, awaiting the bellows’ fire to ignite. Then withdraw and seal the tunnel to kill them.

Construct escape hatches with locks and keys, allowing free movement within. Place a dog in each tunnel; if it barks, someone is present. Assign fifty men. Inside the tunnel, create an opening for relaying messages, receiving six signals. Use oxhide to lower a measuring rope, which can be pulled or thrown. Afterward, seven men guard the retreat. In the tunnel’s center, store a large hemp rope and all tunnel tools. To obstruct tunnels, gather wood from the outer moat’s edge, scattering it to block the tunnel and probe its boundaries. Near the tunnel, prepare iron axes. Provide sufficient four-foot-long metal rods and *fulin* poles. If the enemy tunnels, counter with our own tunnel. Prepare iron hooks four feet long, in sufficient quantity, to probe and hook the enemy’s tunnel. Equip with *ju* spears, short halberds, short crossbows, and barbed arrows, all in sufficient supply, to fight within the tunnel. Use a metal sword for defense, five feet long, with a socket and wooden shaft; the shaft has a crossbar to block the enemy’s tunnel.

Warn those holding jars: if the enemy’s jar weighs thirty *jin* or more, place it in the tunnel, one every *zhang*, to listen for sounds within. Dig tunnels eight feet high and wide, carefully constructing and positioning them. Prepare complete oxhide bellows, including skins and methods, to defend two tunnels. Cover the tunnel with chaff and mugwort, testing smoke penetration. Forge metal axes for chopping, with shafts three feet long, to defend four tunnels. Construct barriers to defend forty tunnels, connecting them in groups of four. Provide sufficient axes, hatchets, saws, chisels, and hoes. Forge iron fetters to defend four tunnels. Build a central shield ten and a half *zhang* high and four feet wide. Construct eight horizontal shields for the tunnel, with ropes and hemp in sufficient supply, to illuminate the tunnel. Prepare vinegar to counter smoke, protecting the eyes. Distribute vinegar in the tunnel, placing at least four *dou* in a *zhang*-sized basin. When smoked, apply vinegar to the eyes and face.

**Defense Against Scaling Ladders (Chapter 63)**
Qinzi bowed twice and said: "I venture to ask: if the enemy is strong or weak, and they scale the city walls, cutting off reinforcements first and attacking later, using the city wall as a base and digging chambers below, halting in front while archers shoot rapidly from behind—what should be done?"

Master Mozi replied: "Are you asking about defending against scaling ladders? Scaling ladders are the enemy’s reckless tactic. Defenders should shoot at them from the parapets, using crossbow mechanisms to strike them, pulling them down, pressing them closely, burning *da* mats to cover them, and raining down sand and stones. Thus, the scaling ladder attack will fail."

To defend against scaling ladders, construct suspended shields: wooden boards two *cun* thick, three feet front and back, five feet wide, and five feet high, mounted on a wheeled base with wheels sixteen *cun* in diameter. Have one person operate a twenty-four-foot-long square pole with blades at both ends, positioned within the suspended shield. Use iron rings to fasten two shields to the upper crossbar, creating a mechanism. Assign four strong men to raise and lower it without difficulty.

Deploy suspended shields at intervals of twenty paces, and six paces where the attack is concentrated. Prepare *jing* shields, each two *zhang* in length and width, with a wooden upper crossbar. Bind them with wide hemp ropes, soaking the ropes in mud. Forge iron hooks to suspend them from both ends. If the enemy scales the city with ladders, burn *da* mats to cover them, linking them together, and rescue them with large nets. Use two-wheeled carts with wide axles to block them, wearing down their ends. Bind the wheels and coat them with mud.

Within the chamber, use elm or hazel wood, with brambles along the sides, naming it the "fire barrier" or alternatively the "boiling broth defense," to counter enemy assault teams. When the enemy mounts the assault, burn the boiling broth defense, sever its cords to bring it down, and have valiant warriors follow to strike, ensuring these warriors advance first while the city walls are promptly repaired where breached.

Below the city wall, prepare "substitute stakes" of catalpa wood, five *chi* in length, with half their girth above ground. Carve their ends into five rows, each row spaced three *chi* apart, with three *chi* between each stake, and plant them with large ears. Construct "linked receptacles," five *chi* in length and ten *chi* in width. The "rods" are two *chi* long and six *cun* in diameter, with cords two *chi* in length. The "mallets" have handles six *chi* long and heads one *chi* five *cun* in length. The "axes" have handles six *chi* long, with blades kept sharp, and all are buried with one side exposed.

The *da* mats are twelve *chi* in width and sixteen *chi* in length, hanging four *cun* below the front crossbar, their ends overlapping by one *chi* without forming a fish-scale pattern, and secured to the rear row. A central wooden rope, twenty-six *chi* long, is used; where the *da* mats and watchtowers do not align, fill gaps with planks. Frequently dry them in the sun. The *da* mats serve as barriers, allowing wind to pass above and below.

For damaged or suspect sections, first plant a ten-*chi* wooden stake every one *chi*, reinforcing weak points. Drive in *lülu* planks to press against the wood, with *lülu* planks eight *chi* in height, seven *cun* in width, and one *chi* one *cun* in thickness. Apply several strikes to drive them down, then secure them with upper and lower clamps. Use one *gou* rope, grain baskets, net-stones, and suspended *da* mats, ensuring stakes are planted inward rather than outward.

The "blocking frames" are buried four *chi* deep, with taller ones reaching ten *chi*, using wood of varying lengths. Sharpen their tops, and coat both inner and outer surfaces thickly with mud. Arrange the front row with walkways and suspended *da* mats. At the corners, construct watchtowers, which must be curved and enclosed.

For every five *bu* of earthwork, ensure no more than twenty *jing*. Dig one sparrow-hole every ten *chi*, with the lower embankment three *chi* deep, widening outward. Rotate provisions atop the city walls, including towers, dispersed supplies, and leather basins for the moat. If under rotation, attacking soldiers strike from behind, and if defenses falter, apply fire to chariot leather.

The method for repelling scaling-ladders and assaults: place *bo* barriers outside the city, ten *chi* from the wall, with a thickness of ten *chi*. The method for felling and handling timber: cut all wood, large and small, into ten-*chi* lengths, then separate and bury them deeply, tamping firmly to prevent extraction. Place one *sha* every twenty *bu*, with fill, ten *chi* thick. Each *sha* has two gates, five *bu* wide; reinforce the gate with *bo* planks and ladders, tamping to allow easy removal. On the city wall, position pounders near the *bo* gates. Hang large [items], with one *yi* every four *chi*, one *ao* every five *bu*; the *ao* gate has a furnace with charcoal.

Transmit the order: once enemy troops are fully committed, set fire to the gates with chariot flames, then lower hanging fire, positioning *zai* in formation. The width accommodates the entire unit, with one fire between every two *zai*. All stand ready, awaiting the drum signal to ignite, then unleash simultaneously. If the enemy retreats from the fire only to attack again, lower the hanging fire once more—the enemy will be severely afflicted.

If the enemy retreats in disarray, order our death-soldiers to sally forth from the side gates and strike their rear guard. Command the valiant troops and the commander-in-chief to listen for the city drum’s signal to advance, and again to withdraw. Exploit this to deploy troops and set ambushes. At midnight, the city walls resound with drums and war cries on all sides, sowing chaos among the enemy, shattering their army and slaying their generals.

Use [dyed] clothing as uniforms, and employ signals for mutual recognition.

*Mozi*, Scroll Fourteen, concludes.

**Textual Variants and Emendations**

1. "亢" (*kàng*): *Xian Yu* (《闲诂》) reads as "穴" (*xué*, "cavity").
2. "麦" (*mài*): *Xian Yu* reads as "突" (*tū*, "assault").
3. "服" (*fú*): *Xian Yu* reads as "敢" (*gǎn*, "dare").
4. Below "则" (*zé*), Yu Yue (俞曲园) supplements "犹" (*yóu*, "still") based on the following sentence.
5. Below "县" (*xuán*), Bi Yuan (毕沅) adds "门" (*mén*, "gate") following *Taiping Yulan* (《太平御览》).
6. The two characters "问扁" (*wèn biǎn*) are emended by Bi Yuan to "门扇" (*mén shàn*, "gate leaves") based on the subsequent text.
7. "未" (*wèi*): *Xian Yu* reads as "末" (*mò*, "end").
8. "孜" (*zī*): Bi Yuan conjecturally emends to "孔" (*kǒng*, "hole").
9. "升" (*shēng*): Wang Niansun (王念孙) emends to "斗" (*dǒu*, "dou-measure").
10. "草" (*cǎo*): Wang Niansun emends to "革" (*gé*, "leather").
11. "濠" (*háo*): Wang Yinzhi (王引之) emends to "涿" (*zhuō*, "lacquer").
12. "尺" (*chǐ*): *Xian Yu* reads as "丈" (*zhàng*, "zhang").
13. "表" (*biǎo*): Ju Yuan (举沅) emends to "裹" (*guǒ*, "wrap") following the *Han Shu* annotation.
14. Below "一" (*yī*), Ju Yuan supplements "宠" (*chǒng*, "favor") following *Taiping Yulan*.
15. "再" (*zài*): Bi Yuan emends to "井" (*jǐng*, "well") following *Taiping Yulan*.
16. Su Shixue (苏时学) notes: "什" (*shí*) should be "斗" (*dǒu*, "dou").
17. Below "五" (*wǔ*), Wang Niansun supplements "尺" (*chǐ*, "chi") following *Mozi’s "Mou Shou"* (《模守篇》).
18. "雨" (*yǔ*): Bi Yuan emends to "两" (*liǎng*, "two") based on context.
19. "士" (*shì*): Bi Yuan conjecturally emends to "土" (*tǔ*, "earth").
20. "籍" (*jí*): Wang Yinzhi emends to "薪" (*xīn*, "firewood").
21. "闲" (*xián*): *Xian Yu* reads as "闭" (*bì*, "close").
22. Below "亭" (*tíng*), Wang Niansun supplements "一" (*yī*, "one") following *Taiping Yulan*.
23. "有序" (*yǒu xù*): Wang Niansun emends to "有重厚" (*yǒu zhòng hòu*, "thick and heavy").
24. "夫" (*fū*): Bi Yuan emends to "矢" (*shǐ*, "arrow") following *Taiping Yulan*.
25. "内" (*nèi*): Bi Yuan conjecturally emends to "穴" (*xué*, "cavity"). (Same as note 29.)
26. "涉" (*shè*): Ju Yuan emends to "沙" (*shā*, "sand").
27. "卞" (*biàn*): Ju Yuan conjecturally emends to "开" (*kāi*, "open"). (Same below.)
28. The character "乎" (*hū*) is suspected by Bi Yuan to be superfluous.
29. Same as note 25.
30. "本" (*běn*): Wang Niansun emends to "士" (*shì*, "soldier").
31. "匝" (*zā*): Wang Niansun emends to "迎" (*yíng*, "welcome").
32. From "城四面四隅" (*chéng sì miàn sì yú*, "the four sides and four corners of the city") to this point, *Xian Yu* relocates the passage to follow "各为二幕二，一凿而系绳，长四尺" (*gè wéi èr mù èr, yī záo ér xì shéng, cháng sì chǐ*, "each makes two curtains, one bored and tied with a four-chi rope") and precede "大铤，前长尺" (*dà tǐng, qián cháng chǐ*, "large stakes, a chi long in front").
33. "也" (*yě*): Wang Yinzhi emends to "池" (*chí*, "moat").

37. "代" (*dài*): Bi Yuan conjecturally emends to "伐" (*fá*, "strike").
38. From "为之奈何" (*wéi zhī nài hé*, "what is to be done") to this point, *Xian Yu* relocates the passage to *Mozi’s "Bei Xue"* (《备穴篇》), following "禽子再拜……或中人一" (*Qínzǐ zài bài... huò zhòng rén yī*, "Qinzi bows again... or strikes one person").
39. From "此十四者无一" (*cǐ shí sì zhě wú yī*, "none of these fourteen") to this point, *Xian Yu* relocates the passage to follow "不然，则赏明可信而罚严足畏也" (*bùrán, zé shǎng míng kě xìn ér fá yán zú wèi yě*, "otherwise, rewards must be clear and trustworthy, and punishments severe and fearsome").

50. "攸" (*yōu*): Bi Yuan conjecturally emends to "版" (*bǎn*, "plank").
51. Wang Yinzhi notes: "徒" (*tú*) should be "从" (*cóng*, "follow"), as the two characters resemble each other in clerical script.
52. From "侯望适人" (*hóu wàng shì rén*, "scouts observe the approaching enemy") to this point, *Xian Yu* relocates the passage to *Mozi’s "Bei Xue"*.
53. Bi Yuan notes: "杖" (*zhàng*) is a miswriting of "材" (*cái*, "material").

59. "入" (*rù*): Wang Yinzhi emends to "人" (*rén*, "person").

64. Same as note #40.
65. Yu Qiuyu notes: "杖" (*zhàng*) should be "材" (*cái*, "material"). (Same below.)

From "大铤，前长尺，蚤长五寸" (*dà tǐng, qián cháng chǐ, zǎo cháng wǔ cùn*, "large halberd, front one chi long, tang five cun long") to this point, *Xian Yu* relocates the passage to the *Bei Chengmen* chapter, following "...斧亦两端。三步一" (*fǔ yì liǎng duān. sān bù yī*, "...axes also with two ends.
