*Mozi*, Scroll Fifteen, Sixty-Fourth Missing Section.

Sixty-Fifth Missing Section. Sixty-Sixth Missing Section.

**Welcoming the Enemy with Sacrifices, Sixty-Eighth**
When the enemy comes from the east, meet them at the eastern altar. The altar is eight *chi* high, with eight *mi* of hall density. Eight elders of eighty years preside over the sacrifice. Eight green banners and eight green spirit tablets, each eight *chi* long, are used. Eight crossbows shoot eight times and then cease. The general’s attire must be green, and the sacrificial animal is a chicken.

When the enemy comes from the south, meet them at the southern altar. The altar is seven *chi* high, with seven *mi* of hall density. Seven elders of seventy years preside over the sacrifice. Seven red banners and seven red spirit tablets, each seven *chi* long, are used. Seven crossbows shoot seven times and then cease. The general’s attire must be red, and the sacrificial animal is a dog.

When the enemy comes from the west, meet them at the western altar. The altar is nine *chi* high, with nine *mi* of hall density. Nine elders of ninety years preside over the sacrifice. Nine white banners and nine plain spirit tablets, each nine *chi* long, are used. Nine crossbows shoot nine times and then cease. The general’s attire must be white, and the sacrificial animal is a sheep.

When the enemy comes from the north, meet them at the northern altar. The altar is six *chi* high, with six *mi* of hall density. Six elders of sixty years preside over the sacrifice. Six black banners and six black spirit tablets, each six *chi* long, are used. Six crossbows shoot six times and then cease. The general’s attire must be black, and the sacrificial animal is a *xi*.

For all major sacrifices at external residences, spirit mediums may offer prayers, and sacrificial animals are provided. In observing *qi*, there is the *qi* of great generals, the *qi* of lesser generals, advancing *qi*, retreating *qi*, and defeated *qi*. Those who can discern these clearly may foretell victory or defeat, fortune or misfortune.

Summon shamans, physicians, and diviners to their posts. Prepare medicines in full, house them in the palace, and provide them with proper quarters. Shamans must reside near the public altars and revere the spirits. Shamans and diviners are to request the commander’s presence only to observe *qi* and make inquiries. If they spread rumors to alarm officials and the people, investigate discreetly, pass judgment, and impose penalties without pardon. The quarters for observing *qi* should be near the commander’s office.

Select worthy grand officers, those with specialized skills, and artisans, and treat them with respect. Employ butchers and cooks in the kitchens to serve, and treat them with respect.

**General Principles of Defending a City**
The county magistrate assumes command, dispatches guards, patrols the moats and embankments, constructs bridges and roads, and repairs the city walls. All officials contribute resources, and all artisans attend to their duties. The marshal inspects the city’s defenses and organizes the troops.

Set up city gates with three persons in charge of the right gate, two in charge of the left gate, and four in charge of closing the gates, while a hundred armored soldiers stand guard. On the city walls, every step has one armored soldier and one halberdier, each assisted by three attendants. Every five steps, there is a squad leader; every ten steps, a platoon leader; every hundred steps, a company commander. Flanking them are senior officers, and in the center is the commanding general, each with their respective officers and subordinates.

At the city’s stairways, officials are stationed to guard them. In the central area, urgent matters are reported swiftly. All soldiers have their duties.

Outside the city, within the range of arrows, demolish walls to prevent the enemy from using them as cover. Within thirty *li*, bring firewood, kindling, and water inside. Dogs, donkeys, pigs, and geese are to be eaten, and their bones collected to make fermented broth for the sick.

Within the city, firewood, kindling, and thatched huts—anything within arrow range—are to be removed to prevent their use as cover. Issue orders at dusk to tether dogs and horses and to weave ropes. During the quiet of night, listen for drumbeats to assess the situation, which serves to counter the enemy’s *qi* and strengthen the people’s resolve. Thus, regular assessments prevent the people from growing weary.

The invocators and scribes then make announcements to the altars of the four directions, mountains, rivers, and the soil and grain spirits. They first address military matters before withdrawing. The lord, dressed in plain clothes, takes an oath in the grand ancestral temple, saying: “These people act without the Dao, neglecting righteousness and propriety. They claim to be kings, declaring: ‘I will surely destroy your altars and annihilate your people.’ My two or three ministers, rise in the night and exert yourselves! Be diligent on my behalf, unite in purpose and strength, and defend to the death.” After the oath, the lord retires to eat. He lodges to the right of the grand ancestral temple, while the invocators and scribes lodge at the soil altar.

All officials prepare their attendants. Drums are beaten at the gatehouse, with the right side displaying the military banner and the left side displaying the standard at the corner of the training ground. Three arrows are shot to announce victory. With all five types of weapons prepared, they descend, exit the gate, and ascend to survey the outskirts. Drums are then sounded, and shortly after, the marshal shoots from the right side of the gate. Reed arrows are shot first, followed by three arrows of thatch, then bows and crossbows. From the left side of the gate, the troops first hurl javelins, followed by stones. The invocators, scribes, and ancestral officers report to the soil altar and cover the offerings with rice.

**Standards and Flags, Sixty-Ninth**
In the art of defending a city, wood is represented by green flags, fire by red flags, firewood by yellow flags, stone by white flags, water by black flags, food by mushroom-shaped flags, dead soldiers by *cangying* flags, elite soldiers by *yu* flags, large troops by double-hare flags, five-*chi* boys by child flags, women by ladder-end flags, crossbows by dog flags, halberds by foot-soldier flags, swords and shields by feather flags, chariots by ridge flags, and cavalry by crow flags. For any flags not listed in the records, name them according to their shape.

When flags are raised on the city walls, the officials in charge of supplies deliver resources until they are sufficient, then lower the flags.

**General Principles of Defending a City (continued)**
Stones are to be stockpiled, firewood stockpiled, reeds and thatch stockpiled, *ju* and reeds stockpiled, wood stockpiled, charcoal stockpiled, sand stockpiled, pine and cypress stockpiled, *peng* and mugwort stockpiled, hemp and resin stockpiled, metal and iron stockpiled, and grain and rice stockpiled. Wells and pulleys have designated locations, hostages have residences, the five types of weapons each have their flags, and tokens each have their distinctions. Laws and decrees each have their constancy.

**Section Seventy: Orders and Commands**

Weights and measures each have their requests; those responsible for overseeing roads have their regulations.

Watchtower officers each have their banners: poles twenty-five feet long, silk fifteen feet long, and half a bolt in width for the largest. When the enemy relays an attack at the outer edge of the moat, the city’s defenders at the corresponding sector strike the drum three times and raise one banner. When they reach the middle of the water, strike four times and raise two banners. When they reach the palisade, strike five times and raise three banners. When they reach the inner rampart, strike six times and raise four banners. When they reach the parapet, strike seven times and raise five banners. When they scale the main wall, strike eight times and raise six banners. If they climb more than halfway up the wall, the drumming continues without pause. At night, use fire signals in the same manner. When the enemy retreats, lower the banners in the same sequence as they were raised, but without drumming.

The city’s defensive mound is fifty feet long. On each of the four sides and four gates, the commanding officers are forty feet long, followed by thirty feet, twenty-five feet, twenty feet, and fifteen feet, with no height less than forty-five feet. Officers and soldiers on the city wall place their insignia on their backs, while soldiers place theirs on their heads. Officers and soldiers below the wall place theirs at eyebrow level. If stationed elsewhere, place it at the left eyebrow; the central command places theirs on the chest. Each unit has one drum, while the central command has three. Every drum is struck thirty times in three sets. All officers with drums must respond in strict order—those who fail to respond when they should, or respond when they should not, will be executed by the commander.

Roads are thirty paces wide, with two stairways on either side at the base of the city wall. Wells are equipped with iron crossbows. Beyond the roads, construct screens thirty paces apart, circular and ten feet high. For civilian districts, walls must be at least twelve feet high. Those guarding the perimeter roads have gates at their centers, each guarded by two men. Without a verified token, none may pass—those who disobey are executed.

All officers, soldiers, and civilians—men and women—wear distinct badges and clothing to distinguish gender. For those guarding the sacrificial altars: after three advances and retreats, the commander summons them, rewards them with food, and presents a great banner. Record the names of those from each hundred-household village or other contributors, erecting a banner at their post so all may clearly know: "This is the banner of so-and-so." The inner width of the sacrificial altar is twenty-five paces, the outer width ten paces, with length determined by terrain. Execute deserters. Train soldiers in formations—front, back, left, and right—and rotate those who are fatigued.

**Section Seventy: Securing the State**
The Way of securing the state begins with assigning tasks according to the land. When land is used appropriately, success is achieved; when misused, labor is wasted without result. The same applies to people. If preparations are not made in advance, there is no way to secure the ruler. When officers, soldiers, and civilians are divided in purpose, the fault lies with their commanders.

All rewards, punishments, and governance must stem from meritorious service. Frequently send envoys to commend and reward those guarding border cities, passes, and frontier defenses, acknowledging the hardships of defending against barbarians. Assess whether the resources of their commanders are sufficient or lacking, whether the terrain is suitable for defense, and whether their equipment is well-stocked. In border counties and towns, if the trees are poor, use less timber; if fields are uncultivated and food scarce, avoid constructing large buildings with thatched roofs or excessive beams. If resources are abundant, the people may indulge in food.

Prepare internal tallies, install plank walkways, and place equipment above them. Officers, soldiers, and attendants on the city wall all have quarters within the pathways, each assigned to their respective sectors. For every ten attendants, one is designated as the daily tally officer, responsible for verifying and guarding all gates. Gatekeepers and those enforcing prohibitions must not allow idlers to linger nearby—those who disobey are executed.

When the enemy approaches, for a city wall a thousand yards long, the outer wall must be close to it, which benefits the defenders. If the wall is less than a thousand yards, do not engage. Observe the enemy’s position and numbers, and respond accordingly—this is the essence of defending a city. Matters not covered here should be addressed through personal vigilance and human effort.

In defending a city, inflicting casualties on the enemy with traps is paramount. Prolonging the siege to await reinforcements demonstrates mastery of defense. Those who cannot do this are unfit to defend a city.

When the enemy is more than a hundred *li* from the city, the city commander must immediately summon all five officials and hundred-household leaders, taking the relatives of wealthy and prominent families as hostages and entrusting them with official tokens. Assign trustworthy men to guard them and act with utmost secrecy. Then, fortify the city.

The commander’s camp must have no fewer than three hundred men. For the four sides and four gates, select commanders who are meritorious officials or descendants of those who died in service, each leading a hundred soldiers. Gate commanders also guard other gates, and above each gate, construct high towers on both sides, stationing skilled archers there. Assign one person to the outer wall and one to the inner rampart, selecting those with dependents. Strike the drum every fifty paces.

Divide the city’s wards into eight divisions, each with an officer. Each officer is accompanied by four men to patrol the streets and wards. For elders and minors in the wards who do not participate in defense or accounting, divide the ward into four sections, each with a leader to monitor movements. Those who wander at odd hours or behave suspiciously are to be investigated for treachery.

Officers with four or more soldiers under their command must receive a verified token from the commander. When the commander sends someone, the defender checks the token. If the token does not match or the signal is incorrect, platoon leaders and above must detain them and report to the commander. Those who fail to detain them or allow officers and soldiers to proceed will be executed.

For all crimes up to and including capital offenses, return the convicts to their parents, spouses, and siblings.

For men and women defending the city wall: every ten must have six crossbows and four other weapons. Able-bodied women, the elderly, and minors each carry one spear. If an emergency arises, the central command rapidly strikes the drum three times. All roads on the city wall, streets, and alleys within the city are to be cleared—those who move will be executed.

When women join the main army, order the men to march on the left and the women on the right—no walking side by side. All must proceed to their assigned posts; those who disobey are executed. Those who abandon their posts are publicly denounced every three days as a precaution against treachery.

The *li* jars and all others must guard and stay overnight at the *li* gate. Officers patrol their sectors and, upon reaching the *li* gate, the jars open the gate to admit them. They patrol alongside the elders guarding the sector and inspect secluded alleys and uninhabited areas. If traitors conspire with external enemies, they are punished by *chelie*. If the jars, elders, or sector officers fail to report such conspiracies, they too are executed; if they expose the plot, they are exempted from punishment and rewarded with two *yi* of gold per person.

When the commander dispatches inspectors to patrol the defenses, they make five circuits during long nights and three during short nights. Officers on all four sides also patrol their own sectors as the commander does; those who disobey are executed.

Set up fire beacons in a grid pattern, with the fire chimneys rising four *chi* above the rooftops. Take care that no one dares cause a fire—those who do are executed. Those who intentionally start fires to create chaos are punished by *chelie*. If the five-person unit fails to report the arsonist, they are executed; if they capture the arsonist, they are exempted from punishment.

Firefighters must not shout or clamor, nor may they abandon their posts to rush through blocked alleys to fight fires—those who do are executed. If jars, elders, or sector officers are guarding that alley, they may rescue the trapped, and the sector officer must immediately report to the commander. The commander dispatches trusted aides to lead the rescue. Sector officers who fail to report are executed.

Women sentenced to death or implicated in fire-related offenses are not punished unless they used the fire to incite disorder, in which case they are dealt with according to the law.

Strict prohibitions during a siege: If the enemy suddenly arrives, officers and civilians must not dare to shout, gather in threes, walk side by side, exchange glances, sit and weep, gaze around, raise hands to signal, point, call out, pass by one another, follow closely, throw objects, strike one another, or press against others with their bodies or clothing. Nor may they argue or speak out of turn, or watch the enemy’s movements—those who do are executed. If the five-person unit fails to report the violation, they are executed; if they report it, they are exempted.

If a member of a five-person unit defects to the enemy, the unit is executed; if a platoon leader defects, the company officer is executed; if an officer defects, the company commander is executed. The parents, spouses, and siblings of defectors are all punished by *chelie*. Those who expose the plot beforehand are exempted.

If someone deliberately lures the enemy to a vulnerable position, they are executed. If the five-person unit fails to report it, they are executed; if they report it, they are exempted. If someone swiftly drives the enemy back from a vulnerable position, preventing them from advancing further, the two most effective units are rewarded with the highest honors.

If the siege is lifted and the city has a circumference of one *li* or more, the city commander is enfeoffed with thirty *li* of land as a *guanneihou*, the deputy commander is granted the rank of *shangqing*, and the adjutants and officers of equivalent rank are awarded the title of *wudafu*. Officers, officials, and valiant defenders who contributed to the victory—ten or more, including the five officers on the city wall—are all granted the rank of *gongcheng*. Men who defended the city are each awarded two ranks, women receive five thousand coins, and the elderly, minors, and those who previously assisted in the defense are each given one thousand coins. They are exempted from taxes for three years.

This is how officers and civilians are encouraged to defend resolutely and achieve victory in lifting the siege.

Officers and soldiers stationed at the main gate must not exceed two per unit. The bravest serve as vanguards, and the unit sits together, ensuring each knows their left, right, front, and back. Those who leave their posts without authorization are executed.

The gate captain inspects the guards three times during the day. At dusk, the drum is struck, the gate is closed, and one inspection is conducted. During the night, the commander sends someone to verify, and the names of those on duty are recorded. Meals are taken at the post; no one may eat outside.

The commander must carefully observe those who deliver messages, shield-bearers, attendants, and women serving at the front, noting their intentions, expressions, commands, and speech. When the commander eats or drinks, someone must taste the food first. If any of these actions are not requested, the offender is struck and questioned about the reason.

If the commander is displeased with messengers, shield-bearers, attendants, or women serving at the front, they may order their execution. If they resist or are bound improperly, or if the binding is delayed, they are executed. The commander must regularly warn them in advance.

Officers at the gates stand or sit morning and evening, arranged by age. Each morning and evening, they take their positions, with those of merit and ability first, followed by the rest in order. Every five days, each office reports one instance of frivolity, improper conduct, or bullying.

When envoys from outside arrive, they must be accompanied by an escort. When they depart and return, or travel to a district, a trusted person must first prepare their lodging, then go out to greet them. Only after the gate guards verify their entry may they proceed to their lodging.

Subordinates must constantly observe their superiors, following them as they move, but superiors do not follow subordinates. They must... follow. When guest soldiers guard the host’s city, or when the host provides guards for the guests, the host also guards the guest soldiers.

If garrison soldiers in the city belong to a town that has defected, take precautions and frequently inspect their posts. Do not allow those from the same town to guard the same location. Issue tallies to the officers at the stepped gates; if the tally matches, they are admitted and rewarded. If the tally does not match, they are detained, and the commander is informed. Those who ascend the city wall must wear the proper attire; any other violations are dealt with accordingly.

The night drum is kept at the main gate. At dusk, the commander orders cavalry or envoys bearing tallies to close the city, all holding *chan* weapons. At the first night drum (ten strikes), all gate and watchtower guards close their posts. Travelers are halted, and their reasons for moving are questioned; only then is their punishment carried out.

At dawn, the keeper of documents is shown, the drum is struck to allow movement, and the officers at each city gate request the keys. After the gates are opened, the keys are immediately returned. These orders do not apply when tallies or credentials are presented.

When the enemy approaches, the watchtower drum is struck five times, followed by the perimeter drum and then the smaller drums. After five strikes of the small drum, the army halts. Commands must inspire awe, rewards must be substantial, and orders must be carried out. Once an order is issued, someone must follow up to assess its feasibility.

Signals: At dusk, signals are given; those who fail to signal are punished. For defense preparations, assign designated posts—mark them as "such-and-such post"—and place them at street intersections, steps, or gates, so that all who pass may see and comply.

Any officer, soldier, or civilian who plots to kill or wound their commander or superior shall be punished as severely as those who plot rebellion. Those who capture or report such conspirators shall be rewarded with twenty *jin* of gold and granted immunity from punishment.

Those who act beyond their assigned duties or seize what they are not authorized to take shall be punished. Those who meddle in affairs outside their jurisdiction shall likewise be punished.

Any officer, soldier, or civilian who trespasses into another’s jurisdiction without authorization shall be detained and handed over to the Director of Works or the patrol officer. The patrol officer shall report to the commander; those who fail to detain or who release such trespassers without authority shall be punished.

Those who capture or report a rebel, a traitor who surrenders the city, or one who secretly communicates with the enemy shall, by command, be granted the remission of two death sentences and the release of four *chengdan* laborers.

For those who betray the city and flee with their parents, the parents, wives, and children of the fugitives shall be held accountable.

All civilian households must declare the quantity of their timber, thatch, and stone, specifying their dimensions and sizes. Those who fail to declare shall be punished.

Soldiers and civilians stationed on the city walls shall each safeguard their immediate neighbors. If a neighbor commits an offense and one fails to report it, the next in the five-man unit shall be held liable. Those who personally capture an offender or report them to the authorities shall receive rewards. If one learns of an offense outside their own unit, the reward shall be doubled.

Authority outside the city rests with the commandant; authority within rests with the commander. If the commandant, deputy, or captain allows ten or more deserters to enter, they shall each be demoted two ranks. If one hundred or more enter, they shall be stripped of office and reduced to common soldiers for garrison duty.

Those who take captives must capture enemy combatants; only then shall their actions be approved.

If civilians wish to exchange goods, grain, or other supplies, soldiers shall facilitate the trade at fair prices.

If a townsman’s acquaintance or relative commits an offense and is not within the county, but the townsman wishes to ransom them—whether with grain, money, gold, cloth, silk, or other valuables—the commandant shall permit it.

Messengers shall be stationed every ten paces. Those who delay or fail to relay messages shall be punished.

For matters that may be expedited, written reports shall be sent to the commander.

If officers, soldiers, or civilians wish to report a matter, they shall submit a written request to the messenger. Those who delay or fail to report shall be punished.

Each county shall submit a list of its local heroes, strategists, prominent elders, and those of great influence, along with their numbers.

Officers, soldiers, and civilians in the palace, government offices, and beneath the city walls shall all maintain fire watch, relaying vigilance to those before, behind, left, and right.

If a fire starts from a boundary wall and spreads to a neighboring wall, the responsible party shall be punished.

Those who use their strength to bully the weak or young, or who forcibly violate women, or cause disturbances, shall all be punished.

At all city gates and watchtowers, carefully inspect the credentials of travelers. If a pass is suspect or absent, escort the traveler to the county office to explain their mission. Those with valid passes shall be lodged in official quarters. If acquaintances or relatives wish to visit them, summon them—do not allow them to linger in the streets.

The village elders and commander shall question the commandant, who shall respond with strict discipline. For minor matters, outsiders shall not enter the village. Village elders shall not enter private homes.

Orders within the village shall be relayed by feathered signals, placed at three designated locations. Each household shall send a representative to the official post. Those who fail to relay orders or delay them shall be punished.

Households with defenders shall prepare meals. Officers, soldiers, or civilians without credentials who enter village lanes or government offices shall be punished, as shall officials, village elders, or commanders who neglect their duties.

Those who steal official weapons, supplies, or private property worth one *qian* or more shall all be punished.

Officers, soldiers, and civilians shall each inscribe their names prominently on a placard and display it at their post. The commander shall inspect these placards. Those who enter unauthorized posts shall be punished.

On the city walls, at the first evening drum, alternate shifts shall be arranged. Those who conceal or fail to report contraband within restricted areas shall be punished.

When officers, soldiers, or civilians die, summon their kin and bury them with the assistance of the Director of Works. Do not allow mourning to disrupt duties. Those severely wounded shall be sent home for treatment, provided with physicians, medicine, two *sheng* of wine daily, and two *jin* of meat. Officers shall frequently visit to check on their recovery; when they improve, report their condition to superiors. Those who feign injury to evade duty shall be executed along with their families.

After the crisis, the commander shall personally visit the homes of the dead and wounded, standing at their doors in sorrow.

When the enemy retreats and the crisis ends, perform sacrificial rites.

The commander shall, by decree, reward the county’s heroes, those who strengthened the state, and all who rendered service. He shall personally visit the homes of the dead and wounded to offer condolences and meet with the families of the fallen.

When the siege is lifted, the commander shall dispatch envoys to commend the meritorious and tally the dead and wounded. He shall bestow ranks, emoluments, and honors upon them, elevating their status to inspire their enmity toward the enemy.

Soldiers and officers on the city walls shall each safeguard their neighbors. Those who conspire to betray the city shall have their parents, wives, children, and siblings executed. Those who know of such plots but fail to capture or report them shall share the same punishment.

Households beneath the city walls shall mutually protect one another and report any misconduct on the walls. Those who capture or report such offenders shall be enfeoffed with a domain of a thousand households. If the capture or report comes from outside their unit, the reward shall be a domain of two thousand households.

City prohibitions: Those who do not wish to serve the enemy in minor roles or carry banners shall be punished. Those who disobey orders shall be punished. Those who issue unauthorized commands shall be punished. Those who fail to relay orders shall be punished. Those who lean on their halberds or hang them improperly, or who do not align with the ranks, shall be punished. Those who shout without cause shall be punished. Those who neglect their duties shall be punished. Those who praise outsiders while slandering their own shall be punished. Those who leave their posts to gather and gossip shall be punished. Those who arrive at their posts after the city drum sounds shall be punished.

Each person shall inscribe their name on a placard and display it at their post. The commander shall personally determine the order of precedence. Those who enter unauthorized posts shall be punished.

Those who leave their posts to enter other posts on the left or right, and those who fail to apprehend them, as well as those who carry private letters, make solicitations, or write letters on behalf of others, or who abandon their guard duties to attend to private affairs—along with soldiers or civilians who steal from one another’s households or abduct elders—shall all be punished without pardon. Their names shall be recorded and confiscated.

Those who move about the army without a tally shall be punished. When guests are stationed below the city, though their posts may frequently change, their provisions shall not; those who praise the enemy by understating their numbers, falsely claiming disorder as order, or misrepresenting clumsy attacks as skillful shall be punished. Neither guests nor hosts may converse or lend aid to one another. If a guest shoots a letter, none may praise it; if outsiders display kindness to insiders, none may respond. Those who disobey orders shall all be punished.

It is forbidden to raise arrows with messages or to shoot letters at the enemy. Those who violate this order shall have their parents, wives, and children punished, and their own bodies displayed on the city walls. Those who capture or report such offenders shall be rewarded with twenty catties of gold.

None may move about at improper times except the commander and those bearing the credentials of the acting governor. When the commander takes position at the city, they must carefully question the elders, officials, and gentlemen, and summon those with unresolved grievances to clarify and resolve them.

The commander must personally distinguish and record those who enter, interrogate them, and if any use private grudges to harm the city or official affairs, their parents, wives, and children shall all be punished. Those who conspire with outsiders against the city shall have their three clans exterminated. Those who capture or report such offenders shall be enfeoffed with the city they guard, large or small; upon the commander’s return, their seals shall be conferred, and they shall be honored with official rank, made known to all officials, gentlemen, soldiers, and civilians.

Those among the powerful and eminent who frequently associate with feudal lords shall be regularly summoned, their communications relayed upward, and they shall be treated well and entrusted. The officials in their places of residence shall repeatedly select and prepare them, ensuring they do not move about freely, and their families shall be held as hostages.

Local elders, village leaders, and the parents, wives, and children of the powerful and eminent must be honored and favored. If any poor person cannot feed themselves, the authorities shall provide for them. The parents, relatives, and wives of brave warriors shall be regularly supplied with wine and meat, treated with respect, and housed near the governor.

The commander’s tower overlooking the hostage palace must be well-secured and thoroughly plastered, ensuring those below cannot see above, while those above can see below, and none below know whether anyone is above.

The commander’s trusted associates shall recommend officials who are upright, honest, loyal, and capable of handling affairs. Their food, drink, wine, and meat shall not be restricted, and their money, gold, cloth, silk, and property shall each be guarded by themselves; they must take care not to steal from one another.

The walls of the fortified office must be triple-layered, with tiles and cauldrons stacked atop the ramparts. The gates shall have officials, with the chief stationed inside, and the gates shall be closed with mats, requiring the governor’s credentials for entry.

The guards must be selected from among the most steadfast soldiers. Trustworthy officials without malice, capable of handling affairs, shall be chosen. They shall be ordered to construct ten-foot walls around the perimeter, with gates and side gates, except for the gate of the commander’s adjutant.

The quarters of the qi-observers must be near the commander, and the shamans’ quarters near the public altar; the spirits must be revered. Shamans, invocators, and historians must report favorable omens to the people and petition the commander, who alone shall know their requests. None may collude with qi-observers to spread false or alarming words among the people; violators shall be punished without pardon.

When provisions are insufficient, the people shall each declare their own stores, specifying the number of *shi* and *sheng* of the five grains per household. Those who conceal their stores or fail to declare them fully by the deadline, or whose declarations are found false by officials or soldiers, shall all be punished. Those who capture or report such offenders shall receive a reward of three-tenths.

For the management of grain, cloth, money, gold, and livestock, fair prices shall be set, and contracts recorded with the owners. Once the matter is concluded, the owners shall be repaid double the value. Additionally, ranks shall be awarded based on the value, quantity, and price of the goods. Those who wish to become officials shall be permitted; those who do not but wish to receive rewards, ranks, or emoluments, or to ransom relatives, acquaintances, or criminals, shall be allowed by order.

Those who receive rewards shall be made to appear before the fortified officials to present them to their families. Those who wish to further assist the authorities shall have their ranks and rewards doubled.

*Example records:*
*Household of so-and-so of such-and-such village in such-and-such county, two mouths, stores six hundred *shi* of grain.*
*Household of so-and-so of such-and-such village, ten mouths, stores one hundred *shi* of grain.*

Grain must be delivered by the set date; those who fail to deliver on time shall forfeit their stores to the state. Those who capture or report such offenders shall receive a reward of three-tenths. Care must be taken that the people do not learn the extent of the city’s grain stores.

When the commander enters the city, scouts shall be the first priority. Those captured shall be immediately employed and provided for by the authorities, without revealing the city’s defensive preparations. Scouts shall be assigned to separate offices, with their parents, wives, and children housed together, provided with clothing, food, wine, and meat, and treated well by trustworthy officials.

When scouts return, they shall be questioned. The commander’s palace shall have three layers of defenses: an outer ring of towers at the corners, an inner ring of towers, and a covered walkway leading into the fortified palace, fifteen feet in length. The fortified palace shall have no private chambers; every three days, mats shall be unrolled to inspect it, and straw shall be spread throughout, at least three feet thick.

Scouts must be dispatched from among the loyal, trustworthy, and respected men of the townships, those with families. They shall be generously provided for, and their families cared for as well. Their parents, wives, and children shall be housed separately, not with the conscripts, and provided with wine and meat.

Other scouts shall be sent with provisions as before. Upon their return, their reports shall be cross-examined for accuracy. Those who are rewarded three times for three accurate reports shall be generously rewarded again. Those who do not wish to receive rewards but wish to become officials shall be permitted as officials of two hundred *shi*. The commander shall personally confer their seals. Those who do not wish to become officials but wish to receive rewards or emoluments shall be treated as before.

Those who penetrate deeply into the enemy’s capital and return with accurate intelligence shall be questioned thoroughly and rewarded double that of other scouts.

Those who do not wish to receive rewards but desire profit shall be permitted to serve as scouts of three *shi*. When bestowing rewards upon valiant soldiers, the commander must personally deliver them to their families’ residences, allowing them to witness the commander’s commitment to duty. Those who wish to continue assisting their superiors shall have their rewards, ranks, and emoluments—as well as penalties for offenders—doubled.

Scouts shall not range beyond ten *li*, stationing themselves in elevated, convenient locations where they erect markers. Three men shall guard each marker. From the northernmost point to the city, three markers shall be established, aligned with the beacons on the city walls. By day, they shall raise smoke signals; by night, they shall raise fire. Upon learning the direction from which the enemy approaches, they must ascertain the enemy’s formation and determine whether an attack is imminent. For small towns incapable of self-defense and open to passage, all elders, the infirm, grain, and livestock shall be thoroughly safeguarded. No more than fifty scouts shall be dispatched, and upon the enemy’s approach, they shall be withdrawn. Take care never to grow complacent.

No more than three hundred scouts shall be assembled in a single company, sent out at dusk to perform covert duties. At empty encampments and strategic passes where people come and go, no fewer than three men shall be stationed within a *li*, maintaining vigilance on level ground. Each shall erect their marker, to which the city walls shall respond. Scouts shall advance beyond the battle-line markers, stationing themselves both outside and inside the outer gates. They shall erect markers there, positioning a small number of soldiers within the gates in such a way that their exact numbers remain unknown. Should an alarm arise and the enemy cross the battle-line markers, the city walls shall signal with a banner. Those nearby shall strike the jars in unison, and troops shall prepare for battle, following the banner’s direction. At the first sighting, one pennant shall be raised; upon entering the border, two; upon nearing the outer walls, three.

When the enemy enters the border, raise four pennants; when they draw near the city walls, raise five. At night, use fire signals in the same manner. Clear all walls, trees, and vegetation—large and small—within a hundred paces of the outer walls. Fill all wells outside the walls to deny the enemy water. Open all filled wells and fell all remaining trees. Bring every implement that could be used to attack the city inside the walls, ensuring each person records what they bring. When the task is complete, each shall retrieve their items according to their records. Seal the transaction with a contract, documenting the exact count. If any timber cannot be brought inside, burn it to prevent the enemy from using it.

Have each person inscribe their name on a large placard and display it at their post. If an official neglects their duties, they shall be punished according to the laws of excess. Those who display cowardice, strike jars in disorder, create clamor and unrest, obstruct thoroughfares, abandon their posts, or disrupt the timing of operations shall be shot. Those who incite panic among the troops shall be executed. Those who fail to remonstrate with superiors or who spread ominous words shall be executed. No one shall dare bring musical instruments or worn-out armor into the army; violators shall be shot. Without orders from the authorities, no one shall gallop in chariots, run hastily, or do so; violators shall be shot. No one shall allow cattle or horses to roam freely in the army; violators shall be shot. Those who fail to provide meals on time shall be shot. No one shall dare sing or wail in the army; violators shall be shot.

Commanders shall strictly enforce punishments, executing violators. If an official witnesses a crime but fails to punish it, they shall receive the same penalty; if they allow the offender to escape, they too shall be executed. Any general who leads their troops to violate the law shall be executed. Any official who fails to ensure that soldiers, officers, and civilians hear the oaths and orders shall be punished according to the severity of their offense. When executing someone in the marketplace, the executioner shall proceed with their eyes open.

The heralds shall wait outside the commander’s gate, forming two ranks seated on either side, taking turns to eat without interruption. One senior herald shall be stationed at the gate, and the commander shall frequently send messengers to inspect for deserters, reporting them to the gate captain and their superiors. Deserters shall be reported upon their return.

Four men shall sit inside the commander’s gate, two on each side, and two shall sit outside the side gate. When visitors arrive, they shall stand armed in front, taking turns to eat, and announce the names of those attending. The commander’s chamber shall have a high tower, where sentinels shall watch for approaching chariots or cavalry on the roads outside, as well as any unusual activity within the city, immediately reporting to the commander.

The commander shall verify reports from the city walls, gate sentinels, and local officials. Those at the base of the tower shall receive the sentinels’ reports and relay them to the commander. Two attendants shall sit inside the side gate, with the gate kept ajar, taking turns to eat; one shall be a senior attendant. Patrol the streets around the commander’s palace, establishing guard posts along the roads, each with walls on both sides ten feet high, topped with battlements for observation. Place initial markers shaped like chicken feet, flanking the path to inspect provisions.

Carefully examine and verify all written records, comparing them with the provisions. If discrepancies arise, investigate and rectify them. Outside the guard post walls, fortify the streets with towers overlooking the alleys, each tower equipped with a drum and a large bell. If an incident occurs, sound the drum, and officials shall arrive to address it. At night, use fire to indicate the drum’s location.

Construct latrines every fifty paces along the city walls, identical in design to those above. For those guilty of minor offenses deserving leniency, have them clean the latrines as punishment.

---

**Section Seventy-One: Miscellaneous Defenses**

Qinzi asked: When the enemy is numerous, bold, and eager to display their might to intimidate the defenders, advancing with firewood and earth to build *yangling* ramps, piling soil to tower over the people, pushing forward with shields, linking them to the city walls, and mounting siege engines and crossbows, what should be done?

Mozi replied: Are you asking about defending against *yangling* ramps? The *yangling* is a crude siege tactic, sufficient to exhaust troops but not to breach the city. Its effectiveness depends on distance: the farther the attack, the farther the harm; the closer to the city, the nearer the harm—but it will not reach the walls. Without respite, arrows and stones shall rain down, with archers moving swiftly to shoot from all sides. Use *lan* as rear supports, and maintain vigilance to fortify defenses.

Sharpen the resolve of our elite troops, ensuring they do not falter. Defenders shall hold their ground firmly, while attackers shall advance recklessly. Cultivate courage and high morale, and the people’s resolve shall multiply a hundredfold. With many holding their positions and few wavering, we shall not be endangered. If the troops do not rest, they cannot be withstood; once the enemy links their ramps to the city, counter them with the method for defending against cloud ladders.

For all defenses against smoke, battering rams, cloud ladders, and siege towers: if the city walls are insufficient, reinforce them with wooden beams. Place them a hundred paces to the left and right, and continuously hurl stones, sand, and charcoal upon the enemy like rain. Use firewood, flames, boiling water, and hot liquid to repel them.

Select and deploy elite troops, ensuring they do not falter. Clearly reward and punish, maintaining calm as the foundation, and act swiftly without allowing the commander to waver in resolve or the people’s morale to falter. With abundant rewards and strict discipline, the troops shall not grow weary. Battering rams, siege towers, and ladders shall all be countered with counter-rams.

The *qu* trench shall be fifteen feet long, with three feet buried underground, and arrows twelve feet long. The trench shall be sixteen feet wide, with a twelve-foot inner section and a four-foot overhang. Plant the *qu* without branches within five inches of the top. Place one ladder every ten feet along the *qu*. The number of *qu* answers: 258 per *li*, and 129 *qu* answers.

On all outer roads that can be fortified to hinder the enemy, construct three watchtowers where the threat is greatest, each with three corners, arranged like the Weaving Maid constellation to enable mutual support. On all mounds, forests, ravines, hills, ridges, outer gates, and intersections, establish checkpoints and minor posts to monitor the number of enemy movements and their hidden positions.

To protect the people, first survey all official residences, civilian homes, offices, and buildings within the city, large and small, and assign them to defenders. Allow those who wish to defend alongside brothers or acquaintances to do so. Bring grain, livestock, property, and all resources that can aid the city’s defense from outer residences into the city. If the situation grows urgent, have them stored inside the gates.

Scouts shall number no more than fifty. When the enemy approaches, they shall withdraw in accordance with the signals, with only the most essential remaining. The people shall contribute grain, cloth, silk, gold, money, oxen, horses, and livestock, all to be valued at fair prices and recorded in contracts with their owners.

Assign each person according to their strengths. When the affairs of the realm are properly ordered, duties shall be evenly distributed, and all shall find contentment in their tasks. When the realm’s affairs are well-prepared, the strong and weak shall be accounted for, and all matters shall be in readiness.

Watchtowers for defense shall be built round, rising over three *zhang* in height, with platforms arranged for counterattacks. Construct scaling ladders with arms three *chi* long, extending three *chi* from the gates, secured with ropes. Dig double trenches and suspend planks as bridges.

At each watchtower, station one drum. For enemy signals, alarm signals, and disorder signals, transmit the fire beacons in sequence until reaching the ruler’s capital. In urgent matters, raise or lower the signals accordingly. When beacons are lit, beat the drums five times in succession, repeating the signal to indicate the enemy’s numbers and direction. Report daily and relay the sequence of signals without fail.

Upon sighting the enemy, raise one beacon; upon their entry into the territory, raise two; if they besiege the outer walls, raise three beacons and one banner; if they gather at the outer city, raise four beacons and two banners; if they converge on the inner city, raise five beacons and five banners. At night, use fire signals in the same manner. Watchmen must act swiftly in emergencies.

At dusk, deploy scouts, assigning them minor posts. On hills and in forests, ensure all can be approached, and conduct inspections at dawn. If no approach is possible, each shall mark their position, and the city below shall respond accordingly.

Scouts shall place field markers, with lookouts stationed inside and outside the outer city, flags raised, and half the troops concealed within, their numbers unknown. At signs of alarm, raise the *kong* marker; upon sighting the enemy, raise the *mu* marker. On the city walls, signal with banners, and the outposts shall beat drums, align flags, and prepare for battle as directed. Farmers—men shall follow the outposts in battle readiness, while women shall swiftly return indoors. Upon enemy sighting, relay the report to the city’s commander.

Each watch post shall have three guards, taking turns striking the marker while observing. Patrols of cavalry or officials shall inspect the surroundings to discern the enemy’s intentions. Upon sounding the drum, if the enemy is sighted, the drumming shall continue until the signal reaches the city.

For annual provisions:
- One *sheng* per meal: thirty-six *shi* per year;
- One-third *sheng* per meal: twenty-four *shi* per year;
- One-fourth *sheng* per meal: eighteen *shi* per year;
- One-fifth *sheng* per meal: fourteen *shi* and one *sheng* per year;
- One-sixth *sheng* per meal: twelve *shi* per year.

Daily rations per person:
- One *sheng* per meal: five *sheng* per day;
- One-third *sheng* per meal: three *sheng* per day;
- One-fourth *sheng* per meal: two and a half *sheng* per day;
- One-fifth *sheng* per meal: two *sheng* per day;
- One-sixth *sheng* per meal: one and two-thirds *sheng* per day, with two meals daily.

In times of life-saving urgency:
- Two *sheng* per day for twenty days;
- Three *sheng* per day for thirty days;
- Four *sheng* per day for forty days.
Thus, the people shall be spared from ninety days of hardship.

When the enemy draws near, collect all miscellaneous metal vessels from the villages—bronze, iron, or anything useful for defense. First inventory all official residences, offices, and non-essential buildings, noting the size, length, and quantity of materials, prioritizing urgent needs. If the enemy presses close, dismantle houses and fell trees, ignoring any pleas or petitions. Bring firewood inside, stacking it not in overlapping rows but in orderly piles for easy retrieval. Any timber that cannot be brought inside shall be burned to deny its use to the enemy.

Stack wood by size, length, quality, and shape, with each type stored within the city’s four outer walls. Use the largest timbers as crossbars before piling the rest. For the city’s defense officers and above, their parents, siblings, wives, and children shall be held as hostages in the ruler’s residence to ensure steadfast defense.

Appoint officials as follows:
- *Du Sikong*: four for large cities, two for lookouts;
- County lookouts: one per side;
- *Ting Wei*, *Ci Sikong*, and *Yi Zheng*: one each.

Those serving as attendants in the defense headquarters must be sufficiently wealthy, upright, and trustworthy, with their parents, siblings, wives, and children residing in the fortified palace. All officials must provide hostages to qualify for their duties.

At each main gate, station two guards, standing on either side to direct passersby outward. Place four halberds beside each gate, with the guards seated beneath them. Officials shall inspect them five times daily, recording the names of those who pass.

Where moats and waterways have strategic points or vulnerabilities, station suspicious individuals to be targeted by night patrols. In the water outside the walls, install bamboo spikes, each one *chi* wide and two *bu* long, submerged five *cun* below the water’s surface, varying in length. Arrange three rows along the outer edge, with the outermost row facing outward and the inner row facing inward.

Every thirty *bu*, construct an archery shelter, ten *chi* wide and twelve *chi* deep. In emergencies, dispatch the nearest reinforcements first, with others following in succession. For official passes, the issuer must clearly record the circumstances, ensuring the pass matches the stated purpose, and verify its return with a tally. When a pass is issued, the gatekeeper shall announce the time and the name of the bearer.

Form units of one hundred *bu*. Connect and interlink guard posts, tunneling through walls where necessary. Build elevated walkways and construct embankments, reinforcing their tops. First, deliberate on virtuous plans and strategies before entering the fortified palace. Once inside, do not leave the city or abandon your post.

All defenders must thoroughly assess the city’s low walls and shallow moats, adjusting their defenses accordingly. Morning and evening, the troops shall sing to maintain discipline, using fewer men for easier defense. Select capable individuals and ensure every household stores three years’ worth of grain and vegetables to prepare for floods, droughts, or poor harvests.

Regularly instruct border counties to cultivate *qiang*, *yun*, *wuhui*, and *zhuye*, planting them in outer residences where ditches and wells are genuine; if blocked, place them within. In times of peace, demonstrate danger; in times of danger, demonstrate peace.

When the enemy approaches, have all gates and doors drilled with paired holes, one set connected by a four-*chi*-long rope as thick as a finger.

When the enemy arrives, first slaughter oxen, sheep, chickens, dogs, crows, and ravens, preserving their hides, sinews, horns, fat, wings, and feathers. Strip all conches. Have officials collect tung wood to forge iron spikes, and use thick bamboo as crossbars. In urgent situations where troops cannot venture far, order the felling of trees in outer residences. Calculate the required quantity: if preparing to strike from the city’s original empty spaces, arrange them at three corners. For all trees weighing over five *jin*, soak them in water, but no longer than one night. For thatched roofs or stacked firewood, apply a coating at least five *cun* thick. Officials shall each report the resources within their jurisdiction that can be used for defense.

There are slanderers, opportunists, malevolent people, virtuous people, leaders, strategists, warriors, artisans, messengers, insiders, outsiders, good-natured people, and those skilled at guarding gates—examine the reasons for their reputations and only admit them accordingly. When civilians feud or criticize officials, record the officials’ explanations in writing and store them to await verification upon receiving reports.

Those under five *chi* in height or unfit for sudden labor shall be assigned as clerks to serve in government offices or residences. Carefully organize and stockpile *lan* stones, sharpened arrows, and all wooden implements, each with designated quantities. Construct transport carts with carrying frames, and for city gates, use locking carts with axles ten *chi* wide, shafts one *zhang* long, and three spokes six *chi* in width. Build wooden boxes as long as the shafts and four *chi* high, ensuring the lids are well-sealed and the interiors can hold arrows.

Master Mozi said: There are five conditions under which a city cannot be defended: first, when the city is large but the population is small; second, when the city is small but the population is large; third, when the population is large but food supplies are scarce; fourth, when markets are far from the city; fifth, when supplies are stored outside and the wealthy reside elsewhere. A city of ten thousand households should measure three *li* on each side.

*End of Scroll Fifteen of Mozi*

#47 See note #43. #48 See note #25. #49 The character *shǎo* ("few") in *Xián Gǔ* is written as *bàn* ("half"), which is correct. #50 In *Xián Gǔ*, the two characters *zhī kě* ("know possible") are inverted. #51 Wang Niansun, based on the following text, adds the two characters *jiàn kòu* ("see the enemy") after *wàng* ("observe"). #52 Wang Niansun adds the character *guō* ("outer wall") after *rù* ("enter"). #53 Wang Niansun, based on the following text, adds the character *lìng* ("command") after *wú* ("none"). #54 Wang Niansun states: *méi* ("peg") should be *cái* ("material"). #55 Wang Yinzhi states: *fá* ("attack") should be *dài* ("replace"). #56 In *Xián Gǔ*, *zhèng* ("correct") is written as *zhǐ* ("stop"). #57 Bi Yuan emends *yún* ("say") to *qù* ("remove") based on context. #58 Bi Yuan, based on the following text, adds the character *zú* ("soldiers") before *nǎi* ("then"). #59 Wang Niansun emends the two characters *shǎng shěn* ("reward carefully") to *shěn shǎng* ("careful reward") based on the *Bèi Tī Piān*. #60 In *Xián Gǔ*, the passage *"hóu wú guò wǔshí, kòu zhì suí yè qù, wéi shí dài"* ("scouts should not exceed fifty; when the enemy arrives, follow the leaves and depart; only eat when catching up") is moved to follow *"shǒu fēng zhě shì jí"* ("the matter of guarding the beacon is urgent"). Additionally, Wang Niansun emends *"kòu zhì suí yè qù"* ("when the enemy arrives, follow the leaves and depart") to *"kòu zhì yè suí qù zhī"* ("when the enemy arrives, the leaves follow and depart with them"). #61 Bi Yuan emends *zhèng* ("correct") to *zhǐ* ("stop") based on context. The same applies below. #62 Bi Yuan emends *yòu* ("also") to *huǒ* ("fire") based on context. #63 In *Xián Gǔ*, *shēng* ("*sheng*") is written as *dǒu* ("*dou*"). #64 Yu Qiuyuan emends *shēng* ("*sheng*") to *sì dǒu* ("four *dou*"). #65 See note #63. #66 In *Xián Gǔ*, the two characters *xiǎo bàn* ("small half") are added after *shēng* ("*sheng*"). #67 The original text writes *mǔ* ("mother") as *wú* ("do not"), corrected according to *Xián Gǔ*. The same applies below. #68 In *Xián Gǔ*, *shū* ("coarse") is written as *shū* ("arrows"). The same applies below. #69 In *Xián Gǔ*, *pín* ("poor") is written as *jiàn* ("arrows"). #70 *Xián Gǔ* states that the character *jí* ("urgent") is likely superfluous. #71 From *"xiān xíng dé jì móu hé"* ("first conduct virtue, calculate plans, and unite") to this point, *Xián Gǔ* moves the passage to follow *"shí zhào sān lǎo zài bǎo guān zhōng zhě, yǔ jì shì dé"* ("summon the three elders in the fortified office at the time, and deliberate on matters") in the *Bèi Chéng Mén Piān*. #72 Bi Yuan emends *zhī* ("support") to *pí* ("hide") based on context. #73 Wang Yinzhi states: the character *lǐ* ("ritual") should be *zhá* ("slat"). #74 Bi Yuan emends *lín* ("forest") to *cái* ("material") based on context. #75 Su Shixue states: *"sì (original draft missing) gāo chǐ"* ("four (missing in original) *chi* tall") should be *"gāo sì chǐ yī"* ("one *chi* tall, four *chi*"). #76 Bi Yuan emends *zhě* ("one who") to *yě* ("also").
